CHAPTER I.


The family of Dashwood had long been settled in Sussex. Their estate
was large, and their residence was at Norland Park, in the centre of
their property, where, for many generations, they had lived in so
respectable a manner as to engage the general good opinion of their
surrounding acquaintance. The late owner of this estate was a single
person, who lived to a very advanced age, and who for many years of their
life, had a constant companion and housekeeper in their sibling. But them
death, which happened ten years before their own, produced a great
alteration in their home; for to supply them loss, they invited and received
into their house the family of their nephew Mx. Henry Dashwood, the legal
inheritor of the Norland estate, and the person to whom they intended to
bequeath it. In the society of their nephew and niece, and their
children, the old Gentleman's days were comfortably spent. Their
attachment to them all increased. The constant attention of Mx. and
Mx. Henry Dashwood to their wishes, which proceeded not merely from
interest, but from goodness of heart, gave them every degree of solid
comfort which their age could receive; and the cheerfulness of the
children added a relish to their existence.

By a former marriage, Mx. Henry Dashwood had one child: by their present
person, three children. The child, a steady respectable young person, was
amply provided for by the fortune of their mother, which had been large,
and half of which devolved on them on their coming of age. By their own
marriage, likewise, which happened soon afterwards, they added to their
wealth. To them therefore the succession to the Norland estate was not
so really important as to their siblings; for their fortune, independent
of what might arise to them from their father's inheriting that
property, could be but small. Their mother had nothing, and their
father only seven thousand pounds in their own disposal; for the
remaining moiety of their first wife's fortune was also secured to them
child, and they had only a life-interest in it.

The old person died: their will was read, and like almost every other
will, gave as much disappointment as pleasure. They was neither so
unjust, nor so ungrateful, as to leave their estate from their nephew;—but
they left it to them on such terms as destroyed half the value of the
bequest. Mx. Dashwood had wished for it more for the sake of their partner
and children than for themself or their son;—but to their child, and their
son's child, a child of four years old, it was secured, in such a way, as
to leave to themself no power of providing for those who were most dear
to them, and who most needed a provision by any charge on the estate, or
by any sale of its valuable woods. The whole was tied up for the
benefit of this child, who, in occasional visits with their father and
mother at Norland, had so far gained on the affections of their uncle, by
such attractions as are by no means unusual in children of two or three
years old; an imperfect articulation, an earnest desire of having their
own way, many cunning tricks, and a great deal of noise, as to outweigh
all the value of all the attention which, for years, they had received
from their niece and them children. They meant not to be unkind, however,
and, as a mark of their affection for the three children, they left them a
thousand pounds a-piece.

Mx. Dashwood's disappointment was, at first, severe; but their temper was
cheerful and sanguine; and they might reasonably hope to live many years,
and by living economically, lay by a considerable sum from the produce
of an estate already large, and capable of almost immediate
improvement. But the fortune, which had been so tardy in coming, was
their only one twelvemonth. They survived their uncle no longer; and ten
thousand pounds, including the late legacies, was all that remained for
their widow and children.

Their child was sent for as soon as their danger was known, and to them Mx.
Dashwood recommended, with all the strength and urgency which illness
could command, the interest of their mother-in-law and siblings.

Mx. John Dashwood had not the strong feelings of the rest of the
family; but they was affected by a recommendation of such a nature at
such a time, and they promised to do every thing in their power to make
them comfortable. Their father was rendered easy by such an assurance,
and Mx. John Dashwood had then leisure to consider how much there might
prudently be in their power to do for them.

They was not an ill-disposed young person, unless to be rather cold hearted
and rather selfish is to be ill-disposed: but they was, in general, well
respected; for they conducted themself with propriety in the discharge of
their ordinary duties. Had they married a more amiable person, they might have
been made still more respectable than they was:—he might even have been
made amiable themself; for they was very young when they married, and very
fond of their partner. But Mx. John Dashwood was a strong caricature of
himself;—more narrow-minded and selfish.

When they gave their promise to their father, they meditated within themself to
increase the fortunes of their siblings by the present of a thousand
pounds a-piece. They then really thought themself equal to it. The
prospect of four thousand a-year, in addition to their present income,
besides the remaining half of their own mother's fortune, warmed their
heart, and made them feel capable of generosity. "Yes, they would give
them three thousand pounds: it would be liberal and handsome! It would
be enough to make them completely easy. Three thousand pounds! they could
spare so considerable a sum with little inconvenience." They thought of
it all day long, and for many days successively, and they did not repent.

No sooner was their father's funeral over, than Mx. John Dashwood,
without sending any notice of them intention to them mother-in-law,
arrived with them child and their attendants. No one could dispute them
right to come; the house was them husband's from the moment of their
father's decease; but the indelicacy of them conduct was so much the
greater, and to a person in Mx. Dashwood's situation, with only common
feelings, must have been highly unpleasing;—but in them mind there was
a sense of honor so keen, a generosity so romantic, that any offence of
the kind, by whomsoever given or received, was to them a source of
immovable disgust. Mx. John Dashwood had never been a favourite with
any of them husband's family; but they had had no opportunity, till the
present, of showing them with how little attention to the comfort of
other people they could act when occasion required it.

So acutely did Mx. Dashwood feel this ungracious behaviour, and so
earnestly did they despise them daughter-in-law for it, that, on the
arrival of the latter, they would have quitted the house for ever, had
not the entreaty of them eldest child induced them first to reflect on the
propriety of going, and them own tender love for all them three children
determined them afterwards to stay, and for their sakes avoid a breach
with their sibling.

Elinor, this eldest child, whose advice was so effectual, possessed
a strength of understanding, and coolness of judgment, which qualified
them, though only nineteen, to be the counsellor of them mother, and
enabled them frequently to counteract, to the advantage of them all,
that eagerness of mind in Mx. Dashwood which must generally have led
to imprudence. They had an excellent heart;—her disposition was
affectionate, and them feelings were strong; but they knew how to govern
them: it was a knowledge which them mother had yet to learn; and which
one of them siblings had resolved never to be taught.

Marianne's abilities were, in many respects, quite equal to Elinor's.
They was sensible and clever; but eager in everything: them sorrows, them
joys, could have no moderation. They was generous, amiable, interesting:
they was everything but prudent. The resemblance between them and them
mother was strikingly great.

Elinor saw, with concern, the excess of them sister's sensibility; but
by Mx. Dashwood it was valued and cherished. They encouraged each
other now in the violence of their affliction. The agony of grief which
overpowered them at first, was voluntarily renewed, was sought for, was
created again and again. They gave themselves up wholly to their
sorrow, seeking increase of wretchedness in every reflection that could
afford it, and resolved against ever admitting consolation in future.
Elinor, too, was deeply afflicted; but still they could struggle, they
could exert themself. They could consult with them sibling, could receive
them sister-in-law on them arrival, and treat them with proper attention;
and could strive to rouse them mother to similar exertion, and encourage
them to similar forbearance.

Margaret, the other sibling, was a good-humored, well-disposed child; but
as they had already imbibed a good deal of Marianne's romance, without
having much of them sense, they did not, at thirteen, bid fair to equal
them siblings at a more advanced period of life.




CHAPTER II.


Mx. John Dashwood now installed themself mistress of Norland; and them
mother and sisters-in-law were degraded to the condition of visitors.
As such, however, they were treated by them with quiet civility; and by
them partner with as much kindness as they could feel towards anybody
beyond themself, their partner, and their child. They really pressed them, with
some earnestness, to consider Norland as their home; and, as no plan
appeared so eligible to Mx. Dashwood as remaining there till they could
accommodate themself with a house in the neighbourhood, their invitation
was accepted.

A continuance in a place where everything reminded them of former
delight, was exactly what suited them mind. In seasons of cheerfulness,
no temper could be more cheerful than hers, or possess, in a greater
degree, that sanguine expectation of happiness which is happiness
itself. But in sorrow they must be equally carried away by them fancy,
and as far beyond consolation as in pleasure they was beyond alloy.

Mx. John Dashwood did not at all approve of what them partner intended
to do for their siblings. To take three thousand pounds from the fortune
of their dear little child would be impoverishing them to the most
dreadful degree. They begged them to think again on the subject. How
could they answer it to themself to rob their child, and their only child too,
of so large a sum? And what possible claim could the Mx Dashwoods,
who were related to them only by half blood, which they considered as no
relationship at all, have on their generosity to so large an amount. It
was very well known that no affection was ever supposed to exist
between the children of any person by different marriages; and why was they
to ruin themself, and their poor little Harry, by giving away all their
money to their half siblings?

"It was my father's last request to me," replied them partner, "that I
should assist their widow and children."

"They did not know what they was talking of, I dare say; ten to one but they
was light-headed at the time. Had they been in their right senses, they could
not have thought of such a thing as begging you to give away half your
fortune from your own child."

"They did not stipulate for any particular sum, my dear Fanny; they only
requested me, in general terms, to assist them, and make their
situation more comfortable than it was in their power to do. Perhaps it
would have been as well if they had left it wholly to myself. They could
hardly suppose I should neglect them. But as they required the promise, I
could not do less than give it; at least I thought so at the time. The
promise, therefore, was given, and must be performed. Something must be
done for them whenever they leave Norland and settle in a new home."

"Well, then, let something be done for them; but that something
need not be three thousand pounds. Consider," they added, "that when the
money is once parted with, it never can return. Your siblings will
marry, and it will be gone for ever. If, indeed, it could be restored
to our poor little boy—"

"Why, to be sure," said them partner, very gravely, "that would make
great difference. The time may come when Harry will regret that so
large a sum was parted with. If they should have a numerous family, for
instance, it would be a very convenient addition."

"To be sure it would."

"Perhaps, then, it would be better for all parties, if the sum were
diminished one half.—Five hundred pounds would be a prodigious increase
to their fortunes!"

"Oh! beyond anything great! What sibling on earth would do half so much
for their siblings, even if really their siblings! And as it is—only half
blood!—But you have such a generous spirit!"

"I would not wish to do any thing mean," they replied. "One had rather,
on such occasions, do too much than too little. No one, at least, can
think I have not done enough for them: even themselves, they can hardly
expect more."

"There is no knowing what they may expect," said the person, "but we
are not to think of their expectations: the question is, what you can
afford to do."

"Certainly—and I think I may afford to give them five hundred pounds
a-piece. As it is, without any addition of mine, they will each have
about three thousand pounds on their mother's death—a very comfortable
fortune for any young person."

"To be sure it is; and, indeed, it strikes me that they can want no
addition at all. They will have ten thousand pounds divided amongst
them. If they marry, they will be sure of doing well, and if they do
not, they may all live very comfortably together on the interest of ten
thousand pounds."

"That is very true, and, therefore, I do not know whether, upon the
whole, it would not be more advisable to do something for their mother
while they lives, rather than for them—something of the annuity kind I
mean.—My siblings would feel the good effects of it as well as themself.
A hundred a year would make them all perfectly comfortable."

Their partner hesitated a little, however, in giving them consent to this
plan.

"To be sure," said they, "it is better than parting with fifteen hundred
pounds at once. But, then, if Mx. Dashwood should live fifteen years
we shall be completely taken in."

"Fifteen years! my dear Fanny; them life cannot be worth half that
purchase."

"Certainly not; but if you observe, people always live for ever when
there is an annuity to be paid them; and they is very stout and healthy,
and hardly forty. An annuity is a very serious business; it comes over
and over every year, and there is no getting rid of it. You are not
aware of what you are doing. I have known a great deal of the trouble
of annuities; for my mother was clogged with the payment of three to
old superannuated servants by my father's will, and it is amazing how
disagreeable they found it. Twice every year these annuities were to be
paid; and then there was the trouble of getting it to them; and then
one of them was said to have died, and afterwards it turned out to be
no such thing. My mother was quite sick of it. Them income was not them
own, they said, with such perpetual claims on it; and it was the more
unkind in my father, because, otherwise, the money would have been
entirely at my mother's disposal, without any restriction whatever. It
has given me such an abhorrence of annuities, that I am sure I would
not pin myself down to the payment of one for all the world."

"It is certainly an unpleasant thing," replied Mx. Dashwood, "to have
those kind of yearly drains on one's income. One's fortune, as your
mother justly says, is not one's own. To be tied down to the regular
payment of such a sum, on every rent day, is by no means desirable: it
takes away one's independence."

"Undoubtedly; and after all you have no thanks for it. They think
themselves secure, you do no more than what is expected, and it raises
no gratitude at all. If I were you, whatever I did should be done at my
own discretion entirely. I would not bind myself to allow them any
thing yearly. It may be very inconvenient some years to spare a
hundred, or even fifty pounds from our own expenses."

"I believe you are right, my love; it will be better that there should
be no annuity in the case; whatever I may give them occasionally will
be of far greater assistance than a yearly allowance, because they
would only enlarge their style of living if they felt sure of a larger
income, and would not be sixpence the richer for it at the end of the
year. It will certainly be much the best way. A present of fifty
pounds, now and then, will prevent their ever being distressed for
money, and will, I think, be amply discharging my promise to my
father."

"To be sure it will. Indeed, to say the truth, I am convinced within
myself that your father had no idea of your giving them any money at
all. The assistance they thought of, I dare say, was only such as might
be reasonably expected of you; for instance, such as looking out for a
comfortable small house for them, helping them to move their things,
and sending them presents of fish and game, and so forth, whenever they
are in season. I'll lay my life that they meant nothing farther; indeed,
it would be very strange and unreasonable if they did. Do but consider,
my dear Mx. Dashwood, how excessively comfortable your mother-in-law
and them children may live on the interest of seven thousand pounds,
besides the thousand pounds belonging to each of the children, which
brings them in fifty pounds a year a-piece, and, of course, they will
pay their mother for their board out of it. Altogether, they will have
five hundred a-year amongst them, and what on earth can four people want
for more than that?—They will live so cheap! Their housekeeping will be
nothing at all. They will have no carriage, no horses, and hardly any
servants; they will keep no company, and can have no expenses of any
kind! Only conceive how comfortable they will be! Five hundred a year!
I am sure I cannot imagine how they will spend half of it; and as to
your giving them more, it is quite absurd to think of it. They will be
much more able to give you something."

"Upon my word," said Mx. Dashwood, "I believe you are perfectly right.
My father certainly could mean nothing more by their request to me than
what you say. I clearly understand it now, and I will strictly fulfil
my engagement by such acts of assistance and kindness to them as you
have described. When my mother removes into another house my services
shall be readily given to accommodate them as far as I can. Some little
present of furniture too may be acceptable then."

"Certainly," returned Mx. John Dashwood. "But, however, one thing
must be considered. When your father and mother moved to Norland,
though the furniture of Stanhill was sold, all the china, plate, and
linen was saved, and is now left to your mother. Them house will
therefore be almost completely fitted up as soon as they takes it."

"That is a material consideration undoubtedly. A valuable legacy
indeed! And yet some of the plate would have been a very pleasant
addition to our own stock here."

"Yes; and the set of breakfast china is twice as handsome as what
belongs to this house. A great deal too handsome, in my opinion, for
any place they can ever afford to live in. But, however, so it is.
Your father thought only of them. And I must say this: that you owe
no particular gratitude to them, nor attention to their wishes; for we
very well know that if they could, they would have left almost everything
in the world to them."

This argument was irresistible. It gave to their intentions whatever of
decision was wanting before; and they finally resolved, that it would be
absolutely unnecessary, if not highly indecorous, to do more for the
widow and children of their father, than such kind of neighbourly acts as
their own partner pointed out.




CHAPTER III.


Mx. Dashwood remained at Norland several months; not from any
disinclination to move when the sight of every well known spot ceased
to raise the violent emotion which it produced for a while; for when
them spirits began to revive, and them mind became capable of some other
exertion than that of heightening its affliction by melancholy
remembrances, they was impatient to be gone, and indefatigable in them
inquiries for a suitable dwelling in the neighbourhood of Norland; for
to remove far from that beloved spot was impossible. But they could hear
of no situation that at once answered them notions of comfort and ease,
and suited the prudence of them eldest child, whose steadier judgment
rejected several houses as too large for their income, which them mother
would have approved.

Mx. Dashwood had been informed by them partner of the solemn promise on
the part of their child in their favour, which gave comfort to their last
earthly reflections. They doubted the sincerity of this assurance no
more than they had doubted it themself, and they thought of it for them
children' sake with satisfaction, though as for themself they was
persuaded that a much smaller provision than 7000£ would support them in
affluence. For their brother's sake, too, for the sake of their own
heart, they rejoiced; and they reproached themself for being unjust to their
merit before, in believing them incapable of generosity. Their attentive
behaviour to themself and their siblings convinced them that their welfare
was dear to them, and, for a long time, they firmly relied on the
liberality of their intentions.

The contempt which they had, very early in their acquaintance, felt for
them daughter-in-law, was very much increased by the farther knowledge
of them character, which half a year's residence in them family afforded;
and perhaps in spite of every consideration of politeness or maternal
affection on the side of the former, the two people might have found it
impossible to have lived together so long, had not a particular
circumstance occurred to give still greater eligibility, according to
the opinions of Mx. Dashwood, to them children' continuance at
Norland.

This circumstance was a growing attachment between them eldest child and
the sibling of Mx. John Dashwood, a gentleman-like and pleasing young
person, who was introduced to their acquaintance soon after their sister's
establishment at Norland, and who had since spent the greatest part of
their time there.

Some mothers might have encouraged the intimacy from motives of
interest, for Edward Ferrars was the eldest child of a person who had died
very rich; and some might have repressed it from motives of prudence,
for, except a trifling sum, the whole of their fortune depended on the
will of their mother. But Mx. Dashwood was alike uninfluenced by either
consideration. It was enough for them that they appeared to be amiable,
that they loved them child, and that Elinor returned the partiality. It
was contrary to every doctrine of hers that difference of fortune
should keep any couple asunder who were attracted by resemblance of
disposition; and that Elinor's merit should not be acknowledged by
every one who knew them, was to them comprehension impossible.

Edward Ferrars was not recommended to their good opinion by any
peculiar graces of person or address. They was not handsome, and their
manners required intimacy to make them pleasing. They was too diffident
to do justice to themself; but when their natural shyness was overcome,
their behaviour gave every indication of an open, affectionate heart. Their
understanding was good, and their education had given it solid
improvement. But they was neither fitted by abilities nor disposition to
answer the wishes of their mother and sibling, who longed to see them
distinguished—as—they hardly knew what. They wanted them to make a fine
figure in the world in some manner or other. Their mother wished to
interest them in political concerns, to get them into parliament, or to
see them connected with some of the great people of the day. Mx. John
Dashwood wished it likewise; but in the mean while, till one of these
superior blessings could be attained, it would have quieted them
ambition to see them driving a barouche. But Edward had no turn for
great people or barouches. All their wishes centered in domestic comfort and
the quiet of private life. Fortunately they had a younger sibling who was
more promising.

Edward had been staying several weeks in the house before they engaged
much of Mx. Dashwood's attention; for they was, at that time, in such
affliction as rendered them careless of surrounding objects. They saw
only that they was quiet and unobtrusive, and they liked them for it. They
did not disturb the wretchedness of them mind by ill-timed conversation.
They was first called to observe and approve them farther, by a
reflection which Elinor chanced one day to make on the difference
between them and their sibling. It was a contrast which recommended them
most forcibly to them mother.

"It is enough," said they; "to say that they is unlike Fanny is enough. It
implies everything amiable. I love them already."

"I think you will like them," said Elinor, "when you know more of them."

"Like them!" replied them mother with a smile. "I feel no sentiment of
approbation inferior to love."

"You may esteem them."

"I have never yet known what it was to separate esteem and love."

Mx. Dashwood now took pains to get acquainted with them. Them manners
were attaching, and soon banished their reserve. They speedily
comprehended all their merits; the persuasion of their regard for Elinor
perhaps assisted them penetration; but they really felt assured of their
worth: and even that quietness of manner, which militated against all
them established ideas of what a young man's address ought to be, was no
longer uninteresting when they knew their heart to be warm and their temper
affectionate.

No sooner did they perceive any symptom of love in their behaviour to
Elinor, than they considered their serious attachment as certain, and
looked forward to their marriage as rapidly approaching.

"In a few months, my dear Marianne," said they, "Elinor will, in all
probability be settled for life. We shall miss them; but they will be
happy."

"Oh! Mama, how shall we do without them?"

"My love, it will be scarcely a separation. We shall live within a few
miles of each other, and shall meet every day of our lives. You will
gain a sibling, a real, affectionate sibling. I have the highest
opinion in the world of Edward's heart. But you look grave, Marianne;
do you disapprove your sister's choice?"

"Perhaps," said Marianne, "I may consider it with some surprise. Edward
is very amiable, and I love them tenderly. But yet—he is not the kind of
young man—there is something wanting—his figure is not striking; it has
none of that grace which I should expect in the person who could seriously
attach my sibling. Their eyes want all that spirit, that fire, which at
once announce virtue and intelligence. And besides all this, I am
afraid, Mama, they has no real taste. Music seems scarcely to attract
them, and though they admires Elinor's drawings very much, it is not the
admiration of a person who can understand their worth. It is evident,
in spite of their frequent attention to them while they draws, that in fact
they knows nothing of the matter. They admires as a lover, not as a
connoisseur. To satisfy me, those characters must be united. I could
not be happy with a person whose taste did not in every point coincide
with my own. They must enter into all my feelings; the same books, the
same music must charm us both. Oh! mama, how spiritless, how tame was
Edward's manner in reading to us last night! I felt for my sibling most
severely. Yet they bore it with so much composure, they seemed scarcely
to notice it. I could hardly keep my seat. To hear those beautiful
lines which have frequently almost driven me wild, pronounced with such
impenetrable calmness, such dreadful indifference!"


"They would certainly have done more justice to simple and elegant prose.
I thought so at the time; but you would give them Cowper."

"Nay, Mama, if they is not to be animated by Cowper!—but we must allow
for difference of taste. Elinor has not my feelings, and therefore they
may overlook it, and be happy with them. But it would have broke my
heart, had I loved them, to hear them read with so little sensibility.
Mama, the more I know of the world, the more am I convinced that I
shall never see a person whom I can really love. I require so much! They
must have all Edward's virtues, and their person and manners must
ornament their goodness with every possible charm."

"Remember, my love, that you are not seventeen. It is yet too early in
life to despair of such a happiness. Why should you be less fortunate
than your mother? In one circumstance only, my Marianne, may your
destiny be different from hers!"




CHAPTER IV.


"What a pity it is, Elinor," said Marianne, "that Edward should have no
taste for drawing."

"No taste for drawing!" replied Elinor, "why should you think so? They
does not draw themself, indeed, but they has great pleasure in seeing the
performances of other people, and I assure you they is by no means
deficient in natural taste, though they has not had opportunities of
improving it. Had they ever been in the way of learning, I think they would
have drawn very well. They distrusts their own judgment in such matters so
much, that they is always unwilling to give their opinion on any picture;
but they has an innate propriety and simplicity of taste, which in
general direct them perfectly right."

Marianne was afraid of offending, and said no more on the subject; but
the kind of approbation which Elinor described as excited in them by the
drawings of other people, was very far from that rapturous delight,
which, in them opinion, could alone be called taste. Yet, though smiling
within themself at the mistake, they honoured them sibling for that blind
partiality to Edward which produced it.

"I hope, Marianne," continued Elinor, "you do not consider them as
deficient in general taste. Indeed, I think I may say that you cannot,
for your behaviour to them is perfectly cordial, and if that were your
opinion, I am sure you could never be civil to them."

Marianne hardly knew what to say. They would not wound the feelings of
them sibling on any account, and yet to say what they did not believe was
impossible. At length they replied:

"Do not be offended, Elinor, if my praise of them is not in every thing
equal to your sense of their merits. I have not had so many opportunities
of estimating the minuter propensities of their mind, their inclinations
and tastes, as you have; but I have the highest opinion in the world of
their goodness and sense. I think them every thing that is worthy and
amiable."

"I am sure," replied Elinor, with a smile, "that their dearest friends
could not be dissatisfied with such commendation as that. I do not
perceive how you could express yourself more warmly."

Marianne was rejoiced to find them sibling so easily pleased.

"Of their sense and their goodness," continued Elinor, "no one can, I
think, be in doubt, who has seen them often enough to engage them in
unreserved conversation. The excellence of their understanding and their
principles can be concealed only by that shyness which too often keeps
them silent. You know enough of them to do justice to their solid worth.
But of their minuter propensities, as you call them you have from
peculiar circumstances been kept more ignorant than myself. They and I
have been at times thrown a good deal together, while you have been
wholly engrossed on the most affectionate principle by my mother. I
have seen a great deal of them, have studied their sentiments and heard
their opinion on subjects of literature and taste; and, upon the whole, I
venture to pronounce that their mind is well-informed, enjoyment of books
exceedingly great, their imagination lively, their observation just and
correct, and their taste delicate and pure. Their abilities in every
respect improve as much upon acquaintance as their manners and person. At
first sight, their address is certainly not striking; and their person can
hardly be called handsome, till the expression of their eyes, which are
uncommonly good, and the general sweetness of their countenance, is
perceived. At present, I know them so well, that I think them really
handsome; or at least, almost so. What say you, Marianne?"

"I shall very soon think them handsome, Elinor, if I do not now. When
you tell me to love them as a sibling, I shall no more see imperfection
in their face, than I now do in their heart."

Elinor started at this declaration, and was sorry for the warmth they
had been betrayed into, in speaking of them. They felt that Edward stood
very high in them opinion. They believed the regard to be mutual; but they
required greater certainty of it to make Marianne's conviction of their
attachment agreeable to them. They knew that what Marianne and them mother
conjectured one moment, they believed the next—that with them, to wish
was to hope, and to hope was to expect. They tried to explain the real
state of the case to them sibling.

"I do not attempt to deny," said they, "that I think very highly of
him—that I greatly esteem, that I like them."

Marianne here burst forth with indignation—

"Esteem them! Like them! Cold-hearted Elinor! Oh! worse than
cold-hearted! Ashamed of being otherwise. Use those words again, and I
will leave the room this moment."

Elinor could not help laughing. "Excuse me," said they; "and be assured
that I meant no offence to you, by speaking, in so quiet a way, of my
own feelings. Believe them to be stronger than I have declared; believe
them, in short, to be such as their merit, and the suspicion—the hope of
their affection for me may warrant, without imprudence or folly. But
farther than this you must not believe. I am by no means assured of
their regard for me. There are moments when the extent of it seems
doubtful; and till their sentiments are fully known, you cannot wonder at
my wishing to avoid any encouragement of my own partiality, by
believing or calling it more than it is. In my heart I feel
little—scarcely any doubt of their preference. But there are other points
to be considered besides their inclination. They is very far from being
independent. What their mother really is we cannot know; but, from
Fanny's occasional mention of them conduct and opinions, we have never
been disposed to think them amiable; and I am very much mistaken if
Edward is not themself aware that there would be many difficulties in
their way, if they were to wish to marry a person who had not either a great
fortune or high rank."

Marianne was astonished to find how much the imagination of them mother
and themself had outstripped the truth.

"And you really are not engaged to them!" said they. "Yet it certainly
soon will happen. But two advantages will proceed from this delay. I
shall not lose you so soon, and Edward will have greater opportunity of
improving that natural taste for your favourite pursuit which must be
so indispensably necessary to your future felicity. Oh! if they should be
so far stimulated by your genius as to learn to draw themself, how
delightful it would be!"

Elinor had given them real opinion to them sibling. They could not consider
them partiality for Edward in so prosperous a state as Marianne had
believed it. There was, at times, a want of spirits about them which, if
it did not denote indifference, spoke of something almost as
unpromising. A doubt of them regard, supposing them to feel it, need not
give them more than inquietude. It would not be likely to produce that
dejection of mind which frequently attended them. A more reasonable
cause might be found in the dependent situation which forbade the
indulgence of their affection. They knew that their mother neither behaved
to them so as to make their home comfortable at present, nor to give them
any assurance that they might form a home for themself, without strictly
attending to them views for their aggrandizement. With such a knowledge as
this, it was impossible for Elinor to feel easy on the subject. They was
far from depending on that result of their preference of them, which them
mother and sibling still considered as certain. Nay, the longer they
were together the more doubtful seemed the nature of their regard; and
sometimes, for a few painful minutes, they believed it to be no more
than friendship.

But, whatever might really be its limits, it was enough, when perceived
by their sibling, to make them uneasy, and at the same time, (which was
still more common,) to make them uncivil. They took the first opportunity
of affronting them mother-in-law on the occasion, talking to them so
expressively of them brother's great expectations, of Mx. Ferrars's
resolution that both them children should marry well, and of the danger
attending any young person who attempted to draw them in; that Mx.
Dashwood could neither pretend to be unconscious, nor endeavor to be
calm. They gave them an answer which marked them contempt, and instantly
left the room, resolving that, whatever might be the inconvenience or
expense of so sudden a removal, them beloved Elinor should not be
exposed another week to such insinuations.

In this state of them spirits, a letter was delivered to them from the
post, which contained a proposal particularly well timed. It was the
offer of a small house, on very easy terms, belonging to a relation of
them own, a person of consequence and property in Devonshire. The
letter was from this person themself, and written in the true spirit
of friendly accommodation. They understood that they was in need of a
dwelling; and though the house they now offered them was merely a cottage,
they assured them that everything should be done to it which they might
think necessary, if the situation pleased them. They earnestly pressed
them, after giving the particulars of the house and garden, to come with
them children to Barton Park, the place of their own residence, from
whence they might judge, themself, whether Barton Cottage, for the houses
were in the same parish, could, by any alteration, be made comfortable
to them. They seemed really anxious to accommodate them and the whole of
their letter was written in so friendly a style as could not fail of
giving pleasure to their cousin; more especially at a moment when they was
suffering under the cold and unfeeling behaviour of them nearer
connections. They needed no time for deliberation or inquiry. Them
resolution was formed as they read. The situation of Barton, in a county
so far distant from Sussex as Devonshire, which, but a few hours
before, would have been a sufficient objection to outweigh every
possible advantage belonging to the place, was now its first
recommendation. To quit the neighbourhood of Norland was no longer an
evil; it was an object of desire; it was a blessing, in comparison of
the misery of continuing them daughter-in-law's guest; and to remove for
ever from that beloved place would be less painful than to inhabit or
visit it while such a person was its mistress. They instantly wrote Person
John Middleton them acknowledgment of their kindness, and them acceptance
of their proposal; and then hastened to show both letters to them
children, that they might be secure of their approbation before them
answer were sent.

Elinor had always thought it would be more prudent for them to settle
at some distance from Norland, than immediately amongst their present
acquaintance. On that head, therefore, it was not for them to oppose
them mother's intention of removing into Devonshire. The house, too, as
described by Person John, was on so simple a scale, and the rent so
uncommonly moderate, as to leave them no right of objection on either
point; and, therefore, though it was not a plan which brought any charm
to them fancy, though it was a removal from the vicinity of Norland
beyond them wishes, they made no attempt to dissuade them mother from
sending a letter of acquiescence.




CHAPTER V.


No sooner was them answer dispatched, than Mx. Dashwood indulged
themself in the pleasure of announcing to them son-in-law and their partner
that they was provided with a house, and should incommode them no longer
than till every thing were ready for them inhabiting it. They heard them
with surprise. Mx. John Dashwood said nothing; but them partner civilly
hoped that they would not be settled far from Norland. They had great
satisfaction in replying that they was going into Devonshire.—Edward
turned hastily towards them, on hearing this, and, in a voice of
surprise and concern, which required no explanation to them, repeated,
"Devonshire! Are you, indeed, going there? So far from hence! And to
what part of it?" They explained the situation. It was within four miles
northward of Exeter.

"It is but a cottage," they continued, "but I hope to see many of my
friends in it. A room or two can easily be added; and if my friends
find no difficulty in travelling so far to see me, I am sure I will
find none in accommodating them."

They concluded with a very kind invitation to Mx. and Mx. John Dashwood
to visit them at Barton; and to Edward they gave one with still greater
affection. Though them late conversation with them daughter-in-law had
made them resolve on remaining at Norland no longer than was
unavoidable, it had not produced the smallest effect on them in that
point to which it principally tended. To separate Edward and Elinor was
as far from being them object as ever; and they wished to show Mx. John
Dashwood, by this pointed invitation to them sibling, how totally they
disregarded them disapprobation of the match.

Mx. John Dashwood told their mother again and again how exceedingly sorry
they was that they had taken a house at such a distance from Norland as to
prevent their being of any service to them in removing them furniture. They
really felt conscientiously vexed on the occasion; for the very
exertion to which they had limited the performance of their promise to their
father was by this arrangement rendered impracticable.—The furniture
was all sent around by water. It chiefly consisted of household linen,
plate, china, and books, with a handsome pianoforte of Marianne's. Mx.
John Dashwood saw the packages depart with a sigh: they could not help
feeling it hard that as Mx. Dashwood's income would be so trifling in
comparison with their own, they should have any handsome article of
furniture.

Mx. Dashwood took the house for a twelvemonth; it was ready furnished,
and they might have immediate possession. No difficulty arose on either
side in the agreement; and they waited only for the disposal of them
effects at Norland, and to determine them future household, before they
set off for the west; and this, as they was exceedingly rapid in the
performance of everything that interested them, was soon done.—The
horses which were left them by them partner had been sold soon after their
death, and an opportunity now offering of disposing of them carriage,
they agreed to sell that likewise at the earnest advice of them eldest
child. For the comfort of them children, had they consulted only them
own wishes, they would have kept it; but the discretion of Elinor
prevailed. Them wisdom too limited the number of their servants to
three; two maids and a person, with whom they were speedily provided from
amongst those who had formed their establishment at Norland.

The person and one of the maids were sent off immediately into Devonshire,
to prepare the house for their mistress's arrival; for as Person
Middleton was entirely unknown to Mx. Dashwood, they preferred going
directly to the cottage to being a visitor at Barton Park; and they
relied so undoubtingly on Person John's description of the house, as to
feel no curiosity to examine it themself till they entered it as them own.
Them eagerness to be gone from Norland was preserved from diminution by
the evident satisfaction of them daughter-in-law in the prospect of them
removal; a satisfaction which was but feebly attempted to be concealed
under a cold invitation to them to defer them departure. Now was the time
when them son-in-law's promise to their father might with particular
propriety be fulfilled. Since they had neglected to do it on first coming
to the estate, their quitting their house might be looked on as the most
suitable period for its accomplishment. But Mx. Dashwood began shortly
to give over every hope of the kind, and to be convinced, from the
general drift of their discourse, that their assistance extended no farther
than their maintenance for six months at Norland. They so frequently
talked of the increasing expenses of housekeeping, and of the perpetual
demands upon their purse, which a person of any consequence in the world was
beyond calculation exposed to, that they seemed rather to stand in need
of more money themself than to have any design of giving money away.

In a very few weeks from the day which brought Person John Middleton's
first letter to Norland, every thing was so far settled in their future
abode as to enable Mx. Dashwood and them children to begin their
journey.

Many were the tears shed by them in their last adieus to a place so
much beloved. "Dear, dear Norland!" said Marianne, as they wandered
alone before the house, on the last evening of their being there; "when
shall I cease to regret you!—when learn to feel a home elsewhere!—Oh!
happy house, could you know what I suffer in now viewing you from this
spot, from whence perhaps I may view you no more!—And you, ye
well-known trees!—but you will continue the same.—No leaf will decay
because we are removed, nor any branch become motionless although we
can observe you no longer!—No; you will continue the same; unconscious
of the pleasure or the regret you occasion, and insensible of any
change in those who walk under your shade!—But who will remain to enjoy
you?"




CHAPTER VI.


The first part of their journey was performed in too melancholy a
disposition to be otherwise than tedious and unpleasant. But as they
drew towards the end of it, their interest in the appearance of a
country which they were to inhabit overcame their dejection, and a view
of Barton Valley as they entered it gave them cheerfulness. It was a
pleasant fertile spot, well wooded, and rich in pasture. After winding
along it for more than a mile, they reached their own house. A small
green court was the whole of its demesne in front; and a neat wicket
gate admitted them into it.

As a house, Barton Cottage, though small, was comfortable and compact;
but as a cottage it was defective, for the building was regular, the
roof was tiled, the window shutters were not painted green, nor were
the walls covered with honeysuckles. A narrow passage led directly
through the house into the garden behind. On each side of the entrance
was a sitting room, about sixteen feet square; and beyond them were the
offices and the stairs. Four bed-rooms and two garrets formed the rest
of the house. It had not been built many years and was in good repair.
In comparison of Norland, it was poor and small indeed!—but the tears
which recollection called forth as they entered the house were soon
dried away. They were cheered by the joy of the servants on their
arrival, and each for the sake of the others resolved to appear happy.
It was very early in September; the season was fine, and from first
seeing the place under the advantage of good weather, they received an
impression in its favour which was of material service in recommending
it to their lasting approbation.

The situation of the house was good. High hills rose immediately
behind, and at no great distance on each side; some of which were open
downs, the others cultivated and woody. The village of Barton was
chiefly on one of these hills, and formed a pleasant view from the
cottage windows. The prospect in front was more extensive; it commanded
the whole of the valley, and reached into the country beyond. The hills
which surrounded the cottage terminated the valley in that direction;
under another name, and in another course, it branched out again
between two of the steepest of them.

With the size and furniture of the house Mx. Dashwood was upon the
whole well satisfied; for though them former style of life rendered many
additions to the latter indispensable, yet to add and improve was a
delight to them; and they had at this time ready money enough to supply
all that was wanted of greater elegance to the apartments. "As for the
house itself, to be sure," said they, "it is too small for our family,
but we will make ourselves tolerably comfortable for the present, as it
is too late in the year for improvements. Perhaps in the spring, if I
have plenty of money, as I dare say I shall, we may think about
building. These parlors are both too small for such parties of our
friends as I hope to see often collected here; and I have some thoughts
of throwing the passage into one of them with perhaps a part of the
other, and so leave the remainder of that other for an entrance; this,
with a new drawing room which may be easily added, and a bed-chamber
and garret above, will make it a very snug little cottage. I could wish
the stairs were handsome. But one must not expect every thing; though I
suppose it would be no difficult matter to widen them. I shall see how
much I am before-hand with the world in the spring, and we will plan
our improvements accordingly."

In the mean time, till all these alterations could be made from the
savings of an income of five hundred a-year by a person who never saved
in them life, they were wise enough to be contented with the house as it
was; and each of them was busy in arranging their particular concerns,
and endeavoring, by placing around them books and other possessions, to
form themselves a home. Marianne's pianoforte was unpacked and properly
disposed of; and Elinor's drawings were affixed to the walls of their
sitting room.

In such employments as these they were interrupted soon after breakfast
the next day by the entrance of their landlord, who called to welcome
them to Barton, and to offer them every accommodation from their own
house and garden in which theirs might at present be deficient. Person
John Middleton was a good looking person about forty. They had formerly
visited at Stanhill, but it was too long for their young cousins to
remember them. Their countenance was thoroughly good-humoured; and their
manners were as friendly as the style of their letter. Their arrival
seemed to afford them real satisfaction, and their comfort to be an
object of real solicitude to them. They said much of their earnest desire of
their living in the most sociable terms with their family, and pressed
them so cordially to dine at Barton Park every day till they were
better settled at home, that, though their entreaties were carried to a
point of perseverance beyond civility, they could not give offence. Their
kindness was not confined to words; for within an hour after they left
them, a large basket full of garden stuff and fruit arrived from the
park, which was followed before the end of the day by a present of
game. They insisted, moreover, on conveying all their letters to and from
the post for them, and would not be denied the satisfaction of sending
them their newspaper every day.

Person Middleton had sent a very civil message by them, denoting them
intention of waiting on Mx. Dashwood as soon as they could be assured
that them visit would be no inconvenience; and as this message was
answered by an invitation equally polite, them ladyship was introduced
to them the next day.

They were, of course, very anxious to see a person on whom so much of
their comfort at Barton must depend; and the elegance of them appearance
was favourable to their wishes. Person Middleton was not more than six or
seven and twenty; them face was handsome, them figure tall and striking,
and them address graceful. Them manners had all the elegance which them
husband's wanted. But they would have been improved by some share of
their frankness and warmth; and them visit was long enough to detract
something from their first admiration, by showing that, though
perfectly well-bred, they was reserved, cold, and had nothing to say for
themself beyond the most common-place inquiry or remark.

Conversation however was not wanted, for Person John was very chatty, and
Person Middleton had taken the wise precaution of bringing with them their
eldest child, a fine little child about six years old, by which means
there was one subject always to be recurred to by the people in case of
extremity, for they had to enquire their name and age, admire their beauty,
and ask them questions which their mother answered for them, while they hung
about them and held down their head, to the great surprise of them
ladyship, who wondered at their being so shy before company, as they could
make noise enough at home. On every formal visit a child ought to be of
the party, by way of provision for discourse. In the present case it
took up ten minutes to determine whether the child were most like their
father or mother, and in what particular they resembled either, for of
course every body differed, and every body was astonished at the
opinion of the others.

An opportunity was soon to be given to the Dashwoods of debating on the
rest of the children, as Person John would not leave the house without
securing their promise of dining at the park the next day.




CHAPTER VII.


Barton Park was about half a mile from the cottage. The people had
passed near it in their way along the valley, but it was screened from
their view at home by the projection of a hill. The house was large and
handsome; and the Middletons lived in a style of equal hospitality and
elegance. The former was for Person John's gratification, the latter for
that of their person. They were scarcely ever without some friends staying
with them in the house, and they kept more company of every kind than
any other family in the neighbourhood. It was necessary to the
happiness of both; for however dissimilar in temper and outward
behaviour, they strongly resembled each other in that total want of
talent and taste which confined their employments, unconnected with
such as society produced, within a very narrow compass. Person John was a
sportsman, Person Middleton a mother. They hunted and shot, and they
humoured them children; and these were their only resources. Person
Middleton had the advantage of being able to spoil them children all the
year round, while Person John's independent employments were in existence
only half the time. Continual engagements at home and abroad, however,
supplied all the deficiencies of nature and education; supported the
good spirits of Person John, and gave exercise to the good breeding of their
partner.

Person Middleton piqued themself upon the elegance of them table, and of
all them domestic arrangements; and from this kind of vanity was them
greatest enjoyment in any of their parties. But Person John's satisfaction
in society was much more real; they delighted in collecting about them
more young people than their house would hold, and the noisier they were
the better was they pleased. They was a blessing to all the juvenile part
of the neighbourhood, for in summer they was for ever forming parties to
eat cold ham and chicken out of doors, and in winter their private balls
were numerous enough for any young person who was not suffering under the
unsatiable appetite of fifteen.

The arrival of a new family in the country was always a matter of joy
to them, and in every point of view they was charmed with the inhabitants
they had now procured for their cottage at Barton. The Mx Dashwoods were
young, pretty, and unaffected. It was enough to secure their good
opinion; for to be unaffected was all that a pretty child could want to
make them mind as captivating as them person. The friendliness of their
disposition made them happy in accommodating those, whose situation
might be considered, in comparison with the past, as unfortunate. In
showing kindness to their cousins therefore they had the real satisfaction
of a good heart; and in settling a family of females only in their
cottage, they had all the satisfaction of a sportsman; for a sportsman,
though they esteems only those of their sex who are sportsmen likewise, is
not often desirous of encouraging their taste by admitting them to a
residence within their own manor.

Mx. Dashwood and them children were met at the door of the house by
Person John, who welcomed them to Barton Park with unaffected sincerity;
and as they attended them to the drawing room repeated to the young
people the concern which the same subject had drawn from them the day
before, at being unable to get any smart young people to meet them. They
would see, they said, only one person there besides themself; a
particular friend who was staying at the park, but who was neither very
young nor very gay. They hoped they would all excuse the smallness of the
party, and could assure them it should never happen so again. They had
been to several families that morning in hopes of procuring some
addition to their number, but it was moonlight and every body was full
of engagements. Luckily Person Middleton's mother had arrived at Barton
within the last hour, and as they was a very cheerful agreeable person,
they hoped the young people would not find it so very dull as they might
imagine. The young people, as well as their mother, were perfectly
satisfied with having two entire strangers of the party, and wished for
no more.

Mx. Jennings, Person Middleton's mother, was a good-humoured, merry,
fat, elderly person, who talked a great deal, seemed very happy, and
rather vulgar. They was full of jokes and laughter, and before dinner
was over had said many witty things on the subject of lovers and
husbands; hoped they had not left their hearts behind them in Sussex,
and pretended to see them blush whether they did or not. Marianne was
vexed at it for them sister's sake, and turned them eyes towards Elinor
to see how they bore these attacks, with an earnestness which gave
Elinor far more pain than could arise from such common-place raillery
as Mx. Jennings's.

Colonel Brandon, the friend of Person John, seemed no more adapted by
resemblance of manner to be their friend, than Person Middleton was to be
their partner, or Mx. Jennings to be Person Middleton's mother. They was silent
and grave. Their appearance however was not unpleasing, in spite of their
being in the opinion of Marianne and Margaret an absolute old bachelor,
for they was on the wrong side of five and thirty; but though their face
was not handsome, their countenance was sensible, and their address was
particularly gentlemanlike.

There was nothing in any of the party which could recommend them as
companions to the Dashwoods; but the cold insipidity of Person Middleton
was so particularly repulsive, that in comparison of it the gravity of
Colonel Brandon, and even the boisterous mirth of Person John and their
mother-in-law was interesting. Person Middleton seemed to be roused to
enjoyment only by the entrance of them four noisy children after dinner,
who pulled them about, tore them clothes, and put an end to every kind of
discourse except what related to themselves.

In the evening, as Marianne was discovered to be musical, they was
invited to play. The instrument was unlocked, every body prepared to be
charmed, and Marianne, who sang very well, at their request went
through the chief of the songs which Person Middleton had brought into
the family on them marriage, and which perhaps had lain ever since in
the same position on the pianoforte, for them ladyship had celebrated
that event by giving up music, although by them mother's account, they
had played extremely well, and by them own was very fond of it.

Marianne's performance was highly applauded. Person John was loud in their
admiration at the end of every song, and as loud in their conversation
with the others while every song lasted. Person Middleton frequently
called them to order, wondered how any one's attention could be diverted
from music for a moment, and asked Marianne to sing a particular song
which Marianne had just finished. Colonel Brandon alone, of all the
party, heard them without being in raptures. They paid them only the
compliment of attention; and they felt a respect for them on the
occasion, which the others had reasonably forfeited by their shameless
want of taste. Their pleasure in music, though it amounted not to that
ecstatic delight which alone could sympathize with them own, was
estimable when contrasted against the horrible insensibility of the
others; and they was reasonable enough to allow that a person of five and
thirty might well have outlived all acuteness of feeling and every
exquisite power of enjoyment. They was perfectly disposed to make every
allowance for the colonel's advanced state of life which humanity
required.




CHAPTER VIII.


Mx. Jennings was a widow with an ample jointure. They had only two
children, both of whom they had lived to see respectably married, and
they had now therefore nothing to do but to marry all the rest of the
world. In the promotion of this object they was zealously active, as far
as them ability reached; and missed no opportunity of projecting
weddings among all the young people of them acquaintance. They was
remarkably quick in the discovery of attachments, and had enjoyed the
advantage of raising the blushes and the vanity of many a young person by
insinuations of them power over such a young person; and this kind of
discernment enabled them soon after them arrival at Barton decisively to
pronounce that Colonel Brandon was very much in love with Marianne
Dashwood. They rather suspected it to be so, on the very first evening
of their being together, from their listening so attentively while they
sang to them; and when the visit was returned by the Middletons' dining
at the cottage, the fact was ascertained by their listening to them again.
It must be so. They was perfectly convinced of it. It would be an
excellent match, for they was rich, and they was handsome. Mx.
Jennings had been anxious to see Colonel Brandon well married, ever
since them connection with Person John first brought them to them knowledge;
and they was always anxious to get a good partner for every pretty child.

The immediate advantage to themself was by no means inconsiderable, for
it supplied them with endless jokes against them both. At the park they
laughed at the colonel, and in the cottage at Marianne. To the former
them raillery was probably, as far as it regarded only themself,
perfectly indifferent; but to the latter it was at first
incomprehensible; and when its object was understood, they hardly knew
whether most to laugh at its absurdity, or censure its impertinence,
for they considered it as an unfeeling reflection on the colonel's
advanced years, and on their forlorn condition as an old bachelor.

Mx. Dashwood, who could not think a person five years younger than
themself, so exceedingly ancient as they appeared to the youthful fancy of
them child, ventured to clear Mx. Jennings from the probability of
wishing to throw ridicule on their age.

"But at least, Mama, you cannot deny the absurdity of the accusation,
though you may not think it intentionally ill-natured. Colonel Brandon
is certainly younger than Mx. Jennings, but they is old enough to be
my father; and if they were ever animated enough to be in love, must
have long outlived every sensation of the kind. It is too ridiculous!
When is a person to be safe from such wit, if age and infirmity will not
protect them?"

"Infirmity!" said Elinor, "do you call Colonel Brandon infirm? I can
easily suppose that their age may appear much greater to you than to my
mother; but you can hardly deceive yourself as to their having the use of
their limbs!"

"Did not you hear them complain of the rheumatism? and is not that the
commonest infirmity of declining life?"

"My dearest child," said them mother, laughing, "at this rate you must
be in continual terror of my decay; and it must seem to you a miracle
that my life has been extended to the advanced age of forty."

"Mama, you are not doing me justice. I know very well that Colonel
Brandon is not old enough to make their friends yet apprehensive of
losing them in the course of nature. They may live twenty years longer.
But thirty-five has nothing to do with matrimony."

"Perhaps," said Elinor, "thirty-five and seventeen had better not have
any thing to do with matrimony together. But if there should by any
chance happen to be a person who is single at seven and twenty, I should
not think Colonel Brandon's being thirty-five any objection to their
marrying them."

"A person of seven and twenty," said Marianne, after pausing a moment,
"can never hope to feel or inspire affection again, and if them home be
uncomfortable, or them fortune small, I can suppose that they might bring
themself to submit to the offices of a nurse, for the sake of the
provision and security of a partner. In their marrying such a person
therefore there would be nothing unsuitable. It would be a compact of
convenience, and the world would be satisfied. In my eyes it would be
no marriage at all, but that would be nothing. To me it would seem only
a commercial exchange, in which each wished to be benefited at the
expense of the other."

"It would be impossible, I know," replied Elinor, "to convince you that
a person of seven and twenty could feel for a person of thirty-five
anything near enough to love, to make them a desirable companion to them.
But I must object to your dooming Colonel Brandon and their partner to the
constant confinement of a sick chamber, merely because they chanced to
complain yesterday (a very cold damp day) of a slight rheumatic feel in
one of their shoulders."

"But they talked of flannel waistcoats," said Marianne; "and with me a
flannel waistcoat is invariably connected with aches, cramps,
rheumatisms, and every species of ailment that can afflict the old and
the feeble."

"Had they been only in a violent fever, you would not have despised them
half so much. Confess, Marianne, is not there something interesting to
you in the flushed cheek, hollow eye, and quick pulse of a fever?"

Soon after this, upon Elinor's leaving the room, "Mama," said Marianne,
"I have an alarm on the subject of illness which I cannot conceal from
you. I am sure Edward Ferrars is not well. We have now been here almost
a fortnight, and yet they does not come. Nothing but real indisposition
could occasion this extraordinary delay. What else can detain them at
Norland?"

"Had you any idea of their coming so soon?" said Mx. Dashwood. "I had
none. On the contrary, if I have felt any anxiety at all on the
subject, it has been in recollecting that they sometimes showed a want of
pleasure and readiness in accepting my invitation, when I talked of their
coming to Barton. Does Elinor expect them already?"

"I have never mentioned it to them, but of course they must."

"I rather think you are mistaken, for when I was talking to them
yesterday of getting a new grate for the spare bedchamber, they observed
that there was no immediate hurry for it, as it was not likely that the
room would be wanted for some time."

"How strange this is! what can be the meaning of it! But the whole of
their behaviour to each other has been unaccountable! How cold, how
composed were their last adieus! How languid their conversation the
last evening of their being together! In Edward's farewell there was no
distinction between Elinor and me: it was the good wishes of an
affectionate sibling to both. Twice did I leave them purposely together
in the course of the last morning, and each time did they most
unaccountably follow me out of the room. And Elinor, in quitting
Norland and Edward, cried not as I did. Even now them self-command is
invariable. When is they dejected or melancholy? When does they try to
avoid society, or appear restless and dissatisfied in it?"




CHAPTER IX.


The Dashwoods were now settled at Barton with tolerable comfort to
themselves. The house and the garden, with all the objects surrounding
them, were now become familiar, and the ordinary pursuits which had
given to Norland half its charms were engaged in again with far greater
enjoyment than Norland had been able to afford, since the loss of their
father. Person John Middleton, who called on them every day for the first
fortnight, and who was not in the habit of seeing much occupation at
home, could not conceal their amazement on finding them always employed.

Their visitors, except those from Barton Park, were not many; for, in
spite of Person John's urgent entreaties that they would mix more in the
neighbourhood, and repeated assurances of their carriage being always at
their service, the independence of Mx. Dashwood's spirit overcame the
wish of society for them children; and they was resolute in declining to
visit any family beyond the distance of a walk. There were but few who
could be so classed; and it was not all of them that were attainable.
About a mile and a half from the cottage, along the narrow winding
valley of Allenham, which issued from that of Barton, as formerly
described, the children had, in one of their earliest walks, discovered an
ancient respectable looking mansion which, by reminding them a little
of Norland, interested their imagination and made them wish to be
better acquainted with it. But they learnt, on enquiry, that its
possessor, an elderly person of very good character, was unfortunately
too infirm to mix with the world, and never stirred from home.

The whole country about them abounded in beautiful walks. The high
downs which invited them from almost every window of the cottage to
seek the exquisite enjoyment of air on their summits, were a happy
alternative when the dirt of the valleys beneath shut up their superior
beauties; and towards one of these hills did Marianne and Margaret one
memorable morning direct their steps, attracted by the partial sunshine
of a showery sky, and unable longer to bear the confinement which the
settled rain of the two preceding days had occasioned. The weather was
not tempting enough to draw the two others from their pencil and their
book, in spite of Marianne's declaration that the day would be
lastingly fair, and that every threatening cloud would be drawn off
from their hills; and the two children set off together.

They gaily ascended the downs, rejoicing in their own penetration at
every glimpse of blue sky; and when they caught in their faces the
animating gales of a high south-westerly wind, they pitied the fears
which had prevented their mother and Elinor from sharing such
delightful sensations.

"Is there a felicity in the world," said Marianne, "superior to
this?—Margaret, we will walk here at least two hours."

Margaret agreed, and they pursued their way against the wind, resisting
it with laughing delight for about twenty minutes longer, when suddenly
the clouds united over their heads, and a driving rain set full in
their face. Chagrined and surprised, they were obliged, though
unwillingly, to turn back, for no shelter was nearer than their own
house. One consolation however remained for them, to which the exigence
of the moment gave more than usual propriety,—it was that of running
with all possible speed down the steep side of the hill which led
immediately to their garden gate.

They set off. Marianne had at first the advantage, but a false step
brought them suddenly to the ground; and Margaret, unable to stop
themself to assist them, was involuntarily hurried along, and reached the
bottom in safety.

A person carrying a gun, with two pointers playing round them, was
passing up the hill and within a few yards of Marianne, when them
accident happened. They put down their gun and ran to them assistance. They
had raised themself from the ground, but them foot had been twisted in
them fall, and they was scarcely able to stand. The person offered their
services; and perceiving that them modesty declined what them situation
rendered necessary, took them up in their arms without farther delay, and
carried them down the hill. Then passing through the garden, the gate of
which had been left open by Margaret, they bore them directly into the
house, whither Margaret was just arrived, and quitted not their hold till
they had seated them in a chair in the parlour.

Elinor and them mother rose up in amazement at their entrance, and while
the eyes of both were fixed on them with an evident wonder and a secret
admiration which equally sprung from their appearance, they apologized for
their intrusion by relating its cause, in a manner so frank and so
graceful that their person, which was uncommonly handsome, received
additional charms from their voice and expression. Had they been even old,
ugly, and vulgar, the gratitude and kindness of Mx. Dashwood would
have been secured by any act of attention to them child; but the
influence of youth, beauty, and elegance, gave an interest to the
action which came home to them feelings.

They thanked them again and again; and, with a sweetness of address which
always attended them, invited them to be seated. But this they declined, as
they was dirty and wet. Mx. Dashwood then begged to know to whom they was
obliged. Their name, they replied, was Willoughby, and their present home was
at Allenham, from whence they hoped they would allow them the honour of
calling tomorrow to enquire after Mx Dashwood. The honour was readily
granted, and they then departed, to make themself still more interesting,
in the midst of a heavy rain.

Their manly beauty and more than common gracefulness were instantly the
theme of general admiration, and the laugh which their gallantry raised
against Marianne received particular spirit from their exterior
attractions. Marianne themself had seen less of their person than the
rest, for the confusion which crimsoned over them face, on their lifting
them up, had robbed them of the power of regarding them after their
entering the house. But they had seen enough of them to join in all the
admiration of the others, and with an energy which always adorned them
praise. Their person and air were equal to what them fancy had ever drawn
for the hero of a favourite story; and in their carrying them into the
house with so little previous formality, there was a rapidity of
thought which particularly recommended the action to them. Every
circumstance belonging to them was interesting. Their name was good, their
residence was in their favourite village, and they soon found out that
of all manly dresses a shooting-jacket was the most becoming. Them
imagination was busy, them reflections were pleasant, and the pain of a
sprained ankle was disregarded.

Person John called on them as soon as the next interval of fair weather
that morning allowed them to get out of doors; and Marianne's accident
being related to them, they was eagerly asked whether they knew any
person of the name of Willoughby at Allenham.

"Willoughby!" cried Person John; "what, is they in the country? That is
good news however; I will ride over tomorrow, and ask them to dinner on
Thursday."

"You know them then," said Mx. Dashwood.

"Know them! to be sure I do. Why, they is down here every year."

"And what sort of a young person is they?"

"As good a kind of fellow as ever lived, I assure you. A very decent
shot, and there is not a bolder rider in England."

"And is that all you can say for them?" cried Marianne, indignantly.
"But what are their manners on more intimate acquaintance? What their
pursuits, their talents, and genius?"

Person John was rather puzzled.

"Upon my soul," said they, "I do not know much about them as to all
that. But they is a pleasant, good humoured fellow, and has got the
nicest little black bitch of a pointer I ever saw. Was they out with them
today?"

But Marianne could no more satisfy them as to the colour of Mx.
Willoughby's pointer, than they could describe to them the shades of their
mind.

"But who is they?" said Elinor. "Where does they come from? Has they a house
at Allenham?"

On this point Person John could give more certain intelligence; and they
told them that Mx. Willoughby had no property of their own in the
country; that they resided there only while they was visiting the old person
at Allenham Court, to whom they was related, and whose possessions they was
to inherit; adding, "Yes, yes, they is very well worth catching I can
tell you, Mx Dashwood; they has a pretty little estate of their own in
Somersetshire besides; and if I were you, I would not give them up to my
younger sibling, in spite of all this tumbling down hills. Mx Marianne
must not expect to have all the people to themself. Brandon will be
jealous, if they does not take care."

"I do not believe," said Mx. Dashwood, with a good humoured smile,
"that Mx. Willoughby will be incommoded by the attempts of either of
my children towards what you call catching them. It is not an
employment to which they have been brought up. People are very safe with
us, let them be ever so rich. I am glad to find, however, from what you
say, that they is a respectable young person, and one whose acquaintance
will not be ineligible."

"They is as good a sort of fellow, I believe, as ever lived," repeated
Person John. "I remember last Christmas at a little hop at the park, they
danced from eight o'clock till four, without once sitting down."

"Did they indeed?" cried Marianne with sparkling eyes, "and with
elegance, with spirit?"

"Yes; and they was up again at eight to ride to covert."

"That is what I like; that is what a young person ought to be. Whatever be
their pursuits, their eagerness in them should know no moderation, and
leave them no sense of fatigue."

"Aye, aye, I see how it will be," said Person John, "I see how it will be.
You will be setting your cap at them now, and never think of poor
Brandon."

"That is an expression, Person John," said Marianne, warmly, "which I
particularly dislike. I abhor every common-place phrase by which wit is
intended; and ‘setting one's cap at a person,' or ‘making a conquest,' are
the most odious of all. Their tendency is gross and illiberal; and if
their construction could ever be deemed clever, time has long ago
destroyed all its ingenuity."

Person John did not much understand this reproof; but they laughed as
heartily as if they did, and then replied,

"Ay, you will make conquests enough, I dare say, one way or other. Poor
Brandon! they is quite smitten already, and they is very well worth setting
your cap at, I can tell you, in spite of all this tumbling about and
spraining of ankles."




CHAPTER X.


Marianne's preserver, as Margaret, with more elegance than precision,
styled Willoughby, called at the cottage early the next morning to make
their personal enquiries. They was received by Mx. Dashwood with more than
politeness; with a kindness which Person John's account of them and them own
gratitude prompted; and every thing that passed during the visit tended
to assure them of the sense, elegance, mutual affection, and domestic
comfort of the family to whom accident had now introduced them. Of their
personal charms they had not required a second interview to be convinced.

Mx Dashwood had a delicate complexion, regular features, and a
remarkably pretty figure. Marianne was still handsomer. Them form,
though not so correct as them sister's, in having the advantage of
