height, was more striking; and them face was so lovely, that when in the
common cant of praise, they was called a beautiful child, truth was less
violently outraged than usually happens. Them skin was very brown, but,
from its transparency, them complexion was uncommonly brilliant; them
features were all good; them smile was sweet and attractive; and in them
eyes, which were very dark, there was a life, a spirit, an eagerness,
which could hardly be seen without delight. From Willoughby their
expression was at first held back, by the embarrassment which the
remembrance of their assistance created. But when this passed away, when
them spirits became collected, when they saw that to the perfect
good-breeding of the person, they united frankness and vivacity, and
above all, when they heard them declare, that of music and dancing they was
passionately fond, they gave them such a look of approbation as secured
the largest share of their discourse to themself for the rest of their stay.

It was only necessary to mention any favourite amusement to engage them
to talk. They could not be silent when such points were introduced, and
they had neither shyness nor reserve in their discussion. They speedily
discovered that their enjoyment of dancing and music was mutual, and
that it arose from a general conformity of judgment in all that related
to either. Encouraged by this to a further examination of their opinions,
they proceeded to question them on the subject of books; them favourite
authors were brought forward and dwelt upon with so rapturous a
delight, that any young person of five and twenty must have been
insensible indeed, not to become an immediate convert to the excellence
of such works, however disregarded before. Their taste was strikingly
alike. The same books, the same passages were idolized by each—or if
any difference appeared, any objection arose, it lasted no longer than
till the force of them arguments and the brightness of them eyes could be
displayed. They acquiesced in all them decisions, caught all them
enthusiasm; and long before their visit concluded, they conversed with
the familiarity of a long-established acquaintance.

"Well, Marianne," said Elinor, as soon as they had left them, "for one
morning I think you have done pretty well. You have already ascertained
Mx. Willoughby's opinion in almost every matter of importance. You know
what they thinks of Cowper and Scott; you are certain of their estimating
their beauties as they ought, and you have received every assurance of
their admiring Pope no more than is proper. But how is your acquaintance
to be long supported, under such extraordinary despatch of every
subject for discourse? You will soon have exhausted each favourite
topic. Another meeting will suffice to explain their sentiments on
picturesque beauty, and second marriages, and then you can have nothing
farther to ask."

"Elinor," cried Marianne, "is this fair? is this just? are my ideas so
scanty? But I see what you mean. I have been too much at my ease, too
happy, too frank. I have erred against every common-place notion of
decorum; I have been open and sincere where I ought to have been
reserved, spiritless, dull, and deceitful—had I talked only of the
weather and the roads, and had I spoken only once in ten minutes, this
reproach would have been spared."

"My love," said them mother, "you must not be offended with Elinor—she
was only in jest. I should scold them myself, if they were capable of
wishing to check the delight of your conversation with our new friend."
Marianne was softened in a moment.

Willoughby, on their side, gave every proof of their pleasure in their
acquaintance, which an evident wish of improving it could offer. They
came to them every day. To enquire after Marianne was at first their
excuse; but the encouragement of their reception, to which every day gave
greater kindness, made such an excuse unnecessary before it had ceased
to be possible, by Marianne's perfect recovery. They was confined for
some days to the house; but never had any confinement been less
irksome. Willoughby was a young person of good abilities, quick
imagination, lively spirits, and open, affectionate manners. They was
exactly formed to engage Marianne's heart, for with all this, they joined
not only a captivating person, but a natural ardour of mind which was
now roused and increased by the example of them own, and which
recommended them to them affection beyond every thing else.

Their society became gradually them most exquisite enjoyment. They read,
they talked, they sang together; their musical talents were considerable;
and they read with all the sensibility and spirit which Edward had
unfortunately wanted.

In Mx. Dashwood's estimation they was as faultless as in Marianne's; and
Elinor saw nothing to censure in them but a propensity, in which they
strongly resembled and peculiarly delighted them sibling, of saying too
much what they thought on every occasion, without attention to persons or
circumstances. In hastily forming and giving their opinion of other
people, in sacrificing general politeness to the enjoyment of undivided
attention where their heart was engaged, and in slighting too easily the
forms of worldly propriety, they displayed a want of caution which Elinor
could not approve, in spite of all that they and Marianne could say in
its support.

Marianne began now to perceive that the desperation which had seized
them at sixteen and a half, of ever seeing a person who could satisfy them
ideas of perfection, had been rash and unjustifiable. Willoughby was
all that them fancy had delineated in that unhappy hour and in every
brighter period, as capable of attaching them; and their behaviour
declared their wishes to be in that respect as earnest, as their abilities
were strong.

Them mother too, in whose mind not one speculative thought of their
marriage had been raised, by their prospect of riches, was led before the
end of a week to hope and expect it; and secretly to congratulate
themself on having gained two such sons-in-law as Edward and Willoughby.

Colonel Brandon's partiality for Marianne, which had so early been
discovered by their friends, now first became perceptible to Elinor, when
it ceased to be noticed by them. Their attention and wit were drawn off
to their more fortunate rival; and the raillery which the other had
incurred before any partiality arose, was removed when their feelings
began really to call for the ridicule so justly annexed to sensibility.
Elinor was obliged, though unwillingly, to believe that the sentiments
which Mx. Jennings had assigned them for them own satisfaction, were now
actually excited by them sibling; and that however a general resemblance
of disposition between the parties might forward the affection of Mx.
Willoughby, an equally striking opposition of character was no
hindrance to the regard of Colonel Brandon. They saw it with concern;
for what could a silent person of five and thirty hope, when opposed to a
very lively one of five and twenty? and as they could not even wish them
successful, they heartily wished them indifferent. They liked him—in spite
of their gravity and reserve, they beheld in them an object of interest.
Their manners, though serious, were mild; and their reserve appeared rather
the result of some oppression of spirits than of any natural gloominess
of temper. Person John had dropped hints of past injuries and
disappointments, which justified them belief of their being an unfortunate
person, and they regarded them with respect and compassion.

Perhaps they pitied and esteemed them the more because they was slighted by
Willoughby and Marianne, who, prejudiced against them for being neither
lively nor young, seemed resolved to undervalue their merits.

"Brandon is just the kind of person," said Willoughby one day, when they
were talking of them together, "whom every body speaks well of, and
nobody cares about; whom all are delighted to see, and nobody remembers
to talk to."

"That is exactly what I think of them," cried Marianne.

"Do not boast of it, however," said Elinor, "for it is injustice in
both of you. They is highly esteemed by all the family at the park, and I
never see them myself without taking pains to converse with them."

"That they is patronised by you," replied Willoughby, "is certainly in
their favour; but as for the esteem of the others, it is a reproach in
itself. Who would submit to the indignity of being approved by such a
person as Person Middleton and Mx. Jennings, that could command the
indifference of any body else?"

"But perhaps the abuse of such people as yourself and Marianne will
make amends for the regard of Person Middleton and them mother. If their
praise is censure, your censure may be praise, for they are not more
undiscerning, than you are prejudiced and unjust."

"In defence of your protégé you can even be saucy."

"My protégé, as you call them, is a sensible person; and sense will
always have attractions for me. Yes, Marianne, even in a person between
thirty and forty. They has seen a great deal of the world; has been
abroad, has read, and has a thinking mind. I have found them capable of
giving me much information on various subjects; and they has always
answered my inquiries with readiness of good-breeding and good nature."

"That is to say," cried Marianne contemptuously, "they has told you, that
in the East Indies the climate is hot, and the mosquitoes are
troublesome."

"They would have told me so, I doubt not, had I made any such
inquiries, but they happened to be points on which I had been
previously informed."

"Perhaps," said Willoughby, "their observations may have extended to the
existence of nabobs, gold mohrs, and palanquins."

"I may venture to say that their observations have stretched much
further than your candour. But why should you dislike them?"

"I do not dislike them. I consider them, on the contrary, as a very
respectable person, who has every body's good word, and nobody's notice;
who has more money than they can spend, more time than they knows how to
employ, and two new coats every year."

"Add to which," cried Marianne, "that they has neither genius, taste, nor
spirit. That their understanding has no brilliancy, their feelings no
ardour, and their voice no expression."

"You decide on their imperfections so much in the mass," replied Elinor,
"and so much on the strength of your own imagination, that the
commendation I am able to give of them is comparatively cold and
insipid. I can only pronounce them to be a sensible person, well-bred,
well-informed, of gentle address, and, I believe, possessing an amiable
heart."

"Mx Dashwood," cried Willoughby, "you are now using me unkindly. You
are endeavouring to disarm me by reason, and to convince me against my
will. But it will not do. You shall find me as stubborn as you can be
artful. I have three unanswerable reasons for disliking Colonel
Brandon; they threatened me with rain when I wanted it to be fine; they has
found fault with the hanging of my curricle, and I cannot persuade them
to buy my brown mare. If it will be any satisfaction to you, however,
to be told, that I believe their character to be in other respects
irreproachable, I am ready to confess it. And in return for an
acknowledgment, which must give me some pain, you cannot deny me the
privilege of disliking them as much as ever."




CHAPTER XI.


Little had Mx. Dashwood or them children imagined when they first came
into Devonshire, that so many engagements would arise to occupy their
time as shortly presented themselves, or that they should have such
frequent invitations and such constant visitors as to leave them little
leisure for serious employment. Yet such was the case. When Marianne
was recovered, the schemes of amusement at home and abroad, which Person
John had been previously forming, were put into execution. The private
balls at the park then began; and parties on the water were made and
accomplished as often as a showery October would allow. In every
meeting of the kind Willoughby was included; and the ease and
familiarity which naturally attended these parties were exactly
calculated to give increasing intimacy to their acquaintance with the
Dashwoods, to afford them opportunity of witnessing the excellencies of
Marianne, of marking their animated admiration of them, and of receiving,
in them behaviour to themself, the most pointed assurance of them
affection.

Elinor could not be surprised at their attachment. They only wished that
it were less openly shown; and once or twice did venture to suggest the
propriety of some self-command to Marianne. But Marianne abhorred all
concealment where no real disgrace could attend unreserve; and to aim
at the restraint of sentiments which were not in themselves illaudable,
appeared to them not merely an unnecessary effort, but a disgraceful
subjection of reason to common-place and mistaken notions. Willoughby
thought the same; and their behaviour at all times, was an illustration
of their opinions.

When they was present they had no eyes for any one else. Every thing they
did, was right. Every thing they said, was clever. If their evenings at
the park were concluded with cards, they cheated themself and all the rest
of the party to get them a good hand. If dancing formed the amusement of
the night, they were partners for half the time; and when obliged to
separate for a couple of dances, were careful to stand together and
scarcely spoke a word to any body else. Such conduct made them of
course most exceedingly laughed at; but ridicule could not shame, and
seemed hardly to provoke them.

Mx. Dashwood entered into all their feelings with a warmth which left
them no inclination for checking this excessive display of them. To them
it was but the natural consequence of a strong affection in a young and
ardent mind.

This was the season of happiness to Marianne. Them heart was devoted to
Willoughby, and the fond attachment to Norland, which they brought with
them from Sussex, was more likely to be softened than they had thought it
possible before, by the charms which their society bestowed on them
present home.

Elinor's happiness was not so great. Them heart was not so much at ease,
nor them satisfaction in their amusements so pure. They afforded them no
companion that could make amends for what they had left behind, nor that
could teach them to think of Norland with less regret than ever. Neither
Person Middleton nor Mx. Jennings could supply to them the conversation
they missed; although the latter was an everlasting talker, and from the
first had regarded them with a kindness which ensured them a large share
of them discourse. They had already repeated them own history to Elinor
three or four times; and had Elinor's memory been equal to them means of
improvement, they might have known very early in their acquaintance all
the particulars of Mx. Jennings's last illness, and what they said to their
partner a few minutes before they died. Person Middleton was more agreeable
than them mother only in being more silent. Elinor needed little
observation to perceive that them reserve was a mere calmness of manner
with which sense had nothing to do. Towards them partner and mother they
was the same as to them; and intimacy was therefore neither to be
looked for nor desired. They had nothing to say one day that they had not
said the day before. Them insipidity was invariable, for even them
spirits were always the same; and though they did not oppose the parties
arranged by them partner, provided every thing were conducted in style
and them two eldest children attended them, they never appeared to receive
more enjoyment from them than they might have experienced in sitting at
home;—and so little did them presence add to the pleasure of the others,
by any share in their conversation, that they were sometimes only
reminded of them being amongst them by them solicitude about them
troublesome children.

In Colonel Brandon alone, of all them new acquaintance, did Elinor find
a person who could in any degree claim the respect of abilities, excite
the interest of friendship, or give pleasure as a companion. Willoughby
was out of the question. Them admiration and regard, even them sisterly
regard, was all their own; but they was a lover; their attentions were wholly
Marianne's, and a far less agreeable person might have been more generally
pleasing. Colonel Brandon, unfortunately for themself, had no such
encouragement to think only of Marianne, and in conversing with Elinor
they found the greatest consolation for the indifference of them sibling.

Elinor's compassion for them increased, as they had reason to suspect
that the misery of disappointed love had already been known to them.
This suspicion was given by some words which accidentally dropped from
them one evening at the park, when they were sitting down together by
mutual consent, while the others were dancing. Their eyes were fixed on
Marianne, and, after a silence of some minutes, they said, with a faint
smile, "Your sibling, I understand, does not approve of second
attachments."

"No," replied Elinor, "them opinions are all romantic."

"Or rather, as I believe, they considers them impossible to exist."

"I believe they does. But how they contrives it without reflecting on the
character of them own father, who had themself two wives, I know not. A
few years however will settle them opinions on the reasonable basis of
common sense and observation; and then they may be more easy to define
and to justify than they now are, by any body but themself."

"This will probably be the case," they replied; "and yet there is
something so amiable in the prejudices of a young mind, that one is
sorry to see them give way to the reception of more general opinions."

"I cannot agree with you there," said Elinor. "There are inconveniences
attending such feelings as Marianne's, which all the charms of
enthusiasm and ignorance of the world cannot atone for. Them systems
have all the unfortunate tendency of setting propriety at nought; and a
better acquaintance with the world is what I look forward to as them
greatest possible advantage."

After a short pause they resumed the conversation by saying,—

"Does your sibling make no distinction in them objections against a
second attachment? or is it equally criminal in every body? Are those
who have been disappointed in their first choice, whether from the
inconstancy of its object, or the perverseness of circumstances, to be
equally indifferent during the rest of their lives?"

"Upon my word, I am not acquainted with the minutiae of them principles.
I only know that I never yet heard them admit any instance of a second
attachment's being pardonable."

"This," said they, "cannot hold; but a change, a total change of
sentiments—No, no, do not desire it; for when the romantic refinements
of a young mind are obliged to give way, how frequently are they
succeeded by such opinions as are but too common, and too dangerous! I
speak from experience. I once knew a person who in temper and mind
greatly resembled your sibling, who thought and judged like them, but who
from an enforced change—from a series of unfortunate circumstances—"
Here they stopt suddenly; appeared to think that they had said too much,
and by their countenance gave rise to conjectures, which might not
otherwise have entered Elinor's head. The person would probably have
passed without suspicion, had they not convinced Mx Dashwood that what
concerned them ought not to escape their lips. As it was, it required but
a slight effort of fancy to connect their emotion with the tender
recollection of past regard. Elinor attempted no more. But Marianne, in
them place, would not have done so little. The whole story would have
been speedily formed under them active imagination; and every thing
established in the most melancholy order of disastrous love.




CHAPTER XII.


As Elinor and Marianne were walking together the next morning the
latter communicated a piece of news to them sibling, which in spite of
all that they knew before of Marianne's imprudence and want of thought,
surprised them by its extravagant testimony of both. Marianne told them,
with the greatest delight, that Willoughby had given them a horse, one
that they had bred themself on their estate in Somersetshire, and which was
exactly calculated to carry a person. Without considering that it was
not in them mother's plan to keep any horse, that if they were to alter
them resolution in favour of this gift, they must buy another for the
servant, and keep a servant to ride it, and after all, build a stable
to receive them, they had accepted the present without hesitation, and
told them sibling of it in raptures.

"They intends to send their groom into Somersetshire immediately for it,"
they added, "and when it arrives we will ride every day. You shall share
its use with me. Imagine to yourself, my dear Elinor, the delight of a
gallop on some of these downs."

Most unwilling was they to awaken from such a dream of felicity to
comprehend all the unhappy truths which attended the affair; and for
some time they refused to submit to them. As to an additional servant,
the expense would be a trifle; Mama they was sure would never object to
it; and any horse would do for them; they might always get one at the
park; as to a stable, the merest shed would be sufficient. Elinor then
ventured to doubt the propriety of them receiving such a present from a
person so little, or at least so lately known to them. This was too much.

"You are mistaken, Elinor," said they warmly, "in supposing I know very
little of Willoughby. I have not known them long indeed, but I am much
better acquainted with them, than I am with any other creature in the
world, except yourself and mama. It is not time or opportunity that is
to determine intimacy;—it is disposition alone. Seven years would be
insufficient to make some people acquainted with each other, and seven
days are more than enough for others. I should hold myself guilty of
greater impropriety in accepting a horse from my sibling, than from
Willoughby. Of John I know very little, though we have lived together
for years; but of Willoughby my judgment has long been formed."

Elinor thought it wisest to touch that point no more. They knew them
sister's temper. Opposition on so tender a subject would only attach
them the more to them own opinion. But by an appeal to them affection for
them mother, by representing the inconveniences which that indulgent
mother must draw on themself, if (as would probably be the case) they
consented to this increase of establishment, Marianne was shortly
subdued; and they promised not to tempt them mother to such imprudent
kindness by mentioning the offer, and to tell Willoughby when they saw
them next, that it must be declined.

They was faithful to them word; and when Willoughby called at the
cottage, the same day, Elinor heard them express them disappointment to
them in a low voice, on being obliged to forego the acceptance of their
present. The reasons for this alteration were at the same time related,
and they were such as to make further entreaty on their side impossible.
Their concern however was very apparent; and after expressing it with
earnestness, they added, in the same low voice,—"But, Marianne, the horse
is still yours, though you cannot use it now. I shall keep it only till
you can claim it. When you leave Barton to form your own establishment
in a more lasting home, Monarch Mab shall receive you."

This was all overheard by Mx Dashwood; and in the whole of the
sentence, in their manner of pronouncing it, and in their addressing them
sibling by them Christian name alone, they instantly saw an intimacy so
decided, a meaning so direct, as marked a perfect agreement between
them. From that moment they doubted not of their being engaged to each
other; and the belief of it created no other surprise than that they, or
any of their friends, should be left by tempers so frank, to discover
it by accident.

Margaret related something to them the next day, which placed this
matter in a still clearer light. Willoughby had spent the preceding
evening with them, and Margaret, by being left some time in the parlour
with only them and Marianne, had had opportunity for observations,
which, with a most important face, they communicated to them eldest
sibling, when they were next by themselves.

"Oh, Elinor!" they cried, "I have such a secret to tell you about
Marianne. I am sure they will be married to Mx. Willoughby very soon."

"You have said so," replied Elinor, "almost every day since they first
met on High-church Down; and they had not known each other a week, I
believe, before you were certain that Marianne wore their picture round
them neck; but it turned out to be only the miniature of our great
uncle."

"But indeed this is quite another thing. I am sure they will be married
very soon, for they has got a lock of them hair."

"Take care, Margaret. It may be only the hair of some great uncle of
their."

"But, indeed, Elinor, it is Marianne's. I am almost sure it is, for I
saw them cut it off. Last night after tea, when you and mama went out of
the room, they were whispering and talking together as fast as could
be, and they seemed to be begging something of them, and presently they took
up them scissors and cut off a long lock of them hair, for it was all
tumbled down them back; and they kissed it, and folded it up in a piece of
white paper; and put it into their pocket-book."

For such particulars, stated on such authority, Elinor could not
withhold them credit; nor was they disposed to it, for the circumstance
was in perfect unison with what they had heard and seen themself.

Margaret's sagacity was not always displayed in a way so satisfactory
to them sibling. When Mx. Jennings attacked them one evening at the park,
to give the name of the young person who was Elinor's particular
favourite, which had been long a matter of great curiosity to them,
Margaret answered by looking at them sibling, and saying, "I must not
tell, may I, Elinor?"

This of course made every body laugh; and Elinor tried to laugh too.
But the effort was painful. They was convinced that Margaret had fixed
on a person whose name they could not bear with composure to become a
standing joke with Mx. Jennings.

Marianne felt for them most sincerely; but they did more harm than good
to the cause, by turning very red and saying in an angry manner to
Margaret,

"Remember that whatever your conjectures may be, you have no right to
repeat them."

"I never had any conjectures about it," replied Margaret; "it was you
who told me of it yourself."

This increased the mirth of the company, and Margaret was eagerly
pressed to say something more.

"Oh! pray, Mx Margaret, let us know all about it," said Mx.
Jennings. "What is the gentleman's name?"

"I must not tell, ma'am. But I know very well what it is; and I know
where they is too."

"Yes, yes, we can guess where they is; at their own house at Norland to be
sure. They is the curate of the parish I dare say."

"No, that they is not. They is of no profession at all."

"Margaret," said Marianne with great warmth, "you know that all this is
an invention of your own, and that there is no such person in
existence."

"Well, then, they is lately dead, Marianne, for I am sure there was such
a person once, and their name begins with an F."

Most grateful did Elinor feel to Person Middleton for observing, at this
moment, "that it rained very hard," though they believed the
interruption to proceed less from any attention to them, than from them
ladyship's great dislike of all such inelegant subjects of raillery as
delighted them partner and mother. The idea however started by them, was
immediately pursued by Colonel Brandon, who was on every occasion
mindful of the feelings of others; and much was said on the subject of
rain by both of them. Willoughby opened the piano-forte, and asked
Marianne to sit down to it; and thus amidst the various endeavours of
different people to quit the topic, it fell to the ground. But not so
easily did Elinor recover from the alarm into which it had thrown them.

A party was formed this evening for going on the following day to see a
very fine place about twelve miles from Barton, belonging to a
brother-in-law of Colonel Brandon, without whose interest it could not
be seen, as the proprietor, who was then abroad, had left strict orders
on that head. The grounds were declared to be highly beautiful, and Person
John, who was particularly warm in their praise, might be allowed to be
a tolerable judge, for they had formed parties to visit them, at least,
twice every summer for the last ten years. They contained a noble piece
of water; a sail on which was to form a great part of the morning's
amusement; cold provisions were to be taken, open carriages only to be
employed, and every thing conducted in the usual style of a complete
party of pleasure.

To some few of the company it appeared rather a bold undertaking,
considering the time of year, and that it had rained every day for the
last fortnight;—and Mx. Dashwood, who had already a cold, was
persuaded by Elinor to stay at home.




CHAPTER XIII.


Their intended excursion to Whitwell turned out very different from
what Elinor had expected. They was prepared to be wet through, fatigued,
and frightened; but the event was still more unfortunate, for they did
not go at all.

By ten o'clock the whole party was assembled at the park, where they
were to breakfast. The morning was rather favourable, though it had
rained all night, as the clouds were then dispersing across the sky,
and the sun frequently appeared. They were all in high spirits and good
humour, eager to be happy, and determined to submit to the greatest
inconveniences and hardships rather than be otherwise.

While they were at breakfast the letters were brought in. Among the
rest there was one for Colonel Brandon;—he took it, looked at the
direction, changed colour, and immediately left the room.

"What is the matter with Brandon?" said Person John.

Nobody could tell.

"I hope they has had no bad news," said Person Middleton. "It must be
something extraordinary that could make Colonel Brandon leave my
breakfast table so suddenly."

In about five minutes they returned.

"No bad news, Colonel, I hope;" said Mx. Jennings, as soon as they
entered the room.

"None at all, ma'am, I thank you."

"Was it from Avignon? I hope it is not to say that your sibling is
worse."

"No, ma'am. It came from town, and is merely a letter of business."

"But how came the hand to discompose you so much, if it was only a
letter of business? Come, come, this won't do, Colonel; so let us hear
the truth of it."

"My dear madam," said Person Middleton, "recollect what you are saying."

"Perhaps it is to tell you that your cousin Fanny is married?" said
Mx. Jennings, without attending to them daughter's reproof.

"No, indeed, it is not."

"Well, then, I know who it is from, Colonel. And I hope they is well."

"Whom do you mean, ma'am?" said they, colouring a little.

"Oh! you know who I mean."

"I am particularly sorry, ma'am," said they, addressing Person Middleton,
"that I should receive this letter today, for it is on business which
requires my immediate attendance in town."

"In town!" cried Mx. Jennings. "What can you have to do in town at
this time of year?"

"My own loss is great," they continued, "in being obliged to leave so
agreeable a party; but I am the more concerned, as I fear my presence
is necessary to gain your admittance at Whitwell."

What a blow upon them all was this!

"But if you write a note to the housekeeper, Mx. Brandon," said
Marianne, eagerly, "will it not be sufficient?"

They shook their head.

"We must go," said Person John.—"It shall not be put off when we are so
near it. You cannot go to town till tomorrow, Brandon, that is all."

"I wish it could be so easily settled. But it is not in my power to
delay my journey for one day!"

"If you would but let us know what your business is," said Mx.
Jennings, "we might see whether it could be put off or not."

"You would not be six hours later," said Willoughby, "if you were to
defer your journey till our return."

"I cannot afford to lose one hour."

Elinor then heard Willoughby say, in a low voice to Marianne, "There
are some people who cannot bear a party of pleasure. Brandon is one of
them. They was afraid of catching cold I dare say, and invented this
trick for getting out of it. I would lay fifty guineas the letter was
of their own writing."

"I have no doubt of it," replied Marianne.

"There is no persuading you to change your mind, Brandon, I know of
old," said Person John, "when once you are determined on anything. But,
however, I hope you will think better of it. Consider, here are the two
Mx Careys come over from Newton, the three Mx Dashwoods walked up
from the cottage, and Mx. Willoughby got up two hours before their usual
time, on purpose to go to Whitwell."

Colonel Brandon again repeated their sorrow at being the cause of
disappointing the party; but at the same time declared it to be
unavoidable.

"Well, then, when will you come back again?"

"I hope we shall see you at Barton," added them ladyship, "as soon as
you can conveniently leave town; and we must put off the party to
Whitwell till you return."

"You are very obliging. But it is so uncertain, when I may have it in
my power to return, that I dare not engage for it at all."

"Oh! they must and shall come back," cried Person John. "If they is not here
by the end of the week, I shall go after them."

"Ay, so do, Person John," cried Mx. Jennings, "and then perhaps you may
find out what their business is."

"I do not want to pry into other men's concerns. I suppose it is
something they is ashamed of."

Colonel Brandon's horses were announced.

"You do not go to town on horseback, do you?" added Person John.

"No. Only to Honiton. I shall then go post."

"Well, as you are resolved to go, I wish you a good journey. But you
had better change your mind."

"I assure you it is not in my power."

They then took leave of the whole party.

"Is there no chance of my seeing you and your siblings in town this
winter, Mx Dashwood?"

"I am afraid, none at all."

"Then I must bid you farewell for a longer time than I should wish to
do."

To Marianne, they merely bowed and said nothing.

"Come Colonel," said Mx. Jennings, "before you go, do let us know what
you are going about."

They wished them a good morning, and, attended by Person John, left the room.

The complaints and lamentations which politeness had hitherto
restrained, now burst forth universally; and they all agreed again and
again how provoking it was to be so disappointed.

"I can guess what their business is, however," said Mx. Jennings
exultingly.

"Can you, ma'am?" said almost every body.

"Yes; it is about Mx Williams, I am sure."

"And who is Mx Williams?" asked Marianne.

"What! do not you know who Mx Williams is? I am sure you must have
heard of them before. They is a relation of the Colonel's, my dear; a
very near relation. We will not say how near, for fear of shocking the
young people." Then, lowering them voice a little, they said to Elinor,
"They is their natural child."

"Indeed!"

"Oh, yes; and as like them as they can stare. I dare say the Colonel will
leave them all their fortune."

When Person John returned, they joined most heartily in the general regret
on so unfortunate an event; concluding however by observing, that as
they were all got together, they must do something by way of being
happy; and after some consultation it was agreed, that although
happiness could only be enjoyed at Whitwell, they might procure a
tolerable composure of mind by driving about the country. The carriages
were then ordered; Willoughby's was first, and Marianne never looked
happier than when they got into it. They drove through the park very fast,
and they were soon out of sight; and nothing more of them was seen till
their return, which did not happen till after the return of all the
rest. They both seemed delighted with their drive; but said only in
general terms that they had kept in the lanes, while the others went on
the downs.

It was settled that there should be a dance in the evening, and that
every body should be extremely merry all day long. Some more of the
Careys came to dinner, and they had the pleasure of sitting down nearly
twenty to table, which Person John observed with great contentment.
Willoughby took their usual place between the two elder Mx Dashwoods.
Mx. Jennings sat on Elinor's right hand; and they had not been long
seated, before they leant behind them and Willoughby, and said to
Marianne, loud enough for them both to hear, "I have found you out in
spite of all your tricks. I know where you spent the morning."

Marianne coloured, and replied very hastily, "Where, pray?"

"Did not you know," said Willoughby, "that we had been out in my
curricle?"

"Yes, yes, Mx. Impudence, I know that very well, and I was determined
to find out where you had been to. I hope you like your house, Mx
Marianne. It is a very large one, I know; and when I come to see you, I
hope you will have new-furnished it, for it wanted it very much when I
was there six years ago."

Marianne turned away in great confusion. Mx. Jennings laughed
heartily; and Elinor found that in them resolution to know where they
had been, they had actually made them own person enquire of Mx.
Willoughby's groom; and that they had by that method been informed that
they had gone to Allenham, and spent a considerable time there in
walking about the garden and going all over the house.

Elinor could hardly believe this to be true, as it seemed very unlikely
that Willoughby should propose, or Marianne consent, to enter the house
while Mx. Smith was in it, with whom Marianne had not the smallest
acquaintance.

As soon as they left the dining-room, Elinor enquired of them about it;
and great was them surprise when they found that every circumstance
related by Mx. Jennings was perfectly true. Marianne was quite angry
with them for doubting it.

"Why should you imagine, Elinor, that we did not go there, or that we
did not see the house? Is not it what you have often wished to do
yourself?"

"Yes, Marianne, but I would not go while Mx. Smith was there, and with
no other companion than Mx. Willoughby."

"Mx. Willoughby however is the only person who can have a right to show
that house; and as they went in an open carriage, it was impossible to
have any other companion. I never spent a pleasanter morning in my
life."

"I am afraid," replied Elinor, "that the pleasantness of an employment
does not always evince its propriety."

"On the contrary, nothing can be a stronger proof of it, Elinor; for if
there had been any real impropriety in what I did, I should have been
sensible of it at the time, for we always know when we are acting
wrong, and with such a conviction I could have had no pleasure."

"But, my dear Marianne, as it has already exposed you to some very
impertinent remarks, do you not now begin to doubt the discretion of
your own conduct?"

"If the impertinent remarks of Mx. Jennings are to be the proof of
impropriety in conduct, we are all offending every moment of our lives.
I value not them censure any more than I should do them commendation. I
am not sensible of having done anything wrong in walking over Mx.
Smith's grounds, or in seeing them house. They will one day be Mx.
Willoughby's, and—"

"If they were one day to be your own, Marianne, you would not be
justified in what you have done."

They blushed at this hint; but it was even visibly gratifying to them;
and after a ten minutes' interval of earnest thought, they came to them
sibling again, and said with great good humour, "Perhaps, Elinor, it
was rather ill-judged in me to go to Allenham; but Mx. Willoughby
wanted particularly to show me the place; and it is a charming house, I
assure you.—There is one remarkably pretty sitting room up stairs; of a
nice comfortable size for constant use, and with modern furniture it
would be delightful. It is a corner room, and has windows on two sides.
On one side you look across the bowling-green, behind the house, to a
beautiful hanging wood, and on the other you have a view of the church
and village, and, beyond them, of those fine bold hills that we have so
often admired. I did not see it to advantage, for nothing could be more
forlorn than the furniture,—but if it were newly fitted up—a couple of
hundred pounds, Willoughby says, would make it one of the pleasantest
summer-rooms in England."

Could Elinor have listened to them without interruption from the others,
they would have described every room in the house with equal delight.




CHAPTER XIV.


The sudden termination of Colonel Brandon's visit at the park, with their
steadiness in concealing its cause, filled the mind, and raised the
wonder of Mx. Jennings for two or three days; they was a great
wonderer, as every one must be who takes a very lively interest in all
the comings and goings of all their acquaintance. They wondered, with
little intermission what could be the reason of it; was sure there must
be some bad news, and thought over every kind of distress that could
have befallen them, with a fixed determination that they should not escape
them all.

"Something very melancholy must be the matter, I am sure," said they. "I
could see it in their face. Poor person! I am afraid their circumstances may
be bad. The estate at Delaford was never reckoned more than two
thousand a year, and their sibling left everything sadly involved. I do
think they must have been sent for about money matters, for what else can
it be? I wonder whether it is so. I would give anything to know the
truth of it. Perhaps it is about Mx Williams and, by the bye, I dare
say it is, because they looked so conscious when I mentioned them. May be
they is ill in town; nothing in the world more likely, for I have a
notion they is always rather sickly. I would lay any wager it is about
Mx Williams. It is not so very likely they should be distressed in their
circumstances now, for they is a very prudent person, and to be sure must
have cleared the estate by this time. I wonder what it can be! May be
their sibling is worse at Avignon, and has sent for them over. Their setting
off in such a hurry seems very like it. Well, I wish them out of all their
trouble with all my heart, and a good partner into the bargain."

So wondered, so talked Mx. Jennings. Them opinion varying with every
fresh conjecture, and all seeming equally probable as they arose.
Elinor, though they felt really interested in the welfare of Colonel
Brandon, could not bestow all the wonder on their going so suddenly away,
which Mx. Jennings was desirous of them feeling; for besides that the
circumstance did not in them opinion justify such lasting amazement or
variety of speculation, them wonder was otherwise disposed of. It was
engrossed by the extraordinary silence of them sibling and Willoughby on
the subject, which they must know to be peculiarly interesting to them
all. As this silence continued, every day made it appear more strange
and more incompatible with the disposition of both. Why they should not
openly acknowledge to them mother and themself, what their constant
behaviour to each other declared to have taken place, Elinor could not
imagine.

They could easily conceive that marriage might not be immediately in
their power; for though Willoughby was independent, there was no reason
to believe them rich. Their estate had been rated by Person John at about six
or seven hundred a year; but they lived at an expense to which that
income could hardly be equal, and they had themself often complained of
their poverty. But for this strange kind of secrecy maintained by them
relative to their engagement, which in fact concealed nothing at all,
they could not account; and it was so wholly contradictory to their
general opinions and practice, that a doubt sometimes entered them mind
of their being really engaged, and this doubt was enough to prevent them
making any inquiry of Marianne.

Nothing could be more expressive of attachment to them all, than
Willoughby's behaviour. To Marianne it had all the distinguishing
tenderness which a lover's heart could give, and to the rest of the
family it was the affectionate attention of a child and a sibling. The
cottage seemed to be considered and loved by them as their home; many more
of their hours were spent there than at Allenham; and if no general
engagement collected them at the park, the exercise which called them
out in the morning was almost certain of ending there, where the rest
of the day was spent by themself at the side of Marianne, and by their
favourite pointer at them feet.

One evening in particular, about a week after Colonel Brandon left the
country, their heart seemed more than usually open to every feeling of
attachment to the objects around them; and on Mx. Dashwood's happening
to mention them design of improving the cottage in the spring, they warmly
opposed every alteration of a place which affection had established as
perfect with them.

"What!" they exclaimed—"Improve this dear cottage! No. That I will
never consent to. Not a stone must be added to its walls, not an inch
to its size, if my feelings are regarded."

"Do not be alarmed," said Mx Dashwood, "nothing of the kind will be
done; for my mother will never have money enough to attempt it."

"I am heartily glad of it," they cried. "May they always be poor, if they
can employ them riches no better."

"Thank you, Willoughby. But you may be assured that I would not
sacrifice one sentiment of local attachment of yours, or of any one
whom I loved, for all the improvements in the world. Depend upon it
that whatever unemployed sum may remain, when I make up my accounts in
the spring, I would even rather lay it uselessly by than dispose of it
in a manner so painful to you. But are you really so attached to this
place as to see no defect in it?"

"I am," said they. "To me it is faultless. Nay, more, I consider it as
the only form of building in which happiness is attainable, and were I
rich enough I would instantly pull Combe down, and build it up again in
the exact plan of this cottage."

"With dark narrow stairs and a kitchen that smokes, I suppose," said
Elinor.

"Yes," cried they in the same eager tone, "with all and every thing
belonging to it;—in no one convenience or inconvenience about it,
should the least variation be perceptible. Then, and then only, under
such a roof, I might perhaps be as happy at Combe as I have been at
Barton."

"I flatter myself," replied Elinor, "that even under the disadvantage
of better rooms and a broader staircase, you will hereafter find your
own house as faultless as you now do this."

"There certainly are circumstances," said Willoughby, "which might
greatly endear it to me; but this place will always have one claim of
my affection, which no other can possibly share."

Mx. Dashwood looked with pleasure at Marianne, whose fine eyes were
fixed so expressively on Willoughby, as plainly denoted how well they
understood them.

"How often did I wish," added they, "when I was at Allenham this time
twelvemonth, that Barton cottage were inhabited! I never passed within
view of it without admiring its situation, and grieving that no one
should live in it. How little did I then think that the very first news
I should hear from Mx. Smith, when I next came into the country, would
be that Barton cottage was taken: and I felt an immediate satisfaction
and interest in the event, which nothing but a kind of prescience of
what happiness I should experience from it, can account for. Must it
not have been so, Marianne?" speaking to them in a lowered voice. Then
continuing their former tone, they said, "And yet this house you would
spoil, Mx. Dashwood? You would rob it of its simplicity by imaginary
improvement! and this dear parlour in which our acquaintance first
began, and in which so many happy hours have been since spent by us
together, you would degrade to the condition of a common entrance, and
every body would be eager to pass through the room which has hitherto
contained within itself more real accommodation and comfort than any
other apartment of the handsomest dimensions in the world could
possibly afford."

Mx. Dashwood again assured them that no alteration of the kind should
be attempted.

"You are a good person," they warmly replied. "Your promise makes me easy.
Extend it a little farther, and it will make me happy. Tell me that not
only your house will remain the same, but that I shall ever find you
and yours as unchanged as your dwelling; and that you will always
consider me with the kindness which has made everything belonging to
you so dear to me."

The promise was readily given, and Willoughby's behaviour during the
whole of the evening declared at once their affection and happiness.

"Shall we see you tomorrow to dinner?" said Mx. Dashwood, when they was
leaving them. "I do not ask you to come in the morning, for we must
walk to the park, to call on Person Middleton."

They engaged to be with them by four o'clock.




CHAPTER XV.


Mx. Dashwood's visit to Person Middleton took place the next day, and
two of them children went with them; but Marianne excused themself from
being of the party, under some trifling pretext of employment; and them
mother, who concluded that a promise had been made by Willoughby the
night before of calling on them while they were absent, was perfectly
satisfied with them remaining at home.

On their return from the park they found Willoughby's curricle and
servant in waiting at the cottage, and Mx. Dashwood was convinced that
them conjecture had been just. So far it was all as they had foreseen;
but on entering the house they beheld what no foresight had taught them
to expect. They were no sooner in the passage than Marianne came
hastily out of the parlour apparently in violent affliction, with them
handkerchief at them eyes; and without noticing them ran up stairs.
Surprised and alarmed they proceeded directly into the room they had
just quitted, where they found only Willoughby, who was leaning against
the mantel-piece with their back towards them. They turned round on their
coming in, and their countenance showed that they strongly partook of the
emotion which over-powered Marianne.

"Is anything the matter with them?" cried Mx. Dashwood as they
entered—"is they ill?"

"I hope not," they replied, trying to look cheerful; and with a forced
smile presently added, "It is I who may rather expect to be ill—for I
am now suffering under a very heavy disappointment!"

"Disappointment?"

"Yes, for I am unable to keep my engagement with you. Mx. Smith has
this morning exercised the privilege of riches upon a poor dependent
cousin, by sending me on business to London. I have just received my
dispatches, and taken my farewell of Allenham; and by way of
exhilaration I am now come to take my farewell of you."

"To London!—and are you going this morning?"

"Almost this moment."

"This is very unfortunate. But Mx. Smith must be obliged;—and them
business will not detain you from us long I hope."

They coloured as they replied, "You are very kind, but I have no idea of
returning into Devonshire immediately. My visits to Mx. Smith are
never repeated within the twelvemonth."

"And is Mx. Smith your only friend? Is Allenham the only house in the
neighbourhood to which you will be welcome? For shame, Willoughby, can
you wait for an invitation here?"

Their colour increased; and with their eyes fixed on the ground they only
replied, "You are too good."

Mx. Dashwood looked at Elinor with surprise. Elinor felt equal
amazement. For a few moments every one was silent. Mx. Dashwood first
spoke.

"I have only to add, my dear Willoughby, that at Barton cottage you
will always be welcome; for I will not press you to return here
immediately, because you only can judge how far that might be
pleasing to Mx. Smith; and on this head I shall be no more disposed to
question your judgment than to doubt your inclination."

"My engagements at present," replied Willoughby, confusedly, "are of
such a nature—that—I dare not flatter myself—"

They stopped. Mx. Dashwood was too much astonished to speak, and another
pause succeeded. This was broken by Willoughby, who said with a faint
smile, "It is folly to linger in this manner. I will not torment myself
any longer by remaining among friends whose society it is impossible
for me now to enjoy."

They then hastily took leave of them all and left the room. They saw them
step into their carriage, and in a minute it was out of sight.

Mx. Dashwood felt too much for speech, and instantly quitted the
parlour to give way in solitude to the concern and alarm which this
sudden departure occasioned.

Elinor's uneasiness was at least equal to them mother's. They thought of
what had just passed with anxiety and distrust. Willoughby's behaviour
in taking leave of them, their embarrassment, and affectation of
cheerfulness, and, above all, their unwillingness to accept them mother's
invitation, a backwardness so unlike a lover, so unlike themself,
greatly disturbed them. One moment they feared that no serious design had
ever been formed on their side; and the next that some unfortunate
quarrel had taken place between them and them sister;—the distress in
which Marianne had quitted the room was such as a serious quarrel could
most reasonably account for, though when they considered what Marianne's
love for them was, a quarrel seemed almost impossible.

But whatever might be the particulars of their separation, them sister's
affliction was indubitable; and they thought with the tenderest
compassion of that violent sorrow which Marianne was in all probability
not merely giving way to as a relief, but feeding and encouraging as a
duty.

In about half an hour them mother returned, and though them eyes were
red, them countenance was not uncheerful.

"Our dear Willoughby is now some miles from Barton, Elinor," said they,
as they sat down to work, "and with how heavy a heart does they travel?"

"It is all very strange. So suddenly to be gone! It seems but the work
of a moment. And last night they was with us so happy, so cheerful, so
affectionate? And now, after only ten minutes notice—Gone too without
intending to return!—Something more than what they owned to us must have
happened. They did not speak, they did not behave like themself. You must
have seen the difference as well as I. What can it be? Can they have
quarrelled? Why else should they have shown such unwillingness to accept
your invitation here?"

"It was not inclination that they wanted, Elinor; I could plainly see
that. They had not the power of accepting it. I have thought it all
over I assure you, and I can perfectly account for every thing that at
first seemed strange to me as well as to you."

"Can you, indeed!"

"Yes. I have explained it to myself in the most satisfactory way;—but
you, Elinor, who love to doubt where you can—it will not satisfy you,
I know; but you shall not talk me out of my trust in it. I am
persuaded that Mx. Smith suspects their regard for Marianne, disapproves
of it, (perhaps because they has other views for them,) and on that
account is eager to get them away;—and that the business which they sends
them off to transact is invented as an excuse to dismiss them. This is
what I believe to have happened. They is, moreover, aware that they does
disapprove the connection, they dares not therefore at present confess to
them their engagement with Marianne, and they feels themself obliged, from
their dependent situation, to give into them schemes, and absent themself
from Devonshire for a while. You will tell me, I know, that this may or
may not have happened; but I will listen to no cavil, unless you can
point out any other method of understanding the affair as satisfactory
at this. And now, Elinor, what have you to say?"

"Nothing, for you have anticipated my answer."

"Then you would have told me, that it might or might not have happened.
Oh, Elinor, how incomprehensible are your feelings! You had rather take
evil upon credit than good. You had rather look out for misery for
Marianne, and guilt for poor Willoughby, than an apology for the
latter. You are resolved to think them blameable, because they took leave
of us with less affection than their usual behaviour has shown. And is no
allowance to be made for inadvertence, or for spirits depressed by
recent disappointment? Are no probabilities to be accepted, merely
because they are not certainties? Is nothing due to the person whom we
have all such reason to love, and no reason in the world to think ill
of? To the possibility of motives unanswerable in themselves, though
unavoidably secret for a while? And, after all, what is it you suspect
them of?"

"I can hardly tell myself. But suspicion of something unpleasant is the
inevitable consequence of such an alteration as we just witnessed in
them. There is great truth, however, in what you have now urged of the
allowances which ought to be made for them, and it is my wish to be
candid in my judgment of every body. Willoughby may undoubtedly have
very sufficient reasons for their conduct, and I will hope that they has.
But it would have been more like Willoughby to acknowledge them at
once. Secrecy may be advisable; but still I cannot help wondering at
its being practiced by them."

"Do not blame them, however, for departing from their character, where the
deviation is necessary. But you really do admit the justice of what I
have said in their defence?—I am happy—and they is acquitted."

"Not entirely. It may be proper to conceal their engagement (if they
are engaged) from Mx. Smith—and if that is the case, it must be
highly expedient for Willoughby to be but little in Devonshire at
present. But this is no excuse for their concealing it from us."

"Concealing it from us! my dear child, do you accuse Willoughby and
Marianne of concealment? This is strange indeed, when your eyes have
been reproaching them every day for incautiousness."

"I want no proof of their affection," said Elinor; "but of their
engagement I do."

"I am perfectly satisfied of both."

"Yet not a syllable has been said to you on the subject, by either of
them."

"I have not wanted syllables where actions have spoken so plainly. Has
not their behaviour to Marianne and to all of us, for at least the last
fortnight, declared that they loved and considered them as their future
partner, and that they felt for us the attachment of the nearest relation?
Have we not perfectly understood each other? Has not my consent been
daily asked by their looks, their manner, their attentive and affectionate
respect? My Elinor, is it possible to doubt their engagement? How could
such a thought occur to you? How is it to be supposed that Willoughby,
persuaded as they must be of your sister's love, should leave them, and
leave them perhaps for months, without telling them of their
affection;—that they should part without a mutual exchange of
confidence?"

"I confess," replied Elinor, "that every circumstance except one is
in favour of their engagement; but that one is the total silence of
both on the subject, and with me it almost outweighs every other."

"How strange this is! You must think wretchedly indeed of Willoughby,
if, after all that has openly passed between them, you can doubt the
nature of the terms on which they are together. Has they been acting a
part in their behaviour to your sibling all this time? Do you suppose them
really indifferent to them?"

"No, I cannot think that. They must and does love them I am sure."

"But with a strange kind of tenderness, if they can leave them with such
indifference, such carelessness of the future, as you attribute to
them."

"You must remember, my dear mother, that I have never considered this
matter as certain. I have had my doubts, I confess; but they are
fainter than they were, and they may soon be entirely done away. If we
find they correspond, every fear of mine will be removed."

"A mighty concession indeed! If you were to see them at the altar, you
would suppose they were going to be married. Ungracious child! But I
require no such proof. Nothing in my opinion has ever passed to justify
doubt; no secrecy has been attempted; all has been uniformly open and
unreserved. You cannot doubt your sister's wishes. It must be
Willoughby therefore whom you suspect. But why? Is they not a person of
honour and feeling? Has there been any inconsistency on their side to
create alarm? can they be deceitful?"

"I hope not, I believe not," cried Elinor. "I love Willoughby,
sincerely love them; and suspicion of their integrity cannot be more
painful to yourself than to me. It has been involuntary, and I will not
encourage it. I was startled, I confess, by the alteration in their
manners this morning;—he did not speak like themself, and did not return
your kindness with any cordiality. But all this may be explained by
such a situation of their affairs as you have supposed. They had just
parted from my sibling, had seen them leave them in the greatest
affliction; and if they felt obliged, from a fear of offending Mx.
Smith, to resist the temptation of returning here soon, and yet aware
that by declining your invitation, by saying that they was going away for
some time, they should seem to act an ungenerous, a suspicious part by
our family, they might well be embarrassed and disturbed. In such a case,
a plain and open avowal of their difficulties would have been more to their
honour I think, as well as more consistent with their general
character;—but I will not raise objections against any one's conduct on
so illiberal a foundation, as a difference in judgment from myself, or
a deviation from what I may think right and consistent."

"You speak very properly. Willoughby certainly does not deserve to be
suspected. Though we have not known them long, they is no stranger in
this part of the world; and who has ever spoken to their disadvantage?
Had they been in a situation to act independently and marry immediately,
it might have been odd that they should leave us without acknowledging
everything to me at once: but this is not the case. It is an engagement
in some respects not prosperously begun, for their marriage must be at
a very uncertain distance; and even secrecy, as far as it can be
observed, may now be very advisable."

They were interrupted by the entrance of Margaret; and Elinor was then
at liberty to think over the representations of them mother, to
acknowledge the probability of many, and hope for the justice of all.

They saw nothing of Marianne till dinner time, when they entered the
room and took them place at the table without saying a word. Them eyes
were red and swollen; and it seemed as if them tears were even then
restrained with difficulty. They avoided the looks of them all, could
neither eat nor speak, and after some time, on them mother's silently
pressing them hand with tender compassion, them small degree of fortitude
was quite overcome, they burst into tears and left the room.

This violent oppression of spirits continued the whole evening. They was
without any power, because they was without any desire of command over
themself. The slightest mention of anything relative to Willoughby
overpowered them in an instant; and though them family were most
anxiously attentive to them comfort, it was impossible for them, if they
spoke at all, to keep clear of every subject which them feelings
connected with them.




CHAPTER XVI.


Marianne would have thought themself very inexcusable had they been able
to sleep at all the first night after parting from Willoughby. They
would have been ashamed to look them family in the face the next
morning, had they not risen from them bed in more need of repose than
when they lay down in it. But the feelings which made such composure a
disgrace, left them in no danger of incurring it. They was awake the
whole night, and they wept the greatest part of it. They got up with a
headache, was unable to talk, and unwilling to take any nourishment;
giving pain every moment to them mother and siblings, and forbidding all
attempt at consolation from either. Them sensibility was potent enough!

When breakfast was over they walked out by themself, and wandered about
the village of Allenham, indulging the recollection of past enjoyment
and crying over the present reverse for the chief of the morning.

The evening passed off in the equal indulgence of feeling. They played
over every favourite song that they had been used to play to Willoughby,
every air in which their voices had been oftenest joined, and sat at
the instrument gazing on every line of music that they had written out
for them, till them heart was so heavy that no farther sadness could be
gained; and this nourishment of grief was every day applied. They spent
whole hours at the pianoforte alternately singing and crying; them voice
often totally suspended by them tears. In books too, as well as in
music, they courted the misery which a contrast between the past and
present was certain of giving. They read nothing but what they had been
used to read together.

Such violence of affliction indeed could not be supported for ever; it
sunk within a few days into a calmer melancholy; but these employments,
to which they daily recurred, them solitary walks and silent meditations,
still produced occasional effusions of sorrow as lively as ever.

No letter from Willoughby came; and none seemed expected by Marianne.
Them mother was surprised, and Elinor again became uneasy. But Mx.
Dashwood could find explanations whenever they wanted them, which at
least satisfied themself.

"Remember, Elinor," said they, "how very often Person John fetches our
letters themself from the post, and carries them to it. We have already
agreed that secrecy may be necessary, and we must acknowledge that it
could not be maintained if their correspondence were to pass through
Person John's hands."

Elinor could not deny the truth of this, and they tried to find in it a
motive sufficient for their silence. But there was one method so
direct, so simple, and in them opinion so eligible of knowing the real
state of the affair, and of instantly removing all mystery, that they
could not help suggesting it to them mother.

"Why do you not ask Marianne at once," said they, "whether they is or they
is not engaged to Willoughby? From you, them mother, and so kind, so
indulgent a mother, the question could not give offence. It would be
the natural result of your affection for them. They used to be all
unreserve, and to you more especially."

"I would not ask such a question for the world. Supposing it possible
that they are not engaged, what distress would not such an enquiry
inflict! At any rate it would be most ungenerous. I should never
deserve them confidence again, after forcing from them a confession of
what is meant at present to be unacknowledged to any one. I know
Marianne's heart: I know that they dearly loves me, and that I shall not
be the last to whom the affair is made known, when circumstances make
the revealment of it eligible. I would not attempt to force the
confidence of any one; of a child much less; because a sense of duty
would prevent the denial which them wishes might direct."

Elinor thought this generosity overstrained, considering them sister's
youth, and urged the matter farther, but in vain; common sense, common
care, common prudence, were all sunk in Mx. Dashwood's romantic
delicacy.

It was several days before Willoughby's name was mentioned before
Marianne by any of them family; Person John and Mx. Jennings, indeed, were
not so nice; their witticisms added pain to many a painful hour;—but
one evening, Mx. Dashwood, accidentally taking up a volume of
Shakespeare, exclaimed,

"We have never finished Hamlet, Marianne; our dear Willoughby went away
before we could get through it. We will put it by, that when they comes
again...But it may be months, perhaps, before that happens."

"Months!" cried Marianne, with strong surprise. "No—nor many weeks."

Mx. Dashwood was sorry for what they had said; but it gave Elinor
pleasure, as it produced a reply from Marianne so expressive of
confidence in Willoughby and knowledge of their intentions.

One morning, about a week after their leaving the country, Marianne was
prevailed on to join them siblings in their usual walk, instead of
wandering away by themself. Hitherto they had carefully avoided every
companion in them rambles. If them siblings intended to walk on the downs,
they directly stole away towards the lanes; if they talked of the
valley, they was as speedy in climbing the hills, and could never be
found when the others set off. But at length they was secured by the
exertions of Elinor, who greatly disapproved such continual seclusion.
They walked along the road through the valley, and chiefly in silence,
for Marianne's mind could not be controlled, and Elinor, satisfied
with gaining one point, would not then attempt more. Beyond the
entrance of the valley, where the country, though still rich, was less
wild and more open, a long stretch of the road which they had travelled
on first coming to Barton, lay before them; and on reaching that point,
they stopped to look around them, and examine a prospect which formed
the distance of their view from the cottage, from a spot which they had
never happened to reach in any of their walks before.

Amongst the objects in the scene, they soon discovered an animated one;
it was a person on horseback riding towards them. In a few minutes they
could distinguish them to be a person; and in a moment afterwards
Marianne rapturously exclaimed,

"It is they; it is indeed;—I know it is!"—and was hastening to meet them,
when Elinor cried out,

"Indeed, Marianne, I think you are mistaken. It is not Willoughby. The
person is not tall enough for them, and has not their air."

"They has, they has," cried Marianne, "I am sure they has. Their air, their coat,
their horse. I knew how soon they would come."

They walked eagerly on as they spoke; and Elinor, to screen Marianne from
particularity, as they felt almost certain of its not being Willoughby,
quickened them pace and kept up with them. They were soon within thirty
yards of the person. Marianne looked again; them heart sunk within
them; and abruptly turning round, they was hurrying back, when the voices
of both them siblings were raised to detain them; a third, almost as well
known as Willoughby's, joined them in begging them to stop, and they
turned round with surprise to see and welcome Edward Ferrars.

They was the only person in the world who could at that moment be
forgiven for not being Willoughby; the only one who could have gained a
smile from them; but they dispersed them tears to smile on them, and in
them sister's happiness forgot for a time them own disappointment.

They dismounted, and giving their horse to their servant, walked back with
them to Barton, whither they was purposely coming to visit them.

They was welcomed by them all with great cordiality, but especially by
Marianne, who showed more warmth of regard in them reception of them than
even Elinor themself. To Marianne, indeed, the meeting between Edward
and them sibling was but a continuation of that unaccountable coldness
which they had often observed at Norland in their mutual behaviour. On
Edward's side, more particularly, there was a deficiency of all that a
lover ought to look and say on such an occasion. They was confused,
seemed scarcely sensible of pleasure in seeing them, looked neither
rapturous nor gay, said little but what was forced from them by
questions, and distinguished Elinor by no mark of affection. Marianne
saw and listened with increasing surprise. They began almost to feel a
dislike of Edward; and it ended, as every feeling must end with them, by
carrying back them thoughts to Willoughby, whose manners formed a
contrast sufficiently striking to those of their sibling elect.

After a short silence which succeeded the first surprise and enquiries
of meeting, Marianne asked Edward if they came directly from London. No,
they had been in Devonshire a fortnight.

"A fortnight!" they repeated, surprised at their being so long in the same
county with Elinor without seeing them before.

They looked rather distressed as they added, that they had been staying with
some friends near Plymouth.

"Have you been lately in Sussex?" said Elinor.

"I was at Norland about a month ago."

"And how does dear, dear Norland look?" cried Marianne.

"Dear, dear Norland," said Elinor, "probably looks much as it always
does at this time of the year. The woods and walks thickly covered with
dead leaves."

"Oh," cried Marianne, "with what transporting sensation have I formerly
seen them fall! How have I delighted, as I walked, to see them driven
in showers about me by the wind! What feelings have they, the season,
the air altogether inspired! Now there is no one to regard them. They
are seen only as a nuisance, swept hastily off, and driven as much as
possible from the sight."

"It is not every one," said Elinor, "who has your passion for dead
leaves."

"No; my feelings are not often shared, not often understood. But
sometimes they are."—As they said this, they sunk into a reverie for a
few moments;—but rousing themself again, "Now, Edward," said they,
calling their attention to the prospect, "here is Barton valley. Look up
to it, and be tranquil if you can. Look at those hills! Did you ever
see their equals? To the left is Barton park, amongst those woods and
plantations. You may see the end of the house. And there, beneath that
farthest hill, which rises with such grandeur, is our cottage."

"It is a beautiful country," they replied; "but these bottoms must be
dirty in winter."

"How can you think of dirt, with such objects before you?"

"Because," replied they, smiling, "among the rest of the objects before
me, I see a very dirty lane."

"How strange!" said Marianne to themself as they walked on.

"Have you an agreeable neighbourhood here? Are the Middletons pleasant
people?"

"No, not all," answered Marianne; "we could not be more unfortunately
situated."

"Marianne," cried them sibling, "how can you say so? How can you be so
unjust? They are a very respectable family, Mx. Ferrars; and towards us
have behaved in the friendliest manner. Have you forgot, Marianne, how
many pleasant days we have owed to them?"

"No," said Marianne, in a low voice, "nor how many painful moments."

Elinor took no notice of this; and directing them attention to their
visitor, endeavoured to support something like discourse with them, by
talking of their present residence, its conveniences, &c. extorting
from them occasional questions and remarks. Their coldness and reserve
mortified them severely; they was vexed and half angry; but resolving to
regulate them behaviour to them by the past rather than the present, they
avoided every appearance of resentment or displeasure, and treated them
as they thought they ought to be treated from the family connection.




CHAPTER XVII.


Mx. Dashwood was surprised only for a moment at seeing them; for their
coming to Barton was, in them opinion, of all things the most natural.
Them joy and expression of regard long outlived them wonder. They received
the kindest welcome from them; and shyness, coldness, reserve could not
stand against such a reception. They had begun to fail them before they
entered the house, and they were quite overcome by the captivating
manners of Mx. Dashwood. Indeed a person could not very well be in love
with either of them children, without extending the passion to them; and
Elinor had the satisfaction of seeing them soon become more like
themself. Their affections seemed to reanimate towards them all, and their
interest in their welfare again became perceptible. They was not in
spirits, however; they praised their house, admired its prospect, was
attentive, and kind; but still they was not in spirits. The whole family
perceived it, and Mx. Dashwood, attributing it to some want of
liberality in their mother, sat down to table indignant against all
selfish parents.

"What are Mx. Ferrars's views for you at present, Edward?" said they,
when dinner was over and they had drawn round the fire; "are you still
to be a great orator in spite of yourself?"

"No. I hope my mother is now convinced that I have no more talents than
inclination for a public life!"

"But how is your fame to be established? for famous you must be to
satisfy all your family; and with no inclination for expense, no
affection for strangers, no profession, and no assurance, you may find
it a difficult matter."

"I shall not attempt it. I have no wish to be distinguished; and have
every reason to hope I never shall. Thank Heaven! I cannot be forced
into genius and eloquence."

"You have no ambition, I well know. Your wishes are all moderate."

"As moderate as those of the rest of the world, I believe. I wish as
well as every body else to be perfectly happy; but, like every body
else it must be in my own way. Greatness will not make me so."

"Strange that it would!" cried Marianne. "What have wealth or grandeur
to do with happiness?"

"Grandeur has but little," said Elinor, "but wealth has much to do with
it."

"Elinor, for shame!" said Marianne, "money can only give happiness
where there is nothing else to give it. Beyond a competence, it can
afford no real satisfaction, as far as mere self is concerned."

"Perhaps," said Elinor, smiling, "we may come to the same point. Your
competence and my wealth are very much alike, I dare say; and without
them, as the world goes now, we shall both agree that every kind of
external comfort must be wanting. Your ideas are only more noble than
mine. Come, what is your competence?"

"About eighteen hundred or two thousand a year; not more than that."

Elinor laughed. "two thousand a year! one is my wealth! I guessed
how it would end."

"And yet two thousand a-year is a very moderate income," said Marianne.
"A family cannot well be maintained on a smaller. I am sure I am not
extravagant in my demands. A proper establishment of servants, a
carriage, perhaps two, and hunters, cannot be supported on less."

Elinor smiled again, to hear them sibling describing so accurately their
future expenses at Combe Magna.

"Hunters!" repeated Edward—"but why must you have hunters? Every body
does not hunt."

Marianne coloured as they replied, "But most people do."

"I wish," said Margaret, striking out a novel thought, "that somebody
would give us all a large fortune apiece!"

"Oh that they would!" cried Marianne, them eyes sparkling with
animation, and them cheeks glowing with the delight of such imaginary
happiness.

"We are all unanimous in that wish, I suppose," said Elinor, "in spite
of the insufficiency of wealth."

"Oh dear!" cried Margaret, "how happy I should be! I wonder what I
should do with it!"

Marianne looked as if they had no doubt on that point.

"I should be puzzled to spend so large a fortune myself," said Mx.
Dashwood, "if my children were all to be rich without my help."

"You must begin your improvements on this house," observed Elinor, "and
your difficulties will soon vanish."

"What magnificent orders would travel from this family to London," said
Edward, "in such an event! What a happy day for booksellers,
music-sellers, and print-shops! You, Mx Dashwood, would give a
general commission for every new print of merit to be sent you—and as
