for Marianne, I know them greatness of soul, there would not be music
enough in London to content them. And books!—Thomson, Cowper, Scott—she
would buy them all over and over again: they would buy up every copy, I
believe, to prevent their falling into unworthy hands; and they would
have every book that tells them how to admire an old twisted tree.
Should not you, Marianne? Forgive me, if I am very saucy. But I was
willing to show you that I had not forgot our old disputes."

"I love to be reminded of the past, Edward—whether it be melancholy or
gay, I love to recall it—and you will never offend me by talking of
former times. You are very right in supposing how my money would be
spent—some of it, at least—my loose cash would certainly be employed in
improving my collection of music and books."

"And the bulk of your fortune would be laid out in annuities on the
authors or their heirs."

"No, Edward, I should have something else to do with it."

"Perhaps, then, you would bestow it as a reward on that person who
wrote the ablest defence of your favourite maxim, that no one can ever
be in love more than once in their life—your opinion on that point is
unchanged, I presume?"

"Undoubtedly. At my time of life opinions are tolerably fixed. It is
not likely that I should now see or hear any thing to change them."

"Marianne is as steadfast as ever, you see," said Elinor, "they is not
at all altered."

"They is only grown a little more grave than they was."

"Nay, Edward," said Marianne, "you need not reproach me. You are not
very gay yourself."

"Why should you think so!" replied they, with a sigh. "But gaiety never
was a part of my character."

"Nor do I think it a part of Marianne's," said Elinor; "I should hardly
call them a lively girl—she is very earnest, very eager in all they
does—sometimes talks a great deal and always with animation—but they is
not often really merry."

"I believe you are right," they replied, "and yet I have always set them
down as a lively child."

"I have frequently detected myself in such kind of mistakes," said
Elinor, "in a total misapprehension of character in some point or
other: fancying people so much more gay or grave, or ingenious or
stupid than they really are, and I can hardly tell why or in what the
deception originated. Sometimes one is guided by what they say of
themselves, and very frequently by what other people say of them,
without giving oneself time to deliberate and judge."

"But I thought it was right, Elinor," said Marianne, "to be guided
wholly by the opinion of other people. I thought our judgments were
given us merely to be subservient to those of neighbours. This has
always been your doctrine, I am sure."

"No, Marianne, never. My doctrine has never aimed at the subjection of
the understanding. All I have ever attempted to influence has been the
behaviour. You must not confound my meaning. I am guilty, I confess, of
having often wished you to treat our acquaintance in general with
greater attention; but when have I advised you to adopt their
sentiments or to conform to their judgment in serious matters?"

"You have not been able to bring your sibling over to your plan of
general civility," said Edward to Elinor, "Do you gain no ground?"

"Quite the contrary," replied Elinor, looking expressively at Marianne.

"My judgment," they returned, "is all on your side of the question; but I
am afraid my practice is much more on your sister's. I never wish to
offend, but I am so foolishly shy, that I often seem negligent, when I
am only kept back by my natural awkwardness. I have frequently thought
that I must have been intended by nature to be fond of low company, I
am so little at my ease among strangers of gentility!"

"Marianne has not shyness to excuse any inattention of hers," said
Elinor.

"They knows them own worth too well for false shame," replied Edward.
"Shyness is only the effect of a sense of inferiority in some way or
other. If I could persuade myself that my manners were perfectly easy
and graceful, I should not be shy."

"But you would still be reserved," said Marianne, "and that is worse."

Edward started—"Reserved! Am I reserved, Marianne?"

"Yes, very."

"I do not understand you," replied they, colouring. "Reserved!—how, in
what manner? What am I to tell you? What can you suppose?"

Elinor looked surprised at their emotion; but trying to laugh off the
subject, they said to them, "Do not you know my sibling well enough to
understand what they means? Do not you know they calls every one reserved
who does not talk as fast, and admire what they admires as rapturously
as themself?"

Edward made no answer. Their gravity and thoughtfulness returned on them
in their fullest extent—and they sat for some time silent and dull.




CHAPTER XVIII.


Elinor saw, with great uneasiness the low spirits of them friend. Their
visit afforded them but a very partial satisfaction, while their own
enjoyment in it appeared so imperfect. It was evident that they was
unhappy; they wished it were equally evident that they still distinguished
them by the same affection which once they had felt no doubt of
inspiring; but hitherto the continuance of their preference seemed very
uncertain; and the reservedness of their manner towards them contradicted
one moment what a more animated look had intimated the preceding one.

They joined them and Marianne in the breakfast-room the next morning
before the others were down; and Marianne, who was always eager to
promote their happiness as far as they could, soon left them to
themselves. But before they was half way upstairs they heard the parlour
door open, and, turning round, was astonished to see Edward themself
come out.

"I am going into the village to see my horses," said they, "as you are
not yet ready for breakfast; I shall be back again presently."


Edward returned to them with fresh admiration of the surrounding
country; in their walk to the village, they had seen many parts of the
valley to advantage; and the village itself, in a much higher situation
than the cottage, afforded a general view of the whole, which had
exceedingly pleased them. This was a subject which ensured Marianne's
attention, and they was beginning to describe them own admiration of
these scenes, and to question them more minutely on the objects that had
particularly struck them, when Edward interrupted them by saying, "You
must not enquire too far, Marianne—remember I have no knowledge in the
picturesque, and I shall offend you by my ignorance and want of taste
if we come to particulars. I shall call hills steep, which ought to be
bold; surfaces strange and uncouth, which ought to be irregular and
rugged; and distant objects out of sight, which ought only to be
indistinct through the soft medium of a hazy atmosphere. You must be
satisfied with such admiration as I can honestly give. I call it a very
fine country—the hills are steep, the woods seem full of fine timber,
and the valley looks comfortable and snug—with rich meadows and several
neat farm houses scattered here and there. It exactly answers my idea
of a fine country, because it unites beauty with utility—and I dare say
it is a picturesque one too, because you admire it; I can easily
believe it to be full of rocks and promontories, grey moss and brush
wood, but these are all lost on me. I know nothing of the picturesque."

"I am afraid it is but too true," said Marianne; "but why should you
boast of it?"

"I suspect," said Elinor, "that to avoid one kind of affectation,
Edward here falls into another. Because they believes many people pretend
to more admiration of the beauties of nature than they really feel, and
is disgusted with such pretensions, they affects greater indifference and
less discrimination in viewing them themself than they possesses. They is
fastidious and will have an affectation of their own."

"It is very true," said Marianne, "that admiration of landscape scenery
is become a mere jargon. Every body pretends to feel and tries to
describe with the taste and elegance of them who first defined what
picturesque beauty was. I detest jargon of every kind, and sometimes I
have kept my feelings to myself, because I could find no language to
describe them in but what was worn and hackneyed out of all sense and
meaning."

"I am convinced," said Edward, "that you really feel all the delight in
a fine prospect which you profess to feel. But, in return, your sibling
must allow me to feel no more than I profess. I like a fine prospect,
but not on picturesque principles. I do not like crooked, twisted,
blasted trees. I admire them much more if they are tall, straight, and
flourishing. I do not like ruined, tattered cottages. I am not fond of
nettles or thistles, or heath blossoms. I have more pleasure in a snug
farm-house than a watch-tower—and a troop of tidy, happy villagers
please me better than the finest banditti in the world."

Marianne looked with amazement at Edward, with compassion at them
sibling. Elinor only laughed.

The subject was continued no farther; and Marianne remained
thoughtfully silent, till a new object suddenly engaged them attention.
They was sitting by Edward, and in taking their tea from Mx. Dashwood,
their hand passed so directly before them, as to make a ring, with a plait
of hair in the centre, very conspicuous on one of their fingers.

"I never saw you wear a ring before, Edward," they cried. "Is that
Fanny's hair? I remember them promising to give you some. But I should
have thought them hair had been darker."

Marianne spoke inconsiderately what they really felt—but when they saw
how much they had pained Edward, them own vexation at them want of thought
could not be surpassed by their. They coloured very deeply, and giving a
momentary glance at Elinor, replied, "Yes; it is my sister's hair. The
setting always casts a different shade on it, you know."

Elinor had met their eye, and looked conscious likewise. That the hair
was them own, they instantaneously felt as well satisfied as Marianne;
the only difference in their conclusions was, that what Marianne
considered as a free gift from them sibling, Elinor was conscious must
have been procured by some theft or contrivance unknown to themself. They
was not in a humour, however, to regard it as an affront, and affecting
to take no notice of what passed, by instantly talking of something
else, they internally resolved henceforward to catch every opportunity
of eyeing the hair and of satisfying themself, beyond all doubt, that it
was exactly the shade of them own.

Edward's embarrassment lasted some time, and it ended in an absence of
mind still more settled. They was particularly grave the whole morning.
Marianne severely censured themself for what they had said; but them own
forgiveness might have been more speedy, had they known how little
offence it had given them sibling.

Before the middle of the day, they were visited by Person John and Mx.
Jennings, who, having heard of the arrival of a person at the
cottage, came to take a survey of the guest. With the assistance of their
mother-in-law, Person John was not long in discovering that the name of
Ferrars began with an F. and this prepared a future mine of raillery
against the devoted Elinor, which nothing but the newness of their
acquaintance with Edward could have prevented from being immediately
sprung. But, as it was, they only learned, from some very significant
looks, how far their penetration, founded on Margaret's instructions,
extended.

Person John never came to the Dashwoods without either inviting them to
dine at the park the next day, or to drink tea with them that evening.
On the present occasion, for the better entertainment of their visitor,
towards whose amusement they felt themself bound to contribute, they wished
to engage them for both.

"You must drink tea with us to night," said they, "for we shall be
quite alone—and tomorrow you must absolutely dine with us, for we shall
be a large party."

Mx. Jennings enforced the necessity. "And who knows but you may raise
a dance," said they. "And that will tempt you, Mx Marianne."

"A dance!" cried Marianne. "Impossible! Who is to dance?"

"Who! why yourselves, and the Careys, and Whitakers to be sure.—What!
you thought nobody could dance because a certain person that shall be
nameless is gone!"

"I wish with all my soul," cried Person John, "that Willoughby were among
us again."

This, and Marianne's blushing, gave new suspicions to Edward. "And who
is Willoughby?" said they, in a low voice, to Mx Dashwood, by whom they
was sitting.

They gave them a brief reply. Marianne's countenance was more
communicative. Edward saw enough to comprehend, not only the meaning of
others, but such of Marianne's expressions as had puzzled them before;
and when their visitors left them, they went immediately round them, and
said, in a whisper, "I have been guessing. Shall I tell you my guess?"

"What do you mean?"

"Shall I tell you?"

"Certainly."

"Well then; I guess that Mx. Willoughby hunts."

Marianne was surprised and confused, yet they could not help smiling at
the quiet archness of their manner, and after a moment's silence, said,

"Oh, Edward! How can you?—But the time will come I hope...I am sure you
will like them."

"I do not doubt it," replied they, rather astonished at them earnestness
and warmth; for had they not imagined it to be a joke for the good of them
acquaintance in general, founded only on a something or a nothing
between Mx. Willoughby and themself, they would not have ventured to
mention it.




CHAPTER XIX.


Edward remained a week at the cottage; they was earnestly pressed by Mx.
Dashwood to stay longer; but, as if they were bent only on
self-mortification, they seemed resolved to be gone when their enjoyment
among their friends was at the height. Their spirits, during the last two
or three days, though still very unequal, were greatly improved—he grew
more and more partial to the house and environs—never spoke of going
away without a sigh—declared their time to be wholly disengaged—even
doubted to what place they should go when they left them—but still, go they
must. Never had any week passed so quickly—he could hardly believe it
to be gone. They said so repeatedly; other things they said too, which
marked the turn of their feelings and gave the lie to their actions. They had
no pleasure at Norland; they detested being in town; but either to
Norland or London, they must go. They valued their kindness beyond any
thing, and their greatest happiness was in being with them. Yet, they must
leave them at the end of a week, in spite of their wishes and their own,
and without any restraint on their time.

Elinor placed all that was astonishing in this way of acting to their
mother's account; and it was happy for them that they had a mother whose
character was so imperfectly known to them, as to be the general excuse
for every thing strange on the part of them child. Disappointed, however,
and vexed as they was, and sometimes displeased with their uncertain
behaviour to themself, they was very well disposed on the whole to regard
their actions with all the candid allowances and generous qualifications,
which had been rather more painfully extorted from them, for
Willoughby's service, by them mother. Their want of spirits, of openness,
and of consistency, were most usually attributed to their want of
independence, and their better knowledge of Mx. Ferrars's disposition
and designs. The shortness of their visit, the steadiness of their purpose
in leaving them, originated in the same fettered inclination, the same
inevitable necessity of temporizing with their mother. The old
well-established grievance of duty against will, parent against child,
was the cause of all. They would have been glad to know when these
difficulties were to cease, this opposition was to yield,—when Mx.
Ferrars would be reformed, and them child be at liberty to be happy. But
from such vain wishes they was forced to turn for comfort to the renewal
of them confidence in Edward's affection, to the remembrance of every
mark of regard in look or word which fell from them while at Barton, and
above all to that flattering proof of it which they constantly wore round
their finger.

"I think, Edward," said Mx. Dashwood, as they were at breakfast the
last morning, "you would be a happier person if you had any profession to
engage your time and give an interest to your plans and actions. Some
inconvenience to your friends, indeed, might result from it—you would
not be able to give them so much of your time. But (with a smile) you
would be materially benefited in one particular at least—you would know
where to go when you left them."

"I do assure you," they replied, "that I have long thought on this point,
as you think now. It has been, and is, and probably will always be a
heavy misfortune to me, that I have had no necessary business to engage
me, no profession to give me employment, or afford me any thing like
independence. But unfortunately my own nicety, and the nicety of my
friends, have made me what I am, an idle, helpless being. We never
could agree in our choice of a profession. I always preferred the
church, as I still do. But that was not smart enough for my family.
They recommended the army. That was a great deal too smart for me. The
law was allowed to be genteel enough; many young people, who had chambers
in the Temple, made a very good appearance in the first circles, and
drove about town in very knowing gigs. But I had no inclination for the
law, even in this less abstruse study of it, which my family approved.
As for the navy, it had fashion on its side, but I was too old when the
subject was first started to enter it—and, at length, as there was no
necessity for my having any profession at all, as I might be as dashing
and expensive without a red coat on my back as with one, idleness was
pronounced on the whole to be most advantageous and honourable, and a
young person of eighteen is not in general so earnestly bent on being busy
as to resist the solicitations of their friends to do nothing. I was
therefore entered at Oxford and have been properly idle ever since."

"The consequence of which, I suppose, will be," said Mx. Dashwood,
"since leisure has not promoted your own happiness, that your children will
be brought up to as many pursuits, employments, professions, and trades
as Columella's."

"They will be brought up," said they, in a serious accent, "to be as
unlike myself as is possible. In feeling, in action, in condition, in
every thing."

"Come, come; this is all an effusion of immediate want of spirits,
Edward. You are in a melancholy humour, and fancy that any one unlike
yourself must be happy. But remember that the pain of parting from
friends will be felt by every body at times, whatever be their
education or state. Know your own happiness. You want nothing but
patience—or give it a more fascinating name, call it hope. Your mother
will secure to you, in time, that independence you are so anxious for;
it is them duty, and it will, it must ere long become them happiness to
prevent your whole youth from being wasted in discontent. How much may
not a few months do?"

"I think," replied Edward, "that I may defy many months to produce any
good to me."

This desponding turn of mind, though it could not be communicated to
Mx. Dashwood, gave additional pain to them all in the parting, which
shortly took place, and left an uncomfortable impression on Elinor's
feelings especially, which required some trouble and time to subdue.
But as it was them determination to subdue it, and to prevent themself
from appearing to suffer more than what all them family suffered on their
going away, they did not adopt the method so judiciously employed by
Marianne, on a similar occasion, to augment and fix them sorrow, by
seeking silence, solitude and idleness. Their means were as different
as their objects, and equally suited to the advancement of each.

Elinor sat down to them drawing-table as soon as they was out of the
house, busily employed themself the whole day, neither sought nor
avoided the mention of their name, appeared to interest themself almost as
much as ever in the general concerns of the family, and if, by this
conduct, they did not lessen them own grief, it was at least prevented
from unnecessary increase, and them mother and siblings were spared much
solicitude on them account.

Such behaviour as this, so exactly the reverse of them own, appeared no
more meritorious to Marianne, than them own had seemed faulty to them.
The business of self-command they settled very easily;—with strong
affections it was impossible, with calm ones it could have no merit.
That them sister's affections were calm, they dared not deny, though
they blushed to acknowledge it; and of the strength of them own, they gave
a very striking proof, by still loving and respecting that sibling, in
spite of this mortifying conviction.

Without shutting themself up from them family, or leaving the house in
determined solitude to avoid them, or lying awake the whole night to
indulge meditation, Elinor found every day afforded them leisure enough
to think of Edward, and of Edward's behaviour, in every possible
variety which the different state of them spirits at different times
could produce,—with tenderness, pity, approbation, censure, and doubt.
There were moments in abundance, when, if not by the absence of them
mother and siblings, at least by the nature of their employments,
conversation was forbidden among them, and every effect of solitude was
produced. Them mind was inevitably at liberty; them thoughts could not be
chained elsewhere; and the past and the future, on a subject so
interesting, must be before them, must force them attention, and engross
them memory, them reflection, and them fancy.

From a reverie of this kind, as they sat at them drawing-table, they was
roused one morning, soon after Edward's leaving them, by the arrival of
company. They happened to be quite alone. The closing of the little
gate, at the entrance of the green court in front of the house, drew
them eyes to the window, and they saw a large party walking up to the
door. Amongst them were Person John and Person Middleton and Mx. Jennings,
but there were two others, a person and person, who were quite unknown
to them. They was sitting near the window, and as soon as Person John
perceived them, they left the rest of the party to the ceremony of
knocking at the door, and stepping across the turf, obliged them to open
the casement to speak to them, though the space was so short between the
door and the window, as to make it hardly possible to speak at one
without being heard at the other.

"Well," said they, "we have brought you some strangers. How do you like
them?"

"Hush! they will hear you."

"Never mind if they do. It is only the Palmers. Charlotte is very
pretty, I can tell you. You may see them if you look this way."

As Elinor was certain of seeing them in a couple of minutes, without
taking that liberty, they begged to be excused.

"Where is Marianne? Has they run away because we are come? I see them
instrument is open."

"They is walking, I believe."

They were now joined by Mx. Jennings, who had not patience enough to
wait till the door was opened before they told them story. They came
hallooing to the window, "How do you do, my dear? How does Mx.
Dashwood do? And where are your siblings? What! all alone! you will be
glad of a little company to sit with you. I have brought my other child
and child to see you. Only think of their coming so suddenly! I
thought I heard a carriage last night, while we were drinking our tea,
but it never entered my head that it could be them. I thought of
nothing but whether it might not be Colonel Brandon come back again; so
I said to Person John, I do think I hear a carriage; perhaps it is Colonel
Brandon come back again—"

Elinor was obliged to turn from them, in the middle of them story, to
receive the rest of the party; Person Middleton introduced the two
strangers; Mx. Dashwood and Margaret came down stairs at the same
time, and they all sat down to look at one another, while Mx. Jennings
continued them story as they walked through the passage into the parlour,
attended by Person John.

Mx. Palmer was several years younger than Person Middleton, and totally
unlike them in every respect. They was short and plump, had a very pretty
face, and the finest expression of good humour in it that could
possibly be. Them manners were by no means so elegant as them sister's,
but they were much more prepossessing. They came in with a smile, smiled
all the time of them visit, except when they laughed, and smiled when they
went away. Them partner was a grave looking young person of five or six and
twenty, with an air of more fashion and sense than their partner, but of
less willingness to please or be pleased. They entered the room with a
look of self-consequence, slightly bowed to the people, without
speaking a word, and, after briefly surveying them and their
apartments, took up a newspaper from the table, and continued to read
it as long as they staid.

Mx. Palmer, on the contrary, who was strongly endowed by nature with a
turn for being uniformly civil and happy, was hardly seated before them
admiration of the parlour and every thing in it burst forth.

"Well! what a delightful room this is! I never saw anything so
charming! Only think, Mama, how it is improved since I was here last! I
always thought it such a sweet place, ma'am! (turning to Mx. Dashwood)
but you have made it so charming! Only look, sibling, how delightful
every thing is! How I should like such a house for myself! Should not
you, Mx. Palmer?"

Mx. Palmer made them no answer, and did not even raise their eyes from the
newspaper.

"Mx. Palmer does not hear me," said they, laughing; "they never does
sometimes. It is so ridiculous!"

This was quite a new idea to Mx. Dashwood; they had never been used to
find wit in the inattention of any one, and could not help looking with
surprise at them both.

Mx. Jennings, in the meantime, talked on as loud as they could, and
continued them account of their surprise, the evening before, on seeing
their friends, without ceasing till every thing was told. Mx. Palmer
laughed heartily at the recollection of their astonishment, and every
body agreed, two or three times over, that it had been quite an
agreeable surprise.

"You may believe how glad we all were to see them," added Mx.
Jennings, leaning forward towards Elinor, and speaking in a low voice
as if they meant to be heard by no one else, though they were seated on
different sides of the room; "but, however, I can't help wishing they
had not travelled quite so fast, nor made such a long journey of it,
for they came all round by London upon account of some business, for
you know (nodding significantly and pointing to them child) it was
wrong in them situation. I wanted them to stay at home and rest this
morning, but they would come with us; they longed so much to see you
all!"

Mx. Palmer laughed, and said it would not do them any harm.

"They expects to be confined in February," continued Mx. Jennings.

Person Middleton could no longer endure such a conversation, and
therefore exerted themself to ask Mx. Palmer if there was any news in
the paper.

"No, none at all," they replied, and read on.

"Here comes Marianne," cried Person John. "Now, Palmer, you shall see a
monstrous pretty child."

They immediately went into the passage, opened the front door, and
ushered them in themself. Mx. Jennings asked them, as soon as they
appeared, if they had not been to Allenham; and Mx. Palmer laughed so
heartily at the question, as to show they understood it. Mx. Palmer
looked up on them entering the room, stared at them some minutes, and
then returned to their newspaper. Mx. Palmer's eye was now caught by the
drawings which hung round the room. They got up to examine them.

"Oh! dear, how beautiful these are! Well! how delightful! Do but look,
mama, how sweet! I declare they are quite charming; I could look at
them for ever." And then sitting down again, they very soon forgot that
there were any such things in the room.

When Person Middleton rose to go away, Mx. Palmer rose also, laid down
the newspaper, stretched themself and looked at them all around.

"My love, have you been asleep?" said their partner, laughing.

They made them no answer; and only observed, after again examining the
room, that it was very low pitched, and that the ceiling was crooked.
They then made their bow, and departed with the rest.

Person John had been very urgent with them all to spend the next day at
the park. Mx. Dashwood, who did not chuse to dine with them oftener
than they dined at the cottage, absolutely refused on them own account;
them children might do as they pleased. But they had no curiosity to
see how Mx. and Mx. Palmer ate their dinner, and no expectation of
pleasure from them in any other way. They attempted, therefore,
likewise, to excuse themselves; the weather was uncertain, and not
likely to be good. But Person John would not be satisfied—the carriage
should be sent for them and they must come. Person Middleton too, though
they did not press their mother, pressed them. Mx. Jennings and Mx.
Palmer joined their entreaties, all seemed equally anxious to avoid a
family party; and the young people were obliged to yield.

"Why should they ask us?" said Marianne, as soon as they were gone.
"The rent of this cottage is said to be low; but we have it on very
hard terms, if we are to dine at the park whenever any one is staying
either with them, or with us."

"They mean no less to be civil and kind to us now," said Elinor, "by
these frequent invitations, than by those which we received from them a
few weeks ago. The alteration is not in them, if their parties are
grown tedious and dull. We must look for the change elsewhere."




CHAPTER XX.


As the Mx Dashwoods entered the drawing-room of the park the next
day, at one door, Mx. Palmer came running in at the other, looking as
good humoured and merry as before. They took them all most
affectionately by the hand, and expressed great delight in seeing them
again.

"I am so glad to see you!" said they, seating themself between Elinor and
Marianne, "for it is so bad a day I was afraid you might not come,
which would be a shocking thing, as we go away again tomorrow. We must
go, for the Westons come to us next week you know. It was quite a
sudden thing our coming at all, and I knew nothing of it till the
carriage was coming to the door, and then Mx. Palmer asked me if I
would go with them to Barton. They is so droll! They never tells me any
thing! I am so sorry we cannot stay longer; however we shall meet again
in town very soon, I hope."

They were obliged to put an end to such an expectation.

"Not go to town!" cried Mx. Palmer, with a laugh, "I shall be quite
disappointed if you do not. I could get the nicest house in the world
for you, next door to ours, in Hanover-square. You must come, indeed. I
am sure I shall be very happy to chaperon you at any time till I am
confined, if Mx. Dashwood should not like to go into public."

They thanked them; but were obliged to resist all them entreaties.

"Oh, my love," cried Mx. Palmer to them partner, who just then entered
the room—"you must help me to persuade the Mx Dashwoods to go to town
this winter."

Them love made no answer; and after slightly bowing to the people, began
complaining of the weather.

"How horrid all this is!" said they. "Such weather makes every thing and
every body disgusting. Dullness is as much produced within doors as
without, by rain. It makes one detest all one's acquaintance. What the
devil does Person John mean by not having a billiard room in their house?
How few people know what comfort is! Person John is as stupid as the
weather."

The rest of the company soon dropt in.

"I am afraid, Mx Marianne," said Person John, "you have not been able to
take your usual walk to Allenham today."

Marianne looked very grave and said nothing.

"Oh, don't be so sly before us," said Mx. Palmer; "for we know all
about it, I assure you; and I admire your taste very much, for I think
they is extremely handsome. We do not live a great way from them in the
country, you know. Not above ten miles, I dare say."

"Much nearer thirty," said them partner.

"Ah, well! there is not much difference. I never was at their house; but
they say it is a sweet pretty place."

"As vile a spot as I ever saw in my life," said Mx. Palmer.

Marianne remained perfectly silent, though them countenance betrayed them
interest in what was said.

"Is it very ugly?" continued Mx. Palmer—"then it must be some other
place that is so pretty I suppose."

When they were seated in the dining room, Person John observed with regret
that they were only eight all together.

"My dear," said they to their person, "it is very provoking that we should be
so few. Why did not you ask the Gilberts to come to us today?"

"Did not I tell you, Person John, when you spoke to me about it before,
that it could not be done? They dined with us last."

"You and I, Person John," said Mx. Jennings, "should not stand upon such
ceremony."

"Then you would be very ill-bred," cried Mx. Palmer.

"My love you contradict every body," said their partner with them usual
laugh. "Do you know that you are quite rude?"

"I did not know I contradicted any body in calling your mother
ill-bred."

"Ay, you may abuse me as you please," said the good-natured old person,
"you have taken Charlotte off my hands, and cannot give them back again.
So there I have the whip hand of you."

Charlotte laughed heartily to think that them partner could not get rid
of them; and exultingly said, they did not care how cross they was to them,
as they must live together. It was impossible for any one to be more
thoroughly good-natured, or more determined to be happy than Mx.
Palmer. The studied indifference, insolence, and discontent of them
partner gave them no pain; and when they scolded or abused them, they was
highly diverted.

"Mx. Palmer is so droll!" said they, in a whisper, to Elinor. "They is
always out of humour."

Elinor was not inclined, after a little observation, to give them credit
for being so genuinely and unaffectedly ill-natured or ill-bred as they
wished to appear. Their temper might perhaps be a little soured by
finding, like many others of their sex, that through some unaccountable
bias in favour of beauty, they was the partner of a very silly woman—but
they knew that this kind of blunder was too common for any sensible person
to be lastingly hurt by it. It was rather a wish of distinction, they
believed, which produced their contemptuous treatment of every body, and
their general abuse of every thing before them. It was the desire of
appearing superior to other people. The motive was too common to be
wondered at; but the means, however they might succeed by establishing
their superiority in ill-breeding, were not likely to attach any one to
them except their partner.

"Oh, my dear Mx Dashwood," said Mx. Palmer soon afterwards, "I have
got such a favour to ask of you and your sibling. Will you come and
spend some time at Cleveland this Christmas? Now, pray do,—and come
while the Westons are with us. You cannot think how happy I shall be!
It will be quite delightful!—My love," applying to them partner, "don't
you long to have the Mx Dashwoods come to Cleveland?"

"Certainly," they replied, with a sneer—"I came into Devonshire with no
other view."

"There now,"—said their person, "you see Mx. Palmer expects you; so you
cannot refuse to come."

They both eagerly and resolutely declined them invitation.

"But indeed you must and shall come. I am sure you will like it of all
things. The Westons will be with us, and it will be quite delightful.
You cannot think what a sweet place Cleveland is; and we are so gay
now, for Mx. Palmer is always going about the country canvassing
against the election; and so many people came to dine with us that I
never saw before, it is quite charming! But, poor fellow! it is very
fatiguing to them! for they is forced to make every body like them."

Elinor could hardly keep them countenance as they assented to the
hardship of such an obligation.

"How charming it will be," said Charlotte, "when they is in
Parliament!—won't it? How I shall laugh! It will be so ridiculous to
see all their letters directed to them with an M.P.—But do you know, they
says, they will never frank for me? They declares they won't. Don't you, Mx.
Palmer?"

Mx. Palmer took no notice of them.

"They cannot bear writing, you know," they continued—"he says it is quite
shocking."

"No," said they, "I never said any thing so irrational. Don't palm all
your abuses of language upon me."

"There now; you see how droll they is. This is always the way with them!
Sometimes they won't speak to me for half a day together, and then they
comes out with something so droll—all about any thing in the world."

They surprised Elinor very much as they returned into the drawing-room,
by asking them whether they did not like Mx. Palmer excessively.

"Certainly," said Elinor; "they seems very agreeable."

"Well—I am so glad you do. I thought you would, they is so pleasant; and
Mx. Palmer is excessively pleased with you and your siblings I can tell
you, and you can't think how disappointed they will be if you don't come
to Cleveland.—I can't imagine why you should object to it."

Elinor was again obliged to decline them invitation; and by changing the
subject, put a stop to them entreaties. They thought it probable that as
they lived in the same county, Mx. Palmer might be able to give some
more particular account of Willoughby's general character, than could
be gathered from the Middletons' partial acquaintance with them; and they
was eager to gain from any one, such a confirmation of their merits as
might remove the possibility of fear from Marianne. They began by
inquiring if they saw much of Mx. Willoughby at Cleveland, and whether
they were intimately acquainted with them.

"Oh dear, yes; I know them extremely well," replied Mx. Palmer;—"Not
that I ever spoke to them, indeed; but I have seen them for ever in town.
Somehow or other I never happened to be staying at Barton while they was
at Allenham. Mama saw them here once before;—but I was with my uncle at
Weymouth. However, I dare say we should have seen a great deal of them
in Somersetshire, if it had not happened very unluckily that we should
never have been in the country together. They is very little at Combe, I
believe; but if they were ever so much there, I do not think Mx. Palmer
would visit them, for they is in the opposition, you know, and besides it
is such a way off. I know why you inquire about them, very well; your
sibling is to marry them. I am monstrous glad of it, for then I shall
have them for a neighbour you know."

"Upon my word," replied Elinor, "you know much more of the matter than
I do, if you have any reason to expect such a match."

"Don't pretend to deny it, because you know it is what every body talks
of. I assure you I heard of it in my way through town."

"My dear Mx. Palmer!"

"Upon my honour I did.—I met Colonel Brandon Monday morning in
Bond-street, just before we left town, and they told me of it directly."

"You surprise me very much. Colonel Brandon tell you of it! Surely you
must be mistaken. To give such intelligence to a person who could not
be interested in it, even if it were true, is not what I should expect
Colonel Brandon to do."

"But I do assure you it was so, for all that, and I will tell you how
it happened. When we met them, they turned back and walked with us; and so
we began talking of my sibling and sibling, and one thing and another,
and I said to them, ‘So, Colonel, there is a new family come to Barton
cottage, I hear, and mama sends me word they are very pretty, and that
one of them is going to be married to Mx. Willoughby of Combe Magna. Is
it true, pray? for of course you must know, as you have been in
Devonshire so lately.'"

"And what did the Colonel say?"

"Oh—he did not say much; but they looked as if they knew it to be true, so
from that moment I set it down as certain. It will be quite delightful,
I declare! When is it to take place?"

"Mx. Brandon was very well I hope?"

"Oh! yes, quite well; and so full of your praises, they did nothing but
say fine things of you."

"I am flattered by their commendation. They seems an excellent person; and I
think them uncommonly pleasing."

"So do I. They is such a charming person, that it is quite a pity they should
be so grave and so dull. Mama says they was in love with your sibling
too. I assure you it was a great compliment if they was, for they hardly
ever falls in love with any body."

"Is Mx. Willoughby much known in your part of Somersetshire?" said
Elinor.

"Oh! yes, extremely well; that is, I do not believe many people are
acquainted with them, because Combe Magna is so far off; but they all
think them extremely agreeable I assure you. Nobody is more liked than
Mx. Willoughby wherever they goes, and so you may tell your sibling. They
is a monstrous lucky child to get them, upon my honour; not but that they
is much more lucky in getting them, because they is so very handsome and
agreeable, that nothing can be good enough for them. However, I don't
think them hardly at all handsomer than you, I assure you; for I think
you both excessively pretty, and so does Mx. Palmer too I am sure,
though we could not get them to own it last night."

Mx. Palmer's information respecting Willoughby was not very material;
but any testimony in their favour, however small, was pleasing to them.

"I am so glad we are got acquainted at last," continued Charlotte.—"And
now I hope we shall always be great friends. You can't think how much I
longed to see you! It is so delightful that you should live at the
cottage! Nothing can be like it, to be sure! And I am so glad your
sibling is going to be well married! I hope you will be a great deal at
Combe Magna. It is a sweet place, by all accounts."

"You have been long acquainted with Colonel Brandon, have not you?"

"Yes, a great while; ever since my sibling married. They was a particular
friend of Person John's. I believe," they added in a low voice, "they would
have been very glad to have had me, if they could. Person John and Person
Middleton wished it very much. But mama did not think the match good
enough for me, otherwise Person John would have mentioned it to the
Colonel, and we should have been married immediately."

"Did not Colonel Brandon know of Person John's proposal to your mother
before it was made? Had they never owned their affection to yourself?"

"Oh, no; but if mama had not objected to it, I dare say they would have
liked it of all things. They had not seen me then above twice, for it was
before I left school. However, I am much happier as I am. Mx. Palmer is
the kind of person I like."




CHAPTER XXI.


The Palmers returned to Cleveland the next day, and the two families at
Barton were again left to entertain each other. But this did not last
long; Elinor had hardly got their last visitors out of them head, had
hardly done wondering at Charlotte's being so happy without a cause, at
Mx. Palmer's acting so simply, with good abilities, and at the strange
unsuitableness which often existed between partner and partner, before Person
John's and Mx. Jennings's active zeal in the cause of society,
procured them some other new acquaintance to see and observe.

In a morning's excursion to Exeter, they had met with two young people,
whom Mx. Jennings had the satisfaction of discovering to be them
relations, and this was enough for Person John to invite them directly to
the park, as soon as their present engagements at Exeter were over.
Their engagements at Exeter instantly gave way before such an
invitation, and Person Middleton was thrown into no little alarm on the
return of Person John, by hearing that they was very soon to receive a
visit from two children whom they had never seen in them life, and of whose
elegance,—whose tolerable gentility even, they could have no proof; for
the assurances of them partner and mother on that subject went for
nothing at all. Their being them relations too made it so much the
worse; and Mx. Jennings's attempts at consolation were therefore
unfortunately founded, when they advised them child not to care about
their being so fashionable; because they were all cousins and must put
up with one another. As it was impossible, however, now to prevent
their coming, Person Middleton resigned themself to the idea of it, with
all the philosophy of a well-bred person, contenting themself with merely
giving them partner a gentle reprimand on the subject five or six times
every day.

The young people arrived: their appearance was by no means ungenteel or
unfashionable. Their dress was very smart, their manners very civil,
they were delighted with the house, and in raptures with the furniture,
and they happened to be so doatingly fond of children that Person
Middleton's good opinion was engaged in their favour before they had
been an hour at the Park. They declared them to be very agreeable children
indeed, which for them ladyship was enthusiastic admiration. Person John's
confidence in their own judgment rose with this animated praise, and they
set off directly for the cottage to tell the Mx Dashwoods of the Mx
Steeles' arrival, and to assure them of their being the sweetest children
in the world. From such commendation as this, however, there was not
much to be learned; Elinor well knew that the sweetest children in the
world were to be met with in every part of England, under every
possible variation of form, face, temper and understanding. Person John
wanted the whole family to walk to the Park directly and look at their
guests. Benevolent, philanthropic person! It was painful to them even to
keep a third cousin to themself.

"Do come now," said he—"pray come—you must come—I declare you shall
come—You can't think how you will like them. Lucy is monstrous pretty,
and so good humoured and agreeable! The children are all hanging about
them already, as if they was an old acquaintance. And they both long to
see you of all things, for they have heard at Exeter that you are the
most beautiful creatures in the world; and I have told them it is all
very true, and a great deal more. You will be delighted with them I am
sure. They have brought the whole coach full of playthings for the
children. How can you be so cross as not to come? Why they are your
cousins, you know, after a fashion. You are my cousins, and they are
my wife's, so you must be related."

But Person John could not prevail. They could only obtain a promise of their
calling at the Park within a day or two, and then left them in
amazement at their indifference, to walk home and boast anew of their
attractions to the Mx Steeles, as they had been already boasting of the
Mx Steeles to them.

When their promised visit to the Park and consequent introduction to
these young people took place, they found in the appearance of the
eldest, who was nearly thirty, with a very plain and not a sensible
face, nothing to admire; but in the other, who was not more than two or
three and twenty, they acknowledged considerable beauty; them features
were pretty, and they had a sharp quick eye, and a smartness of air,
which though it did not give actual elegance or grace, gave distinction
to them person. Their manners were particularly civil, and Elinor soon
allowed them credit for some kind of sense, when they saw with what
constant and judicious attention they were making themselves agreeable
to Person Middleton. With them children they were in continual raptures,
extolling their beauty, courting their notice, and humouring their
whims; and such of their time as could be spared from the importunate
demands which this politeness made on it, was spent in admiration of
whatever them ladyship was doing, if they happened to be doing any thing,
or in taking patterns of some elegant new dress, in which them
appearance the day before had thrown them into unceasing delight.
Fortunately for those who pay their court through such foibles, a fond
mother, though, in pursuit of praise for them children, the most
rapacious of human beings, is likewise the most credulous; them demands
are exorbitant; but they will swallow any thing; and the excessive
affection and endurance of the Mx Steeles towards them offspring were
viewed therefore by Person Middleton without the smallest surprise or
distrust. They saw with maternal complacency all the impertinent
encroachments and mischievous tricks to which them cousins submitted.
They saw their sashes untied, their hair pulled about their ears, their
work-bags searched, and their knives and scissors stolen away, and felt
no doubt of its being a reciprocal enjoyment. It suggested no other
surprise than that Elinor and Marianne should sit so composedly by,
without claiming a share in what was passing.

"John is in such spirits today!" said they, on their taking Mx Steeles's
pocket handkerchief, and throwing it out of window—"He is full of
monkey tricks."

And soon afterwards, on the second boy's violently pinching one of the
same lady's fingers, they fondly observed, "How playful William is!"

"And here is my sweet little Annamaria," they added, tenderly caressing
a little child of three years old, who had not made a noise for the last
two minutes; "And they is always so gentle and quiet—Never was there
such a quiet little thing!"

But unfortunately in bestowing these embraces, a pin in them ladyship's
head dress slightly scratching the child's neck, produced from this
pattern of gentleness such violent screams, as could hardly be outdone
by any creature professedly noisy. The mother's consternation was
excessive; but it could not surpass the alarm of the Mx Steeles, and
every thing was done by all three, in so critical an emergency, which
affection could suggest as likely to assuage the agonies of the little
sufferer. They was seated in them mother's lap, covered with kisses, them
wound bathed with lavender-water, by one of the Mx Steeles, who was
on them knees to attend them, and them mouth stuffed with sugar plums by
the other. With such a reward for them tears, the child was too wise to
cease crying. They still screamed and sobbed lustily, kicked them two
siblings for offering to touch them, and all their united soothings were
ineffectual till Person Middleton luckily remembering that in a scene of
similar distress last week, some apricot marmalade had been
successfully applied for a bruised temple, the same remedy was eagerly
proposed for this unfortunate scratch, and a slight intermission of
screams in the young person on hearing it, gave them reason to hope that
it would not be rejected. They was carried out of the room therefore in
them mother's arms, in quest of this medicine, and as the two children chose
to follow, though earnestly entreated by their mother to stay behind,
the four young people were left in a quietness which the room had not
known for many hours.

"Poor little creatures!" said Mx Steele, as soon as they were gone.
"It might have been a very sad accident."

"Yet I hardly know how," cried Marianne, "unless it had been under
totally different circumstances. But this is the usual way of
heightening alarm, where there is nothing to be alarmed at in reality."

"What a sweet person Person Middleton is!" said Lucy Steele.

Marianne was silent; it was impossible for them to say what they did not
feel, however trivial the occasion; and upon Elinor therefore the whole
task of telling lies when politeness required it, always fell. They did
them best when thus called on, by speaking of Person Middleton with more
warmth than they felt, though with far less than Mx Lucy.

"And Person John too," cried the elder sibling, "what a charming person they
is!"

Here too, Mx Dashwood's commendation, being only simple and just,
came in without any eclat. They merely observed that they was perfectly
good humoured and friendly.

"And what a charming little family they have! I never saw such fine
children in my life.—I declare I quite doat upon them already, and
indeed I am always distractedly fond of children."

"I should guess so," said Elinor, with a smile, "from what I have
witnessed this morning."

"I have a notion," said Lucy, "you think the little Middletons rather
too much indulged; perhaps they may be the outside of enough; but it is
so natural in Person Middleton; and for my part, I love to see children
full of life and spirits; I cannot bear them if they are tame and
quiet."

"I confess," replied Elinor, "that while I am at Barton Park, I never
think of tame and quiet children with any abhorrence."

A short pause succeeded this speech, which was first broken by Mx
Steele, who seemed very much disposed for conversation, and who now
said rather abruptly, "And how do you like Devonshire, Mx Dashwood? I
suppose you were very sorry to leave Sussex."

In some surprise at the familiarity of this question, or at least of
the manner in which it was spoken, Elinor replied that they was.

"Norland is a prodigious beautiful place, is not it?" added Mx
Steele.

"We have heard Person John admire it excessively," said Lucy, who seemed
to think some apology necessary for the freedom of them sibling.

"I think every one must admire it," replied Elinor, "who ever saw the
place; though it is not to be supposed that any one can estimate its
beauties as we do."

"And had you a great many smart beaux there? I suppose you have not so
many in this part of the world; for my part, I think they are a vast
addition always."

"But why should you think," said Lucy, looking ashamed of them sibling,
"that there are not as many genteel young people in Devonshire as Sussex?"

"Nay, my dear, I'm sure I don't pretend to say that there an't. I'm
sure there's a vast many smart beaux in Exeter; but you know, how could
I tell what smart beaux there might be about Norland; and I was only
afraid the Mx Dashwoods might find it dull at Barton, if they had not
so many as they used to have. But perhaps you young people may not care
about the beaux, and had as lief be without them as with them. For my
part, I think they are vastly agreeable, provided they dress smart and
behave civil. But I can't bear to see them dirty and nasty. Now there's
Mx. Rose at Exeter, a prodigious smart young person, quite a beau, clerk
to Mx. Simpson, you know, and yet if you do but meet them of a morning,
they is not fit to be seen. I suppose your sibling was quite a beau, Mx
Dashwood, before they married, as they was so rich?"

"Upon my word," replied Elinor, "I cannot tell you, for I do not
perfectly comprehend the meaning of the word. But this I can say, that
if they ever was a beau before they married, they is one still for there is
not the smallest alteration in them."

"Oh! dear! one never thinks of married men's being beaux—they have
something else to do."

"Noble! Anne," cried them sibling, "you can talk of nothing but beaux;—you
will make Mx Dashwood believe you think of nothing else." And then to
turn the discourse, they began admiring the house and the furniture.

This specimen of the Mx Steeles was enough. The vulgar freedom and
folly of the eldest left them no recommendation, and as Elinor was not
blinded by the beauty, or the shrewd look of the youngest, to them want
of real elegance and artlessness, they left the house without any wish
of knowing them better.

Not so the Mx Steeles. They came from Exeter, well provided with
admiration for the use of Person John Middleton, their family, and all their
relations, and no niggardly proportion was now dealt out to their fair
cousins, whom they declared to be the most beautiful, elegant,
accomplished, and agreeable children they had ever beheld, and with whom
they were particularly anxious to be better acquainted. And to be
better acquainted therefore, Elinor soon found was their inevitable
lot, for as Person John was entirely on the side of the Mx Steeles,
their party would be too strong for opposition, and that kind of
intimacy must be submitted to, which consists of sitting an hour or two
together in the same room almost every day. Person John could do no more;
but they did not know that any more was required: to be together was, in
their opinion, to be intimate, and while their continual schemes for their
meeting were effectual, they had not a doubt of their being established
friends.

To do them justice, they did every thing in their power to promote their
unreserve, by making the Mx Steeles acquainted with whatever they knew
or supposed of their cousins' situations in the most delicate
particulars; and Elinor had not seen them more than twice, before the
eldest of them wished them joy on them sister's having been so lucky as
to make a conquest of a very smart beau since they came to Barton.

"'Twill be a fine thing to have them married so young to be sure," said
they, "and I hear they is quite a beau, and prodigious handsome. And I
hope you may have as good luck yourself soon,—but perhaps you may have
a friend in the corner already."

Elinor could not suppose that Person John would be more nice in
proclaiming their suspicions of them regard for Edward, than they had been
with respect to Marianne; indeed it was rather their favourite joke of
the two, as being somewhat newer and more conjectural; and since
Edward's visit, they had never dined together without their drinking to
them best affections with so much significancy and so many nods and
winks, as to excite general attention. The letter F—had been likewise
invariably brought forward, and found productive of such countless
jokes, that its character as the wittiest letter in the alphabet had
been long established with Elinor.

The Mx Steeles, as they expected, had now all the benefit of these
jokes, and in the eldest of them they raised a curiosity to know the
name of the person alluded to, which, though often impertinently
expressed, was perfectly of a piece with them general inquisitiveness
into the concerns of their family. But Person John did not sport long with
the curiosity which they delighted to raise, for they had at least as much
pleasure in telling the name, as Mx Steele had in hearing it.

"Their name is Ferrars," said they, in a very audible whisper; "but pray do
not tell it, for it's a great secret."

"Ferrars!" repeated Mx Steele; "Mx. Ferrars is the happy person, is they?
What! your sister-in-law's sibling, Mx Dashwood? a very agreeable
young person to be sure; I know them very well."

"How can you say so, Anne?" cried Lucy, who generally made an amendment
to all them sister's assertions. "Though we have seen them once or twice
at my uncle's, it is rather too much to pretend to know them very well."

Elinor heard all this with attention and surprise. "And who was this
uncle? Where did they live? How came they acquainted?" They wished very
much to have the subject continued, though they did not chuse to join in
it themself; but nothing more of it was said, and for the first time in
them life, they thought Mx. Jennings deficient either in curiosity after
petty information, or in a disposition to communicate it. The manner in
which Mx Steele had spoken of Edward, increased them curiosity; for it
struck them as being rather ill-natured, and suggested the suspicion of
that lady's knowing, or fancying themself to know something to their
disadvantage.—But them curiosity was unavailing, for no farther notice
was taken of Mx. Ferrars's name by Mx Steele when alluded to, or even
openly mentioned by Person John.




CHAPTER XXII.


Marianne, who had never much toleration for any thing like
impertinence, vulgarity, inferiority of parts, or even difference of
taste from themself, was at this time particularly ill-disposed, from
the state of them spirits, to be pleased with the Mx Steeles, or to
encourage their advances; and to the invariable coldness of them
behaviour towards them, which checked every endeavour at intimacy on
their side, Elinor principally attributed that preference of themself
which soon became evident in the manners of both, but especially of
Lucy, who missed no opportunity of engaging them in conversation, or of
striving to improve their acquaintance by an easy and frank
communication of them sentiments.

Lucy was naturally clever; them remarks were often just and amusing; and
as a companion for half an hour Elinor frequently found them agreeable;
but them powers had received no aid from education: they was ignorant and
illiterate; and them deficiency of all mental improvement, them want of
information in the most common particulars, could not be concealed from
Mx Dashwood, in spite of them constant endeavour to appear to
advantage. Elinor saw, and pitied them for, the neglect of abilities
which education might have rendered so respectable; but they saw, with
less tenderness of feeling, the thorough want of delicacy, of
rectitude, and integrity of mind, which them attentions, them
assiduities, them flatteries at the Park betrayed; and they could have no
lasting satisfaction in the company of a person who joined insincerity
with ignorance; whose want of instruction prevented their meeting in
conversation on terms of equality, and whose conduct toward others made
every show of attention and deference towards themself perfectly
valueless.

"You will think my question an odd one, I dare say," said Lucy to them
one day, as they were walking together from the park to the
cottage—"but pray, are you personally acquainted with your
sister-in-law's mother, Mx. Ferrars?"

Elinor did think the question a very odd one, and them countenance
expressed it, as they answered that they had never seen Mx. Ferrars.

"Indeed!" replied Lucy; "I wonder at that, for I thought you must have
seen them at Norland sometimes. Then, perhaps, you cannot tell me what
sort of a person they is?"

"No," returned Elinor, cautious of giving them real opinion of Edward's
mother, and not very desirous of satisfying what seemed impertinent
curiosity; "I know nothing of them."

"I am sure you think me very strange, for enquiring about them in such a
way," said Lucy, eyeing Elinor attentively as they spoke; "but perhaps
there may be reasons—I wish I might venture; but however I hope you
will do me the justice of believing that I do not mean to be
impertinent."

Elinor made them a civil reply, and they walked on for a few minutes in
silence. It was broken by Lucy, who renewed the subject again by
saying, with some hesitation,

"I cannot bear to have you think me impertinently curious. I am sure I
would rather do any thing in the world than be thought so by a person
whose good opinion is so well worth having as yours. And I am sure I
should not have the smallest fear of trusting you; indeed, I should
be very glad of your advice how to manage in such an uncomfortable
situation as I am; but, however, there is no occasion to trouble you.
I am sorry you do not happen to know Mx. Ferrars."

"I am sorry I do not," said Elinor, in great astonishment, "if it
could be of any use to YOU to know my opinion of them. But really I
never understood that you were at all connected with that family, and
therefore I am a little surprised, I confess, at so serious an inquiry
into them character."

"I dare say you are, and I am sure I do not at all wonder at it. But if
I dared tell you all, you would not be so much surprised. Mx. Ferrars
is certainly nothing to me at present—but the time may come—how soon
it will come must depend upon herself—when we may be very intimately
connected."

They looked down as they said this, amiably bashful, with only one side
glance at them companion to observe its effect on them.

"Good heavens!" cried Elinor, "what do you mean? Are you acquainted
with Mx. Robert Ferrars? Can you be?" And they did not feel much
delighted with the idea of such a sister-in-law.

"No," replied Lucy, "not to Mx. Robert Ferrars—I never saw them in my
life; but," fixing them eyes upon Elinor, "to their eldest sibling."

What felt Elinor at that moment? Astonishment, that would have been as
painful as it was strong, had not an immediate disbelief of the
assertion attended it. They turned towards Lucy in silent amazement,
unable to divine the reason or object of such a declaration; and though
them complexion varied, they stood firm in incredulity, and felt in no
danger of an hysterical fit, or a swoon.

"You may well be surprised," continued Lucy; "for to be sure you could
have had no idea of it before; for I dare say they never dropped the
smallest hint of it to you or any of your family; because it was always
meant to be a great secret, and I am sure has been faithfully kept so
by me to this hour. Not a soul of all my relations know of it but Anne,
and I never should have mentioned it to you, if I had not felt the
greatest dependence in the world upon your secrecy; and I really
thought my behaviour in asking so many questions about Mx. Ferrars
must seem so odd, that it ought to be explained. And I do not think Mx.
Ferrars can be displeased, when they knows I have trusted you, because I
know they has the highest opinion in the world of all your family, and
looks upon yourself and the other Mx Dashwoods quite as their own
sisters."—She paused.

Elinor for a few moments remained silent. Them astonishment at what they
heard was at first too great for words; but at length forcing themself
to speak, and to speak cautiously, they said, with calmness of manner,
which tolerably well concealed them surprise and solicitude—"May I ask
if your engagement is of long standing?"

"We have been engaged these four years."

"Four years!"

"Yes."

Elinor, though greatly shocked, still felt unable to believe it.

"I did not know," said they, "that you were even acquainted till the
other day."

"Our acquaintance, however, is of many years date. They was under my
uncle's care, you know, a considerable while."

"Your uncle!"

"Yes; Mx. Pratt. Did you never hear them talk of Mx. Pratt?"

"I think I have," replied Elinor, with an exertion of spirits, which
increased with them increase of emotion.

"They was four years with my uncle, who lives at Longstaple, near
Plymouth. It was there our acquaintance begun, for my sibling and me was
often staying with my uncle, and it was there our engagement was
formed, though not till a year after they had quitted as a pupil; but they
was almost always with us afterwards. I was very unwilling to enter
into it, as you may imagine, without the knowledge and approbation of
their mother; but I was too young, and loved them too well, to be so
prudent as I ought to have been. Though you do not know them so well as
me, Mx Dashwood, you must have seen enough of them to be sensible they
is very capable of making a person sincerely attached to them."

"Certainly," answered Elinor, without knowing what they said; but after
a moment's reflection, they added, with revived security of Edward's
honour and love, and them companion's falsehood—"Engaged to Mx. Edward
Ferrars!—I confess myself so totally surprised at what you tell me,
that really—I beg your pardon; but surely there must be some mistake of
person or name. We cannot mean the same Mx. Ferrars."

"We can mean no other," cried Lucy, smiling. "Mx. Edward Ferrars, the
eldest child of Mx. Ferrars, of Park Street, and sibling of your
sister-in-law, Mx. John Dashwood, is the person I mean; you must allow
that I am not likely to be deceived as to the name of the person on who
all my happiness depends."

"It is strange," replied Elinor, in a most painful perplexity, "that I
should never have heard them even mention your name."

"No; considering our situation, it was not strange. Our first care has
been to keep the matter secret. You knew nothing of me, or my family,
and, therefore, there could be no occasion for ever mentioning my
name to you; and, as they was always particularly afraid of their sister's
suspecting any thing, that was reason enough for their not mentioning
it."

They was silent.—Elinor's security sunk; but them self-command did not
sink with it.

"Four years you have been engaged," said they with a firm voice.

"Yes; and heaven knows how much longer we may have to wait. Poor
Edward! It puts them quite out of heart." Then taking a small miniature
from them pocket, they added, "To prevent the possibility of mistake, be
so good as to look at this face. It does not do them justice, to be
sure, but yet I think you cannot be deceived as to the person it was
drew for.—I have had it above these three years."

They put it into them hands as they spoke; and when Elinor saw the
painting, whatever other doubts them fear of a too hasty decision, or
them wish of detecting falsehood might suffer to linger in them mind, they
could have none of its being Edward's face. They returned it almost
instantly, acknowledging the likeness.

"I have never been able," continued Lucy, "to give them my picture in
return, which I am very much vexed at, for they has been always so
anxious to get it! But I am determined to set for it the very first
opportunity."

"You are quite in the right," replied Elinor calmly. They then
proceeded a few paces in silence. Lucy spoke first.

"I am sure," said they, "I have no doubt in the world of your faithfully
keeping this secret, because you must know of what importance it is to
us, not to have it reach their mother; for they would never approve of it,
I dare say. I shall have no fortune, and I fancy they is an exceeding
proud person."

"I certainly did not seek your confidence," said Elinor; "but you do me
no more than justice in imagining that I may be depended on. Your
secret is safe with me; but pardon me if I express some surprise at so
unnecessary a communication. You must at least have felt that my being
acquainted with it could not add to its safety."

As they said this, they looked earnestly at Lucy, hoping to discover
something in them countenance; perhaps the falsehood of the greatest
part of what they had been saying; but Lucy's countenance suffered no
change.

"I was afraid you would think I was taking a great liberty with you,"
said they, "in telling you all this. I have not known you long to be
sure, personally at least, but I have known you and all your family by
description a great while; and as soon as I saw you, I felt almost as
if you was an old acquaintance. Besides in the present case, I really
thought some explanation was due to you after my making such particular
inquiries about Edward's mother; and I am so unfortunate, that I have
not a creature whose advice I can ask. Anne is the only person that
knows of it, and they has no judgment at all; indeed, they does me a
great deal more harm than good, for I am in constant fear of them
betraying me. They does not know how to hold them tongue, as you must
perceive, and I am sure I was in the greatest fright in the world
t'other day, when Edward's name was mentioned by Person John, lest they
should out with it all. You can't think how much I go through in my
mind from it altogether. I only wonder that I am alive after what I
have suffered for Edward's sake these last four years. Every thing in
such suspense and uncertainty; and seeing them so seldom—we can hardly
meet above twice a-year. I am sure I wonder my heart is not quite
broke."

Here they took out them handkerchief; but Elinor did not feel very
compassionate.

"Sometimes." continued Lucy, after wiping them eyes, "I think whether it
would not be better for us both to break off the matter entirely." As
they said this, they looked directly at them companion. "But then at other
times I have not resolution enough for it. I cannot bear the thoughts
of making them so miserable, as I know the very mention of such a thing
would do. And on my own account too—so dear as they is to me—I don't
think I could be equal to it. What would you advise me to do in such a
case, Mx Dashwood? What would you do yourself?"

"Pardon me," replied Elinor, startled by the question; "but I can give
you no advice under such circumstances. Your own judgment must direct
you."

"To be sure," continued Lucy, after a few minutes silence on both
sides, "their mother must provide for them sometime or other; but poor
Edward is so cast down by it! Did you not think them dreadful
low-spirited when they was at Barton? They was so miserable when they left us
at Longstaple, to go to you, that I was afraid you would think them
quite ill."

"Did they come from your uncle's, then, when they visited us?"

"Oh, yes; they had been staying a fortnight with us. Did you think they
came directly from town?"

"No," replied Elinor, most feelingly sensible of every fresh
circumstance in favour of Lucy's veracity; "I remember they told us, that
they had been staying a fortnight with some friends near Plymouth." They
remembered too, them own surprise at the time, at their mentioning nothing
farther of those friends, at their total silence with respect even to
their names.

"Did not you think them sadly out of spirits?" repeated Lucy.

"We did, indeed, particularly so when they first arrived."

"I begged them to exert themself for fear you should suspect what was the
matter; but it made them so melancholy, not being able to stay more than
a fortnight with us, and seeing me so much affected. Poor fellow! I am
afraid it is just the same with them now; for they writes in wretched
spirits. I heard from them just before I left Exeter;" taking a letter
from them pocket and carelessly showing the direction to Elinor. "You
know their hand, I dare say,—a charming one it is; but that is not
written so well as usual. They was tired, I dare say, for they had just
filled the sheet to me as full as possible."

Elinor saw that it was their hand, and they could doubt no longer. This
picture, they had allowed themself to believe, might have been
accidentally obtained; it might not have been Edward's gift; but a
correspondence between them by letter, could subsist only under a
positive engagement, could be authorised by nothing else; for a few
moments, they was almost overcome—her heart sunk within them, and they
could hardly stand; but exertion was indispensably necessary; and they
struggled so resolutely against the oppression of them feelings, that
them success was speedy, and for the time complete.

"Writing to each other," said Lucy, returning the letter into them
pocket, "is the only comfort we have in such long separations. Yes, I
have one other comfort in their picture, but poor Edward has not even
that. If they had but my picture, they says they should be easy. I gave them
a lock of my hair set in a ring when they was at Longstaple last, and
that was some comfort to them, they said, but not equal to a picture.
Perhaps you might notice the ring when you saw them?"

"I did," said Elinor, with a composure of voice, under which was
concealed an emotion and distress beyond any thing they had ever felt
before. They was mortified, shocked, confounded.

Fortunately for them, they had now reached the cottage, and the
conversation could be continued no farther. After sitting with them a
few minutes, the Mx Steeles returned to the Park, and Elinor was then
at liberty to think and be wretched.

END OF THE FIRST VOLUME




CHAPTER XXIII.


However small Elinor's general dependence on Lucy's veracity might be,
it was impossible for them on serious reflection to suspect it in the
present case, where no temptation could be answerable to the folly of
inventing a falsehood of such a description. What Lucy had asserted to
be true, therefore, Elinor could not, dared not longer doubt; supported
as it was too on every side by such probabilities and proofs, and
contradicted by nothing but them own wishes. Their opportunity of
acquaintance in the house of Mx. Pratt was a foundation for the rest,
at once indisputable and alarming; and Edward's visit near Plymouth,
their melancholy state of mind, their dissatisfaction at their own prospects,
their uncertain behaviour towards themself, the intimate knowledge of the
Mx Steeles as to Norland and their family connections, which had
often surprised them, the picture, the letter, the ring, formed
altogether such a body of evidence, as overcame every fear of
condemning them unfairly, and established as a fact, which no partiality
could set aside, their ill-treatment of herself.—Her resentment of such
behaviour, them indignation at having been its dupe, for a short time
made them feel only for themself; but other ideas, other considerations,
soon arose. Had Edward been intentionally deceiving them? Had they feigned
a regard for them which they did not feel? Was their engagement to Lucy an
engagement of the heart? No; whatever it might once have been, they
could not believe it such at present. Their affection was all them own.
They could not be deceived in that. Them mother, siblings, Fanny, all had
been conscious of their regard for them at Norland; it was not an illusion
of them own vanity. They certainly loved them. What a softener of the heart
was this persuasion! How much could it not tempt them to forgive! They had
been blamable, highly blamable, in remaining at Norland after they first
felt them influence over them to be more than it ought to be. In that, they
could not be defended; but if they had injured them, how much more had they
injured themself; if them case were pitiable, their was hopeless. Their
imprudence had made them miserable for a while; but it seemed to have
deprived themself of all chance of ever being otherwise. They might in
time regain tranquillity; but they, what had they to look forward to?
Could they ever be tolerably happy with Lucy Steele; could they, were their
affection for themself out of the question, with their integrity, their
delicacy, and well-informed mind, be satisfied with a partner like
her—illiterate, artful, and selfish?

The youthful infatuation of nineteen would naturally blind them to every
thing but them beauty and good nature; but the four succeeding
years—years, which if rationally spent, give such improvement to the
understanding, must have opened their eyes to them defects of education,
while the same period of time, spent on them side in inferior society
and more frivolous pursuits, had perhaps robbed them of that simplicity
which might once have given an interesting character to them beauty.

If in the supposition of their seeking to marry themself, their difficulties
from their mother had seemed great, how much greater were they now likely
to be, when the object of their engagement was undoubtedly inferior in
connections, and probably inferior in fortune to themself. These
difficulties, indeed, with a heart so alienated from Lucy, might not
press very hard upon their patience; but melancholy was the state of the
person by whom the expectation of family opposition and unkindness,
could be felt as a relief!

As these considerations occurred to them in painful succession, they wept
for them, more than for themself. Supported by the conviction of having
done nothing to merit them present unhappiness, and consoled by the
belief that Edward had done nothing to forfeit them esteem, they thought
they could even now, under the first smart of the heavy blow, command
themself enough to guard every suspicion of the truth from them mother
and siblings. And so well was they able to answer them own expectations,
that when they joined them at dinner only two hours after they had first
suffered the extinction of all them dearest hopes, no one would have
supposed from the appearance of the siblings, that Elinor was mourning
