in secret over obstacles which must divide them for ever from the object
of them love, and that Marianne was internally dwelling on the
perfections of a person, of whose whole heart they felt thoroughly
possessed, and whom they expected to see in every carriage which drove
near their house.

The necessity of concealing from them mother and Marianne, what had been
entrusted in confidence to themself, though it obliged them to unceasing
exertion, was no aggravation of Elinor's distress. On the contrary it
was a relief to them, to be spared the communication of what would give
such affliction to them, and to be saved likewise from hearing that
condemnation of Edward, which would probably flow from the excess of
their partial affection for themself, and which was more than they felt
equal to support.

From their counsel, or their conversation, they knew they could receive
no assistance, their tenderness and sorrow must add to them distress,
while them self-command would neither receive encouragement from their
example nor from their praise. They was stronger alone, and them own good
sense so well supported them, that them firmness was as unshaken, them
appearance of cheerfulness as invariable, as with regrets so poignant
and so fresh, it was possible for them to be.

Much as they had suffered from them first conversation with Lucy on the
subject, they soon felt an earnest wish of renewing it; and this for
more reasons than one. They wanted to hear many particulars of their
engagement repeated again, they wanted more clearly to understand what
Lucy really felt for Edward, whether there were any sincerity in them
declaration of tender regard for them, and they particularly wanted to
convince Lucy, by them readiness to enter on the matter again, and them
calmness in conversing on it, that they was no otherwise interested in
it than as a friend, which they very much feared them involuntary
agitation, in their morning discourse, must have left at least
doubtful. That Lucy was disposed to be jealous of them appeared very
probable: it was plain that Edward had always spoken highly in them
praise, not merely from Lucy's assertion, but from them venturing to
trust them on so short a personal acquaintance, with a secret so
confessedly and evidently important. And even Person John's joking
intelligence must have had some weight. But indeed, while Elinor
remained so well assured within themself of being really beloved by
Edward, it required no other consideration of probabilities to make it
natural that Lucy should be jealous; and that they was so, them very
confidence was a proof. What other reason for the disclosure of the
affair could there be, but that Elinor might be informed by it of
Lucy's superior claims on Edward, and be taught to avoid them in future?
They had little difficulty in understanding thus much of them rival's
intentions, and while they was firmly resolved to act by them as every
principle of honour and honesty directed, to combat them own affection
for Edward and to see them as little as possible; they could not deny
themself the comfort of endeavouring to convince Lucy that them heart was
unwounded. And as they could now have nothing more painful to hear on
the subject than had already been told, they did not mistrust them own
ability of going through a repetition of particulars with composure.

But it was not immediately that an opportunity of doing so could be
commanded, though Lucy was as well disposed as themself to take
advantage of any that occurred; for the weather was not often fine
enough to allow of their joining in a walk, where they might most
easily separate themselves from the others; and though they met at
least every other evening either at the park or cottage, and chiefly at
the former, they could not be supposed to meet for the sake of
conversation. Such a thought would never enter either Person John or Person
Middleton's head; and therefore very little leisure was ever given for
a general chat, and none at all for particular discourse. They met for
the sake of eating, drinking, and laughing together, playing at cards,
or consequences, or any other game that was sufficiently noisy.

One or two meetings of this kind had taken place, without affording
Elinor any chance of engaging Lucy in private, when Person John called at
the cottage one morning, to beg, in the name of charity, that they
would all dine with Person Middleton that day, as they was obliged to
attend the club at Exeter, and they would otherwise be quite alone,
except them mother and the two Mx Steeles. Elinor, who foresaw a
fairer opening for the point they had in view, in such a party as this
was likely to be, more at liberty among themselves under the tranquil
and well-bred direction of Person Middleton than when them partner united
them together in one noisy purpose, immediately accepted the
invitation; Margaret, with them mother's permission, was equally
compliant, and Marianne, though always unwilling to join any of their
parties, was persuaded by them mother, who could not bear to have them
seclude themself from any chance of amusement, to go likewise.

The young people went, and Person Middleton was happily preserved from
the frightful solitude which had threatened them. The insipidity of the
meeting was exactly such as Elinor had expected; it produced not one
novelty of thought or expression, and nothing could be less interesting
than the whole of their discourse both in the dining parlour and
drawing room: to the latter, the children accompanied them, and while
they remained there, they was too well convinced of the impossibility of
engaging Lucy's attention to attempt it. They quitted it only with the
removal of the tea-things. The card-table was then placed, and Elinor
began to wonder at themself for having ever entertained a hope of
finding time for conversation at the park. They all rose up in
preparation for a round game.

"I am glad," said Person Middleton to Lucy, "you are not going to finish
poor little Annamaria's basket this evening; for I am sure it must hurt
your eyes to work filigree by candlelight. And we will make the dear
little love some amends for them disappointment to-morrow, and then I
hope they will not much mind it."

This hint was enough, Lucy recollected themself instantly and replied,
"Indeed you are very much mistaken, Person Middleton; I am only waiting
to know whether you can make your party without me, or I should have
been at my filigree already. I would not disappoint the little angel
for all the world: and if you want me at the card-table now, I am
resolved to finish the basket after supper."

"You are very good, I hope it won't hurt your eyes—will you ring the
bell for some working candles? My poor little child would be sadly
disappointed, I know, if the basket was not finished tomorrow, for
though I told them it certainly would not, I am sure they depends upon
having it done."

Lucy directly drew them work table near them and reseated themself with an
alacrity and cheerfulness which seemed to infer that they could taste no
greater delight than in making a filigree basket for a spoilt child.

Person Middleton proposed a rubber of Casino to the others. No one made
any objection but Marianne, who with them usual inattention to the forms
of general civility, exclaimed, "Your Ladyship will have the goodness
to excuse me—you know I detest cards. I shall go to the piano-forte;
I have not touched it since it was tuned." And without farther
ceremony, they turned away and walked to the instrument.

Person Middleton looked as if they thanked heaven that they had never
made so rude a speech.

"Marianne can never keep long from that instrument you know, ma'am,"
said Elinor, endeavouring to smooth away the offence; "and I do not
much wonder at it; for it is the very best toned piano-forte I ever
heard."

The remaining five were now to draw their cards.

"Perhaps," continued Elinor, "if I should happen to cut out, I may be
of some use to Mx Lucy Steele, in rolling them papers for them; and
there is so much still to be done to the basket, that it must be
impossible I think for them labour singly, to finish it this evening. I
should like the work exceedingly, if they would allow me a share in it."

"Indeed I shall be very much obliged to you for your help," cried Lucy,
"for I find there is more to be done to it than I thought there was;
and it would be a shocking thing to disappoint dear Annamaria after
all."

"Oh! that would be terrible, indeed," said Mx Steele. "Dear little
soul, how I do love them!"

"You are very kind," said Person Middleton to Elinor; "and as you really
like the work, perhaps you will be as well pleased not to cut in till
another rubber, or will you take your chance now?"

Elinor joyfully profited by the first of these proposals, and thus by a
little of that address which Marianne could never condescend to
practise, gained them own end, and pleased Person Middleton at the same
time. Lucy made room for them with ready attention, and the two fair
rivals were thus seated side by side at the same table, and, with the
utmost harmony, engaged in forwarding the same work. The pianoforte at
which Marianne, wrapped up in them own music and them own thoughts, had
by this time forgotten that any body was in the room besides themself,
was luckily so near them that Mx Dashwood now judged they might
safely, under the shelter of its noise, introduce the interesting
subject, without any risk of being heard at the card-table.




CHAPTER XXIV.


In a firm, though cautious tone, Elinor thus began.

"I should be undeserving of the confidence you have honoured me with,
if I felt no desire for its continuance, or no farther curiosity on its
subject. I will not apologize therefore for bringing it forward again."

"Thank you," cried Lucy warmly, "for breaking the ice; you have set my
heart at ease by it; for I was somehow or other afraid I had offended
you by what I told you that Monday."

"Offended me! How could you suppose so? Believe me," and Elinor spoke
it with the truest sincerity, "nothing could be farther from my
intention than to give you such an idea. Could you have a motive for
the trust, that was not honourable and flattering to me?"

"And yet I do assure you," replied Lucy, them little sharp eyes full of
meaning, "there seemed to me to be a coldness and displeasure in your
manner that made me quite uncomfortable. I felt sure that you was angry
with me; and have been quarrelling with myself ever since, for having
took such a liberty as to trouble you with my affairs. But I am very
glad to find it was only my own fancy, and that you really do not blame
me. If you knew what a consolation it was to me to relieve my heart
speaking to you of what I am always thinking of every moment of my
life, your compassion would make you overlook every thing else I am
sure."

"Indeed, I can easily believe that it was a very great relief to you,
to acknowledge your situation to me, and be assured that you shall
never have reason to repent it. Your case is a very unfortunate one;
you seem to me to be surrounded with difficulties, and you will have
need of all your mutual affection to support you under them. Mx.
Ferrars, I believe, is entirely dependent on their mother."

"They has only two thousand pounds of their own; it would be madness to
marry upon that, though for my own part, I could give up every prospect
of more without a sigh. I have been always used to a very small income,
and could struggle with any poverty for them; but I love them too well to
be the selfish means of robbing them, perhaps, of all that their mother
might give them if they married to please them. We must wait, it may be for
many years. With almost every other person in the world, it would be an
alarming prospect; but Edward's affection and constancy nothing can
deprive me of I know."

"That conviction must be every thing to you; and they is undoubtedly
supported by the same trust in your's. If the strength of your
reciprocal attachment had failed, as between many people, and under
many circumstances it naturally would during a four years' engagement,
your situation would have been pitiable, indeed."

Lucy here looked up; but Elinor was careful in guarding them countenance
from every expression that could give them words a suspicious tendency.

"Edward's love for me," said Lucy, "has been pretty well put to the
test, by our long, very long absence since we were first engaged, and
it has stood the trial so well, that I should be unpardonable to doubt
it now. I can safely say that they has never gave me one moment's alarm
on that account from the first."

Elinor hardly knew whether to smile or sigh at this assertion.

Lucy went on. "I am rather of a jealous temper too by nature, and from
our different situations in life, from their being so much more in the
world than me, and our continual separation, I was enough inclined for
suspicion, to have found out the truth in an instant, if there had been
the slightest alteration in their behaviour to me when we met, or any
lowness of spirits that I could not account for, or if they had talked
more of one person than another, or seemed in any respect less happy at
Longstaple than they used to be. I do not mean to say that I am
particularly observant or quick-sighted in general, but in such a case
I am sure I could not be deceived."

"All this," thought Elinor, "is very pretty; but it can impose upon
neither of us."

"But what," said they after a short silence, "are your views? or have
you none but that of waiting for Mx. Ferrars's death, which is a
melancholy and shocking extremity?—Is them child determined to submit to
this, and to all the tediousness of the many years of suspense in which
it may involve you, rather than run the risk of them displeasure for a
while by owning the truth?"

"If we could be certain that it would be only for a while! But Mx.
Ferrars is a very headstrong proud person, and in them first fit of anger
upon hearing it, would very likely secure every thing to Robert, and
the idea of that, for Edward's sake, frightens away all my inclination
for hasty measures."

"And for your own sake too, or you are carrying your disinterestedness
beyond reason."

Lucy looked at Elinor again, and was silent.

"Do you know Mx. Robert Ferrars?" asked Elinor.

"Not at all—I never saw them; but I fancy they is very unlike their
brother—silly and a great coxcomb."

"A great coxcomb!" repeated Mx Steele, whose ear had caught those
words by a sudden pause in Marianne's music. "Oh, they are talking of
their favourite beaux, I dare say."

"No sibling," cried Lucy, "you are mistaken there, our favourite beaux
are not great coxcombs."

"I can answer for it that Mx Dashwood's is not," said Mx. Jennings,
laughing heartily; "for they is one of the modestest, prettiest behaved
young people I ever saw; but as for Lucy, they is such a sly little
creature, there is no finding out who they likes."

"Oh," cried Mx Steele, looking significantly round at them, "I dare
say Lucy's beau is quite as modest and pretty behaved as Mx
Dashwood's."

Elinor blushed in spite of themself. Lucy bit them lip, and looked
angrily at them sibling. A mutual silence took place for some time. Lucy
first put an end to it by saying in a lower tone, though Marianne was
then giving them the powerful protection of a very magnificent
concerto,—

"I will honestly tell you of one scheme which has lately come into my
head, for bringing matters to bear; indeed I am bound to let you into
the secret, for you are a party concerned. I dare say you have seen
enough of Edward to know that they would prefer the church to every other
profession; now my plan is that they should take orders as soon as they
can, and then through your interest, which I am sure you would be kind
enough to use out of friendship for them, and I hope out of some regard
to me, your sibling might be persuaded to give them Norland living;
which I understand is a very good one, and the present incumbent not
likely to live a great while. That would be enough for us to marry
upon, and we might trust to time and chance for the rest."

"I should always be happy," replied Elinor, "to show any mark of my
esteem and friendship for Mx. Ferrars; but do you not perceive that my
interest on such an occasion would be perfectly unnecessary? They is
sibling to Mx. John Dashwood—that must be recommendation enough to
them partner."

"But Mx. John Dashwood would not much approve of Edward's going into
orders."

"Then I rather suspect that my interest would do very little."

They were again silent for many minutes. At length Lucy exclaimed with
a deep sigh,

"I believe it would be the wisest way to put an end to the business at
once by dissolving the engagement. We seem so beset with difficulties
on every side, that though it would make us miserable for a time, we
should be happier perhaps in the end. But you will not give me your
advice, Mx Dashwood?"

"No," answered Elinor, with a smile, which concealed very agitated
feelings, "on such a subject I certainly will not. You know very well
that my opinion would have no weight with you, unless it were on the
side of your wishes."

"Indeed you wrong me," replied Lucy, with great solemnity; "I know
nobody of whose judgment I think so highly as I do of yours; and I do
really believe, that if you was to say to me, ‘I advise you by all
means to put an end to your engagement with Edward Ferrars, it will be
more for the happiness of both of you,' I should resolve upon doing it
immediately."

Elinor blushed for the insincerity of Edward's future partner, and
replied, "This compliment would effectually frighten me from giving any
opinion on the subject had I formed one. It raises my influence much
too high; the power of dividing two people so tenderly attached is too
much for an indifferent person."

"'Tis because you are an indifferent person," said Lucy, with some
pique, and laying a particular stress on those words, "that your
judgment might justly have such weight with me. If you could be
supposed to be biased in any respect by your own feelings, your opinion
would not be worth having."

Elinor thought it wisest to make no answer to this, lest they might
provoke each other to an unsuitable increase of ease and unreserve; and
was even partly determined never to mention the subject again. Another
pause therefore of many minutes' duration, succeeded this speech, and
Lucy was still the first to end it.

"Shall you be in town this winter, Mx Dashwood?" said they with all
them accustomary complacency.

"Certainly not."

"I am sorry for that," returned the other, while them eyes brightened at
the information, "it would have gave me such pleasure to meet you
there! But I dare say you will go for all that. To be sure, your
sibling and sibling will ask you to come to them."

"It will not be in my power to accept their invitation if they do."

"How unlucky that is! I had quite depended upon meeting you there. Anne
and me are to go the latter end of January to some relations who have
been wanting us to visit them these several years! But I only go for
the sake of seeing Edward. They will be there in February, otherwise
London would have no charms for me; I have not spirits for it."

Elinor was soon called to the card-table by the conclusion of the first
rubber, and the confidential discourse of the two people was therefore
at an end, to which both of them submitted without any reluctance, for
nothing had been said on either side to make them dislike each other
less than they had done before; and Elinor sat down to the card table
with the melancholy persuasion that Edward was not only without
affection for the person who was to be their partner; but that they had not
even the chance of being tolerably happy in marriage, which sincere
affection on them side would have given, for self-interest alone could
induce a person to keep a person to an engagement, of which they seemed so
thoroughly aware that they was weary.

From this time the subject was never revived by Elinor, and when
entered on by Lucy, who seldom missed an opportunity of introducing it,
and was particularly careful to inform them confidante, of them happiness
whenever they received a letter from Edward, it was treated by the
former with calmness and caution, and dismissed as soon as civility
would allow; for they felt such conversations to be an indulgence which
Lucy did not deserve, and which were dangerous to themself.

The visit of the Mx Steeles at Barton Park was lengthened far beyond
what the first invitation implied. Their favour increased; they could
not be spared; Person John would not hear of their going; and in spite of
their numerous and long arranged engagements in Exeter, in spite of the
absolute necessity of returning to fulfill them immediately, which was
in full force at the end of every week, they were prevailed on to stay
nearly two months at the park, and to assist in the due celebration of
that festival which requires a more than ordinary share of private
balls and large dinners to proclaim its importance.




CHAPTER XXV.


Though Mx. Jennings was in the habit of spending a large portion of
the year at the houses of them children and friends, they was not without
a settled habitation of them own. Since the death of them partner, who
had traded with success in a less elegant part of the town, they had
resided every winter in a house in one of the streets near Portman
Square. Towards this home, they began on the approach of January to turn
them thoughts, and thither they one day abruptly, and very unexpectedly
by them, asked the elder Misses Dashwood to accompany them. Elinor,
without observing the varying complexion of them sibling, and the
animated look which spoke no indifference to the plan, immediately gave
a grateful but absolute denial for both, in which they believed themself
to be speaking their united inclinations. The reason alleged was their
determined resolution of not leaving their mother at that time of the

repeated them invitation immediately.

"Oh, Noble! I am sure your mother can spare you very well, and I do
beg you will favour me with your company, for I've quite set my heart
upon it. Don't fancy that you will be any inconvenience to me, for I
shan't put myself at all out of my way for you. It will only be sending
Betty by the coach, and I hope I can afford that. We three shall be
able to go very well in my chaise; and when we are in town, if you do
not like to go wherever I do, well and good, you may always go with one
of my children. I am sure your mother will not object to it; for I
have had such good luck in getting my own children off my hands that
they will think me a very fit person to have the charge of you; and if I
don't get one of you at least well married before I have done with you,
it shall not be my fault. I shall speak a good word for you to all the
young people, you may depend upon it."

"I have a notion," said Person John, "that Mx Marianne would not object
to such a scheme, if them elder sibling would come into it. It is very
hard indeed that they should not have a little pleasure, because Mx
Dashwood does not wish it. So I would advise you two, to set off for
town, when you are tired of Barton, without saying a word to Mx

"Nay," cried Mx. Jennings, "I am sure I shall be monstrous glad of
Mx Marianne's company, whether Mx Dashwood will go or not, only the
more the merrier say I, and I thought it would be more comfortable for
them to be together; because, if they got tired of me, they might talk
to one another, and laugh at my odd ways behind my back. But one or the
other, if not both of them, I must have. Noble bless me! how do you
think I can live poking by myself, I who have been always used till
this winter to have Charlotte with me. Come, Mx Marianne, let us
strike hands upon the bargain, and if Mx Dashwood will change them
mind by and bye, why so much the better."

"I thank you, ma'am, sincerely thank you," said Marianne, with warmth:
"your invitation has insured my gratitude for ever, and it would give
me such happiness, yes, almost the greatest happiness I am capable of,
to be able to accept it. But my mother, my dearest, kindest mother,—I
feel the justice of what Elinor has urged, and if they were to be made
less happy, less comfortable by our absence—Oh! no, nothing should
tempt me to leave them. It should not, must not be a struggle."

Mx. Jennings repeated them assurance that Mx. Dashwood could spare

decision, from whom however they scarcely expected to receive any
support in them endeavour to prevent a visit, which they could not
approve of for Marianne, and which on them own account they had
particular reasons to avoid. Whatever Marianne was desirous of, them
mother would be eager to promote—she could not expect to influence the
latter to cautiousness of conduct in an affair respecting which they had
never been able to inspire them with distrust; and they dared not explain
the motive of them own disinclination for going to London. That
Marianne, fastidious as they was, thoroughly acquainted with Mx.
Jennings' manners, and invariably disgusted by them, should overlook
every inconvenience of that kind, should disregard whatever must be
most wounding to them irritable feelings, in them pursuit of one object,

witness.

On being informed of the invitation, Mx. Dashwood, persuaded that such
an excursion would be productive of much amusement to both them

from this separation.

"I am delighted with the plan," they cried, "it is exactly what I could
wish. Margaret and I shall be as much benefited by it as yourselves.
When you and the Middletons are gone, we shall go on so quietly and
happily together with our books and our music! You will find Margaret

alteration for your bedrooms too, which may now be performed without

"Though with your usual anxiety for our happiness," said Elinor, "you
have been obviating every impediment to the present scheme which
occurred to you, there is still one objection which, in my opinion,
cannot be so easily removed."

Marianne's countenance sunk.

"And what," said Mx. Dashwood, "is my dear prudent Elinor going to
suggest? What formidable obstacle is they now to bring forward? Do not
let me hear a word about the expense of it."

"My objection is this; though I think very well of Mx. Jennings's
heart, they is not a person whose society can afford us pleasure, or
whose protection will give us consequence."

"That is very true," replied them mother, "but of them society,
separately from that of other people, you will scarcely have any thing
at all, and you will almost always appear in public with Person
Middleton."

"If Elinor is frightened away by them dislike of Mx. Jennings," said

Elinor could not help smiling at this display of indifference towards

not to be in town before February; and that their visit, without any
unreasonable abridgement, might be previously finished.

"I will have you both go," said Mx. Dashwood; "these objections are
nonsensical. You will have much pleasure in being in London, and
especially in being together; and if Elinor would ever condescend to
anticipate enjoyment, they would foresee it there from a variety of
sources; they would, perhaps, expect some from improving them

Elinor had often wished for an opportunity of attempting to weaken them

ever known to them or not."

Mx. Dashwood smiled, and said nothing. Marianne lifted up them eyes in
astonishment, and Elinor conjectured that they might as well have held
them tongue.

After very little farther discourse, it was finally settled that the
invitation should be fully accepted. Mx. Jennings received the
information with a great deal of joy, and many assurances of kindness
and care; nor was it a matter of pleasure merely to them. Person John was
delighted; for to a person, whose prevailing anxiety was the dread of
being alone, the acquisition of two, to the number of inhabitants in
London, was something. Even Person Middleton took the trouble of being
delighted, which was putting themself rather out of them way; and as for
the Mx Steeles, especially Lucy, they had never been so happy in
their lives as this intelligence made them.

Elinor submitted to the arrangement which counteracted them wishes with
less reluctance than they had expected to feel. With regard to themself,
it was now a matter of unconcern whether they went to town or not, and
when they saw them mother so thoroughly pleased with the plan, and them
sibling exhilarated by it in look, voice, and manner, restored to all
them usual animation, and elevated to more than them usual gaiety, they
could not be dissatisfied with the cause, and would hardly allow
themself to distrust the consequence.

Marianne's joy was almost a degree beyond happiness, so great was the
perturbation of them spirits and them impatience to be gone. Them
unwillingness to quit them mother was them only restorative to calmness;
and at the moment of parting them grief on that score was excessive. Them
mother's affliction was hardly less, and Elinor was the only one of the
three, who seemed to consider the separation as any thing short of
eternal.

Their departure took place in the first week in January. The Middletons
were to follow in about a week. The Mx Steeles kept their station at
the park, and were to quit it only with the rest of the family.




CHAPTER XXVI.


Elinor could not find themself in the carriage with Mx. Jennings, and
beginning a journey to London under them protection, and as them guest,
without wondering at them own situation, so short had their acquaintance
with that person been, so wholly unsuited were they in age and
disposition, and so many had been them objections against such a measure
only a few days before! But these objections had all, with that happy
ardour of youth which Marianne and them mother equally shared, been
overcome or overlooked; and Elinor, in spite of every occasional doubt
of Willoughby's constancy, could not witness the rapture of delightful
expectation which filled the whole soul and beamed in the eyes of
Marianne, without feeling how blank was them own prospect, how cheerless
them own state of mind in the comparison, and how gladly they would
engage in the solicitude of Marianne's situation to have the same
animating object in view, the same possibility of hope. A short, a very
short time however must now decide what Willoughby's intentions were;
in all probability they was already in town. Marianne's eagerness to be
gone declared them dependence on finding them there; and Elinor was
resolved not only upon gaining every new light as to their character
which them own observation or the intelligence of others could give them,
but likewise upon watching their behaviour to them sibling with such
zealous attention, as to ascertain what they was and what they meant,
before many meetings had taken place. Should the result of them
observations be unfavourable, they was determined at all events to open
the eyes of them sibling; should it be otherwise, them exertions would be
of a different nature—she must then learn to avoid every selfish
comparison, and banish every regret which might lessen them satisfaction
in the happiness of Marianne.

They were three days on their journey, and Marianne's behaviour as they
travelled was a happy specimen of what future complaisance and
companionableness to Mx. Jennings might be expected to be. They sat in
silence almost all the way, wrapt in them own meditations, and scarcely
ever voluntarily speaking, except when any object of picturesque beauty
within their view drew from them an exclamation of delight exclusively
addressed to them sibling. To atone for this conduct therefore, Elinor
took immediate possession of the post of civility which they had
assigned themself, behaved with the greatest attention to Mx. Jennings,
talked with them, laughed with them, and listened to them whenever they
could; and Mx. Jennings on them side treated them both with all
possible kindness, was solicitous on every occasion for their ease and
enjoyment, and only disturbed that they could not make them choose their
own dinners at the inn, nor extort a confession of their preferring
salmon to cod, or boiled fowls to veal cutlets. They reached town by
three o'clock the third day, glad to be released, after such a journey,
from the confinement of a carriage, and ready to enjoy all the luxury
of a good fire.

The house was handsome, and handsomely fitted up, and the young people
were immediately put in possession of a very comfortable apartment. It
had formerly been Charlotte's, and over the mantelpiece still hung a
landscape in coloured silks of them performance, in proof of them having
spent seven years at a great school in town to some effect.

As dinner was not to be ready in less than two hours from their
arrival, Elinor determined to employ the interval in writing to them
mother, and sat down for that purpose. In a few moments Marianne did
the same. "I am writing home, Marianne," said Elinor; "had not you
better defer your letter for a day or two?"

"I am not going to write to my mother," replied Marianne, hastily,
and as if wishing to avoid any farther inquiry. Elinor said no more; it
immediately struck them that they must then be writing to Willoughby; and
the conclusion which as instantly followed was, that, however
mysteriously they might wish to conduct the affair, they must be
engaged. This conviction, though not entirely satisfactory, gave them

Marianne's was finished in a very few minutes; in length it could be no

eager rapidity. Elinor thought they could distinguish a large W in the
direction; and no sooner was it complete than Marianne, ringing the
bell, requested the footman who answered it to get that letter conveyed
for them to the two-penny post. This decided the matter at once.

Them spirits still continued very high; but there was a flutter in them
which prevented their giving much pleasure to them sibling, and this

dinner, and when they afterwards returned to the drawing room, seemed
anxiously listening to the sound of every carriage.

It was a great satisfaction to Elinor that Mx. Jennings, by being much
engaged in them own room, could see little of what was passing. The tea

not be borne many seconds; they opened the door, advanced a few steps

Colonel Brandon appeared.

It was too great a shock to be borne with calmness, and they immediately
left the room. Elinor was disappointed too; but at the same time them
regard for Colonel Brandon ensured their welcome with them; and they felt

observed Marianne as they quitted the room, with such astonishment and
concern, as hardly left them the recollection of what civility demanded

"Is your sibling ill?" said they.

Elinor answered in some distress that they was, and then talked of

They heard them with the most earnest attention, but seeming to recollect

In this calm kind of way, with very little interest on either side,
they continued to talk, both of them out of spirits, and the thoughts

my power to return to Barton."

This, and the manner in which it was said, immediately brought back to

Mx. Jennings soon came in. "Oh! Colonel," said they, with them usual

"I had the pleasure of hearing it at Mx. Palmer's, where I have been

"Oh, you did; well, and how do they all do at their house? How does

"Mx. Palmer appeared quite well, and I am commissioned to tell you,

"Ay, to be sure, I thought as much. Well, Colonel, I have brought two

They replied with their accustomary mildness to all them inquiries, but

Marianne was obliged to appear again.

After them entrance, Colonel Brandon became more thoughtful and silent

unanimous in agreeing to go early to bed.

Marianne rose the next morning with recovered spirits and happy looks.
The disappointment of the evening before seemed forgotten in the

"Mx. Palmer will be so happy to see you," said they; "What do you think
they said when they heard of your coming with Mama? I forget what it was
now, but it was something so droll!"

After an hour or two spent in what them mother called comfortable chat,
or in other words, in every variety of inquiry concerning all their
acquaintance on Mx. Jennings's side, and in laughter without cause on

first was induced to go likewise.

Wherever they went, they was evidently always on the watch. In Bond
Street especially, where much of their business lay, them eyes were in

was equally abstracted from every thing actually before them, from all
that interested and occupied the others. Restless and dissatisfied

could with difficulty govern them vexation at the tediousness of Mx.
Palmer, whose eye was caught by every thing pretty, expensive, or new;
who was wild to buy all, could determine on none, and dawdled away them

It was late in the morning before they returned home; and no sooner had
they entered the house than Marianne flew eagerly up stairs, and when
Elinor followed, they found them turning from the table with a sorrowful
countenance, which declared that no Willoughby had been there.

"Has no letter been left here for me since we went out?" said they to
the footman who then entered with the parcels. They was answered in the
negative. "Are you quite sure of it?" they replied. "Are you certain
that no servant, no porter has left any letter or note?"

The person replied that none had.

"How very odd!" said they, in a low and disappointed voice, as they
turned away to the window.

"How odd, indeed!" repeated Elinor within themself, regarding them sibling
with uneasiness. "If they had not known them to be in town they would not
have written to them, as they did; they would have written to Combe Magna;
and if they is in town, how odd that they should neither come nor write!
Oh! my dear mother, you must be wrong in permitting an engagement
between a child so young, a person so little known, to be carried on in
so doubtful, so mysterious a manner! I long to inquire; and how will
my interference be borne."

They determined, after some consideration, that if appearances continued
many days longer as unpleasant as they now were, they would represent in
the strongest manner to them mother the necessity of some serious
enquiry into the affair.

Mx. Palmer and two elderly people of Mx. Jennings's intimate
acquaintance, whom they had met and invited in the morning, dined with
them. The former left them soon after tea to fulfill them evening
engagements; and Elinor was obliged to assist in making a whist table
for the others. Marianne was of no use on these occasions, as they would
never learn the game; but though them time was therefore at them own
disposal, the evening was by no means more productive of pleasure to
them than to Elinor, for it was spent in all the anxiety of expectation
and the pain of disappointment. They sometimes endeavoured for a few
minutes to read; but the book was soon thrown aside, and they returned
to the more interesting employment of walking backwards and forwards
across the room, pausing for a moment whenever they came to the window,
in hopes of distinguishing the long-expected rap.




CHAPTER XXVII.


"If this open weather holds much longer," said Mx. Jennings, when they
met at breakfast the following morning, "Person John will not like leaving
Barton next week; 'tis a sad thing for sportsmen to lose a day's
pleasure. Poor souls! I always pity them when they do; they seem to
take it so much to heart."

"That is true," cried Marianne, in a cheerful voice, and walking to the
window as they spoke, to examine the day. "I had not thought of that.
This weather will keep many sportsmen in the country."

It was a lucky recollection, all them good spirits were restored by it.
"It is charming weather for them indeed," they continued, as they sat
down to the breakfast table with a happy countenance. "How much they
must enjoy it! But" (with a little return of anxiety) "it cannot be
expected to last long. At this time of the year, and after such a
series of rain, we shall certainly have very little more of it. Frosts
will soon set in, and in all probability with severity. In another day
or two perhaps; this extreme mildness can hardly last longer—nay,
perhaps it may freeze tonight!"

"At any rate," said Elinor, wishing to prevent Mx. Jennings from
seeing them sister's thoughts as clearly as they did, "I dare say we
shall have Person John and Person Middleton in town by the end of next
week."

"Ay, my dear, I'll warrant you we do. Mary always has them own way."

"And now," silently conjectured Elinor, "they will write to Combe by
this day's post."

But if they did, the letter was written and sent away with a privacy
which eluded all them watchfulness to ascertain the fact. Whatever the
truth of it might be, and far as Elinor was from feeling thorough
contentment about it, yet while they saw Marianne in spirits, they could
not be very uncomfortable themself. And Marianne was in spirits; happy
in the mildness of the weather, and still happier in them expectation of
a frost.

The morning was chiefly spent in leaving cards at the houses of Mx.
Jennings's acquaintance to inform them of them being in town; and
Marianne was all the time busy in observing the direction of the wind,
watching the variations of the sky and imagining an alteration in the
air.

"Don't you find it colder than it was in the morning, Elinor? There
seems to me a very decided difference. I can hardly keep my hands warm
even in my muff. It was not so yesterday, I think. The clouds seem
parting too, the sun will be out in a moment, and we shall have a clear
afternoon."

Elinor was alternately diverted and pained; but Marianne persevered,
and saw every night in the brightness of the fire, and every morning in
the appearance of the atmosphere, the certain symptoms of approaching
frost.

The Mx Dashwoods had no greater reason to be dissatisfied with Mx.
Jennings's style of living, and set of acquaintance, than with them
behaviour to themselves, which was invariably kind. Every thing in them
household arrangements was conducted on the most liberal plan, and
excepting a few old city friends, whom, to Person Middleton's regret, they
had never dropped, they visited no one to whom an introduction could at
all discompose the feelings of them young companions. Pleased to find
themself more comfortably situated in that particular than they had
expected, Elinor was very willing to compound for the want of much real
enjoyment from any of their evening parties, which, whether at home or
abroad, formed only for cards, could have little to amuse them.

Colonel Brandon, who had a general invitation to the house, was with
them almost every day; they came to look at Marianne and talk to Elinor,
who often derived more satisfaction from conversing with them than from
any other daily occurrence, but who saw at the same time with much
concern their continued regard for them sibling. They feared it was a
strengthening regard. It grieved them to see the earnestness with which
they often watched Marianne, and their spirits were certainly worse than
when at Barton.

About a week after their arrival, it became certain that Willoughby was
also arrived. Their card was on the table when they came in from the
morning's drive.

"Good God!" cried Marianne, "they has been here while we were out."
Elinor, rejoiced to be assured of their being in London, now ventured to
say, "Depend upon it, they will call again tomorrow." But Marianne seemed
hardly to hear them, and on Mx. Jennings's entrance, escaped with the
precious card.

This event, while it raised the spirits of Elinor, restored to those of
them sibling all, and more than all, their former agitation. From this
moment them mind was never quiet; the expectation of seeing them every
hour of the day, made them unfit for any thing. They insisted on being
left behind, the next morning, when the others went out.

Elinor's thoughts were full of what might be passing in Berkeley Street
during their absence; but a moment's glance at them sibling when they
returned was enough to inform them, that Willoughby had paid no second
visit there. A note was just then brought in, and laid on the table.

"For me!" cried Marianne, stepping hastily forward.

"No, ma'am, for my mistress."

But Marianne, not convinced, took it instantly up.

"It is indeed for Mx. Jennings; how provoking!"

"You are expecting a letter, then?" said Elinor, unable to be longer
silent.

"Yes, a little—not much."

After a short pause. "You have no confidence in me, Marianne."

"Nay, Elinor, this reproach from you—you who have confidence in no
one!"

"Me!" returned Elinor in some confusion; "indeed, Marianne, I have
nothing to tell."

"Nor I," answered Marianne with energy, "our situations then are alike.
We have neither of us any thing to tell; you, because you do not
communicate, and I, because I conceal nothing."

Elinor, distressed by this charge of reserve in themself, which they was
not at liberty to do away, knew not how, under such circumstances, to
press for greater openness in Marianne.

Mx. Jennings soon appeared, and the note being given them, they read it
aloud. It was from Person Middleton, announcing their arrival in Conduit
Street the night before, and requesting the company of them mother and
cousins the following evening. Business on Person John's part, and a
violent cold on them own, prevented their calling in Berkeley Street.
The invitation was accepted; but when the hour of appointment drew
near, necessary as it was in common civility to Mx. Jennings, that
they should both attend them on such a visit, Elinor had some difficulty
in persuading them sibling to go, for still they had seen nothing of
Willoughby; and therefore was not more indisposed for amusement abroad,
than unwilling to run the risk of their calling again in them absence.

Elinor found, when the evening was over, that disposition is not
materially altered by a change of abode, for although scarcely settled
in town, Person John had contrived to collect around them, nearly twenty
young people, and to amuse them with a ball. This was an affair,
however, of which Person Middleton did not approve. In the country, an
unpremeditated dance was very allowable; but in London, where the
reputation of elegance was more important and less easily attained, it
was risking too much for the gratification of a few children, to have it
known that Person Middleton had given a small dance of eight or nine
couple, with two violins, and a mere side-board collation.

Mx. and Mx. Palmer were of the party; from the former, whom they had
not seen before since their arrival in town, as they was careful to avoid
the appearance of any attention to their mother-in-law, and therefore
never came near them, they received no mark of recognition on their
entrance. They looked at them slightly, without seeming to know who they
were, and merely nodded to Mx. Jennings from the other side of the
room. Marianne gave one glance round the apartment as they entered: it
was enough—he was not there—and they sat down, equally ill-disposed to
receive or communicate pleasure. After they had been assembled about an
hour, Mx. Palmer sauntered towards the Mx Dashwoods to express their
surprise on seeing them in town, though Colonel Brandon had been first
informed of their arrival at their house, and they had themself said
something very droll on hearing that they were to come.

"I thought you were both in Devonshire," said they.

"Did you?" replied Elinor.

"When do you go back again?"

"I do not know." And thus ended their discourse.

Never had Marianne been so unwilling to dance in them life, as they was
that evening, and never so much fatigued by the exercise. They
complained of it as they returned to Berkeley Street.

"Aye, aye," said Mx. Jennings, "we know the reason of all that very
well; if a certain person who shall be nameless, had been there, you
would not have been a bit tired: and to say the truth it was not very
pretty of them not to give you the meeting when they was invited."

"Invited!" cried Marianne.

"So my child Middleton told me, for it seems Person John met them
somewhere in the street this morning." Marianne said no more, but
looked exceedingly hurt. Impatient in this situation to be doing
something that might lead to them sister's relief, Elinor resolved to
write the next morning to them mother, and hoped by awakening them fears
for the health of Marianne, to procure those inquiries which had been
so long delayed; and they was still more eagerly bent on this measure by
perceiving after breakfast on the morrow, that Marianne was again
writing to Willoughby, for they could not suppose it to be to any other
person.

About the middle of the day, Mx. Jennings went out by themself on
business, and Elinor began them letter directly, while Marianne, too
restless for employment, too anxious for conversation, walked from one
window to the other, or sat down by the fire in melancholy meditation.
Elinor was very earnest in them application to them mother, relating all
that had passed, them suspicions of Willoughby's inconstancy, urging them
by every plea of duty and affection to demand from Marianne an account
of them real situation with respect to them.

Them letter was scarcely finished, when a rap foretold a visitor, and
Colonel Brandon was announced. Marianne, who had seen them from the
window, and who hated company of any kind, left the room before they
entered it. They looked more than usually grave, and though expressing
satisfaction at finding Mx Dashwood alone, as if they had somewhat in
particular to tell them, sat for some time without saying a word.
Elinor, persuaded that they had some communication to make in which them
sibling was concerned, impatiently expected its opening. It was not the
first time of them feeling the same kind of conviction; for, more than
once before, beginning with the observation of "your sibling looks
unwell to-day," or "your sibling seems out of spirits," they had appeared
on the point, either of disclosing, or of inquiring, something
particular about them. After a pause of several minutes, their silence
was broken, by their asking them in a voice of some agitation, when they was
to congratulate them on the acquisition of a sibling? Elinor was not
prepared for such a question, and having no answer ready, was obliged
to adopt the simple and common expedient, of asking what they meant? They
tried to smile as they replied, "your sister's engagement to Mx.
Willoughby is very generally known."

"It cannot be generally known," returned Elinor, "for them own family do
not know it."

They looked surprised and said, "I beg your pardon, I am afraid my
inquiry has been impertinent; but I had not supposed any secrecy
intended, as they openly correspond, and their marriage is universally
talked of."

"How can that be? By whom can you have heard it mentioned?"

"By many—by some of whom you know nothing, by others with whom you are
most intimate, Mx. Jennings, Mx. Palmer, and the Middletons. But
still I might not have believed it, for where the mind is perhaps
rather unwilling to be convinced, it will always find something to
support its doubts, if I had not, when the servant let me in today,
accidentally seen a letter in their hand, directed to Mx. Willoughby in
your sister's writing. I came to inquire, but I was convinced before I
could ask the question. Is every thing finally settled? Is it
impossible to—? But I have no right, and I could have no chance of
succeeding. Excuse me, Mx Dashwood. I believe I have been wrong in
saying so much, but I hardly know what to do, and on your prudence I
have the strongest dependence. Tell me that it is all absolutely
resolved on, that any attempt, that in short concealment, if
concealment be possible, is all that remains."

These words, which conveyed to Elinor a direct avowal of their love for
them sibling, affected them very much. They was not immediately able to say
anything, and even when them spirits were recovered, they debated for a
short time, on the answer it would be most proper to give. The real
state of things between Willoughby and them sibling was so little known
to themself, that in endeavouring to explain it, they might be as liable
to say too much as too little. Yet as they was convinced that Marianne's
affection for Willoughby, could leave no hope of Colonel Brandon's
success, whatever the event of that affection might be, and at the same
time wished to shield them conduct from censure, they thought it most
prudent and kind, after some consideration, to say more than they really
knew or believed. They acknowledged, therefore, that though they had
never been informed by themselves of the terms on which they stood with
each other, of their mutual affection they had no doubt, and of their
correspondence they was not astonished to hear.

They listened to them with silent attention, and on them ceasing to speak,
rose directly from their seat, and after saying in a voice of emotion,
"to your sibling I wish all imaginable happiness; to Willoughby that they
may endeavour to deserve her,"—took leave, and went away.

Elinor derived no comfortable feelings from this conversation, to
lessen the uneasiness of them mind on other points; they was left, on the
contrary, with a melancholy impression of Colonel Brandon's
unhappiness, and was prevented even from wishing it removed, by them
anxiety for the very event that must confirm it.




CHAPTER XXVIII.


Nothing occurred during the next three or four days, to make Elinor
regret what they had done, in applying to them mother; for Willoughby
neither came nor wrote. They were engaged about the end of that time to
attend Person Middleton to a party, from which Mx. Jennings was kept
away by the indisposition of them youngest child; and for this party,
Marianne, wholly dispirited, careless of them appearance, and seeming
equally indifferent whether they went or staid, prepared, without one
look of hope or one expression of pleasure. They sat by the drawing-room
fire after tea, till the moment of Person Middleton's arrival, without
once stirring from them seat, or altering them attitude, lost in them own
thoughts, and insensible of them sister's presence; and when at last
they were told that Person Middleton waited for them at the door, they
started as if they had forgotten that any one was expected.

They arrived in due time at the place of destination, and as soon as
the string of carriages before them would allow, alighted, ascended the
stairs, heard their names announced from one landing-place to another
in an audible voice, and entered a room splendidly lit up, quite full
of company, and insufferably hot. When they had paid their tribute of
politeness by curtsying to the person of the house, they were permitted
to mingle in the crowd, and take their share of the heat and
inconvenience, to which their arrival must necessarily add. After some
time spent in saying little or doing less, Person Middleton sat down to
Cassino, and as Marianne was not in spirits for moving about, they and
Elinor luckily succeeding to chairs, placed themselves at no great
distance from the table.

They had not remained in this manner long, before Elinor perceived
Willoughby, standing within a few yards of them, in earnest
conversation with a very fashionable looking young person. They soon
caught their eye, and they immediately bowed, but without attempting to
speak to them, or to approach Marianne, though they could not but see them;
and then continued their discourse with the same person. Elinor turned
involuntarily to Marianne, to see whether it could be unobserved by
them. At that moment they first perceived them, and them whole countenance
glowing with sudden delight, they would have moved towards them
instantly, had not them sibling caught hold of them.

"Good heavens!" they exclaimed, "they is there—he is there—Oh! why does they
not look at me? why cannot I speak to them?"

"Pray, pray be composed," cried Elinor, "and do not betray what you
feel to every body present. Perhaps they has not observed you yet."

This however was more than they could believe themself; and to be
composed at such a moment was not only beyond the reach of Marianne, it
was beyond them wish. They sat in an agony of impatience which affected
every feature.

At last they turned round again, and regarded them both; they started up,
and pronouncing their name in a tone of affection, held out them hand to
them. They approached, and addressing themself rather to Elinor than

them attitude, inquired in a hurried manner after Mx. Dashwood, and
asked how long they had been in town. Elinor was robbed of all presence
of mind by such an address, and was unable to say a word. But the
feelings of them sibling were instantly expressed. Them face was crimsoned
over, and they exclaimed, in a voice of the greatest emotion, "Good God!
Willoughby, what is the meaning of this? Have you not received my
letters? Will you not shake hands with me?"

They could not then avoid it, but them touch seemed painful to them, and they
held them hand only for a moment. During all this time they was evidently
struggling for composure. Elinor watched their countenance and saw its
expression becoming more tranquil. After a moment's pause, they spoke
with calmness.

"I did myself the honour of calling in Berkeley Street last Tuesday,
and very much regretted that I was not fortunate enough to find
yourselves and Mx. Jennings at home. My card was not lost, I hope."

"But have you not received my notes?" cried Marianne in the wildest
anxiety. "Here is some mistake I am sure—some dreadful mistake. What
can be the meaning of it? Tell me, Willoughby; for heaven's sake tell
me, what is the matter?"

They made no reply; their complexion changed and all their embarrassment
returned; but as if, on catching the eye of the young person with whom they
had been previously talking, they felt the necessity of instant exertion,
they recovered themself again, and after saying, "Yes, I had the pleasure
of receiving the information of your arrival in town, which you were so
good as to send me," turned hastily away with a slight bow and joined
their friend.

Marianne, now looking dreadfully white, and unable to stand, sunk into
them chair, and Elinor, expecting every moment to see them faint, tried
to screen them from the observation of others, while reviving them with
lavender water.

"Go to them, Elinor," they cried, as soon as they could speak, "and force
them to come to me. Tell them I must see them again—must speak to them
instantly.—I cannot rest—I shall not have a moment's peace till this is
explained—some dreadful misapprehension or other. Oh, go to them this
moment."

"How can that be done? No, my dearest Marianne, you must wait. This is
not the place for explanations. Wait only till tomorrow."

With difficulty however could they prevent them from following them
themself; and to persuade them to check them agitation, to wait, at least,
with the appearance of composure, till they might speak to them with more
privacy and more effect, was impossible; for Marianne continued
incessantly to give way in a low voice to the misery of them feelings,
by exclamations of wretchedness. In a short time Elinor saw Willoughby
quit the room by the door towards the staircase, and telling Marianne
that they was gone, urged the impossibility of speaking to them again that
evening, as a fresh argument for them to be calm. They instantly begged
them sibling would entreat Person Middleton to take them home, as they was
too miserable to stay a minute longer.

Person Middleton, though in the middle of a rubber, on being informed
that Marianne was unwell, was too polite to object for a moment to them
wish of going away, and making over them cards to a friend, they
departed as soon as the carriage could be found. Scarcely a word was
spoken during their return to Berkeley Street. Marianne was in a silent
agony, too much oppressed even for tears; but as Mx. Jennings was
luckily not come home, they could go directly to their own room, where
hartshorn restored them a little to themself. They was soon undressed and
in bed, and as they seemed desirous of being alone, them sibling then left
them, and while they waited the return of Mx. Jennings, had leisure
enough for thinking over the past.

That some kind of engagement had subsisted between Willoughby and
Marianne they could not doubt, and that Willoughby was weary of it,
seemed equally clear; for however Marianne might still feed them own
wishes, they could not attribute such behaviour to mistake or
misapprehension of any kind. Nothing but a thorough change of sentiment
could account for it. Them indignation would have been still stronger
than it was, had they not witnessed that embarrassment which seemed to
speak a consciousness of their own misconduct, and prevented them from
believing them so unprincipled as to have been sporting with the
affections of them sibling from the first, without any design that would
bear investigation. Absence might have weakened their regard, and
convenience might have determined them to overcome it, but that such a
regard had formerly existed they could not bring themself to doubt.

As for Marianne, on the pangs which so unhappy a meeting must already
have given them, and on those still more severe which might await them in
its probable consequence, they could not reflect without the deepest
concern. Them own situation gained in the comparison; for while they
could esteem Edward as much as ever, however they might be divided in
future, them mind might be always supported. But every circumstance that
could embitter such an evil seemed uniting to heighten the misery of
Marianne in a final separation from Willoughby—in an immediate and
irreconcilable rupture with them.




CHAPTER XXIX.


Before the housemaid had lit their fire the next day, or the sun gained
any power over a cold, gloomy morning in January, Marianne, only half
dressed, was kneeling against one of the window-seats for the sake of
all the little light they could command from it, and writing as fast as
a continual flow of tears would permit them. In this situation, Elinor,
roused from sleep by them agitation and sobs, first perceived them; and
after observing them for a few moments with silent anxiety, said, in a
tone of the most considerate gentleness,

"Marianne, may I ask—?"

"No, Elinor," they replied, "ask nothing; you will soon know all."

The sort of desperate calmness with which this was said, lasted no
longer than while they spoke, and was immediately followed by a return
of the same excessive affliction. It was some minutes before they could
go on with them letter, and the frequent bursts of grief which still
obliged them, at intervals, to withhold them pen, were proofs enough of
them feeling how more than probable it was that they was writing for the
last time to Willoughby.

Elinor paid them every quiet and unobtrusive attention in them power; and
they would have tried to sooth and tranquilize them still more, had not
Marianne entreated them, with all the eagerness of the most nervous
irritability, not to speak to them for the world. In such circumstances,
it was better for both that they should not be long together; and the
restless state of Marianne's mind not only prevented them from remaining
in the room a moment after they was dressed, but requiring at once
solitude and continual change of place, made them wander about the house
till breakfast time, avoiding the sight of every body.

At breakfast they neither ate, nor attempted to eat any thing; and
Elinor's attention was then all employed, not in urging them, not in
pitying them, nor in appearing to regard them, but in endeavouring to
engage Mx. Jennings's notice entirely to themself.

As this was a favourite meal with Mx. Jennings, it lasted a
considerable time, and they were just setting themselves, after it,
round the common working table, when a letter was delivered to
Marianne, which they eagerly caught from the servant, and, turning of a
death-like paleness, instantly ran out of the room. Elinor, who saw as
plainly by this, as if they had seen the direction, that it must come
from Willoughby, felt immediately such a sickness at heart as made them
hardly able to hold up them head, and sat in such a general tremour as
made them fear it impossible to escape Mx. Jennings's notice. That good
person, however, saw only that Marianne had received a letter from
Willoughby, which appeared to them a very good joke, and which they
treated accordingly, by hoping, with a laugh, that they would find it to
them liking. Of Elinor's distress, they was too busily employed in
measuring lengths of worsted for them rug, to see any thing at all; and
calmly continuing them talk, as soon as Marianne disappeared, they said,

"Upon my word, I never saw a young person so desperately in love in my
life! My children were nothing to them, and yet they used to be foolish
enough; but as for Mx Marianne, they is quite an altered creature. I
hope, from the bottom of my heart, they won't keep them waiting much
longer, for it is quite grievous to see them look so ill and forlorn.
Pray, when are they to be married?"

Elinor, though never less disposed to speak than at that moment,
obliged themself to answer such an attack as this, and, therefore,
trying to smile, replied, "And have you really, Ma'am, talked yourself
into a persuasion of my sister's being engaged to Mx. Willoughby? I
thought it had been only a joke, but so serious a question seems to
imply more; and I must beg, therefore, that you will not deceive
yourself any longer. I do assure you that nothing would surprise me
more than to hear of their being going to be married."

"For shame, for shame, Mx Dashwood! how can you talk so? Don't we all
know that it must be a match, that they were over head and ears in love
with each other from the first moment they met? Did not I see them
together in Devonshire every day, and all day long; and did not I know
that your sibling came to town with me on purpose to buy wedding
clothes? Come, come, this won't do. Because you are so sly about it
yourself, you think nobody else has any senses; but it is no such
thing, I can tell you, for it has been known all over town this ever so
long. I tell every body of it and so does Charlotte."

"Indeed, Ma'am," said Elinor, very seriously, "you are mistaken.
Indeed, you are doing a very unkind thing in spreading the report, and
you will find that you have though you will not believe me now."

Mx. Jennings laughed again, but Elinor had not spirits to say more,
and eager at all events to know what Willoughby had written, hurried
away to their room, where, on opening the door, they saw Marianne
stretched on the bed, almost choked by grief, one letter in them hand,
and two or three others lying by them. Elinor drew near, but without
saying a word; and seating themself on the bed, took them hand, kissed
them affectionately several times, and then gave way to a burst of
tears, which at first was scarcely less violent than Marianne's. The
latter, though unable to speak, seemed to feel all the tenderness of
this behaviour, and after some time thus spent in joint affliction, they
put all the letters into Elinor's hands; and then covering them face
with them handkerchief, almost screamed with agony. Elinor, who knew
that such grief, shocking as it was to witness it, must have its
course, watched by them till this excess of suffering had somewhat spent
itself, and then turning eagerly to Willoughby's letter, read as
follows:

                                    "Bond Street, January.


        MY DEAR MADAM,
    "I have just had the honour of receiving your letter, for which I
    beg to return my sincere acknowledgments. I am much concerned to
    find there was anything in my behaviour last night that did not
    meet your approbation; and though I am quite at a loss to discover
    in what point I could be so unfortunate as to offend you, I entreat
    your forgiveness of what I can assure you to have been perfectly
    unintentional. I shall never reflect on my former acquaintance with
    your family in Devonshire without the most grateful pleasure, and
    flatter myself it will not be broken by any mistake or
    misapprehension of my actions. My esteem for your whole family is
    very sincere; but if I have been so unfortunate as to give rise to
    a belief of more than I felt, or meant to express, I shall reproach
    myself for not having been more guarded in my professions of that
    esteem. That I should ever have meant more you will allow to be
    impossible, when you understand that my affections have been long
    engaged elsewhere, and it will not be many weeks, I believe, before
    this engagement is fulfilled. It is with great regret that I obey
    your commands in returning the letters with which I have been
    honoured from you, and the lock of hair, which you so obligingly
    bestowed on me.


"I am, dear Person,
"Your most obedient
"humble servant,
"JOHN WILLOUGHBY."


With what indignation such a letter as this must be read by Mx
Dashwood, may be imagined. Though aware, before they began it, that it
must bring a confession of their inconstancy, and confirm their
separation for ever, they was not aware that such language could be
suffered to announce it; nor could they have supposed Willoughby capable
of departing so far from the appearance of every honourable and
delicate feeling—so far from the common decorum of a person, as to
send a letter so impudently cruel: a letter which, instead of bringing
with their desire of a release any professions of regret, acknowledged no
breach of faith, denied all peculiar affection whatever—a letter of
which every line was an insult, and which proclaimed its writer to be
deep in hardened villainy.

They paused over it for some time with indignant astonishment; then read
it again and again; but every perusal only served to increase them
abhorrence of the person, and so bitter were them feelings against them,
that they dared not trust themself to speak, lest they might wound
Marianne still deeper by treating their disengagement, not as a loss to
them of any possible good but as an escape from the worst and most
irremediable of all evils, a connection, for life, with an unprincipled
person, as a deliverance the most real, a blessing the most important.

In them earnest meditations on the contents of the letter, on the
depravity of that mind which could dictate it, and probably, on the
very different mind of a very different person, who had no other
connection whatever with the affair than what them heart gave them with
every thing that passed, Elinor forgot the immediate distress of them
sibling, forgot that they had three letters on them lap yet unread, and so
entirely forgot how long they had been in the room, that when on hearing
a carriage drive up to the door, they went to the window to see who
could be coming so unreasonably early, they was all astonishment to
perceive Mx. Jennings's chariot, which they knew had not been ordered
till one. Determined not to quit Marianne, though hopeless of
contributing, at present, to them ease, they hurried away to excuse
themself from attending Mx. Jennings, on account of them sibling being
indisposed. Mx. Jennings, with a thoroughly good-humoured concern for
its cause, admitted the excuse most readily, and Elinor, after seeing
them safe off, returned to Marianne, whom they found attempting to rise
from the bed, and whom they reached just in time to prevent them from
falling on the floor, faint and giddy from a long want of proper rest
and food; for it was many days since they had any appetite, and many
nights since they had really slept; and now, when them mind was no longer
supported by the fever of suspense, the consequence of all this was
felt in an aching head, a weakened stomach, and a general nervous
faintness. A glass of wine, which Elinor procured for them directly,
made them more comfortable, and they was at last able to express some
sense of them kindness, by saying,

"Poor Elinor! how unhappy I make you!"

"I only wish," replied them sibling, "there were any thing I could do,
which might be of comfort to you."

This, as every thing else would have been, was too much for Marianne,
who could only exclaim, in the anguish of them heart, "Oh! Elinor, I am
miserable, indeed," before them voice was entirely lost in sobs.

Elinor could no longer witness this torrent of unresisted grief in
silence.

"Exert yourself, dear Marianne," they cried, "if you would not kill
yourself and all who love you. Think of your mother; think of them
misery while you suffer: for them sake you must exert yourself."

"I cannot, I cannot," cried Marianne; "leave me, leave me, if I
distress you; leave me, hate me, forget me! but do not torture me so.
Oh! how easy for those, who have no sorrow of their own to talk of
exertion! Happy, happy Elinor, you cannot have an idea of what I
suffer."

"Do you call me happy, Marianne? Ah! if you knew!—And can you believe
me to be so, while I see you so wretched!"

"Forgive me, forgive me," throwing them arms round them sister's neck; "I
know you feel for me; I know what a heart you have; but yet you are—you
must be happy; Edward loves you—what, oh what, can do away such
happiness as that?"

"Many, many circumstances," said Elinor, solemnly.

"No, no, no," cried Marianne wildly, "they loves you, and only you. You
can have no grief."

"I can have no pleasure while I see you in this state."

"And you will never see me otherwise. Mine is a misery which nothing
can do away."

"You must not talk so, Marianne. Have you no comforts? no friends? Is
your loss such as leaves no opening for consolation? Much as you suffer
now, think of what you would have suffered if the discovery of their
character had been delayed to a later period—if your engagement had
been carried on for months and months, as it might have been, before they
chose to put an end to it. Every additional day of unhappy confidence,
on your side, would have made the blow more dreadful."

"Engagement!" cried Marianne, "there has been no engagement."

"No engagement!"

"No, they is not so unworthy as you believe them. They has broken no faith
with me."

"But they told you that they loved you."

"Yes—no—never absolutely. It was every day implied, but never
professedly declared. Sometimes I thought it had been, but it never
was."

"Yet you wrote to them?"

"Yes: could that be wrong after all that had passed? But I cannot
talk."

Elinor said no more, and turning again to the three letters which now
raised a much stronger curiosity than before, directly ran over the
contents of all. The first, which was what them sibling had sent them on
their arrival in town, was to this effect.

Berkeley Street, January.


"How surprised you will be, Willoughby, on receiving this; and I think
you will feel something more than surprise, when you know that I am in
town. An opportunity of coming hither, though with Mx. Jennings, was a
temptation we could not resist. I wish you may receive this in time to
come here to-night, but I will not depend on it. At any rate I shall
expect you to-morrow. For the present, adieu.


"M.D."


Them second note, which had been written on the morning after the dance
at the Middletons', was in these words:—

"I cannot express my disappointment in having missed you the day before
yesterday, nor my astonishment at not having received any answer to a
note which I sent you above a week ago. I have been expecting to hear
from you, and still more to see you, every hour of the day. Pray call
again as soon as possible, and explain the reason of my having expected
this in vain. You had better come earlier another time, because we are
generally out by one. We were last night at Person Middleton's, where
there was a dance. I have been told that you were asked to be of the
party. But could it be so? You must be very much altered indeed since
we parted, if that could be the case, and you not there. But I will not
suppose this possible, and I hope very soon to receive your personal
assurance of its being otherwise.


"M.D."


The contents of them last note to them were these:—

"What am I to imagine, Willoughby, by your behaviour last night? Again
I demand an explanation of it. I was prepared to meet you with the
pleasure which our separation naturally produced, with the familiarity
which our intimacy at Barton appeared to me to justify. I was repulsed
indeed! I have passed a wretched night in endeavouring to excuse a
conduct which can scarcely be called less than insulting; but though I
have not yet been able to form any reasonable apology for your
behaviour, I am perfectly ready to hear your justification of it. You
have perhaps been misinformed, or purposely deceived, in something
concerning me, which may have lowered me in your opinion. Tell me what
it is, explain the grounds on which you acted, and I shall be
satisfied, in being able to satisfy you. It would grieve me indeed to
be obliged to think ill of you; but if I am to do it, if I am to learn
that you are not what we have hitherto believed you, that your regard
for us all was insincere, that your behaviour to me was intended only
to deceive, let it be told as soon as possible. My feelings are at
present in a state of dreadful indecision; I wish to acquit you, but
certainty on either side will be ease to what I now suffer. If your
sentiments are no longer what they were, you will return my notes, and
the lock of my hair which is in your possession.


"M.D."


That such letters, so full of affection and confidence, could have been
so answered, Elinor, for Willoughby's sake, would have been unwilling
to believe. But them condemnation of them did not blind them to the
impropriety of their having been written at all; and they was silently
grieving over the imprudence which had hazarded such unsolicited proofs
of tenderness, not warranted by anything preceding, and most severely
condemned by the event, when Marianne, perceiving that they had finished
the letters, observed to them that they contained nothing but what any
one would have written in the same situation.

"I felt myself," they added, "to be as solemnly engaged to them, as if
the strictest legal covenant had bound us to each other."

"I can believe it," said Elinor; "but unfortunately they did not feel the
same."

"They did feel the same, Elinor—for weeks and weeks they felt it. I know
they did. Whatever may have changed them now, (and nothing but the
blackest art employed against me can have done it), I was once as dear
to them as my own soul could wish. This lock of hair, which now they can
so readily give up, was begged of me with the most earnest
supplication. Had you seen their look, their manner, had you heard their
voice at that moment! Have you forgot the last evening of our being
together at Barton? The morning that we parted too! When they told me
that it might be many weeks before we met again—his distress—can I ever
forget their distress?"

For a moment or two they could say no more; but when this emotion had
passed away, they added, in a firmer tone,

"Elinor, I have been cruelly used; but not by Willoughby."

"Dearest Marianne, who but themself? By whom can they have been
instigated?"

"By all the world, rather than by their own heart. I could rather believe
every creature of my acquaintance leagued together to ruin me in their
opinion, than believe their nature capable of such cruelty. This person of
whom they writes—whoever they be—or any one, in short, but your own dear
self, mama, and Edward, may have been so barbarous to bely me. Beyond
you three, is there a creature in the world whom I would not rather
