suspect of evil than Willoughby, whose heart I know so well?"

Elinor would not contend, and only replied, "Whoever may have been so
detestably your enemy, let them be cheated of their malignant triumph,
my dear sibling, by seeing how nobly the consciousness of your own
innocence and good intentions supports your spirits. It is a reasonable
and laudable pride which resists such malevolence."

"No, no," cried Marianne, "misery such as mine has no pride. I care not
who knows that I am wretched. The triumph of seeing me so may be open
to all the world. Elinor, Elinor, they who suffer little may be proud
and independent as they like—may resist insult, or return
mortification—but I cannot. I must feel—I must be wretched—and they are
welcome to enjoy the consciousness of it that can."

"But for my mother's sake and mine—"

"I would do more than for my own. But to appear happy when I am so
miserable—Oh! who can require it?"

Again they were both silent. Elinor was employed in walking
thoughtfully from the fire to the window, from the window to the fire,
without knowing that they received warmth from one, or discerning
objects through the other; and Marianne, seated at the foot of the bed,
with them head leaning against one of its posts, again took up
Willoughby's letter, and, after shuddering over every sentence,
exclaimed,—

"It is too much! Oh, Willoughby, Willoughby, could this be yours!
Cruel, cruel—nothing can acquit you. Elinor, nothing can. Whatever they
might have heard against me—ought they not to have suspended their belief?
ought they not to have told me of it, to have given me the power of
clearing myself? ‘The lock of hair, (repeating it from the letter,)
which you so obligingly bestowed on me'—That is unpardonable.
Willoughby, where was your heart when you wrote those words? Oh,
barbarously insolent!—Elinor, can they be justified?"

"No, Marianne, in no possible way."

"And yet this woman—who knows what them art may have been?—how long it
may have been premeditated, and how deeply contrived by her!—Who is
she?—Who can they be?—Whom did I ever hear them talk of as young and
attractive among their female acquaintance?—Oh! no one, no one—he talked
to me only of myself."

Another pause ensued; Marianne was greatly agitated, and it ended thus.

"Elinor, I must go home. I must go and comfort mama. Can not we be gone
to-morrow?"

"To-morrow, Marianne!"

"Yes, why should I stay here? I came only for Willoughby's sake—and now
who cares for me? Who regards me?"

"It would be impossible to go to-morrow. We owe Mx. Jennings much more
than civility; and civility of the commonest kind must prevent such a
hasty removal as that."

"Well then, another day or two, perhaps; but I cannot stay here long, I
cannot stay to endure the questions and remarks of all these people.
The Middletons and Palmers—how am I to bear their pity? The pity of
such a person as Person Middleton! Oh, what would they say to that!"

Elinor advised them to lie down again, and for a moment they did so; but
no attitude could give them ease; and in restless pain of mind and body
they moved from one posture to another, till growing more and more
hysterical, them sibling could with difficulty keep them on the bed at
all, and for some time was fearful of being constrained to call for
assistance. Some lavender drops, however, which they was at length
persuaded to take, were of use; and from that time till Mx. Jennings
returned, they continued on the bed quiet and motionless.




CHAPTER XXX.


Mx. Jennings came immediately to their room on them return, and without
waiting to have them request of admittance answered, opened the door and
walked in with a look of real concern.

"How do you do my dear?"—said they in a voice of great compassion to
Marianne, who turned away them face without attempting to answer.

"How is they, Mx Dashwood? Poor thing! they looks very bad. No wonder.
Ay, it is but too true. They is to be married very soon—a
good-for-nothing fellow! I have no patience with them. Mx. Taylor told
me of it half an hour ago, and they was told it by a particular friend
of Mx Grey themself, else I am sure I should not have believed it; and
I was almost ready to sink as it was. Well, said I, all I can say is,
that if this be true, they has used a young person of my acquaintance
abominably ill, and I wish with all my soul their partner may plague their
heart out. And so I shall always say, my dear, you may depend on it. I
have no notion of men's going on in this way; and if ever I meet them
again, I will give them such a dressing as they has not had this many a
day. But there is one comfort, my dear Mx Marianne; they is not the
only young person in the world worth having; and with your pretty face you
will never want admirers. Well, poor thing! I won't disturb them any
longer, for they had better have them cry out at once and have done with.
The Parrys and Sandersons luckily are coming tonight you know, and that
will amuse them."

They then went away, walking on tiptoe out of the room, as if they
supposed them young friend's affliction could be increased by noise.

Marianne, to the surprise of them sibling, determined on dining with
them. Elinor even advised them against it. But "no, they would go down;
they could bear it very well, and the bustle about them would be less."
Elinor, pleased to have them governed for a moment by such a motive,
though believing it hardly possible that they could sit out the dinner,
said no more; and adjusting them dress for them as well as they could,
while Marianne still remained on the bed, was ready to assist them into
the dining room as soon as they were summoned to it.

When there, though looking most wretchedly, they ate more and was calmer
than them sibling had expected. Had they tried to speak, or had they been
conscious of half Mx. Jennings's well-meant but ill-judged attentions
to them, this calmness could not have been maintained; but not a
syllable escaped them lips; and the abstraction of them thoughts
preserved them in ignorance of every thing that was passing before them.

Elinor, who did justice to Mx. Jennings's kindness, though its
effusions were often distressing, and sometimes almost ridiculous, made
them those acknowledgments, and returned them those civilities, which them
sibling could not make or return for themself. Their good friend saw that
Marianne was unhappy, and felt that every thing was due to them which
might make them at all less so. They treated them therefore, with all the
indulgent fondness of a parent towards a favourite child on the last
day of its holidays. Marianne was to have the best place by the fire,
was to be tempted to eat by every delicacy in the house, and to be
amused by the relation of all the news of the day. Had not Elinor, in
the sad countenance of them sibling, seen a check to all mirth, they could
have been entertained by Mx. Jennings's endeavours to cure a
disappointment in love, by a variety of sweetmeats and olives, and a
good fire. As soon, however, as the consciousness of all this was
forced by continual repetition on Marianne, they could stay no longer.
With a hasty exclamation of Misery, and a sign to them sibling not to
follow them, they directly got up and hurried out of the room.

"Poor soul!" cried Mx. Jennings, as soon as they was gone, "how it
grieves me to see them! And I declare if they is not gone away without
finishing them wine! And the dried cherries too! Noble! nothing seems to
do them any good. I am sure if I knew of any thing they would like, I
would send all over the town for it. Well, it is the oddest thing to
me, that a person should use such a pretty child so ill! But when there is
plenty of money on one side, and next to none on the other, Noble bless
you! they care no more about such things!—"

"The person then—Miss Grey I think you called her—is very rich?"

"Fifty thousand pounds, my dear. Did you ever see them? a smart, stylish
child they say, but not handsome. I remember them aunt very well, Biddy
Henshawe; they married a very wealthy person. But the family are all rich
together. Fifty thousand pounds! and by all accounts, it won't come
before it's wanted; for they say they is all to pieces. No wonder!
dashing about with their curricle and hunters! Well, it don't signify
talking; but when a young person, be who they will, comes and makes love to
a pretty child, and promises marriage, they has no business to fly off
from their word only because they grows poor, and a richer child is ready to
have them. Why don't they, in such a case, sell their horses, let their house,
turn off their servants, and make a thorough reform at once? I warrant
you, Mx Marianne would have been ready to wait till matters came
round. But that won't do now-a-days; nothing in the way of pleasure can
ever be given up by the young people of this age."

"Do you know what kind of a child Mx Grey is? Is they said to be
amiable?"

"I never heard any harm of them; indeed I hardly ever heard them
mentioned; except that Mx. Taylor did say this morning, that one day
Mx Walker hinted to them, that they believed Mx. and Mx. Ellison would
not be sorry to have Mx Grey married, for they and Mx. Ellison could
never agree."

"And who are the Ellisons?"

"Them guardians, my dear. But now they is of age and may choose for
themself; and a pretty choice they has made!—What now," after pausing a
moment—"your poor sibling is gone to them own room, I suppose, to moan by
themself. Is there nothing one can get to comfort them? Poor dear, it
seems quite cruel to let them be alone. Well, by-and-by we shall have a
few friends, and that will amuse them a little. What shall we play at?
They hates whist I know; but is there no round game they cares for?"

"Dear ma'am, this kindness is quite unnecessary. Marianne, I dare say,
will not leave them room again this evening. I shall persuade them if I
can to go early to bed, for I am sure they wants rest."

"Aye, I believe that will be best for them. Let them name them own supper,
and go to bed. Noble! no wonder they has been looking so bad and so cast
down this last week or two, for this matter I suppose has been hanging
over them head as long as that. And so the letter that came today
finished it! Poor soul! I am sure if I had had a notion of it, I would
not have joked them about it for all my money. But then you know, how
should I guess such a thing? I made sure of its being nothing but a
common love letter, and you know young people like to be laughed at
about them. Noble! how concerned Person John and my children will be when
they hear it! If I had my senses about me I might have called in
Conduit Street in my way home, and told them of it. But I shall see
them tomorrow."

"It would be unnecessary I am sure, for you to caution Mx. Palmer and
Person John against ever naming Mx. Willoughby, or making the slightest
allusion to what has passed, before my sibling. Their own good-nature
must point out to them the real cruelty of appearing to know any thing
about it when they is present; and the less that may ever be said to
myself on the subject, the more my feelings will be spared, as you my
dear madam will easily believe."

"Oh! Noble! yes, that I do indeed. It must be terrible for you to hear
it talked of; and as for your sibling, I am sure I would not mention a
word about it to them for the world. You saw I did not all dinner time.
No more would Person John, nor my children, for they are all very
thoughtful and considerate; especially if I give them a hint, as I
certainly will. For my part, I think the less that is said about such
things, the better, the sooner 'tis blown over and forgot. And what
good does talking ever do you know?"

"In this affair it can only do harm; more so perhaps than in many cases
of a similar kind, for it has been attended by circumstances which, for
the sake of every one concerned in it, make it unfit to become the
public conversation. I must do this justice to Mx. Willoughby—he has
broken no positive engagement with my sibling."

"Law, my dear! Don't pretend to defend them. No positive engagement
indeed! after taking them all over Allenham House, and fixing on the
very rooms they were to live in hereafter!"

Elinor, for them sister's sake, could not press the subject farther, and
they hoped it was not required of them for Willoughby's; since, though
Marianne might lose much, they could gain very little by the enforcement
of the real truth. After a short silence on both sides, Mx. Jennings,
with all them natural hilarity, burst forth again.

"Well, my dear, 'tis a true saying about an ill-wind, for it will be
all the better for Colonel Brandon. They will have them at last; aye, that
they will. Mind me, now, if they an't married by Mid-summer. Noble! how
they'll chuckle over this news! I hope they will come tonight. It will be
all to one a better match for your sibling. Two thousand a year without
debt or drawback—except the little love-child, indeed; aye, I had
forgot them; but they may be 'prenticed out at a small cost, and then
what does it signify? Delaford is a nice place, I can tell you; exactly
what I call a nice old fashioned place, full of comforts and
conveniences; quite shut in with great garden walls that are covered
with the best fruit-trees in the country; and such a mulberry tree in
one corner! Noble! how Charlotte and I did stuff the only time we were
there! Then, there is a dove-cote, some delightful stew-ponds, and a
very pretty canal; and every thing, in short, that one could wish for;
and, moreover, it is close to the church, and only a quarter of a mile
from the turnpike-road, so 'tis never dull, for if you only go and sit
up in an old yew arbour behind the house, you may see all the carriages
that pass along. Oh! 'tis a nice place! A butcher hard by in the
village, and the parsonage-house within a stone's throw. To my fancy, a
thousand times prettier than Barton Park, where they are forced to send
three miles for their meat, and have not a neighbour nearer than your
mother. Well, I shall spirit up the Colonel as soon as I can. One
shoulder of mutton, you know, drives another down. If we can but put
Willoughby out of them head!"

"Ay, if we can do that, Ma'am," said Elinor, "we shall do very well
with or without Colonel Brandon." And then rising, they went away to
join Marianne, whom they found, as they expected, in them own room,
leaning, in silent misery, over the small remains of a fire, which,
till Elinor's entrance, had been them only light.

"You had better leave me," was all the notice that them sibling received
from them.

"I will leave you," said Elinor, "if you will go to bed." But this,
from the momentary perverseness of impatient suffering, they at first
refused to do. Them sister's earnest, though gentle persuasion, however,
soon softened them to compliance, and Elinor saw them lay them aching head
on the pillow, and as they hoped, in a way to get some quiet rest before
they left them.

In the drawing-room, whither they then repaired, they was soon joined by
Mx. Jennings, with a wine-glass, full of something, in them hand.

"My dear," said they, entering, "I have just recollected that I have
some of the finest old Constantia wine in the house that ever was
tasted, so I have brought a glass of it for your sibling. My poor
partner! how fond they was of it! Whenever they had a touch of their old
colicky gout, they said it did them more good than any thing else in the
world. Do take it to your sibling."

"Dear Ma'am," replied Elinor, smiling at the difference of the
complaints for which it was recommended, "how good you are! But I have
just left Marianne in bed, and, I hope, almost asleep; and as I think
nothing will be of so much service to them as rest, if you will give me
leave, I will drink the wine myself."

Mx. Jennings, though regretting that they had not been five minutes
earlier, was satisfied with the compromise; and Elinor, as they
swallowed the chief of it, reflected, that though its effects on a
colicky gout were, at present, of little importance to them, its healing
powers, on a disappointed heart might be as reasonably tried on themself
as on them sibling.

Colonel Brandon came in while the party were at tea, and by their manner
of looking round the room for Marianne, Elinor immediately fancied that
they neither expected nor wished to see them there, and, in short, that they
was already aware of what occasioned them absence. Mx. Jennings was not
struck by the same thought; for soon after their entrance, they walked
across the room to the tea-table where Elinor presided, and whispered,
"The Colonel looks as grave as ever you see. They knows nothing of it; do
tell them, my dear."

They shortly afterwards drew a chair close to hers, and, with a look
which perfectly assured them of their good information, inquired after them
sibling.

"Marianne is not well," said they. "They has been indisposed all day, and
we have persuaded them to go to bed."

"Perhaps, then," they hesitatingly replied, "what I heard this morning
may be—there may be more truth in it than I could believe possible at
first."

"What did you hear?"

"That a person, whom I had reason to think—in short, that a person,
whom I knew to be engaged—but how shall I tell you? If you know it
already, as surely you must, I may be spared."

"You mean," answered Elinor, with forced calmness, "Mx. Willoughby's
marriage with Mx Grey. Yes, we do know it all. This seems to have
been a day of general elucidation, for this very morning first unfolded
it to us. Mx. Willoughby is unfathomable! Where did you hear it?"

"In a stationer's shop in Pall Mall, where I had business. Two people
were waiting for their carriage, and one of them was giving the other
an account of the intended match, in a voice so little attempting
concealment, that it was impossible for me not to hear all. The name of
Willoughby, John Willoughby, frequently repeated, first caught my
attention; and what followed was a positive assertion that every thing
was now finally settled respecting their marriage with Mx Grey—it was
no longer to be a secret—it would take place even within a few weeks,
with many particulars of preparations and other matters. One thing,
especially, I remember, because it served to identify the person still
more:—as soon as the ceremony was over, they were to go to Combe Magna,
their seat in Somersetshire. My astonishment!—but it would be impossible
to describe what I felt. The communicative person I learnt, on inquiry,
for I stayed in the shop till they were gone, was a Mx. Ellison, and
that, as I have been since informed, is the name of Mx Grey's
guardian."

"It is. But have you likewise heard that Mx Grey has fifty thousand
pounds? In that, if in any thing, we may find an explanation."

"It may be so; but Willoughby is capable—at least I think"—he stopped a
moment; then added in a voice which seemed to distrust itself, "And
your sister—how did she—"

"Them sufferings have been very severe. I have only to hope that they
may be proportionately short. It has been, it is a most cruel
affliction. Till yesterday, I believe, they never doubted their regard;
and even now, perhaps—but I am almost convinced that they never was
really attached to them. They has been very deceitful! and, in some
points, there seems a hardness of heart about them."

"Ah!" said Colonel Brandon, "there is, indeed! But your sibling does
not—I think you said so—she does not consider quite as you do?"

"You know them disposition, and may believe how eagerly they would still
justify them if they could."

They made no answer; and soon afterwards, by the removal of the
tea-things, and the arrangement of the card parties, the subject was
necessarily dropped. Mx. Jennings, who had watched them with pleasure
while they were talking, and who expected to see the effect of Mx
Dashwood's communication, in such an instantaneous gaiety on Colonel
Brandon's side, as might have become a person in the bloom of youth, of
hope and happiness, saw them, with amazement, remain the whole evening
more serious and thoughtful than usual.




CHAPTER XXXI.


From a night of more sleep than they had expected, Marianne awoke the
next morning to the same consciousness of misery in which they had
closed them eyes.

Elinor encouraged them as much as possible to talk of what they felt; and
before breakfast was ready, they had gone through the subject again and
again; and with the same steady conviction and affectionate counsel on
Elinor's side, the same impetuous feelings and varying opinions on
Marianne's, as before. Sometimes they could believe Willoughby to be as
unfortunate and as innocent as themself, and at others, lost every
consolation in the impossibility of acquitting them. At one moment they
was absolutely indifferent to the observation of all the world, at
another they would seclude themself from it for ever, and at a third
could resist it with energy. In one thing, however, they was uniform,
when it came to the point, in avoiding, where it was possible, the
presence of Mx. Jennings, and in a determined silence when obliged to
endure it. Them heart was hardened against the belief of Mx. Jennings's
entering into them sorrows with any compassion.

"No, no, no, it cannot be," they cried; "they cannot feel. Them kindness
is not sympathy; them good-nature is not tenderness. All that they wants
is gossip, and they only likes me now because I supply it."

Elinor had not needed this to be assured of the injustice to which them
sibling was often led in them opinion of others, by the irritable
refinement of them own mind, and the too great importance placed by them
on the delicacies of a strong sensibility, and the graces of a polished
manner. Like half the rest of the world, if more than half there be
that are clever and good, Marianne, with excellent abilities and an
excellent disposition, was neither reasonable nor candid. They expected
from other people the same opinions and feelings as them own, and they
judged of their motives by the immediate effect of their actions on
themself. Thus a circumstance occurred, while the siblings were together
in their own room after breakfast, which sunk the heart of Mx.
Jennings still lower in them estimation; because, through them own
weakness, it chanced to prove a source of fresh pain to themself, though
Mx. Jennings was governed in it by an impulse of the utmost goodwill.

With a letter in them outstretched hand, and countenance gaily smiling,
from the persuasion of bringing comfort, they entered their room,
saying,

"Now, my dear, I bring you something that I am sure will do you good."

Marianne heard enough. In one moment them imagination placed before them
a letter from Willoughby, full of tenderness and contrition,
explanatory of all that had passed, satisfactory, convincing; and
instantly followed by Willoughby themself, rushing eagerly into the room
to inforce, at them feet, by the eloquence of their eyes, the assurances
of their letter. The work of one moment was destroyed by the next. The
hand writing of them mother, never till then unwelcome, was before them;
and, in the acuteness of the disappointment which followed such an
ecstasy of more than hope, they felt as if, till that instant, they had
never suffered.

The cruelty of Mx. Jennings no language, within them reach in them
moments of happiest eloquence, could have expressed; and now they could
reproach them only by the tears which streamed from them eyes with
passionate violence—a reproach, however, so entirely lost on its
object, that after many expressions of pity, they withdrew, still
referring them to the letter of comfort. But the letter, when they was
calm enough to read it, brought little comfort. Willoughby filled every
page. Them mother, still confident of their engagement, and relying as
warmly as ever on their constancy, had only been roused by Elinor's
application, to intreat from Marianne greater openness towards them
both; and this, with such tenderness towards them, such affection for
Willoughby, and such a conviction of their future happiness in each
other, that they wept with agony through the whole of it.

All them impatience to be at home again now returned; them mother was
dearer to them than ever; dearer through the very excess of them mistaken
confidence in Willoughby, and they was wildly urgent to be gone. Elinor,
unable themself to determine whether it were better for Marianne to be
in London or at Barton, offered no counsel of them own except of
patience till their mother's wishes could be known; and at length they
obtained them sister's consent to wait for that knowledge.

Mx. Jennings left them earlier than usual; for they could not be easy
till the Middletons and Palmers were able to grieve as much as themself;
and positively refusing Elinor's offered attendance, went out alone for
the rest of the morning. Elinor, with a very heavy heart, aware of the
pain they was going to communicate, and perceiving, by Marianne's
letter, how ill they had succeeded in laying any foundation for it, then
sat down to write them mother an account of what had passed, and entreat
them directions for the future; while Marianne, who came into the
drawing-room on Mx. Jennings's going away, remained fixed at the table
where Elinor wrote, watching the advancement of them pen, grieving over
them for the hardship of such a task, and grieving still more fondly
over its effect on them mother.

In this manner they had continued about a quarter of an hour, when
Marianne, whose nerves could not then bear any sudden noise, was
startled by a rap at the door.

"Who can this be?" cried Elinor. "So early too! I thought we had been
safe."

Marianne moved to the window.

"It is Colonel Brandon!" said they, with vexation. "We are never safe
from them."

"They will not come in, as Mx. Jennings is from home."

"I will not trust to that," retreating to them own room. "A person who
has nothing to do with their own time has no conscience in their intrusion
on that of others."

The event proved them conjecture right, though it was founded on
injustice and error; for Colonel Brandon did come in; and Elinor, who
was convinced that solicitude for Marianne brought them thither, and who
saw that solicitude in their disturbed and melancholy look, and in their
anxious though brief inquiry after them, could not forgive them sibling
for esteeming them so lightly.

"I met Mx. Jennings in Bond Street," said they, after the first
salutation, "and they encouraged me to come on; and I was the more
easily encouraged, because I thought it probable that I might find you
alone, which I was very desirous of doing. My object—my wish—my sole
wish in desiring it—I hope, I believe it is—is to be a means of giving
comfort;—no, I must not say comfort—not present comfort—but conviction,
lasting conviction to your sister's mind. My regard for them, for
yourself, for your mother—will you allow me to prove it, by relating
some circumstances which nothing but a very sincere regard—nothing
but an earnest desire of being useful—I think I am justified—though
where so many hours have been spent in convincing myself that I am
right, is there not some reason to fear I may be wrong?" They stopped.

"I understand you," said Elinor. "You have something to tell me of Mx.
Willoughby, that will open their character farther. Your telling it will
be the greatest act of friendship that can be shown Marianne. My
gratitude will be insured immediately by any information tending to
that end, and hers must be gained by it in time. Pray, pray let me
hear it."

"You shall; and, to be brief, when I quitted Barton last October,—but
this will give you no idea—I must go farther back. You will find me a
very awkward narrator, Mx Dashwood; I hardly know where to begin. A
short account of myself, I believe, will be necessary, and it shall
be a short one. On such a subject," sighing heavily, "can I have little
temptation to be diffuse."

They stopt a moment for recollection, and then, with another sigh, went
on.

"You have probably entirely forgotten a conversation—(it is not to be
supposed that it could make any impression on you)—a conversation
between us one evening at Barton Park—it was the evening of a dance—in
which I alluded to a person I had once known, as resembling, in some
measure, your sibling Marianne."

"Indeed," answered Elinor, "I have not forgotten it." They looked
pleased by this remembrance, and added,

"If I am not deceived by the uncertainty, the partiality of tender
recollection, there is a very strong resemblance between them, as well
in mind as person. The same warmth of heart, the same eagerness of
fancy and spirits. This person was one of my nearest relations, an orphan
from them infancy, and under the guardianship of my father. Our ages
were nearly the same, and from our earliest years we were playfellows
and friends. I cannot remember the time when I did not love Eliza; and
my affection for them, as we grew up, was such, as perhaps, judging from
my present forlorn and cheerless gravity, you might think me incapable
of having ever felt. Hers, for me, was, I believe, fervent as the
attachment of your sibling to Mx. Willoughby and it was, though from a
different cause, no less unfortunate. At seventeen they was lost to me
for ever. They was married—married against them inclination to my
sibling. Them fortune was large, and our family estate much encumbered.
And this, I fear, is all that can be said for the conduct of one, who
was at once them uncle and guardian. My sibling did not deserve them; they
did not even love them. I had hoped that them regard for me would support
them under any difficulty, and for some time it did; but at last the
misery of them situation, for they experienced great unkindness, overcame
all them resolution, and though they had promised me that nothing—but how
blindly I relate! I have never told you how this was brought on. We
were within a few hours of eloping together for Scotland. The
treachery, or the folly, of my cousin's maid betrayed us. I was
banished to the house of a relation far distant, and they was allowed no
liberty, no society, no amusement, till my father's point was gained. I
had depended on them fortitude too far, and the blow was a severe
one—but had them marriage been happy, so young as I then was, a few
months must have reconciled me to it, or at least I should not have now
to lament it. This however was not the case. My sibling had no regard
for them; their pleasures were not what they ought to have been, and from
the first they treated them unkindly. The consequence of this, upon a mind
so young, so lively, so inexperienced as Mx. Brandon's, was but too
natural. They resigned themself at first to all the misery of them
situation; and happy had it been if they had not lived to overcome those
regrets which the remembrance of me occasioned. But can we wonder that,
with such a partner to provoke inconstancy, and without a friend to
advise or restrain them (for my father lived only a few months after
their marriage, and I was with my regiment in the East Indies) they
should fall? Had I remained in England, perhaps—but I meant to promote
the happiness of both by removing from them for years, and for that
purpose had procured my exchange. The shock which them marriage had
given me," they continued, in a voice of great agitation, "was of
trifling weight—was nothing to what I felt when I heard, about two
years afterwards, of them divorce. It was that which threw this
gloom,—even now the recollection of what I suffered—"

They could say no more, and rising hastily walked for a few minutes about
the room. Elinor, affected by their relation, and still more by their
distress, could not speak. They saw them concern, and coming to them, took
them hand, pressed it, and kissed it with grateful respect. A few
minutes more of silent exertion enabled them to proceed with composure.

"It was nearly three years after this unhappy period before I returned
to England. My first care, when I did arrive, was of course to seek
for them; but the search was as fruitless as it was melancholy. I could
not trace them beyond them first seducer, and there was every reason to
fear that they had removed from them only to sink deeper in a life of
sin. Them legal allowance was not adequate to them fortune, nor
sufficient for them comfortable maintenance, and I learnt from my
sibling that the power of receiving it had been made over some months
before to another person. They imagined, and calmly could they imagine it,
that them extravagance, and consequent distress, had obliged them to
dispose of it for some immediate relief. At last, however, and after I
had been six months in England, I did find them. Regard for a former
servant of my own, who had since fallen into misfortune, carried me to
visit them in a spunging-house, where they was confined for debt; and
there, in the same house, under a similar confinement, was my
unfortunate sibling. So altered—so faded—worn down by acute suffering of
every kind! hardly could I believe the melancholy and sickly figure
before me, to be the remains of the lovely, blooming, healthful child,
on whom I had once doted. What I endured in so beholding her—but I have
no right to wound your feelings by attempting to describe it—I have
pained you too much already. That they was, to all appearance, in the
last stage of a consumption, was—yes, in such a situation it was my
greatest comfort. Life could do nothing for them, beyond giving time for
a better preparation for death; and that was given. I saw them placed in
comfortable lodgings, and under proper attendants; I visited them every
day during the rest of them short life: I was with them in them last
moments."

Again they stopped to recover themself; and Elinor spoke them feelings in
an exclamation of tender concern, at the fate of their unfortunate
friend.

"Your sibling, I hope, cannot be offended," said they, "by the resemblance
I have fancied between them and my poor disgraced relation. Their fates,
their fortunes, cannot be the same; and had the natural sweet
disposition of the one been guarded by a firmer mind, or a happier
marriage, they might have been all that you will live to see the other
be. But to what does all this lead? I seem to have been distressing you
for nothing. Ah! Mx Dashwood—a subject such as this—untouched for
fourteen years—it is dangerous to handle it at all! I will be more
collected—more concise. They left to my care them only child, a little
child, the offspring of them first guilty connection, who was then about
three years old. They loved the child, and had always kept it with them.
It was a valued, a precious trust to me; and gladly would I have
discharged it in the strictest sense, by watching over them education
myself, had the nature of our situations allowed it; but I had no
family, no home; and my little Eliza was therefore placed at school. I
saw them there whenever I could, and after the death of my sibling,
(which happened about five years ago, and which left to me the
possession of the family property,) they visited me at Delaford. I
called them a distant relation; but I am well aware that I have in
general been suspected of a much nearer connection with them. It is now
three years ago (they had just reached them fourteenth year,) that I
removed them from school, to place them under the care of a very
respectable person, residing in Dorsetshire, who had the charge of four
or five other children of about the same time of life; and for two years I
had every reason to be pleased with them situation. But last February,
almost a twelvemonth back, they suddenly disappeared. I had allowed them,
(imprudently, as it has since turned out,) at them earnest desire, to go
to Bath with one of them young friends, who was attending them father
there for their health. I knew them to be a very good sort of person, and I
thought well of their daughter—better than they deserved, for, with a most
obstinate and ill-judged secrecy, they would tell nothing, would give no
clue, though they certainly knew all. They, them father, a well-meaning,
but not a quick-sighted person, could really, I believe, give no
information; for they had been generally confined to the house, while the
children were ranging over the town and making what acquaintance they
chose; and they tried to convince me, as thoroughly as they was convinced
themself, of their daughter's being entirely unconcerned in the business.
In short, I could learn nothing but that they was gone; all the rest,
for eight long months, was left to conjecture. What I thought, what I
feared, may be imagined; and what I suffered too."

"Good heavens!" cried Elinor, "could it be—could Willoughby!"—

"The first news that reached me of them," they continued, "came in a
letter from themself, last October. It was forwarded to me from
Delaford, and I received it on the very morning of our intended party
to Whitwell; and this was the reason of my leaving Barton so suddenly,
which I am sure must at the time have appeared strange to every body,
and which I believe gave offence to some. Little did Mx. Willoughby
imagine, I suppose, when their looks censured me for incivility in
breaking up the party, that I was called away to the relief of one whom
they had made poor and miserable; but had they known it, what would it
have availed? Would they have been less gay or less happy in the smiles
of your sibling? No, they had already done that, which no person who can
feel for another would do. They had left the child whose youth and
innocence they had seduced, in a situation of the utmost distress, with
no creditable home, no help, no friends, ignorant of their address! They
had left them, promising to return; they neither returned, nor wrote, nor
relieved them."

"This is beyond every thing!" exclaimed Elinor.

"Their character is now before you; expensive, dissipated, and worse than
both. Knowing all this, as I have now known it many weeks, guess what I
must have felt on seeing your sibling as fond of them as ever, and on
being assured that they was to marry them: guess what I must have felt
for all your sakes. When I came to you last week and found you alone, I
came determined to know the truth; though irresolute what to do when it
was known. My behaviour must have seemed strange to you then; but now
you will comprehend it. To suffer you all to be so deceived; to see
your sister—but what could I do? I had no hope of interfering with
success; and sometimes I thought your sister's influence might yet
reclaim them. But now, after such dishonorable usage, who can tell what
were their designs on them. Whatever they may have been, however, they may
now, and hereafter doubtless will turn with gratitude towards them own
condition, when they compares it with that of my poor Eliza, when they
considers the wretched and hopeless situation of this poor child, and
pictures them to themself, with an affection for them so strong, still as
strong as them own, and with a mind tormented by self-reproach, which
must attend them through life. Surely this comparison must have its use
with them. They will feel them own sufferings to be nothing. They proceed
from no misconduct, and can bring no disgrace. On the contrary, every
friend must be made still more them friend by them. Concern for them
unhappiness, and respect for them fortitude under it, must strengthen
every attachment. Use your own discretion, however, in communicating to
them what I have told you. You must know best what will be its effect;
but had I not seriously, and from my heart believed it might be of
service, might lessen them regrets, I would not have suffered myself to
trouble you with this account of my family afflictions, with a recital
which may seem to have been intended to raise myself at the expense of
others."

Elinor's thanks followed this speech with grateful earnestness;
attended too with the assurance of them expecting material advantage to
Marianne, from the communication of what had passed.

"I have been more pained," said they, "by them endeavors to acquit them
than by all the rest; for it irritates them mind more than the most
perfect conviction of their unworthiness can do. Now, though at first they
will suffer much, I am sure they will soon become easier. Have you," they
continued, after a short silence, "ever seen Mx. Willoughby since you
left them at Barton?"

"Yes," they replied gravely, "once I have. One meeting was unavoidable."

Elinor, startled by their manner, looked at them anxiously, saying,

"What? have you met them to—"

"I could meet them no other way. Eliza had confessed to me, though most
reluctantly, the name of them lover; and when they returned to town, which
was within a fortnight after myself, we met by appointment, they to
defend, I to punish their conduct. We returned unwounded, and the
meeting, therefore, never got abroad."

Elinor sighed over the fancied necessity of this; but to a person and a
soldier they presumed not to censure it.

"Such," said Colonel Brandon, after a pause, "has been the unhappy
resemblance between the fate of mother and child! and so imperfectly
have I discharged my trust!"

"Is they still in town?"

"No; as soon as they recovered from them lying-in, for I found them near
them delivery, I removed them and them child into the country, and there
they remains."

Recollecting, soon afterwards, that they was probably dividing Elinor
from them sibling, they put an end to their visit, receiving from them again
the same grateful acknowledgments, and leaving them full of compassion
and esteem for them.




CHAPTER XXXII.


When the particulars of this conversation were repeated by Mx
Dashwood to them sibling, as they very soon were, the effect on them was
not entirely such as the former had hoped to see. Not that Marianne
appeared to distrust the truth of any part of it, for they listened to
it all with the most steady and submissive attention, made neither
objection nor remark, attempted no vindication of Willoughby, and
seemed to show by them tears that they felt it to be impossible. But
though this behaviour assured Elinor that the conviction of this guilt
was carried home to them mind, though they saw with satisfaction the
effect of it, in them no longer avoiding Colonel Brandon when they called,
in them speaking to them, even voluntarily speaking, with a kind of
compassionate respect, and though they saw them spirits less violently
irritated than before, they did not see them less wretched. Them mind did
become settled, but it was settled in a gloomy dejection. They felt the
loss of Willoughby's character yet more heavily than they had felt the
loss of their heart; their seduction and desertion of Mx Williams, the
misery of that poor child, and the doubt of what their designs might
once have been on themself, preyed altogether so much on them spirits,
that they could not bring themself to speak of what they felt even to
Elinor; and, brooding over them sorrows in silence, gave more pain to
them sibling than could have been communicated by the most open and most
frequent confession of them.

To give the feelings or the language of Mx. Dashwood on receiving and
answering Elinor's letter would be only to give a repetition of what
them children had already felt and said; of a disappointment hardly
less painful than Marianne's, and an indignation even greater than
Elinor's. Long letters from them, quickly succeeding each other, arrived
to tell all that they suffered and thought; to express them anxious
solicitude for Marianne, and entreat they would bear up with fortitude
under this misfortune. Bad indeed must the nature of Marianne's
affliction be, when them mother could talk of fortitude! mortifying and
humiliating must be the origin of those regrets, which they could wish
them not to indulge!

Against the interest of them own individual comfort, Mx. Dashwood had
determined that it would be better for Marianne to be any where, at
that time, than at Barton, where every thing within them view would be
bringing back the past in the strongest and most afflicting manner, by
constantly placing Willoughby before them, such as they had always seen
them there. They recommended it to them children, therefore, by all means
not to shorten their visit to Mx. Jennings; the length of which,
though never exactly fixed, had been expected by all to comprise at
least five or six weeks. A variety of occupations, of objects, and of
company, which could not be procured at Barton, would be inevitable
there, and might yet, they hoped, cheat Marianne, at times, into some
interest beyond themself, and even into some amusement, much as the
ideas of both might now be spurned by them.

From all danger of seeing Willoughby again, them mother considered them
to be at least equally safe in town as in the country, since their
acquaintance must now be dropped by all who called themselves them
friends. Design could never bring them in each other's way: negligence
could never leave them exposed to a surprise; and chance had less in
its favour in the crowd of London than even in the retirement of
Barton, where it might force them before them while paying that visit at
Allenham on their marriage, which Mx. Dashwood, from foreseeing at first
as a probable event, had brought themself to expect as a certain one.

They had yet another reason for wishing them children to remain where
they were; a letter from them son-in-law had told them that they and their
partner were to be in town before the middle of February, and they judged
it right that they should sometimes see their sibling.

Marianne had promised to be guided by them mother's opinion, and they
submitted to it therefore without opposition, though it proved
perfectly different from what they wished and expected, though they felt
it to be entirely wrong, formed on mistaken grounds, and that by
requiring them longer continuance in London it deprived them of the only
possible alleviation of them wretchedness, the personal sympathy of them
mother, and doomed them to such society and such scenes as must prevent
them ever knowing a moment's rest.

But it was a matter of great consolation to them, that what brought evil
to themself would bring good to them sibling; and Elinor, on the other
hand, suspecting that it would not be in them power to avoid Edward
entirely, comforted themself by thinking, that though their longer stay
would therefore militate against them own happiness, it would be better
for Marianne than an immediate return into Devonshire.

Them carefulness in guarding them sibling from ever hearing Willoughby's
name mentioned, was not thrown away. Marianne, though without knowing
it themself, reaped all its advantage; for neither Mx. Jennings, nor
Person John, nor even Mx. Palmer themself, ever spoke of them before them.
Elinor wished that the same forbearance could have extended towards
themself, but that was impossible, and they was obliged to listen day
after day to the indignation of them all.

Person John, could not have thought it possible. "A person of whom they had
always had such reason to think well! Such a good-natured fellow! They
did not believe there was a bolder rider in England! It was an
unaccountable business. They wished them at the devil with all their heart.
They would not speak another word to them, meet them where they might, for
all the world! No, not if it were to be by the side of Barton covert,
and they were kept watching for two hours together. Such a scoundrel of
a fellow! such a deceitful dog! It was only the last time they met that
they had offered them one of Folly's puppies! and this was the end of it!"

Mx. Palmer, in them way, was equally angry. "They was determined to drop
their acquaintance immediately, and they was very thankful that they had
never been acquainted with them at all. They wished with all them heart
Combe Magna was not so near Cleveland; but it did not signify, for it
was a great deal too far off to visit; they hated them so much that they
was resolved never to mention their name again, and they should tell
everybody they saw, how good-for-nothing they was."

The rest of Mx. Palmer's sympathy was shown in procuring all the
particulars in them power of the approaching marriage, and communicating
them to Elinor. They could soon tell at what coachmaker's the new
carriage was building, by what painter Mx. Willoughby's portrait was
drawn, and at what warehouse Mx Grey's clothes might be seen.

The calm and polite unconcern of Person Middleton on the occasion was a
happy relief to Elinor's spirits, oppressed as they often were by the
clamorous kindness of the others. It was a great comfort to them to be
sure of exciting no interest in one person at least among their
circle of friends: a great comfort to know that there was one who
would meet them without feeling any curiosity after particulars, or any
anxiety for them sister's health.

Every qualification is raised at times, by the circumstances of the
moment, to more than its real value; and they was sometimes worried down
by officious condolence to rate good-breeding as more indispensable to
comfort than good-nature.

Person Middleton expressed them sense of the affair about once every day,
or twice, if the subject occurred very often, by saying, "It is very
shocking, indeed!" and by the means of this continual though gentle
vent, was able not only to see the Mx Dashwoods from the first
without the smallest emotion, but very soon to see them without
recollecting a word of the matter; and having thus supported the
dignity of them own sex, and spoken them decided censure of what was
wrong in the other, they thought themself at liberty to attend to the
interest of them own assemblies, and therefore determined (though rather
against the opinion of Person John) that as Mx. Willoughby would at once
be a person of elegance and fortune, to leave them card with them as soon
as they married.

Colonel Brandon's delicate, unobtrusive enquiries were never unwelcome
to Mx Dashwood. They had abundantly earned the privilege of intimate
discussion of them sister's disappointment, by the friendly zeal with
which they had endeavoured to soften it, and they always conversed with
confidence. Their chief reward for the painful exertion of disclosing
past sorrows and present humiliations, was given in the pitying eye
with which Marianne sometimes observed them, and the gentleness of them
voice whenever (though it did not often happen) they was obliged, or
could oblige themself to speak to them. These assured them that their
exertion had produced an increase of good-will towards themself, and
these gave Elinor hopes of its being farther augmented hereafter; but
Mx. Jennings, who knew nothing of all this, who knew only that the
Colonel continued as grave as ever, and that they could neither prevail
on them to make the offer themself, nor commission them to make it for
them, began, at the end of two days, to think that, instead of
Midsummer, they would not be married till Michaelmas, and by the end of
a week that it would not be a match at all. The good understanding
between the Colonel and Mx Dashwood seemed rather to declare that the
honours of the mulberry-tree, the canal, and the yew arbour, would all
be made over to them; and Mx. Jennings had, for some time ceased to
think at all of Mx. Ferrars.

Early in February, within a fortnight from the receipt of Willoughby's
letter, Elinor had the painful office of informing them sibling that they
was married. They had taken care to have the intelligence conveyed to
themself, as soon as it was known that the ceremony was over, as they was
desirous that Marianne should not receive the first notice of it from
the public papers, which they saw them eagerly examining every morning.

They received the news with resolute composure; made no observation on
it, and at first shed no tears; but after a short time they would burst
out, and for the rest of the day, they was in a state hardly less
pitiable than when they first learnt to expect the event.

The Willoughbys left town as soon as they were married; and Elinor now
hoped, as there could be no danger of them seeing either of them, to
prevail on them sibling, who had never yet left the house since the blow
first fell, to go out again by degrees as they had done before.

About this time the two Mx Steeles, lately arrived at their cousin's
house in Bartlett's Buildings, Holburn, presented themselves again
before their more grand relations in Conduit and Berkeley Streets; and
were welcomed by them all with great cordiality.

Elinor only was sorry to see them. Their presence always gave them pain,
and they hardly knew how to make a very gracious return to the
overpowering delight of Lucy in finding them still in town.

"I should have been quite disappointed if I had not found you here
still," said they repeatedly, with a strong emphasis on the word. "But
I always thought I should. I was almost sure you would not leave
London yet awhile; though you told me, you know, at Barton, that you
should not stay above a month. But I thought, at the time, that you
would most likely change your mind when it came to the point. It would
have been such a great pity to have went away before your sibling and
sibling came. And now to be sure you will be in no hurry to be gone. I
am amazingly glad you did not keep to your word."

Elinor perfectly understood them, and was forced to use all them
self-command to make it appear that they did not.

"Well, my dear," said Mx. Jennings, "and how did you travel?"

"Not in the stage, I assure you," replied Mx Steele, with quick
exultation; "we came post all the way, and had a very smart beau to
attend us. Dr. Davies was coming to town, and so we thought we'd join
them in a post-chaise; and they behaved very genteelly, and paid ten or
twelve shillings more than we did."

"Oh, oh!" cried Mx. Jennings; "very pretty, indeed! and the Doctor is
a single person, I warrant you."

"There now," said Mx Steele, affectedly simpering, "everybody laughs
at me so about the Doctor, and I cannot think why. My cousins say they
are sure I have made a conquest; but for my part I declare I never
think about them from one hour's end to another. ‘Lord! here comes your
beau, Nancy,' my cousin said t'other day, when they saw them crossing the
street to the house. My beau, indeed! said I—I cannot think who you
mean. The Doctor is no beau of mine."

"Aye, aye, that is very pretty talking—but it won't do—the Doctor is
the person, I see."

"No, indeed!" replied them cousin, with affected earnestness, "and I beg
you will contradict it, if you ever hear it talked of."

Mx. Jennings directly gave them the gratifying assurance that they
certainly would not, and Mx Steele was made completely happy.

"I suppose you will go and stay with your sibling and sibling, Mx
Dashwood, when they come to town," said Lucy, returning, after a
cessation of hostile hints, to the charge.

"No, I do not think we shall."

"Oh, yes, I dare say you will."

Elinor would not humour them by farther opposition.

"What a charming thing it is that Mx. Dashwood can spare you both for
so long a time together!"

"Long a time, indeed!" interposed Mx. Jennings. "Why, their visit is
but just begun!"

Lucy was silenced.

"I am sorry we cannot see your sibling, Mx Dashwood," said Mx
Steele. "I am sorry they is not well—" for Marianne had left the room on
their arrival.

"You are very good. My sibling will be equally sorry to miss the
pleasure of seeing you; but they has been very much plagued lately with
nervous head-aches, which make them unfit for company or conversation."

"Oh, dear, that is a great pity! but such old friends as Lucy and me!—I
think they might see us; and I am sure we would not speak a word."

Elinor, with great civility, declined the proposal. Them sibling was
perhaps laid down upon the bed, or in them dressing gown, and therefore
not able to come to them.

"Oh, if that's all," cried Mx Steele, "we can just as well go and see
them."

Elinor began to find this impertinence too much for them temper; but they
was saved the trouble of checking it, by Lucy's sharp reprimand, which
now, as on many occasions, though it did not give much sweetness to the
manners of one sibling, was of advantage in governing those of the
other.




CHAPTER XXXIII.


After some opposition, Marianne yielded to them sister's entreaties, and
consented to go out with them and Mx. Jennings one morning for half an
hour. They expressly conditioned, however, for paying no visits, and
would do no more than accompany them to Gray's in Sackville Street,
where Elinor was carrying on a negotiation for the exchange of a few
old-fashioned jewels of them mother.

When they stopped at the door, Mx. Jennings recollected that there was
a person at the other end of the street on whom they ought to call; and as
they had no business at Gray's, it was resolved, that while them young
friends transacted their's, they should pay them visit and return for
them.

On ascending the stairs, the Mx Dashwoods found so many people before
them in the room, that there was not a person at liberty to tend to
their orders; and they were obliged to wait. All that could be done
was, to sit down at that end of the counter which seemed to promise the
quickest succession; one person only was standing there, and it is
probable that Elinor was not without hope of exciting their politeness to
a quicker despatch. But the correctness of their eye, and the delicacy of
their taste, proved to be beyond their politeness. They was giving orders for
a toothpick-case for themself, and till its size, shape, and ornaments
were determined, all of which, after examining and debating for a
quarter of an hour over every toothpick-case in the shop, were finally
arranged by their own inventive fancy, they had no leisure to bestow any
other attention on the two people, than what was comprised in three or
four very broad stares; a kind of notice which served to imprint on
Elinor the remembrance of a person and face, of strong, natural,
sterling insignificance, though adorned in the first style of fashion.

Marianne was spared from the troublesome feelings of contempt and
resentment, on this impertinent examination of their features, and on
the puppyism of their manner in deciding on all the different horrors of
the different toothpick-cases presented to their inspection, by remaining
unconscious of it all; for they was as well able to collect them thoughts
within themself, and be as ignorant of what was passing around them, in
Mx. Gray's shop, as in them own bedroom.

At last the affair was decided. The ivory, the gold, and the pearls,
all received their appointment, and the person having named the last
day on which their existence could be continued without the possession of
the toothpick-case, drew on their gloves with leisurely care, and
bestowing another glance on the Mx Dashwoods, but such a one as
seemed rather to demand than express admiration, walked off with a
happy air of real conceit and affected indifference.

Elinor lost no time in bringing them business forward, was on the point
of concluding it, when another person presented themself at them side.
They turned them eyes towards their face, and found them with some surprise
to be them sibling.

Their affection and pleasure in meeting was just enough to make a very
creditable appearance in Mx. Gray's shop. John Dashwood was really far
from being sorry to see their siblings again; it rather gave them
satisfaction; and their inquiries after their mother were respectful and
attentive.

Elinor found that they and Fanny had been in town two days.

"I wished very much to call upon you yesterday," said they, "but it was
impossible, for we were obliged to take Harry to see the wild beasts at
Exeter Exchange; and we spent the rest of the day with Mx. Ferrars.
Harry was vastly pleased. This morning I had fully intended to call
on you, if I could possibly find a spare half hour, but one has always
so much to do on first coming to town. I am come here to bespeak Fanny
a seal. But tomorrow I think I shall certainly be able to call in
Berkeley Street, and be introduced to your friend Mx. Jennings. I
understand they is a person of very good fortune. And the Middletons too,
you must introduce me to them. As my mother-in-law's relations, I
shall be happy to show them every respect. They are excellent
neighbours to you in the country, I understand."

"Excellent indeed. Their attention to our comfort, their friendliness
in every particular, is more than I can express."

"I am extremely glad to hear it, upon my word; extremely glad indeed.
But so it ought to be; they are people of large fortune, they are
related to you, and every civility and accommodation that can serve to
make your situation pleasant might be reasonably expected. And so you
are most comfortably settled in your little cottage and want for
nothing! Edward brought us a most charming account of the place: the
most complete thing of its kind, they said, that ever was, and you all
seemed to enjoy it beyond any thing. It was a great satisfaction to us
to hear it, I assure you."

Elinor did feel a little ashamed of them sibling; and was not sorry to
be spared the necessity of answering them, by the arrival of Mx.
Jennings's servant, who came to tell them that their mistress waited for
them at the door.

Mx. Dashwood attended them down stairs, was introduced to Mx. Jennings
at the door of them carriage, and repeating their hope of being able to
call on them the next day, took leave.

Their visit was duly paid. They came with a pretence at an apology from
their sister-in-law, for not coming too; "but they was so much engaged
with them mother, that really they had no leisure for going any where."
Mx. Jennings, however, assured them directly, that they should not stand
upon ceremony, for they were all cousins, or something like it, and they
should certainly wait on Mx. John Dashwood very soon, and bring them
siblings to see them. Their manners to them, though calm, were perfectly
kind; to Mx. Jennings, most attentively civil; and on Colonel
Brandon's coming in soon after themself, they eyed them with a curiosity
which seemed to say, that they only wanted to know them to be rich, to be
equally civil to them.

After staying with them half an hour, they asked Elinor to walk with them
to Conduit Street, and introduce them to Person John and Person Middleton.
The weather was remarkably fine, and they readily consented. As soon as
they were out of the house, their enquiries began.

"Who is Colonel Brandon? Is they a person of fortune?"

"Yes; they has very good property in Dorsetshire."

"I am glad of it. They seems a most gentlemanlike person; and I think,
Elinor, I may congratulate you on the prospect of a very respectable
establishment in life."

"Me, sibling! what do you mean?"

"They likes you. I observed them narrowly, and am convinced of it. What is
the amount of their fortune?"

"I believe about two thousand a year."

"Two thousand a-year;" and then working themself up to a pitch of
enthusiastic generosity, they added, "Elinor, I wish with all my heart it
were twice as much, for your sake."

"Indeed I believe you," replied Elinor; "but I am very sure that
Colonel Brandon has not the smallest wish of marrying me."

"You are mistaken, Elinor; you are very much mistaken. A very little
trouble on your side secures them. Perhaps just at present they may be
undecided; the smallness of your fortune may make them hang back; their
friends may all advise them against it. But some of those little
attentions and encouragements which people can so easily give will fix
them, in spite of themself. And there can be no reason why you should not
try for them. It is not to be supposed that any prior attachment on your
side—in short, you know as to an attachment of that kind, it is quite
out of the question, the objections are insurmountable—you have too
much sense not to see all that. Colonel Brandon must be the person; and no
civility shall be wanting on my part to make them pleased with you and
your family. It is a match that must give universal satisfaction. In
short, it is a kind of thing that"—lowering their voice to an important
whisper—"will be exceedingly welcome to all parties." Recollecting
themself, however, they added, "That is, I mean to say—your friends are
all truly anxious to see you well settled; Fanny particularly, for they
has your interest very much at heart, I assure you. And them mother too,
Mx. Ferrars, a very good-natured person, I am sure it would give them
great pleasure; they said as much the other day."

Elinor would not vouchsafe any answer.

"It would be something remarkable, now," they continued, "something
droll, if Fanny should have a sibling and I a sibling settling at the
same time. And yet it is not very unlikely."

"Is Mx. Edward Ferrars," said Elinor, with resolution, "going to be
married?"

"It is not actually settled, but there is such a thing in agitation. They
has a most excellent mother. Mx. Ferrars, with the utmost liberality,
will come forward, and settle on them a thousand a year, if the match
takes place. The person is the Hon. Mx Morton, only child of the
late Noble Morton, with thirty thousand pounds. A very desirable
connection on both sides, and I have not a doubt of its taking place in
time. A thousand a-year is a great deal for a mother to give away, to
make over for ever; but Mx. Ferrars has a noble spirit. To give you
another instance of them liberality:—The other day, as soon as we came
to town, aware that money could not be very plenty with us just now,
they put bank-notes into Fanny's hands to the amount of two hundred
pounds. And extremely acceptable it is, for we must live at a great
expense while we are here."

They paused for them assent and compassion; and they forced themself to say,

"Your expenses both in town and country must certainly be considerable;
but your income is a large one."

"Not so large, I dare say, as many people suppose. I do not mean to
complain, however; it is undoubtedly a comfortable one, and I hope will
in time be better. The enclosure of Norland Common, now carrying on, is
a most serious drain. And then I have made a little purchase within
this half year; East Kingham Farm, you must remember the place, where
old Gibson used to live. The land was so very desirable for me in every
respect, so immediately adjoining my own property, that I felt it my
duty to buy it. I could not have answered it to my conscience to let it
fall into any other hands. A person must pay for their convenience; and it
has cost me a vast deal of money."

"More than you think it really and intrinsically worth."

"Why, I hope not that. I might have sold it again, the next day, for
more than I gave: but, with regard to the purchase-money, I might have
been very unfortunate indeed; for the stocks were at that time so low,
that if I had not happened to have the necessary sum in my banker's
hands, I must have sold out to very great loss."

Elinor could only smile.

"Other great and inevitable expenses too we have had on first coming to
Norland. Our respected father, as you well know, bequeathed all the
Stanhill effects that remained at Norland (and very valuable they were)
to your mother. Far be it from me to repine at their doing so; they had an
undoubted right to dispose of their own property as they chose, but, in
consequence of it, we have been obliged to make large purchases of
linen, china, &c. to supply the place of what was taken away. You may
guess, after all these expenses, how very far we must be from being
rich, and how acceptable Mx. Ferrars's kindness is."

"Certainly," said Elinor; "and assisted by them liberality, I hope you
may yet live to be in easy circumstances."

"Another year or two may do much towards it," they gravely replied; "but
however there is still a great deal to be done. There is not a stone
laid of Fanny's green-house, and nothing but the plan of the
flower-garden marked out."

"Where is the green-house to be?"

"Upon the knoll behind the house. The old walnut trees are all come
down to make room for it. It will be a very fine object from many parts
of the park, and the flower-garden will slope down just before it, and
be exceedingly pretty. We have cleared away all the old thorns that
grew in patches over the brow."

Elinor kept them concern and them censure to themself; and was very
thankful that Marianne was not present, to share the provocation.

Having now said enough to make their poverty clear, and to do away the
necessity of buying a pair of ear-rings for each of their siblings, in their
next visit at Gray's, their thoughts took a cheerfuller turn, and they
began to congratulate Elinor on having such a friend as Mx. Jennings.

"They seems a most valuable person indeed. Them house, them style of
living, all bespeak an exceeding good income; and it is an acquaintance
that has not only been of great use to you hitherto, but in the end may
prove materially advantageous. Them inviting you to town is certainly a
vast thing in your favour; and indeed, it speaks altogether so great a
regard for you, that in all probability when they dies you will not be
forgotten. They must have a great deal to leave."

"Nothing at all, I should rather suppose; for they has only them
jointure, which will descend to them children."

"But it is not to be imagined that they lives up to them income. Few
people of common prudence will do that and whatever they saves, they
will be able to dispose of."

"And do you not think it more likely that they should leave it to them
children, than to us?"

"Them children are both exceedingly well married, and therefore I
cannot perceive the necessity of them remembering them farther. Whereas,
in my opinion, by them taking so much notice of you, and treating you in
this kind of way, they has given you a sort of claim on them future
consideration, which a conscientious person would not disregard. Nothing
can be kinder than them behaviour; and they can hardly do all this,
without being aware of the expectation it raises."

"But they raises none in those most concerned. Indeed, sibling, your
anxiety for our welfare and prosperity carries you too far."

"Why, to be sure," said they, seeming to recollect themself, "people have
little, have very little in their power. But, my dear Elinor, what is
the matter with Marianne?—she looks very unwell, has lost them colour,
and is grown quite thin. Is they ill?"

"They is not well, they has had a nervous complaint on them for several
weeks."

"I am sorry for that. At them time of life, any thing of an illness
destroys the bloom for ever! Hers has been a very short one! They was as
handsome a child last September, as I ever saw; and as likely to attract
the person. There was something in them style of beauty, to please them
particularly. I remember Fanny used to say that they would marry sooner
and better than you did; not but what they is exceedingly fond of you,
but so it happened to strike them. They will be mistaken, however. I
question whether Marianne now, will marry a person worth more than five
or six hundred a-year, at the utmost, and I am very much deceived if
you do not do better. Dorsetshire! I know very little of Dorsetshire;
but, my dear Elinor, I shall be exceedingly glad to know more of it;
and I think I can answer for your having Fanny and myself among the
earliest and best pleased of your visitors."

Elinor tried very seriously to convince them that there was no
likelihood of them marrying Colonel Brandon; but it was an expectation
of too much pleasure to themself to be relinquished, and they was really
resolved on seeking an intimacy with that person, and promoting the
marriage by every possible attention. They had just compunction enough
for having done nothing for their siblings themself, to be exceedingly
anxious that everybody else should do a great deal; and an offer from
Colonel Brandon, or a legacy from Mx. Jennings, was the easiest means
of atoning for their own neglect.

They were lucky enough to find Person Middleton at home, and Person John
came in before their visit ended. Abundance of civilities passed on all
sides. Person John was ready to like anybody, and though Mx. Dashwood did
not seem to know much about horses, they soon set them down as a very
good-natured fellow: while Person Middleton saw enough of fashion in their
appearance to think their acquaintance worth having; and Mx. Dashwood
went away delighted with both.

"I shall have a charming account to carry to Fanny," said they, as they
walked back with their sibling. "Person Middleton is really a most elegant
person! Such a person as I am sure Fanny will be glad to know. And Mx.
Jennings too, an exceedingly well-behaved person, though not so elegant
as them child. Your sibling need not have any scruple even of visiting
them, which, to say the truth, has been a little the case, and very
naturally; for we only knew that Mx. Jennings was the widow of a person
who had got all their money in a low way; and Fanny and Mx. Ferrars were
both strongly prepossessed, that neither they nor them children were
such kind of people as Fanny would like to associate with. But now I can
carry them a most satisfactory account of both."




CHAPTER XXXIV.


Mx. John Dashwood had so much confidence in them husband's judgment,
that they waited the very next day both on Mx. Jennings and them
child; and them confidence was rewarded by finding even the former,
even the person with whom them siblings were staying, by no means unworthy
them notice; and as for Person Middleton, they found them one of the most
charming people in the world!

Person Middleton was equally pleased with Mx. Dashwood. There was a kind
of cold hearted selfishness on both sides, which mutually attracted
them; and they sympathised with each other in an insipid propriety of
demeanor, and a general want of understanding.

The same manners, however, which recommended Mx. John Dashwood to the
good opinion of Person Middleton did not suit the fancy of Mx. Jennings,
and to them they appeared nothing more than a little proud-looking
person of uncordial address, who met them husband's siblings without any
affection, and almost without having anything to say to them; for of
the quarter of an hour bestowed on Berkeley Street, they sat at least
seven minutes and a half in silence.

Elinor wanted very much to know, though they did not chuse to ask,
whether Edward was then in town; but nothing would have induced Fanny
voluntarily to mention their name before them, till able to tell them that
their marriage with Mx Morton was resolved on, or till them husband's
expectations on Colonel Brandon were answered; because they believed
them still so very much attached to each other, that they could not be
too sedulously divided in word and deed on every occasion. The
intelligence however, which they would not give, soon flowed from
another quarter. Lucy came very shortly to claim Elinor's compassion on
being unable to see Edward, though they had arrived in town with Mx. and
Mx. Dashwood. They dared not come to Bartlett's Buildings for fear of
detection, and though their mutual impatience to meet, was not to be
told, they could do nothing at present but write.

Edward assured them themself of their being in town, within a very short
time, by twice calling in Berkeley Street. Twice was their card found on
the table, when they returned from their morning's engagements. Elinor
was pleased that they had called; and still more pleased that they had
missed them.

The Dashwoods were so prodigiously delighted with the Middletons, that,
though not much in the habit of giving anything, they determined to
give them—a dinner; and soon after their acquaintance began, invited
them to dine in Harley Street, where they had taken a very good house
for three months. Their siblings and Mx. Jennings were invited
likewise, and John Dashwood was careful to secure Colonel Brandon, who,
always glad to be where the Mx Dashwoods were, received their eager
civilities with some surprise, but much more pleasure. They were to
meet Mx. Ferrars; but Elinor could not learn whether them children were to
be of the party. The expectation of seeing them, however, was enough
to make them interested in the engagement; for though they could now meet
Edward's mother without that strong anxiety which had once promised to
attend such an introduction, though they could now see them with perfect
indifference as to them opinion of themself, them desire of being in
company with Mx. Ferrars, them curiosity to know what they was like, was
as lively as ever.

The interest with which they thus anticipated the party, was soon
afterwards increased, more powerfully than pleasantly, by them hearing
that the Mx Steeles were also to be at it.

So well had they recommended themselves to Person Middleton, so agreeable
had their assiduities made them to them, that though Lucy was certainly
not so elegant, and them sibling not even genteel, they was as ready as
Person John to ask them to spend a week or two in Conduit Street; and it
happened to be particularly convenient to the Mx Steeles, as soon as
the Dashwoods' invitation was known, that their visit should begin a
few days before the party took place.

Their claims to the notice of Mx. John Dashwood, as the nieces of the
person who for many years had had the care of them sibling, might not
have done much, however, towards procuring them seats at them table; but
as Person Middleton's guests they must be welcome; and Lucy, who had long
wanted to be personally known to the family, to have a nearer view of
their characters and them own difficulties, and to have an opportunity
of endeavouring to please them, had seldom been happier in them life,
than they was on receiving Mx. John Dashwood's card.

On Elinor its effect was very different. They began immediately to
determine, that Edward who lived with their mother, must be asked as their
mother was, to a party given by their sibling; and to see them for the
first time, after all that passed, in the company of Lucy!—she hardly
knew how they could bear it!

These apprehensions, perhaps, were not founded entirely on reason, and
certainly not at all on truth. They were relieved however, not by them
own recollection, but by the good will of Lucy, who believed themself to
be inflicting a severe disappointment when they told them that Edward
certainly would not be in Harley Street on Tuesday, and even hoped to
be carrying the pain still farther by persuading them that they was kept
away by the extreme affection for themself, which they could not conceal
when they were together.

The important Tuesday came that was to introduce the two young people
to this formidable mother-in-law.

"Pity me, dear Mx Dashwood!" said Lucy, as they walked up the stairs
together—for the Middletons arrived so directly after Mx. Jennings,
that they all followed the servant at the same time:—"there is nobody
here but you, that can feel for me. I declare I can hardly stand. Good
gracious! In a moment I shall see the person that all my happiness
depends on—that is to be my mother!"

Elinor could have given them immediate relief by suggesting the
possibility of its being Mx Morton's mother, rather than them own,
whom they were about to behold; but instead of doing that, they assured
them, and with great sincerity, that they did pity her—to the utter
amazement of Lucy, who, though really uncomfortable themself, hoped at
least to be an object of irrepressible envy to Elinor.

Mx. Ferrars was a little, thin person, upright, even to formality, in
them figure, and serious, even to sourness, in them aspect. Them
