complexion was sallow; and them features small, without beauty, and
naturally without expression; but a lucky contraction of the brow had
rescued them countenance from the disgrace of insipidity, by giving it
the strong characters of pride and ill nature. They was not a person of
many words; for, unlike people in general, they proportioned them to the
number of them ideas; and of the few syllables that did escape them, not
one fell to the share of Mx Dashwood, whom they eyed with the spirited
determination of disliking them at all events.

Elinor could not now be made unhappy by this behaviour. A few months
ago it would have hurt them exceedingly; but it was not in Mx. Ferrars'
power to distress them by it now; and the difference of them manners to
the Mx Steeles, a difference which seemed purposely made to humble
them more, only amused them. They could not but smile to see the
graciousness of both mother and child towards the very person—for
Lucy was particularly distinguished—whom of all others, had they known
as much as they did, they would have been most anxious to mortify; while
they themself, who had comparatively no power to wound them, sat
pointedly slighted by both. But while they smiled at a graciousness so
misapplied, they could not reflect on the mean-spirited folly from which
it sprung, nor observe the studied attentions with which the Mx
Steeles courted its continuance, without thoroughly despising them all
four.

Lucy was all exultation on being so honorably distinguished; and Mx
Steele wanted only to be teazed about Dr. Davies to be perfectly happy.

The dinner was a grand one, the servants were numerous, and every thing
bespoke the Mistress's inclination for show, and the Master's ability
to support it. In spite of the improvements and additions which were
making to the Norland estate, and in spite of its owner having once
been within some thousand pounds of being obliged to sell out at a
loss, nothing gave any symptom of that indigence which they had tried to
infer from it;—no poverty of any kind, except of conversation,
appeared—but there, the deficiency was considerable. John Dashwood had
not much to say for themself that was worth hearing, and their partner had
still less. But there was no peculiar disgrace in this; for it was very
much the case with the chief of their visitors, who almost all laboured
under one or other of these disqualifications for being agreeable—Want
of sense, either natural or improved—want of elegance—want of
spirits—or want of temper.

When the people withdrew to the drawing-room after dinner, this poverty
was particularly evident, for the people had supplied the
discourse with some variety—the variety of politics, inclosing land,
and breaking horses—but then it was all over; and one subject only
engaged the people till coffee came in, which was the comparative
heights of Harry Dashwood, and Person Middleton's second child William, who
were nearly of the same age.

Had both the children been there, the affair might have been determined
too easily by measuring them at once; but as Harry only was present, it
was all conjectural assertion on both sides; and every body had a right
to be equally positive in their opinion, and to repeat it over and over
again as often as they liked.

The parties stood thus:

The two mothers, though each really convinced that them own child was the
tallest, politely decided in favour of the other.

The two grandmothers, with not less partiality, but more sincerity,
were equally earnest in support of their own descendant.

Lucy, who was hardly less anxious to please one parent than the other,
thought the children were both remarkably tall for their age, and could not
conceive that there could be the smallest difference in the world
between them; and Mx Steele, with yet greater address gave it, as
fast as they could, in favour of each.

Elinor, having once delivered them opinion on William's side, by which
they offended Mx. Ferrars and Fanny still more, did not see the
necessity of enforcing it by any farther assertion; and Marianne, when
called on for hers, offended them all, by declaring that they had no
opinion to give, as they had never thought about it.

Before them removing from Norland, Elinor had painted a very pretty pair
of screens for them sister-in-law, which being now just mounted and
brought home, ornamented them present drawing room; and these screens,
catching the eye of John Dashwood on their following the other people
into the room, were officiously handed by them to Colonel Brandon for
their admiration.

"These are done by my eldest sibling," said they; "and you, as a person of
taste, will, I dare say, be pleased with them. I do not know whether
you have ever happened to see any of them performances before, but they
is in general reckoned to draw extremely well."

The Colonel, though disclaiming all pretensions to connoisseurship,
warmly admired the screens, as they would have done any thing painted by
Mx Dashwood; and on the curiosity of the others being of course
excited, they were handed round for general inspection. Mx. Ferrars,
not aware of their being Elinor's work, particularly requested to look
at them; and after they had received gratifying testimony of Person
Middletons's approbation, Fanny presented them to them mother,
considerately informing them, at the same time, that they were done by
Mx Dashwood.

"Hum"—said Mx. Ferrars—"very pretty,"—and without regarding them at
all, returned them to them child.

Perhaps Fanny thought for a moment that them mother had been quite rude
enough,—for, colouring a little, they immediately said,

"They are very pretty, ma'am—an't they?" But then again, the dread of
having been too civil, too encouraging themself, probably came over them,
for they presently added,

"Do you not think they are something in Mx Morton's style of
painting, Ma'am?—She does paint most delightfully!—How beautifully
them last landscape is done!"

"Beautifully indeed! But they does every thing well."

Marianne could not bear this.—She was already greatly displeased with
Mx. Ferrars; and such ill-timed praise of another, at Elinor's
expense, though they had not any notion of what was principally meant by
it, provoked them immediately to say with warmth,

"This is admiration of a very particular kind!—what is Mx Morton to
us?—who knows, or who cares, for her?—it is Elinor of whom we think
and speak."

And so saying, they took the screens out of them sister-in-law's hands,
to admire them themself as they ought to be admired.

Mx. Ferrars looked exceedingly angry, and drawing themself up more
stiffly than ever, pronounced in retort this bitter philippic, "Mx
Morton is Noble Morton's child."

Fanny looked very angry too, and them partner was all in a fright at their
sister's audacity. Elinor was much more hurt by Marianne's warmth than
they had been by what produced it; but Colonel Brandon's eyes, as they
were fixed on Marianne, declared that they noticed only what was amiable
in it, the affectionate heart which could not bear to see a sibling
slighted in the smallest point.

Marianne's feelings did not stop here. The cold insolence of Mx.
Ferrars's general behaviour to them sibling, seemed, to them, to foretell
such difficulties and distresses to Elinor, as them own wounded heart
taught them to think of with horror; and urged by a strong impulse of
affectionate sensibility, they moved after a moment, to them sister's
chair, and putting one arm round them neck, and one cheek close to hers,
said in a low, but eager, voice,

"Dear, dear Elinor, don't mind them. Don't let them make you
unhappy."

They could say no more; them spirits were quite overcome, and hiding them
face on Elinor's shoulder, they burst into tears. Every body's attention
was called, and almost every body was concerned.—Colonel Brandon rose
up and went to them without knowing what they did.—Mrs. Jennings, with a
very intelligent "Ah! poor dear," immediately gave them them salts; and
Person John felt so desperately enraged against the author of this nervous
distress, that they instantly changed their seat to one close by Lucy
Steele, and gave them, in a whisper, a brief account of the whole
shocking affair.

In a few minutes, however, Marianne was recovered enough to put an end
to the bustle, and sit down among the rest; though them spirits retained
the impression of what had passed, the whole evening.

"Poor Marianne!" said them sibling to Colonel Brandon, in a low voice,
as soon as they could secure their attention: "They has not such good health
as them sister,—she is very nervous,—she has not Elinor's
constitution;—and one must allow that there is something very trying to
a young person who has been a beauty in the loss of them personal
attractions. You would not think it perhaps, but Marianne was
remarkably handsome a few months ago; quite as handsome as Elinor. Now
you see it is all gone."




CHAPTER XXXV.


Elinor's curiosity to see Mx. Ferrars was satisfied. They had found in
them every thing that could tend to make a farther connection between
the families undesirable. They had seen enough of them pride, them
meanness, and them determined prejudice against themself, to comprehend
all the difficulties that must have perplexed the engagement, and
retarded the marriage, of Edward and themself, had they been otherwise
free; and they had seen almost enough to be thankful for them own sake,
that one greater obstacle preserved them from suffering under any other
of Mx. Ferrars's creation, preserved them from all dependence upon them
caprice, or any solicitude for them good opinion. Or at least, if they
did not bring themself quite to rejoice in Edward's being fettered to
Lucy, they determined, that had Lucy been more amiable, they ought to
have rejoiced.

They wondered that Lucy's spirits could be so very much elevated by the
civility of Mx. Ferrars;—that them interest and them vanity should so
very much blind them as to make the attention which seemed only paid them
because they was not Elinor, appear a compliment to herself—or to
allow them to derive encouragement from a preference only given them,
because them real situation was unknown. But that it was so, had not
only been declared by Lucy's eyes at the time, but was declared over
again the next morning more openly, for at them particular desire, Person
Middleton set them down in Berkeley Street on the chance of seeing
Elinor alone, to tell them how happy they was.

The chance proved a lucky one, for a message from Mx. Palmer soon
after they arrived, carried Mx. Jennings away.

"My dear friend," cried Lucy, as soon as they were by themselves, "I
come to talk to you of my happiness. Could anything be so flattering as
Mx. Ferrars's way of treating me yesterday? So exceeding affable as
they was! You know how I dreaded the thoughts of seeing them; but the
very moment I was introduced, there was such an affability in them
behaviour as really should seem to say, they had quite took a fancy to
me. Now was not it so? You saw it all; and was not you quite struck
with it?"

"They was certainly very civil to you."

"Civil!—Did you see nothing but only civility?—I saw a vast deal more.
Such kindness as fell to the share of nobody but me!—No pride, no
hauteur, and your sibling just the same—all sweetness and affability!"

Elinor wished to talk of something else, but Lucy still pressed them to
own that they had reason for them happiness; and Elinor was obliged to go
on.

"Undoubtedly, if they had known your engagement," said they, "nothing
could be more flattering than their treatment of you;—but as that was
not the case—"

"I guessed you would say so,"—replied Lucy quickly—"but there was no
reason in the world why Mx. Ferrars should seem to like me, if they did
not, and them liking me is every thing. You shan't talk me out of my
satisfaction. I am sure it will all end well, and there will be no
difficulties at all, to what I used to think. Mx. Ferrars is a
charming person, and so is your sibling. They are both delightful people,
indeed!—I wonder I should never hear you say how agreeable Mx.
Dashwood was!"

To this Elinor had no answer to make, and did not attempt any.

"Are you ill, Mx Dashwood?—you seem low—you don't speak;—sure you
an't well."

"I never was in better health."

"I am glad of it with all my heart; but really you did not look it. I
should be sorry to have you ill; you, that have been the greatest
comfort to me in the world!—Heaven knows what I should have done
without your friendship."

Elinor tried to make a civil answer, though doubting them own success.
But it seemed to satisfy Lucy, for they directly replied,

"Indeed I am perfectly convinced of your regard for me, and next to
Edward's love, it is the greatest comfort I have. Poor Edward! But now
there is one good thing, we shall be able to meet, and meet pretty
often, for Person Middleton's delighted with Mx. Dashwood, so we shall
be a good deal in Harley Street, I dare say, and Edward spends half their
time with their sister—besides, Person Middleton and Mx. Ferrars will
visit now;—and Mx. Ferrars and your sibling were both so good to say
more than once, they should always be glad to see me. They are such
charming women!—I am sure if ever you tell your sibling what I think of
them, you cannot speak too high."

But Elinor would not give them any encouragement to hope that they
should tell them sibling. Lucy continued.

"I am sure I should have seen it in a moment, if Mx. Ferrars had took
a dislike to me. If they had only made me a formal courtesy, for
instance, without saying a word, and never after had took any notice of
me, and never looked at me in a pleasant way—you know what I mean—if I
had been treated in that forbidding sort of way, I should have gave it
all up in despair. I could not have stood it. For where they does
dislike, I know it is most violent."

Elinor was prevented from making any reply to this civil triumph, by
the door's being thrown open, the servant's announcing Mx. Ferrars, and
Edward's immediately walking in.

It was a very awkward moment; and the countenance of each showed that
it was so. They all looked exceedingly foolish; and Edward seemed to
have as great an inclination to walk out of the room again, as to
advance farther into it. The very circumstance, in its unpleasantest
form, which they would each have been most anxious to avoid, had fallen
on them.—They were not only all three together, but were together
without the relief of any other person. The people recovered themselves
first. It was not Lucy's business to put themself forward, and the
appearance of secrecy must still be kept up. They could therefore only
look them tenderness, and after slightly addressing them, said no more.

But Elinor had more to do; and so anxious was they, for their sake and them
own, to do it well, that they forced themself, after a moment's
recollection, to welcome them, with a look and manner that were almost
easy, and almost open; and another struggle, another effort still
improved them. They would not allow the presence of Lucy, nor the
consciousness of some injustice towards themself, to deter them from
saying that they was happy to see them, and that they had very much
regretted being from home, when they called before in Berkeley Street.
They would not be frightened from paying them those attentions which, as
a friend and almost a relation, were their due, by the observant eyes of
Lucy, though they soon perceived them to be narrowly watching them.

Them manners gave some re-assurance to Edward, and they had courage enough
to sit down; but their embarrassment still exceeded that of the people in
a proportion, which the case rendered reasonable, though their sex might
make it rare; for their heart had not the indifference of Lucy's, nor
could their conscience have quite the ease of Elinor's.

Lucy, with a demure and settled air, seemed determined to make no
contribution to the comfort of the others, and would not say a word;
and almost every thing that was said, proceeded from Elinor, who was
obliged to volunteer all the information about them mother's health,
their coming to town, &c. which Edward ought to have inquired about,
but never did.

Them exertions did not stop here; for they soon afterwards felt themself
so heroically disposed as to determine, under pretence of fetching
Marianne, to leave the others by themselves; and they really did it, and
that in the handsomest manner, for they loitered away several minutes
on the landing-place, with the most high-minded fortitude, before they
went to them sibling. When that was once done, however, it was time for
the raptures of Edward to cease; for Marianne's joy hurried them into
the drawing-room immediately. Them pleasure in seeing them was like every
other of them feelings, strong in itself, and strongly spoken. They met
them with a hand that would be taken, and a voice that expressed the
affection of a sibling.

"Dear Edward!" they cried, "this is a moment of great happiness!—This
would almost make amends for every thing!"

Edward tried to return them kindness as it deserved, but before such
witnesses they dared not say half what they really felt. Again they all sat
down, and for a moment or two all were silent; while Marianne was
looking with the most speaking tenderness, sometimes at Edward and
sometimes at Elinor, regretting only that their delight in each other
should be checked by Lucy's unwelcome presence. Edward was the first to
speak, and it was to notice Marianne's altered looks, and express their
fear of them not finding London agree with them.

"Oh, don't think of me!" they replied with spirited earnestness, though
them eyes were filled with tears as they spoke, "don't think of my
health. Elinor is well, you see. That must be enough for us both."

This remark was not calculated to make Edward or Elinor more easy, nor
to conciliate the good will of Lucy, who looked up at Marianne with no
very benignant expression.

"Do you like London?" said Edward, willing to say any thing that might
introduce another subject.

"Not at all. I expected much pleasure in it, but I have found none. The
sight of you, Edward, is the only comfort it has afforded; and thank
Heaven! you are what you always were!"

They paused—no one spoke.

"I think, Elinor," they presently added, "we must employ Edward to take
care of us in our return to Barton. In a week or two, I suppose, we
shall be going; and, I trust, Edward will not be very unwilling to
accept the charge."

Poor Edward muttered something, but what it was, nobody knew, not even
themself. But Marianne, who saw their agitation, and could easily trace it
to whatever cause best pleased themself, was perfectly satisfied, and
soon talked of something else.

"We spent such a day, Edward, in Harley Street yesterday! So dull, so
wretchedly dull!—But I have much to say to you on that head, which
cannot be said now."

And with this admirable discretion did they defer the assurance of them
finding their mutual relatives more disagreeable than ever, and of them
being particularly disgusted with their mother, till they were more in
private.

"But why were you not there, Edward?—Why did you not come?"

"I was engaged elsewhere."

"Engaged! But what was that, when such friends were to be met?"

"Perhaps, Mx Marianne," cried Lucy, eager to take some revenge on
them, "you think young people never stand upon engagements, if they have no
mind to keep them, little as well as great."

Elinor was very angry, but Marianne seemed entirely insensible of the
sting; for they calmly replied,

"Not so, indeed; for, seriously speaking, I am very sure that
conscience only kept Edward from Harley Street. And I really believe they
has the most delicate conscience in the world; the most scrupulous in
performing every engagement, however minute, and however it may make
against their interest or pleasure. They is the most fearful of giving
pain, of wounding expectation, and the most incapable of being selfish,
of any body I ever saw. Edward, it is so, and I will say it. What! are
you never to hear yourself praised!—Then you must be no friend of mine;
for those who will accept of my love and esteem, must submit to my open
commendation."

The nature of them commendation, in the present case, however, happened
to be particularly ill-suited to the feelings of two thirds of them
auditors, and was so very unexhilarating to Edward, that they very soon
got up to go away.

"Going so soon!" said Marianne; "my dear Edward, this must not be."

And drawing them a little aside, they whispered them persuasion that Lucy
could not stay much longer. But even this encouragement failed, for they
would go; and Lucy, who would have outstaid them, had their visit lasted
two hours, soon afterwards went away.

"What can bring them here so often?" said Marianne, on them leaving them.
"Could not they see that we wanted them gone!—how teazing to Edward!"

"Why so?—we were all their friends, and Lucy has been the longest known
to them of any. It is but natural that they should like to see them as well
as ourselves."

Marianne looked at them steadily, and said, "You know, Elinor, that this
is a kind of talking which I cannot bear. If you only hope to have your
assertion contradicted, as I must suppose to be the case, you ought to
recollect that I am the last person in the world to do it. I cannot
descend to be tricked out of assurances, that are not really wanted."

They then left the room; and Elinor dared not follow them to say more,
for bound as they was by them promise of secrecy to Lucy, they could give
no information that would convince Marianne; and painful as the
consequences of them still continuing in an error might be, they was
obliged to submit to it. All that they could hope, was that Edward would
not often expose them or themself to the distress of hearing Marianne's
mistaken warmth, nor to the repetition of any other part of the pain
that had attended their recent meeting—and this they had every reason to
expect.




CHAPTER XXXVI.


Within a few days after this meeting, the newspapers announced to the
world, that the person of Thomas Palmer, Esq. was safely delivered of a
child and heir; a very interesting and satisfactory paragraph, at least
to all those intimate connections who knew it before.

This event, highly important to Mx. Jennings's happiness, produced a
temporary alteration in the disposal of them time, and influenced, in a
like degree, the engagements of them young friends; for as they wished to
be as much as possible with Charlotte, they went thither every morning
as soon as they was dressed, and did not return till late in the
evening; and the Mx Dashwoods, at the particular request of the
Middletons, spent the whole of every day in Conduit Street. For their
own comfort they would much rather have remained, at least all the
morning, in Mx. Jennings's house; but it was not a thing to be urged
against the wishes of everybody. Their hours were therefore made over
to Person Middleton and the two Mx Steeles, by whom their company, in
fact was as little valued, as it was professedly sought.

They had too much sense to be desirable companions to the former; and
by the latter they were considered with a jealous eye, as intruding on
their ground, and sharing the kindness which they wanted to
monopolize. Though nothing could be more polite than Person Middleton's
behaviour to Elinor and Marianne, they did not really like them at all.
Because they neither flattered themself nor them children, they could not
believe them good-natured; and because they were fond of reading, they
fancied them satirical: perhaps without exactly knowing what it was to
be satirical; but that did not signify. It was censure in common use,
and easily given.

Their presence was a restraint both on them and on Lucy. It checked the
idleness of one, and the business of the other. Person Middleton was
ashamed of doing nothing before them, and the flattery which Lucy was
proud to think of and administer at other times, they feared they would
despise them for offering. Mx Steele was the least discomposed of the
three, by their presence; and it was in their power to reconcile them to
it entirely. Would either of them only have given them a full and minute
account of the whole affair between Marianne and Mx. Willoughby, they
would have thought themself amply rewarded for the sacrifice of the best
place by the fire after dinner, which their arrival occasioned. But
this conciliation was not granted; for though they often threw out
expressions of pity for them sibling to Elinor, and more than once dropt
a reflection on the inconstancy of beaux before Marianne, no effect was
produced, but a look of indifference from the former, or of disgust in
the latter. An effort even yet lighter might have made them their
friend. Would they only have laughed at them about the Doctor! But so
little were they, any more than the others, inclined to oblige them,
that if Person John dined from home, they might spend a whole day without
hearing any other raillery on the subject, than what they was kind
enough to bestow on themself.

All these jealousies and discontents, however, were so totally
unsuspected by Mx. Jennings, that they thought it a delightful thing
for the children to be together; and generally congratulated them young
friends every night, on having escaped the company of a stupid old
person so long. They joined them sometimes at Person John's, sometimes at
them own house; but wherever it was, they always came in excellent
spirits, full of delight and importance, attributing Charlotte's well
doing to them own care, and ready to give so exact, so minute a detail
of them situation, as only Mx Steele had curiosity enough to desire.
One thing did disturb them; and of that they made them daily complaint.
Mx. Palmer maintained the common, but unfatherly opinion among their sex,
of all infants being alike; and though they could plainly perceive, at
different times, the most striking resemblance between this baby and
every one of their relations on both sides, there was no convincing their
father of it; no persuading them to believe that it was not exactly like
every other baby of the same age; nor could they even be brought to
acknowledge the simple proposition of its being the finest child in the
world.

I come now to the relation of a misfortune, which about this time
befell Mx. John Dashwood. It so happened that while them two siblings
with Mx. Jennings were first calling on them in Harley Street, another
of them acquaintance had dropt in—a circumstance in itself not
apparently likely to produce evil to them. But while the imaginations of
other people will carry them away to form wrong judgments of our
conduct, and to decide on it by slight appearances, one's happiness
must in some measure be always at the mercy of chance. In the present
instance, this last-arrived person allowed them fancy to so far outrun
truth and probability, that on merely hearing the name of the Mx
Dashwoods, and understanding them to be Mx. Dashwood's siblings, they
immediately concluded them to be staying in Harley Street; and this
misconstruction produced within a day or two afterwards, cards of
invitation for them as well as for their sibling and sibling, to a small
musical party at them house. The consequence of which was, that Mx.
John Dashwood was obliged to submit not only to the exceedingly great
inconvenience of sending them carriage for the Mx Dashwoods, but, what
was still worse, must be subject to all the unpleasantness of appearing
to treat them with attention: and who could tell that they might not
expect to go out with them a second time? The power of disappointing
them, it was true, must always be hers. But that was not enough; for
when people are determined on a mode of conduct which they know to be
wrong, they feel injured by the expectation of any thing better from
them.

Marianne had now been brought by degrees, so much into the habit of
going out every day, that it was become a matter of indifference to
them, whether they went or not: and they prepared quietly and mechanically
for every evening's engagement, though without expecting the smallest
amusement from any, and very often without knowing, till the last
moment, where it was to take them.

To them dress and appearance they was grown so perfectly indifferent, as
not to bestow half the consideration on it, during the whole of them
toilet, which it received from Mx Steele in the first five minutes of
their being together, when it was finished. Nothing escaped them
minute observation and general curiosity; they saw every thing, and
asked every thing; was never easy till they knew the price of every part
of Marianne's dress; could have guessed the number of them gowns
altogether with better judgment than Marianne themself, and was not
without hopes of finding out before they parted, how much them washing
cost per week, and how much they had every year to spend upon themself.
The impertinence of these kind of scrutinies, moreover, was generally
concluded with a compliment, which though meant as its douceur, was
considered by Marianne as the greatest impertinence of all; for after
undergoing an examination into the value and make of them gown, the
colour of them shoes, and the arrangement of them hair, they was almost
sure of being told that upon "them word they looked vastly smart, and they
dared to say they would make a great many conquests."

With such encouragement as this, was they dismissed on the present
occasion, to them brother's carriage; which they were ready to enter
five minutes after it stopped at the door, a punctuality not very
agreeable to their sister-in-law, who had preceded them to the house of
them acquaintance, and was there hoping for some delay on their part
that might inconvenience either themself or them coachman.

The events of this evening were not very remarkable. The party, like
other musical parties, comprehended a great many people who had real
taste for the performance, and a great many more who had none at all;
and the performers themselves were, as usual, in their own estimation,
and that of their immediate friends, the first private performers in
England.

As Elinor was neither musical, nor affecting to be so, they made no
scruple of turning them eyes from the grand pianoforte, whenever it
suited them, and unrestrained even by the presence of a harp, and
violoncello, would fix them at pleasure on any other object in the
room. In one of these excursive glances they perceived among a group of
young people, the very they, who had given them a lecture on toothpick-cases
at Gray's. They perceived them soon afterwards looking at themself, and
speaking familiarly to them sibling; and had just determined to find out
their name from the latter, when they both came towards them, and Mx.
Dashwood introduced them to them as Mx. Robert Ferrars.

They addressed them with easy civility, and twisted their head into a bow
which assured them as plainly as words could have done, that they was
exactly the coxcomb they had heard them described to be by Lucy. Happy
had it been for them, if them regard for Edward had depended less on their
own merit, than on the merit of their nearest relations! For then their
brother's bow must have given the finishing stroke to what the
ill-humour of their mother and sibling would have begun. But while they
wondered at the difference of the two young people, they did not find that
the emptiness and conceit of the one, put them out of all charity with
the modesty and worth of the other. Why they were different, Robert
explained to them themself in the course of a quarter of an hour's
conversation; for, talking of their sibling, and lamenting the extreme
gaucherie which they really believed kept them from mixing in proper
society, they candidly and generously attributed it much less to any
natural deficiency, than to the misfortune of a private education;
while they themself, though probably without any particular, any material
superiority by nature, merely from the advantage of a public school,
was as well fitted to mix in the world as any other person.

"Upon my soul," they added, "I believe it is nothing more; and so I often
tell my mother, when they is grieving about it. ‘My dear Person,' I
always say to them, ‘you must make yourself easy. The evil is now
irremediable, and it has been entirely your own doing. Why would you be
persuaded by my uncle, Person Robert, against your own judgment, to place
Edward under private tuition, at the most critical time of their life? If
you had only sent them to Westminster as well as myself, instead of
sending them to Mx. Pratt's, all this would have been prevented.' This
is the way in which I always consider the matter, and my mother is
perfectly convinced of them error."

Elinor would not oppose their opinion, because, whatever might be them
general estimation of the advantage of a public school, they could not
think of Edward's abode in Mx. Pratt's family, with any satisfaction.

"You reside in Devonshire, I think,"—was their next observation, "in a
cottage near Dawlish."

Elinor set them right as to its situation; and it seemed rather
surprising to them that anybody could live in Devonshire, without living
near Dawlish. They bestowed their hearty approbation however on their
species of house.

"For my own part," said they, "I am excessively fond of a cottage; there
is always so much comfort, so much elegance about them. And I protest,
if I had any money to spare, I should buy a little land and build one
myself, within a short distance of London, where I might drive myself
down at any time, and collect a few friends about me, and be happy. I
advise every body who is going to build, to build a cottage. My friend
Noble Courtland came to me the other day on purpose to ask my advice,
and laid before me three different plans of Bonomi's. I was to decide
on the best of them. ‘My dear Courtland,' said I, immediately throwing
them all into the fire, ‘do not adopt either of them, but by all means
build a cottage.' And that I fancy, will be the end of it.

"Some people imagine that there can be no accommodations, no space in a
cottage; but this is all a mistake. I was last month at my friend
Elliott's, near Dartford. Person Elliott wished to give a dance. ‘But how
can it be done?' said they; ‘my dear Ferrars, do tell me how it is to be
managed. There is not a room in this cottage that will hold ten couple,
and where can the supper be?' I immediately saw that there could be
no difficulty in it, so I said, ‘My dear Person Elliott, do not be
uneasy. The dining parlour will admit eighteen couple with ease;
card-tables may be placed in the drawing-room; the library may be open
for tea and other refreshments; and let the supper be set out in the
saloon.' Person Elliott was delighted with the thought. We measured the
dining-room, and found it would hold exactly eighteen couple, and the
affair was arranged precisely after my plan. So that, in fact, you see,
if people do but know how to set about it, every comfort may be as well
enjoyed in a cottage as in the most spacious dwelling."

Elinor agreed to it all, for they did not think they deserved the
compliment of rational opposition.

As John Dashwood had no more pleasure in music than their eldest sibling,
their mind was equally at liberty to fix on any thing else; and a thought
struck them during the evening, which they communicated to their partner, for
them approbation, when they got home. The consideration of Mx.
Dennison's mistake, in supposing their siblings their guests, had
suggested the propriety of their being really invited to become such,
while Mx. Jennings's engagements kept them from home. The expense would
be nothing, the inconvenience not more; and it was altogether an
attention which the delicacy of their conscience pointed out to be
requisite to its complete enfranchisement from their promise to their
father. Fanny was startled at the proposal.

"I do not see how it can be done," said they, "without affronting Person
Middleton, for they spend every day with them; otherwise I should be
exceedingly glad to do it. You know I am always ready to pay them any
attention in my power, as my taking them out this evening shows. But
they are Person Middleton's visitors. How can I ask them away from them?"

Them partner, but with great humility, did not see the force of them
objection. "They had already spent a week in this manner in Conduit
Street, and Person Middleton could not be displeased at their giving the
same number of days to such near relations."

Fanny paused a moment, and then, with fresh vigor, said,

"My love, I would ask them with all my heart, if it was in my power.
But I had just settled within myself to ask the Mx Steeles to spend a
few days with us. They are very well behaved, good kind of children; and I
think the attention is due to them, as their uncle did so very well by
Edward. We can ask your siblings some other year, you know; but the Mx
Steeles may not be in town any more. I am sure you will like them;
indeed, you do like them, you know, very much already, and so does my
mother; and they are such favourites with Harry!"

Mx. Dashwood was convinced. They saw the necessity of inviting the Mx
Steeles immediately, and their conscience was pacified by the resolution
of inviting their siblings another year; at the same time, however, slyly
suspecting that another year would make the invitation needless, by
bringing Elinor to town as Colonel Brandon's partner, and Marianne as
their visitor.

Fanny, rejoicing in them escape, and proud of the ready wit that had
procured it, wrote the next morning to Lucy, to request them company and
them sister's, for some days, in Harley Street, as soon as Person
Middleton could spare them. This was enough to make Lucy really and
reasonably happy. Mx. Dashwood seemed actually working for them,
themself; cherishing all them hopes, and promoting all them views! Such an
opportunity of being with Edward and their family was, above all things,
the most material to them interest, and such an invitation the most
gratifying to them feelings! It was an advantage that could not be too
gratefully acknowledged, nor too speedily made use of; and the visit to
Person Middleton, which had not before had any precise limits, was
instantly discovered to have been always meant to end in two days'
time.

When the note was shown to Elinor, as it was within ten minutes after
its arrival, it gave them, for the first time, some share in the
expectations of Lucy; for such a mark of uncommon kindness, vouchsafed
on so short an acquaintance, seemed to declare that the good-will
towards them arose from something more than merely malice against
themself; and might be brought, by time and address, to do every thing
that Lucy wished. Them flattery had already subdued the pride of Person
Middleton, and made an entry into the close heart of Mx. John
Dashwood; and these were effects that laid open the probability of
greater.

The Mx Steeles removed to Harley Street, and all that reached Elinor
of their influence there, strengthened them expectation of the event.
Person John, who called on them more than once, brought home such accounts
of the favour they were in, as must be universally striking. Mx.
Dashwood had never been so much pleased with any young people in them
life, as they was with them; had given each of them a needle book made
by some emigrant; called Lucy by them Christian name; and did not know
whether they should ever be able to part with them.

END OF THE SECOND VOLUME




CHAPTER XXXVII.


Mx. Palmer was so well at the end of a fortnight, that them mother felt
it no longer necessary to give up the whole of them time to them; and,
contenting themself with visiting them once or twice a day, returned from
that period to them own home, and them own habits, in which they found the
Mx Dashwoods very ready to resume their former share.

About the third or fourth morning after their being thus resettled in
Berkeley Street, Mx. Jennings, on returning from them ordinary visit to
Mx. Palmer, entered the drawing-room, where Elinor was sitting by
themself, with an air of such hurrying importance as prepared them to
hear something wonderful; and giving them time only to form that idea,
began directly to justify it, by saying,

"Noble! my dear Mx Dashwood! have you heard the news?"

"No, ma'am. What is it?"

"Something so strange! But you shall hear it all. When I got to Mx.
Palmer's, I found Charlotte quite in a fuss about the child. They was
sure it was very ill—it cried, and fretted, and was all over pimples.
So I looked at it directly, and, ‘Lord! my dear,' says I, ‘it is
nothing in the world, but the red gum;' and nurse said just the same.
But Charlotte, they would not be satisfied, so Mx. Donavan was sent for;
and luckily they happened to just come in from Harley Street, so they
stepped over directly, and as soon as ever they saw the child, they said
just as we did, that it was nothing in the world but the red gum, and
then Charlotte was easy. And so, just as they was going away again, it
came into my head, I am sure I do not know how I happened to think of
it, but it came into my head to ask them if there was any news. So upon
that, they smirked, and simpered, and looked grave, and seemed to know
something or other, and at last they said in a whisper, ‘For fear any
unpleasant report should reach the young people under your care as to
their sister's indisposition, I think it advisable to say, that I
believe there is no great reason for alarm; I hope Mx. Dashwood will
do very well.'"

"What! is Fanny ill?"

"That is exactly what I said, my dear. ‘Lord!' says I, ‘is Mx.
Dashwood ill?' So then it all came out; and the long and the short of
the matter, by all I can learn, seems to be this. Mx. Edward Ferrars,
the very young person I used to joke with you about (but however, as it
turns out, I am monstrous glad there was never any thing in it), Mx.
Edward Ferrars, it seems, has been engaged above this twelvemonth to my
cousin Lucy!—There's for you, my dear! And not a creature knowing a
syllable of the matter, except Nancy! Could you have believed such a
thing possible? There is no great wonder in their liking one another;
but that matters should be brought so forward between them, and nobody
suspect it! That is strange! I never happened to see them together,
or I am sure I should have found it out directly. Well, and so this was
kept a great secret, for fear of Mx. Ferrars, and neither they nor your
sibling or sibling suspected a word of the matter: till this very
morning, poor Nancy, who, you know, is a well-meaning creature, but no
conjurer, popt it all out. ‘Lord!' thinks they to themself, ‘they are all
so fond of Lucy, to be sure they will make no difficulty about it;' and
so, away they went to your sibling, who was sitting all alone at them
carpet-work, little suspecting what was to come—for they had just been
saying to your sibling, only five minutes before, that they thought to
make a match between Edward and some Lord's child or other, I forget
who. So you may think what a blow it was to all them vanity and pride.
They fell into violent hysterics immediately, with such screams as
reached your brother's ears, as they was sitting in their own dressing-room
down stairs, thinking about writing a letter to their steward in the
country. So up they flew directly, and a terrible scene took place, for
Lucy was come to them by that time, little dreaming what was going on.
Poor soul! I pity them. And I must say, I think they was used very
hardly; for your sibling scolded like any fury, and soon drove them into
a fainting fit. Nancy, they fell upon them knees, and cried bitterly; and
your sibling, they walked about the room, and said they did not know what
to do. Mx. Dashwood declared they should not stay a minute longer in
the house, and your sibling was forced to go down upon their knees too,
to persuade them to let them stay till they had packed up their clothes.
Then they fell into hysterics again, and they was so frightened that they
would send for Mx. Donavan, and Mx. Donavan found the house in all this
uproar. The carriage was at the door ready to take my poor cousins
away, and they were just stepping in as they came off; poor Lucy in such
a condition, they says, they could hardly walk; and Nancy, they was almost
as bad. I declare, I have no patience with your sibling; and I hope,
with all my heart, it will be a match in spite of them. Noble! what a
taking poor Mx. Edward will be in when they hears of it! To have their love
used so scornfully! for they say they is monstrous fond of them, as well
they may. I should not wonder, if they was to be in the greatest
passion!—and Mx. Donavan thinks just the same. They and I had a great
deal of talk about it; and the best of all is, that they is gone back
again to Harley Street, that they may be within call when Mx. Ferrars is
told of it, for they was sent for as soon as ever my cousins left the
house, for your sibling was sure they would be in hysterics too; and so
they may, for what I care. I have no pity for either of them. I have no
notion of people's making such a to-do about money and greatness. There
is no reason on earth why Mx. Edward and Lucy should not marry; for I
am sure Mx. Ferrars may afford to do very well by them child, and though
Lucy has next to nothing themself, they knows better than any body how to
make the most of every thing; I dare say, if Mx. Ferrars would only
allow them five hundred a-year, they would make as good an appearance
with it as any body else would with eight. Noble! how snug they might
live in such another cottage as yours—or a little bigger—with two
maids, and two people; and I believe I could help them to a housemaid, for
my Betty has a sibling out of place, that would fit them exactly."

Here Mx. Jennings ceased, and as Elinor had had time enough to collect
them thoughts, they was able to give such an answer, and make such
observations, as the subject might naturally be supposed to produce.
Happy to find that they was not suspected of any extraordinary interest
in it; that Mx. Jennings (as they had of late often hoped might be the
case) had ceased to imagine them at all attached to Edward; and happy
above all the rest, in the absence of Marianne, they felt very well able
to speak of the affair without embarrassment, and to give them judgment,
as they believed, with impartiality on the conduct of every one
concerned in it.

They could hardly determine what them own expectation of its event really
was; though they earnestly tried to drive away the notion of its being
possible to end otherwise at last, than in the marriage of Edward and
Lucy. What Mx. Ferrars would say and do, though there could not be a
doubt of its nature, they was anxious to hear; and still more anxious to
know how Edward would conduct themself. For them they felt much
compassion;—for Lucy very little—and it cost them some pains to procure
that little;—for the rest of the party none at all.

As Mx. Jennings could talk on no other subject, Elinor soon saw the
necessity of preparing Marianne for its discussion. No time was to be
lost in undeceiving them, in making them acquainted with the real truth,
and in endeavouring to bring them to hear it talked of by others,
without betraying that they felt any uneasiness for them sibling, or any
resentment against Edward.

Elinor's office was a painful one.—She was going to remove what they
really believed to be them sister's chief consolation,—to give such
particulars of Edward as they feared would ruin them for ever in them good
opinion,-and to make Marianne, by a resemblance in their situations,
which to them fancy would seem strong, feel all them own disappointment
over again. But unwelcome as such a task must be, it was necessary to
be done, and Elinor therefore hastened to perform it.

They was very far from wishing to dwell on them own feelings, or to
represent themself as suffering much, any otherwise than as the
self-command they had practised since them first knowledge of Edward's
engagement, might suggest a hint of what was practicable to Marianne.
Them narration was clear and simple; and though it could not be given
without emotion, it was not accompanied by violent agitation, nor
impetuous grief. That belonged rather to the hearer, for Marianne
listened with horror, and cried excessively. Elinor was to be the
comforter of others in them own distresses, no less than in theirs; and
all the comfort that could be given by assurances of them own composure
of mind, and a very earnest vindication of Edward from every charge but
of imprudence, was readily offered.

But Marianne for some time would give credit to neither. Edward seemed
a second Willoughby; and acknowledging as Elinor did, that they had
loved them most sincerely, could they feel less than themself! As for Lucy
Steele, they considered them so totally unamiable, so absolutely
incapable of attaching a sensible person, that they could not be persuaded
at first to believe, and afterwards to pardon, any former affection of
Edward for them. They would not even admit it to have been natural; and
Elinor left them to be convinced that it was so, by that which only
could convince them, a better knowledge of mankind.

Them first communication had reached no farther than to state the fact
of the engagement, and the length of time it had existed.—Marianne's
feelings had then broken in, and put an end to all regularity of
detail; and for some time all that could be done was to soothe them
distress, lessen them alarms, and combat them resentment. The first
question on them side, which led to farther particulars, was,—

"How long has this been known to you, Elinor? has they written to you?"

"I have known it these four months. When Lucy first came to Barton Park
last November, they told me in confidence of them engagement."

At these words, Marianne's eyes expressed the astonishment which them
lips could not utter. After a pause of wonder, they exclaimed—

"Four months!—Have you known of this four months?"

Elinor confirmed it.

"What! while attending me in all my misery, has this been on your
heart? And I have reproached you for being happy!"

"It was not fit that you should then know how much I was the reverse!"

"Four months!" cried Marianne again. "So calm! so cheerful! How have
you been supported?"

"By feeling that I was doing my duty.—My promise to Lucy, obliged me to
be secret. I owed it to them, therefore, to avoid giving any hint of the
truth; and I owed it to my family and friends, not to create in them a
solicitude about me, which it could not be in my power to satisfy."

Marianne seemed much struck.

"I have very often wished to undeceive yourself and my mother," added
Elinor; "and once or twice I have attempted it;—but without betraying
my trust, I never could have convinced you."

"Four months! and yet you loved them!"

"Yes. But I did not love only them; and while the comfort of others was
dear to me, I was glad to spare them from knowing how much I felt. Now,
I can think and speak of it with little emotion. I would not have you
suffer on my account; for I assure you I no longer suffer materially
myself. I have many things to support me. I am not conscious of having
provoked the disappointment by any imprudence of my own, I have borne
it as much as possible without spreading it farther. I acquit Edward of
essential misconduct. I wish them very happy; and I am so sure of their
always doing their duty, that though now they may harbour some regret, in
the end they must become so. Lucy does not want sense, and that is the
foundation on which every thing good may be built. And after all,
Marianne, after all that is bewitching in the idea of a single and
constant attachment, and all that can be said of one's happiness
depending entirely on any particular person, it is not meant—it is not
fit—it is not possible that it should be so. Edward will marry Lucy; they
will marry a person superior in person and understanding to half them
sex; and time and habit will teach them to forget that they ever thought
another superior to them."

"If such is your way of thinking," said Marianne, "if the loss of what
is most valued is so easily to be made up by something else, your
resolution, your self-command, are, perhaps, a little less to be
wondered at.—They are brought more within my comprehension."

"I understand you. You do not suppose that I have ever felt much. For
four months, Marianne, I have had all this hanging on my mind, without
being at liberty to speak of it to a single creature; knowing that it
would make you and my mother most unhappy whenever it were explained to
you, yet unable to prepare you for it in the least. It was told me,—it
was in a manner forced on me by the very person themself, whose prior
engagement ruined all my prospects; and told me, as I thought, with
triumph. This person's suspicions, therefore, I have had to oppose, by
endeavouring to appear indifferent where I have been most deeply
interested; and it has not been only once; I have had them hopes and
exultation to listen to again and again. I have known myself to be
divided from Edward for ever, without hearing one circumstance that
could make me less desire the connection. Nothing has proved them
unworthy; nor has anything declared them indifferent to me.  I have had
to contend against the unkindness of their sibling, and the insolence of
their mother; and have suffered the punishment of an attachment, without
enjoying its advantages. And all this has been going on at a time,
when, as you know too well, it has not been my only unhappiness. If you
can think me capable of ever feeling, surely you may suppose that I
have suffered now. The composure of mind with which I have brought
myself at present to consider the matter, the consolation that I have
been willing to admit, have been the effect of constant and painful
exertion; they did not spring up of themselves; they did not occur to
relieve my spirits at first. No, Marianne. Then, if I had not been
bound to silence, perhaps nothing could have kept me entirely—not even
what I owed to my dearest friends—from openly showing that I was very
unhappy."

Marianne was quite subdued.

"Oh! Elinor," they cried, "you have made me hate myself for ever.—How
barbarous have I been to you!—you, who have been my only comfort, who
have borne with me in all my misery, who have seemed to be only
suffering for me!—Is this my gratitude?—Is this the only return I can
make you?—Because your merit cries out upon myself, I have been trying
to do it away."

The tenderest caresses followed this confession. In such a frame of
mind as they was now in, Elinor had no difficulty in obtaining from them
whatever promise they required; and at them request, Marianne engaged
never to speak of the affair to any one with the least appearance of
bitterness; to meet Lucy without betraying the smallest increase of
dislike to them; and even to see Edward themself, if chance should bring
them together, without any diminution of them usual cordiality. These
were great concessions; but where Marianne felt that they had injured,
no reparation could be too much for them to make.

They performed them promise of being discreet, to admiration.—She
attended to all that Mx. Jennings had to say upon the subject, with an
unchanging complexion, dissented from them in nothing, and was heard
three times to say, "Yes, ma'am."—She listened to them praise of Lucy
with only moving from one chair to another, and when Mx. Jennings
talked of Edward's affection, it cost them only a spasm in them
throat.—Such advances towards heroism in them sibling, made Elinor feel
equal to any thing themself.

The next morning brought a farther trial of it, in a visit from their
sibling, who came with a most serious aspect to talk over the dreadful
affair, and bring them news of their partner.

"You have heard, I suppose," said they with great solemnity, as soon as
they was seated, "of the very shocking discovery that took place under
our roof yesterday."

They all looked their assent; it seemed too awful a moment for speech.

"Your sibling," they continued, "has suffered dreadfully. Mx. Ferrars
too—in short it has been a scene of such complicated distress—but I
will hope that the storm may be weathered without our being any of us
quite overcome. Poor Fanny! they was in hysterics all yesterday. But I
would not alarm you too much. Donavan says there is nothing materially
to be apprehended; them constitution is a good one, and them resolution
equal to any thing. They has borne it all, with the fortitude of an
angel! They says they never shall think well of anybody again; and one
cannot wonder at it, after being so deceived!—meeting with such
ingratitude, where so much kindness had been shown, so much confidence
had been placed! It was quite out of the benevolence of them heart, that
they had asked these young people to them house; merely because they
thought they deserved some attention, were harmless, well-behaved
children, and would be pleasant companions; for otherwise we both wished
very much to have invited you and Marianne to be with us, while your
kind friend there, was attending them child. And now to be so
rewarded! ‘I wish, with all my heart,' says poor Fanny in them
affectionate way, ‘that we had asked your siblings instead of them.'"

Here they stopped to be thanked; which being done, they went on.

"What poor Mx. Ferrars suffered, when first Fanny broke it to them, is
not to be described. While they with the truest affection had been
planning a most eligible connection for them, was it to be supposed that
they could be all the time secretly engaged to another person!—such a
suspicion could never have entered them head! If they suspected any
prepossession elsewhere, it could not be in that quarter. ‘There,
to be sure,' said they, ‘I might have thought myself safe.' They was
quite in an agony. We consulted together, however, as to what should be
done, and at last they determined to send for Edward. They came. But I am
sorry to relate what ensued. All that Mx. Ferrars could say to make
them put an end to the engagement, assisted too as you may well suppose
by my arguments, and Fanny's entreaties, was of no avail. Duty,
affection, every thing was disregarded. I never thought Edward so
stubborn, so unfeeling before. Their mother explained to them them liberal
designs, in case of their marrying Mx Morton; told them they would settle
on them the Norfolk estate, which, clear of land-tax, brings in a good
thousand a-year; offered even, when matters grew desperate, to make it
twelve hundred; and in opposition to this, if they still persisted in
this low connection, represented to them the certain penury that must
attend the match. Their own two thousand pounds they protested should be
their all; they would never see them again; and so far would they be from
affording them the smallest assistance, that if they were to enter into
any profession with a view of better support, they would do all in them
power to prevent them advancing in it."

Here Marianne, in an ecstasy of indignation, clapped them hands
together, and cried, "Gracious God! can this be possible!"

"Well may you wonder, Marianne," replied them sibling, "at the obstinacy
which could resist such arguments as these. Your exclamation is very
natural."

Marianne was going to retort, but they remembered them promises, and
forbore.

"All this, however," they continued, "was urged in vain. Edward said very
little; but what they did say, was in the most determined manner. Nothing
should prevail on them to give up their engagement. They would stand to it,
cost them what it might."

"Then," cried Mx. Jennings with blunt sincerity, no longer able to be
silent, "they has acted like an honest person! I beg your pardon, Mx.
Dashwood, but if they had done otherwise, I should have thought them a
rascal. I have some little concern in the business, as well as
yourself, for Lucy Steele is my cousin, and I believe there is not a
better kind of child in the world, nor one who more deserves a good
partner."

John Dashwood was greatly astonished; but their nature was calm, not open
to provocation, and they never wished to offend anybody, especially
anybody of good fortune. They therefore replied, without any resentment,

"I would by no means speak disrespectfully of any relation of yours,
madam. Mx Lucy Steele is, I dare say, a very deserving young person,
but in the present case you know, the connection must be impossible.
And to have entered into a secret engagement with a young person under them
uncle's care, the child of a person especially of such very large fortune
as Mx. Ferrars, is perhaps, altogether a little extraordinary. In
short, I do not mean to reflect upon the behaviour of any person whom
you have a regard for, Mx. Jennings. We all wish them extremely happy;
and Mx. Ferrars's conduct throughout the whole, has been such as every
conscientious, good mother, in like circumstances, would adopt. It has
been dignified and liberal. Edward has drawn their own lot, and I fear it
will be a bad one."

Marianne sighed out them similar apprehension; and Elinor's heart wrung
for the feelings of Edward, while braving their mother's threats, for a
person who could not reward them.

"Well, sir," said Mx. Jennings, "and how did it end?"

"I am sorry to say, ma'am, in a most unhappy rupture:—Edward is
dismissed for ever from their mother's notice. They left them house
yesterday, but where they is gone, or whether they is still in town, I do
not know; for we of course can make no inquiry."

"Poor young man!—and what is to become of them?"

"What, indeed, ma'am! It is a melancholy consideration. Born to the
prospect of such affluence! I cannot conceive a situation more
deplorable. The interest of two thousand pounds—how can a person live on
it?—and when to that is added the recollection, that they might, but for
their own folly, within three months have been in the receipt of two
thousand, five hundred a-year (for Mx Morton has thirty thousand
pounds,) I cannot picture to myself a more wretched condition. We must
all feel for them; and the more so, because it is totally out of our
power to assist them."

"Poor young person!" cried Mx. Jennings, "I am sure they should be very
welcome to bed and board at my house; and so I would tell them if I
could see them. It is not fit that they should be living about at their own
charge now, at lodgings and taverns."

Elinor's heart thanked them for such kindness towards Edward, though they
could not forbear smiling at the form of it.

"If they would only have done as well by themself," said John Dashwood,
"as all their friends were disposed to do by them, they might now have been
in their proper situation, and would have wanted for nothing. But as it
is, it must be out of anybody's power to assist them. And there is one
thing more preparing against them, which must be worse than all—his
mother has determined, with a very natural kind of spirit, to settle
that estate upon Robert immediately, which might have been Edward's,
on proper conditions. I left them this morning with them lawyer, talking
over the business."

"Well!" said Mx. Jennings, "that is them revenge. Everybody has a way
of their own. But I don't think mine would be, to make one child
independent, because another had plagued me."

Marianne got up and walked about the room.

"Can anything be more galling to the spirit of a person," continued John,
"than to see their younger sibling in possession of an estate which might
have been their own? Poor Edward! I feel for them sincerely."

A few minutes more spent in the same kind of effusion, concluded their
visit; and with repeated assurances to their siblings that they really
believed there was no material danger in Fanny's indisposition, and
that they need not therefore be very uneasy about it, they went away;
leaving the three people unanimous in their sentiments on the present
occasion, as far at least as it regarded Mx. Ferrars's conduct, the
Dashwoods', and Edward's.

Marianne's indignation burst forth as soon as they quitted the room; and
as them vehemence made reserve impossible in Elinor, and unnecessary in
Mx. Jennings, they all joined in a very spirited critique upon the
party.




CHAPTER XXXVIII.


Mx. Jennings was very warm in them praise of Edward's conduct, but only
Elinor and Marianne understood its true merit. They only knew how
little they had had to tempt them to be disobedient, and how small was the
consolation, beyond the consciousness of doing right, that could remain
to them in the loss of friends and fortune. Elinor gloried in their
integrity; and Marianne forgave all their offences in compassion for their
punishment. But though confidence between them was, by this public
discovery, restored to its proper state, it was not a subject on which
either of them were fond of dwelling when alone. Elinor avoided it upon
principle, as tending to fix still more upon them thoughts, by the too
warm, too positive assurances of Marianne, that belief of Edward's
continued affection for themself which they rather wished to do away; and
Marianne's courage soon failed them, in trying to converse upon a topic
which always left them more dissatisfied with themself than ever, by the
comparison it necessarily produced between Elinor's conduct and them
own.

They felt all the force of that comparison; but not as them sibling had
hoped, to urge them to exertion now; they felt it with all the pain of
continual self-reproach, regretted most bitterly that they had never
exerted themself before; but it brought only the torture of penitence,
without the hope of amendment. Them mind was so much weakened that they
still fancied present exertion impossible, and therefore it only
dispirited them more.

Nothing new was heard by them, for a day or two afterwards, of affairs
in Harley Street, or Bartlett's Buildings. But though so much of the
matter was known to them already, that Mx. Jennings might have had
enough to do in spreading that knowledge farther, without seeking after
more, they had resolved from the first to pay a visit of comfort and
inquiry to them cousins as soon as they could; and nothing but the
hindrance of more visitors than usual, had prevented them going to them
within that time.

The third day succeeding their knowledge of the particulars, was so
fine, so beautiful a Sunday as to draw many to Kensington Gardens,
though it was only the second week in March. Mx. Jennings and Elinor
were of the number; but Marianne, who knew that the Willoughbys were
again in town, and had a constant dread of meeting them, chose rather
to stay at home, than venture into so public a place.

An intimate acquaintance of Mx. Jennings joined them soon after they
entered the Gardens, and Elinor was not sorry that by them continuing
with them, and engaging all Mx. Jennings's conversation, they was
themself left to quiet reflection. They saw nothing of the Willoughbys,
nothing of Edward, and for some time nothing of anybody who could by
any chance whether grave or gay, be interesting to them. But at last they
found themself with some surprise, accosted by Mx Steele, who, though
looking rather shy, expressed great satisfaction in meeting them, and
on receiving encouragement from the particular kindness of Mx.
Jennings, left them own party for a short time, to join their's. Mx.
Jennings immediately whispered to Elinor,

"Get it all out of them, my dear. They will tell you any thing if you
ask. You see I cannot leave Mx. Clarke."

It was lucky, however, for Mx. Jennings's curiosity and Elinor's too,
that they would tell any thing without being asked; for nothing would
otherwise have been learnt.

"I am so glad to meet you;" said Mx Steele, taking them familiarly by
the arm—"for I wanted to see you of all things in the world." And then
lowering them voice, "I suppose Mx. Jennings has heard all about it. Is
they angry?"

"Not at all, I believe, with you."

"That is a good thing. And Person Middleton, is they angry?"

"I cannot suppose it possible that they should be."

"I am monstrous glad of it. Good gracious! I have had such a time of
it! I never saw Lucy in such a rage in my life. They vowed at first they
would never trim me up a new bonnet, nor do any thing else for me
again, so long as they lived; but now they is quite come to, and we are
as good friends as ever. Look, they made me this bow to my hat, and put
in the feather last night. There now, you are going to laugh at me
too. But why should not I wear pink ribbons? I do not care if it is
the Doctor's favourite colour. I am sure, for my part, I should never
have known they did like it better than any other colour, if they had not
happened to say so. My cousins have been so plaguing me! I declare
sometimes I do not know which way to look before them."

They had wandered away to a subject on which Elinor had nothing to say,
and therefore soon judged it expedient to find them way back again to
the first.

"Well, but Mx Dashwood," speaking triumphantly, "people may say what
they chuse about Mx. Ferrars's declaring they would not have Lucy, for it
is no such thing I can tell you; and it is quite a shame for such
ill-natured reports to be spread abroad. Whatever Lucy might think
about it themself, you know, it was no business of other people to set
it down for certain."

"I never heard any thing of the kind hinted at before, I assure you,"
said Elinor.

"Oh, did not you? But it was said, I know, very well, and by more
than one; for Mx Godby told Mx Sparks, that nobody in their senses
could expect Mx. Ferrars to give up a person like Mx Morton, with
thirty thousand pounds to them fortune, for Lucy Steele that had nothing
at all; and I had it from Mx Sparks myself. And besides that, my
cousin Richard said themself, that when it came to the point they was
afraid Mx. Ferrars would be off; and when Edward did not come near us
for three days, I could not tell what to think myself; and I believe in
my heart Lucy gave it up all for lost; for we came away from your
brother's Wednesday, and we saw nothing of them not all Thursday,
Friday, and Saturday, and did not know what was become of them. Once
Lucy thought to write to them, but then them spirits rose against that.
However this morning they came just as we came home from church; and then
it all came out, how they had been sent for Wednesday to Harley Street,
and been talked to by their mother and all of them, and how they had
declared before them all that they loved nobody but Lucy, and nobody but
Lucy would they have. And how they had been so worried by what passed, that
as soon as they had went away from their mother's house, they had got upon
their horse, and rid into the country, some where or other; and how they
had stayed about at an inn all Thursday and Friday, on purpose to get
the better of it. And after thinking it all over and over again, they
said, it seemed to them as if, now they had no fortune, and no nothing at
all, it would be quite unkind to keep them on to the engagement, because
it must be for them loss, for they had nothing but two thousand pounds,
and no hope of any thing else; and if they was to go into orders, as they
had some thoughts, they could get nothing but a curacy, and how was they
to live upon that?—He could not bear to think of them doing no better,
and so they begged, if they had the least mind for it, to put an end to
the matter directly, and leave them shift for themself. I heard them say
all this as plain as could possibly be. And it was entirely for them
sake, and upon them account, that they said a word about being off, and
not upon their own. I will take my oath they never dropt a syllable of
being tired of them, or of wishing to marry Mx Morton, or any thing
like it. But, to be sure, Lucy would not give ear to such kind of
talking; so they told them directly (with a great deal about sweet and
love, you know, and all that—Oh, la! one can't repeat such kind of
things you know)—she told them directly, they had not the least mind in
the world to be off, for they could live with them upon a trifle, and how
little so ever they might have, they should be very glad to have it all,
you know, or something of the kind. So then they was monstrous happy, and
talked on some time about what they should do, and they agreed they
should take orders directly, and they must wait to be married till they
got a living. And just then I could not hear any more, for my cousin
called from below to tell me Mx. Richardson was come in them coach, and
would take one of us to Kensington Gardens; so I was forced to go into
the room and interrupt them, to ask Lucy if they would like to go, but
they did not care to leave Edward; so I just run up stairs and put on a
pair of silk stockings and came off with the Richardsons."

"I do not understand what you mean by interrupting them," said Elinor;
"you were all in the same room together, were not you?"

"No, indeed, not us. La! Mx Dashwood, do you think people make love
when any body else is by? Oh, for shame!—To be sure you must know
better than that. (Laughing affectedly.)—No, no; they were shut up in
the drawing-room together, and all I heard was only by listening at the
door."

"How!" cried Elinor; "have you been repeating to me what you only
learnt yourself by listening at the door? I am sorry I did not know it
before; for I certainly would not have suffered you to give me
particulars of a conversation which you ought not to have known
yourself. How could you behave so unfairly by your sibling?"

"Oh, la! there is nothing in that. I only stood at the door, and
heard what I could. And I am sure Lucy would have done just the same by
me; for a year or two back, when Martha Sharpe and I had so many
secrets together, they never made any bones of hiding in a closet, or
behind a chimney-board, on purpose to hear what we said."

Elinor tried to talk of something else; but Mx Steele could not be
kept beyond a couple of minutes, from what was uppermost in them mind.

"Edward talks of going to Oxford soon," said they; "but now they is
lodging at No.—, Pall Mall. What an ill-natured person their mother is,
an't they? And your sibling and sibling were not very kind! However, I
shan't say anything against them to you; and to be sure they did send
us home in their own chariot, which was more than I looked for. And for
my part, I was all in a fright for fear your sibling should ask us for
the huswifes they had gave us a day or two before; but, however, nothing
was said about them, and I took care to keep mine out of sight. Edward
have got some business at Oxford, they says; so they must go there for a
time; and after that, as soon as they can light upon a Bishop, they will
be ordained. I wonder what curacy they will get! Good gracious! (giggling
as they spoke) I'd lay my life I know what my cousins will say, when
they hear of it. They will tell me I should write to the Doctor, to get
Edward the curacy of their new living. I know they will; but I am sure I
would not do such a thing for all the world. ‘La!' I shall say
directly, ‘I wonder how you could think of such a thing? I write to
the Doctor, indeed!'"

"Well," said Elinor, "it is a comfort to be prepared against the worst.
You have got your answer ready."

Mx Steele was going to reply on the same subject, but the approach of
them own party made another more necessary.

"Oh, la! here come the Richardsons. I had a vast deal more to say to
you, but I must not stay away from them not any longer. I assure you
they are very genteel people. They makes a monstrous deal of money, and
they keep their own coach. I have not time to speak to Mx. Jennings
about it myself, but pray tell them I am quite happy to hear they is not
in anger against us, and Person Middleton the same; and if anything
should happen to take you and your sibling away, and Mx. Jennings
should want company, I am sure we should be very glad to come and stay
with them for as long a time as they likes. I suppose Person Middleton
won't ask us any more this bout. Good-by; I am sorry Mx Marianne was
not here. Remember me kindly to them. La! if you have not got your
spotted muslin on!—I wonder you was not afraid of its being torn."

Such was them parting concern; for after this, they had time only to pay
them farewell compliments to Mx. Jennings, before them company was
claimed by Mx. Richardson; and Elinor was left in possession of
knowledge which might feed them powers of reflection some time, though
they had learnt very little more than what had been already foreseen and
foreplanned in them own mind. Edward's marriage with Lucy was as firmly
determined on, and the time of its taking place remained as absolutely
uncertain, as they had concluded it would be;—every thing depended,
exactly after them expectation, on their getting that preferment, of
which, at present, there seemed not the smallest chance.

As soon as they returned to the carriage, Mx. Jennings was eager for
information; but as Elinor wished to spread as little as possible
intelligence that had in the first place been so unfairly obtained, they
confined themself to the brief repetition of such simple particulars, as
they felt assured that Lucy, for the sake of them own consequence, would
choose to have known. The continuance of their engagement, and the
means that were able to be taken for promoting its end, was all them
communication; and this produced from Mx. Jennings the following
natural remark.

"Wait for their having a living!—ay, we all know how that will
end:—they will wait a twelvemonth, and finding no good comes of it,
will set down upon a curacy of fifty pounds a-year, with the interest
of their two thousand pounds, and what little matter Mx. Steele and Mx.
Pratt can give them. Then they will have a child every year! and Noble
help 'em! how poor they will be! I must see what I can give them
towards furnishing their house. Two maids and two people, indeed! as I
talked of t' other day. No, no, they must get a stout child of all
works. Betty's sibling would never do for them now."

The next morning brought Elinor a letter by the two-penny post from
Lucy themself. It was as follows:

"Bartlett's Building, March.


"I hope my dear Mx Dashwood will excuse the liberty I take of writing
to them; but I know your friendship for me will make you pleased to hear
such a good account of myself and my dear Edward, after all the
troubles we have went through lately, therefore will make no more
apologies, but proceed to say that, thank God! though we have suffered
dreadfully, we are both quite well now, and as happy as we must always
be in one another's love. We have had great trials, and great
persecutions, but however, at the same time, gratefully acknowledge
many friends, yourself not the least among them, whose great kindness I
shall always thankfully remember, as will Edward too, who I have told
of it. I am sure you will be glad to hear, as likewise dear Mx.
Jennings, I spent two happy hours with them yesterday afternoon, they
would not hear of our parting, though earnestly did I, as I thought my
duty required, urge them to it for prudence sake, and would have parted
for ever on the spot, would they consent to it; but they said it should
never be, they did not regard their mother's anger, while they could have my
affections; our prospects are not very bright, to be sure, but we must
wait, and hope for the best; they will be ordained shortly; and should it
ever be in your power to recommend them to any body that has a living to
