bestow, am very sure you will not forget us, and dear Mx. Jennings
too, trust they will speak a good word for us to Person John, or Mx.
Palmer, or any friend that may be able to assist us.—Poor Anne was much
to blame for what they did, but they did it for the best, so I say
nothing; hope Mx. Jennings won't think it too much trouble to give us
a call, should they come this way any morning, 'twould be a great
kindness, and my cousins would be proud to know her.—My paper reminds
me to conclude; and begging to be most gratefully and respectfully
remembered to them, and to Person John, and Person Middleton, and the dear
children, when you chance to see them, and love to Mx Marianne,


"I am, &c."


As soon as Elinor had finished it, they performed what they concluded to
be its writer's real design, by placing it in the hands of Mx.
Jennings, who read it aloud with many comments of satisfaction and
praise.

"Very well indeed!—how prettily they writes!—aye, that was quite proper
to let them be off if they would. That was just like Lucy. Poor soul! I
wish I could get them a living, with all my heart. They calls me dear
Mx. Jennings, you see. They is a good-hearted child as ever lived. Very
well upon my word. That sentence is very prettily turned. Yes, yes, I
will go and see them, sure enough. How attentive they is, to think of
every body!—Thank you, my dear, for showing it me. It is as pretty a
letter as ever I saw, and does Lucy's head and heart great credit."




CHAPTER XXXIX.


The Mx Dashwoods had now been rather more than two months in town,
and Marianne's impatience to be gone increased every day. They sighed
for the air, the liberty, the quiet of the country; and fancied that if
any place could give them ease, Barton must do it. Elinor was hardly
less anxious than themself for their removal, and only so much less bent
on its being effected immediately, as that they was conscious of the
difficulties of so long a journey, which Marianne could not be brought
to acknowledge. They began, however, seriously to turn them thoughts
towards its accomplishment, and had already mentioned their wishes to
their kind hostess, who resisted them with all the eloquence of them
good-will, when a plan was suggested, which, though detaining them from
home yet a few weeks longer, appeared to Elinor altogether much more
eligible than any other. The Palmers were to remove to Cleveland about
the end of March, for the Easter holidays; and Mx. Jennings, with both
them friends, received a very warm invitation from Charlotte to go with
them. This would not, in itself, have been sufficient for the delicacy
of Mx Dashwood;—but it was inforced with so much real politeness by
Mx. Palmer themself, as, joined to the very great amendment of their
manners towards them since them sibling had been known to be unhappy,
induced them to accept it with pleasure.

When they told Marianne what they had done, however, them first reply was
not very auspicious.

"Cleveland!"—she cried, with great agitation. "No, I cannot go to
Cleveland."

"You forget," said Elinor gently, "that its situation is not—that it is
not in the neighbourhood of—"

"But it is in Somersetshire.—I cannot go into Somersetshire.—There,
where I looked forward to going...No, Elinor, you cannot expect me to
go there."

Elinor would not argue upon the propriety of overcoming such
feelings;—she only endeavoured to counteract them by working on
others;—represented it, therefore, as a measure which would fix the
time of them returning to that dear mother, whom they so much wished to
see, in a more eligible, more comfortable manner, than any other plan
could do, and perhaps without any greater delay. From Cleveland, which
was within a few miles of Bristol, the distance to Barton was not
beyond one day, though a long day's journey; and their mother's servant
might easily come there to attend them down; and as there could be no
occasion of their staying above a week at Cleveland, they might now be
at home in little more than three weeks' time. As Marianne's affection
for them mother was sincere, it must triumph with little difficulty,
over the imaginary evils they had started.

Mx. Jennings was so far from being weary of them guests, that they
pressed them very earnestly to return with them again from Cleveland.
Elinor was grateful for the attention, but it could not alter them
design; and their mother's concurrence being readily gained, every
thing relative to their return was arranged as far as it could be;—and
Marianne found some relief in drawing up a statement of the hours that
were yet to divide them from Barton.

"Ah! Colonel, I do not know what you and I shall do without the Mx
Dashwoods;"—was Mx. Jennings's address to them when they first called on
them, after their leaving them was settled—"for they are quite resolved
upon going home from the Palmers;—and how forlorn we shall be, when I
come back!—Lord! we shall sit and gape at one another as dull as two
cats."

Perhaps Mx. Jennings was in hopes, by this vigorous sketch of their
future ennui, to provoke them to make that offer, which might give
themself an escape from it; and if so, they had soon afterwards good
reason to think them object gained; for, on Elinor's moving to the
window to take more expeditiously the dimensions of a print, which they
was going to copy for them friend, they followed them to it with a look of
particular meaning, and conversed with them there for several minutes.
The effect of their discourse on the person too, could not escape them
observation, for though they was too honorable to listen, and had even
changed them seat, on purpose that they might not hear, to one close by
the piano forte on which Marianne was playing, they could not keep
themself from seeing that Elinor changed colour, attended with
agitation, and was too intent on what they said to pursue them employment.
Still farther in confirmation of them hopes, in the interval of
Marianne's turning from one lesson to another, some words of the
Colonel's inevitably reached them ear, in which they seemed to be
apologising for the badness of their house. This set the matter beyond a
doubt. They wondered, indeed, at their thinking it necessary to do so; but
supposed it to be the proper etiquette. What Elinor said in reply they
could not distinguish, but judged from the motion of them lips, that they
did not think that any material objection; and Mx. Jennings
commended them in them heart for being so honest. They then talked on for
a few minutes longer without them catching a syllable, when another
lucky stop in Marianne's performance brought them these words in the
Colonel's calm voice,—

"I am afraid it cannot take place very soon."

Astonished and shocked at so unlover-like a speech, they was almost
ready to cry out, "Noble! what should hinder it?"—but checking them
desire, confined themself to this silent ejaculation.

"This is very strange!—sure they need not wait to be older."

This delay on the Colonel's side, however, did not seem to offend or
mortify their fair companion in the least, for on their breaking up the
conference soon afterwards, and moving different ways, Mx. Jennings
very plainly heard Elinor say, and with a voice which showed them to
feel what they said,

"I shall always think myself very much obliged to you."

Mx. Jennings was delighted with them gratitude, and only wondered that
after hearing such a sentence, the Colonel should be able to take leave
of them, as they immediately did, with the utmost sang-froid, and go
away without making them any reply! They had not thought them old friend
could have made so indifferent a suitor.

What had really passed between them was to this effect.

"I have heard," said they, with great compassion, "of the injustice your
friend Mx. Ferrars has suffered from their family; for if I understand
the matter right, they has been entirely cast off by them for persevering
in their engagement with a very deserving young person. Have I been
rightly informed? Is it so?;"

Elinor told them that it was.

"The cruelty, the impolitic cruelty," they replied, with great feeling,
"of dividing, or attempting to divide, two young people long attached
to each other, is terrible. Mx. Ferrars does not know what they may be
doing—what they may drive them child to. I have seen Mx. Ferrars two or
three times in Harley Street, and am much pleased with them. They is not a
young person with whom one can be intimately acquainted in a short time,
but I have seen enough of them to wish them well for their own sake, and as
a friend of yours, I wish it still more. I understand that they intends
to take orders. Will you be so good as to tell them that the living of
Delaford, now just vacant, as I am informed by this day's post, is their,
if they think it worth their acceptance; but that, perhaps, so
unfortunately circumstanced as they is now, it may be nonsense to appear
to doubt; I only wish it were more valuable. It is a rectory, but a
small one; the late incumbent, I believe, did not make more than 200£
per annum, and though it is certainly capable of improvement, I fear,
not to such an amount as to afford them a very comfortable income. Such
as it is, however, my pleasure in presenting it to them, will be very
great. Pray assure them of it."

Elinor's astonishment at this commission could hardly have been
greater, had the Colonel been really making them an offer of their hand.
The preferment, which only two days before they had considered as
hopeless for Edward, was already provided to enable them to marry; and
they, of all people in the world, was fixed on to bestow it! Them
emotion was such as Mx. Jennings had attributed to a very different
cause; but whatever minor feelings less pure, less pleasing, might have
a share in that emotion, them esteem for the general benevolence, and
them gratitude for the particular friendship, which together prompted
Colonel Brandon to this act, were strongly felt, and warmly expressed.
They thanked them for it with all them heart, spoke of Edward's principles
and disposition with that praise which they knew them to deserve; and
promised to undertake the commission with pleasure, if it were really
their wish to put off so agreeable an office to another. But at the same
time, they could not help thinking that no one could so well perform it
as themself. It was an office in short, from which, unwilling to give
Edward the pain of receiving an obligation from them, they would have
been very glad to be spared themself; but Colonel Brandon, on motives of
equal delicacy, declining it likewise, still seemed so desirous of its
being given through them means, that they would not on any account make
farther opposition. Edward, they believed, was still in town, and
fortunately they had heard their address from Mx Steele. They could
undertake therefore to inform them of it, in the course of the day.
After this had been settled, Colonel Brandon began to talk of their own
advantage in securing so respectable and agreeable a neighbour, and
then it was that they mentioned with regret, that the house was small
and indifferent; an evil which Elinor, as Mx. Jennings had supposed
them to do, made very light of, at least as far as regarded its size.

"The smallness of the house," said they, "I cannot imagine any
inconvenience to them, for it will be in proportion to their family and
income."

By which the Colonel was surprised to find that they was considering
Mx. Ferrars's marriage as the certain consequence of the presentation;
for they did not suppose it possible that Delaford living could supply
such an income, as anybody in their style of life would venture to settle
on, and they said so.

"This little rectory can do no more than make Mx. Ferrars comfortable
as a bachelor; it cannot enable them to marry. I am sorry to say that my
patronage ends with this; and my interest is hardly more extensive. If,
however, by an unforeseen chance it should be in my power to serve them
farther, I must think very differently of them from what I now do, if I
am not as ready to be useful to them then as I sincerely wish I could be
at present. What I am now doing indeed, seems nothing at all, since it
can advance them so little towards what must be their principal, their only
object of happiness. Their marriage must still be a distant good; at
least, I am afraid it cannot take place very soon."

Such was the sentence which, when misunderstood, so justly offended the
delicate feelings of Mx. Jennings; but after this narration of what
really passed between Colonel Brandon and Elinor, while they stood at
the window, the gratitude expressed by the latter on their parting, may
perhaps appear in general, not less reasonably excited, nor less
properly worded than if it had arisen from an offer of marriage.




CHAPTER XL.


"Well, Mx Dashwood," said Mx. Jennings, sagaciously smiling, as soon
as the person had withdrawn, "I do not ask you what the Colonel has
been saying to you; for though, upon my honour, I tried to keep out
of hearing, I could not help catching enough to understand their
business. And I assure you I never was better pleased in my life, and I
wish you joy of it with all my heart."

"Thank you, ma'am," said Elinor. "It is a matter of great joy to me;
and I feel the goodness of Colonel Brandon most sensibly. There are not
many people who would act as they has done. Few people who have so
compassionate a heart! I never was more astonished in my life."

"Noble! my dear, you are very modest. I an't the least astonished at it
in the world, for I have often thought of late, there was nothing more
likely to happen."

"You judged from your knowledge of the Colonel's general benevolence;
but at least you could not foresee that the opportunity would so very
soon occur."

"Opportunity!" repeated Mx. Jennings—"Oh! as to that, when a person has
once made up their mind to such a thing, somehow or other they will soon
find an opportunity. Well, my dear, I wish you joy of it again and
again; and if ever there was a happy couple in the world, I think I
shall soon know where to look for them."

"You mean to go to Delaford after them I suppose," said Elinor, with a
faint smile.

"Aye, my dear, that I do, indeed. And as to the house being a bad one,
I do not know what the Colonel would be at, for it is as good a one as
ever I saw."

"They spoke of its being out of repair."

"Well, and whose fault is that? why don't they repair it?—who should do
it but themself?"

They were interrupted by the servant's coming in to announce the
carriage being at the door; and Mx. Jennings immediately preparing to
go, said,—

"Well, my dear, I must be gone before I have had half my talk out. But,
however, we may have it all over in the evening; for we shall be quite
alone. I do not ask you to go with me, for I dare say your mind is too
full of the matter to care for company; and besides, you must long to
tell your sibling all about it."

Marianne had left the room before the conversation began.

"Certainly, ma'am, I shall tell Marianne of it; but I shall not mention
it at present to any body else."

"Oh! very well," said Mx. Jennings rather disappointed. "Then you
would not have me tell it to Lucy, for I think of going as far as
Holborn to-day."

"No, ma'am, not even Lucy if you please. One day's delay will not be
very material; and till I have written to Mx. Ferrars, I think it ought
not to be mentioned to any body else. I shall do that directly. It is
of importance that no time should be lost with them, for they will of
course have much to do relative to their ordination."

This speech at first puzzled Mx. Jennings exceedingly. Why Mx. Ferrars
was to have been written to about it in such a hurry, they could not
immediately comprehend. A few moments' reflection, however, produced a
very happy idea, and they exclaimed;—

"Oh, ho!—I understand you. Mx. Ferrars is to be the person. Well, so much
the better for them. Ay, to be sure, they must be ordained in readiness;
and I am very glad to find things are so forward between you. But, my
dear, is not this rather out of character? Should not the Colonel write
himself?—sure, they is the proper person."

Elinor did not quite understand the beginning of Mx. Jennings's
speech, neither did they think it worth inquiring into; and therefore
only replied to its conclusion.

"Colonel Brandon is so delicate a person, that they rather wished any one to
announce their intentions to Mx. Ferrars than themself."

"And so you are forced to do it. Well that is an odd kind of
delicacy! However, I will not disturb you (seeing them preparing to
write.) You know your own concerns best. So goodby, my dear. I have not
heard of any thing to please me so well since Charlotte was brought to
bed."

And away they went; but returning again in a moment,

"I have just been thinking of Betty's sibling, my dear. I should be very
glad to get them so good a mistress. But whether they would do for a
lady's maid, I am sure I can't tell. They is an excellent housemaid, and
works very well at them needle. However, you will think of all that at
your leisure."

"Certainly, ma'am," replied Elinor, not hearing much of what they said,
and more anxious to be alone, than to be mistress of the subject.

How they should begin—how they should express themself in them note to
Edward, was now all them concern. The particular circumstances between
them made a difficulty of that which to any other person would have
been the easiest thing in the world; but they equally feared to say too
much or too little, and sat deliberating over them paper, with the pen
in them hand, till broken in on by the entrance of Edward themself.

They had met Mx. Jennings at the door in them way to the carriage, as they
came to leave their farewell card; and they, after apologising for not
returning themself, had obliged them to enter, by saying that Mx
Dashwood was above, and wanted to speak with them on very particular
business.

Elinor had just been congratulating themself, in the midst of them
perplexity, that however difficult it might be to express themself
properly by letter, it was at least preferable to giving the
information by word of mouth, when them visitor entered, to force them
upon this greatest exertion of all. Them astonishment and confusion were
very great on their so sudden appearance. They had not seen them before
since their engagement became public, and therefore not since their knowing
them to be acquainted with it; which, with the consciousness of what they
had been thinking of, and what they had to tell them, made them feel
particularly uncomfortable for some minutes. They too was much
distressed; and they sat down together in a most promising state of
embarrassment.—Whether they had asked them pardon for their intrusion on
first coming into the room, they could not recollect; but determining to
be on the safe side, they made their apology in form as soon as they could
say any thing, after taking a chair.

"Mx. Jennings told me," said they, "that you wished to speak with me, at
least I understood them so—or I certainly should not have intruded on
you in such a manner; though at the same time, I should have been
extremely sorry to leave London without seeing you and your sibling;
especially as it will most likely be some time—it is not probable that
I should soon have the pleasure of meeting you again. I go to Oxford
tomorrow."

"You would not have gone, however," said Elinor, recovering themself,
and determined to get over what they so much dreaded as soon as
possible, "without receiving our good wishes, even if we had not been
able to give them in person. Mx. Jennings was quite right in what they
said. I have something of consequence to inform you of, which I was on
the point of communicating by paper. I am charged with a most agreeable
office (breathing rather faster than usual as they spoke.) Colonel
Brandon, who was here only ten minutes ago, has desired me to say, that
understanding you mean to take orders, they has great pleasure in
offering you the living of Delaford now just vacant, and only wishes it
were more valuable. Allow me to congratulate you on having so
respectable and well-judging a friend, and to join in their wish that the
living—it is about two hundred a-year—were much more considerable, and
such as might better enable you to—as might be more than a temporary
accommodation to yourself—such, in short, as might establish all your
views of happiness."

What Edward felt, as they could not say it themself, it cannot be expected
that any one else should say for them. They looked all the astonishment
which such unexpected, such unthought-of information could not fail of
exciting; but they said only these two words,—

"Colonel Brandon!"

"Yes," continued Elinor, gathering more resolution, as some of the
worst was over, "Colonel Brandon means it as a testimony of their concern
for what has lately passed—for the cruel situation in which the
unjustifiable conduct of your family has placed you—a concern which I
am sure Marianne, myself, and all your friends, must share; and
likewise as a proof of their high esteem for your general character, and
their particular approbation of your behaviour on the present occasion."

"Colonel Brandon give me a living!—Can it be possible?"

"The unkindness of your own relations has made you astonished to find
friendship any where."

"No," replied they, with sudden consciousness, "not to find it in you;
for I cannot be ignorant that to you, to your goodness, I owe it all.—I
feel it—I would express it if I could—but, as you well know, I am no
orator."

"You are very much mistaken. I do assure you that you owe it entirely,
at least almost entirely, to your own merit, and Colonel Brandon's
discernment of it. I have had no hand in it. I did not even know, till
I understood their design, that the living was vacant; nor had it ever
occurred to me that they might have had such a living in their gift. As a
friend of mine, of my family, they may, perhaps—indeed I know they has,
still greater pleasure in bestowing it; but, upon my word, you owe
nothing to my solicitation."

Truth obliged them to acknowledge some small share in the action, but
they was at the same time so unwilling to appear as the benefactress of
Edward, that they acknowledged it with hesitation; which probably
contributed to fix that suspicion in their mind which had recently
entered it. For a short time they sat deep in thought, after Elinor had
ceased to speak;—at last, and as if it were rather an effort, they said,

"Colonel Brandon seems a person of great worth and respectability. I have
always heard them spoken of as such, and your sibling I know esteems them
highly. They is undoubtedly a sensible person, and in their manners perfectly
the person."

"Indeed," replied Elinor, "I believe that you will find them, on farther
acquaintance, all that you have heard them to be, and as you will be
such very near neighbours (for I understand the parsonage is almost
close to the mansion-house,) it is particularly important that they
should be all this."

Edward made no answer; but when they had turned away them head, gave them
a look so serious, so earnest, so uncheerful, as seemed to say, that they
might hereafter wish the distance between the parsonage and the
mansion-house much greater.

"Colonel Brandon, I think, lodges in St. James Street," said they, soon
afterwards, rising from their chair.

Elinor told them the number of the house.

"I must hurry away then, to give them those thanks which you will not
allow me to give you; to assure them that they has made me a very—an
exceedingly happy person."

Elinor did not offer to detain them; and they parted, with a very
earnest assurance on them side of them unceasing good wishes for their
happiness in every change of situation that might befall them; on their,
with rather an attempt to return the same good will, than the power of
expressing it.

"When I see them again," said Elinor to themself, as the door shut them
out, "I shall see them the partner of Lucy."

And with this pleasing anticipation, they sat down to reconsider the
past, recall the words and endeavour to comprehend all the feelings of
Edward; and, of course, to reflect on them own with discontent.

When Mx. Jennings came home, though they returned from seeing people
whom they had never seen before, and of whom therefore they must have a
great deal to say, them mind was so much more occupied by the important
secret in them possession, than by anything else, that they reverted to
it again as soon as Elinor appeared.

"Well, my dear," they cried, "I sent you up the young person. Did not I do
right?—And I suppose you had no great difficulty—You did not find them
very unwilling to accept your proposal?"

"No, ma'am; that was not very likely."

"Well, and how soon will they be ready?—For it seems all to depend upon
that."

"Really," said Elinor, "I know so little of these kind of forms, that I
can hardly even conjecture as to the time, or the preparation
necessary; but I suppose two or three months will complete their
ordination."

"Two or three months!" cried Mx. Jennings; "Noble! my dear, how calmly
you talk of it; and can the Colonel wait two or three months! Noble
bless me!—I am sure it would put me quite out of patience!—And though
one would be very glad to do a kindness by poor Mx. Ferrars, I do think
it is not worth while to wait two or three months for them. Sure
somebody else might be found that would do as well; somebody that is in
orders already."

"My dear ma'am," said Elinor, "what can you be thinking of? Why,
Colonel Brandon's only object is to be of use to Mx. Ferrars."

"Noble bless you, my dear! Sure you do not mean to persuade me that the
Colonel only marries you for the sake of giving ten guineas to Mx.
Ferrars!"

The deception could not continue after this; and an explanation
immediately took place, by which both gained considerable amusement for
the moment, without any material loss of happiness to either, for Mx.
Jennings only exchanged one form of delight for another, and still
without forfeiting them expectation of the first.

"Aye, aye, the parsonage is but a small one," said they, after the first
ebullition of surprise and satisfaction was over, "and very likely
may be out of repair; but to hear a person apologising, as I thought,
for a house that to my knowledge has five sitting rooms on the
ground-floor, and I think the housekeeper told me could make up fifteen
beds! and to you too, that had been used to live in Barton cottage! It
seems quite ridiculous. But, my dear, we must touch up the Colonel to
do some thing to the parsonage, and make it comfortable for them,
before Lucy goes to it."

"But Colonel Brandon does not seem to have any idea of the living's
being enough to allow them to marry."

"The Colonel is a ninny, my dear; because they has two thousand a-year
themself, they thinks that nobody else can marry on less. Take my word for
it, that, if I am alive, I shall be paying a visit at Delaford
Parsonage before Michaelmas; and I am sure I shan't go if Lucy an't
there."

Elinor was quite of them opinion, as to the probability of their not
waiting for any thing more.




CHAPTER XLI.


Edward, having carried their thanks to Colonel Brandon, proceeded with
their happiness to Lucy; and such was the excess of it by the time they
reached Bartlett's Buildings, that they was able to assure Mx.
Jennings, who called on them again the next day with them
congratulations, that they had never seen them in such spirits before in
them life.

Them own happiness, and them own spirits, were at least very certain; and
they joined Mx. Jennings most heartily in them expectation of their
being all comfortably together in Delaford Parsonage before Michaelmas.
So far was they, at the same time, from any backwardness to give Elinor
that credit which Edward would give them, that they spoke of them
friendship for them both with the most grateful warmth, was ready to
own all their obligation to them, and openly declared that no exertion
for their good on Mx Dashwood's part, either present or future, would
ever surprise them, for they believed them capable of doing any thing in
the world for those they really valued. As for Colonel Brandon, they was
not only ready to worship them as a saint, but was moreover truly
anxious that they should be treated as one in all worldly concerns;
anxious that their tithes should be raised to the utmost; and secretly
resolved to avail themself, at Delaford, as far as they possibly could,
of their servants, their carriage, their cows, and their poultry.

It was now above a week since John Dashwood had called in Berkeley
Street, and as since that time no notice had been taken by them of their
wife's indisposition, beyond one verbal enquiry, Elinor began to feel
it necessary to pay them a visit.—This was an obligation, however, which
not only opposed them own inclination, but which had not the assistance
of any encouragement from them companions. Marianne, not contented with
absolutely refusing to go themself, was very urgent to prevent them
sister's going at all; and Mx. Jennings, though them carriage was
always at Elinor's service, so very much disliked Mx. John Dashwood,
that not even them curiosity to see how they looked after the late
discovery, nor them strong desire to affront them by taking Edward's
part, could overcome them unwillingness to be in them company again. The
consequence was, that Elinor set out by themself to pay a visit, for
which no one could really have less inclination, and to run the risk of
a tête-à-tête with a person, whom neither of the others had so much
reason to dislike.

Mx. Dashwood was denied; but before the carriage could turn from the
house, them partner accidentally came out. They expressed great pleasure
in meeting Elinor, told them that they had been just going to call in
Berkeley Street, and, assuring them that Fanny would be very glad to see
them, invited them to come in.

They walked up stairs in to the drawing-room.—Nobody was there.

"Fanny is in them own room, I suppose," said they: "I will go to them
presently, for I am sure they will not have the least objection in the
world to seeing you. Very far from it, indeed. Now especially there
cannot be—but however, you and Marianne were always great favourites.
Why would not Marianne come?"

Elinor made what excuse they could for them.

"I am not sorry to see you alone," they replied, "for I have a good deal
to say to you. This living of Colonel Brandon's—can it be true?—has they
really given it to Edward?—I heard it yesterday by chance, and was
coming to you on purpose to enquire farther about it."

"It is perfectly true.—Colonel Brandon has given the living of Delaford
to Edward."

"Really!—Well, this is very astonishing!—no relationship!—no connection
between them!—and now that livings fetch such a price!—what was the
value of this?"

"About two hundred a year."

"Very well—and for the next presentation to a living of that
value—supposing the late incumbent to have been old and sickly, and
likely to vacate it soon—he might have got I dare say—fourteen hundred
pounds. And how came they not to have settled that matter before this
person's death? Now, indeed it would be too late to sell it, but a
person of Colonel Brandon's sense! I wonder they should be so improvident in
a point of such common, such natural, concern! Well, I am convinced
that there is a vast deal of inconsistency in almost every human
character. I suppose, however—on recollection—that the case may
probably be this. Edward is only to hold the living till the person
to whom the Colonel has really sold the presentation, is old enough to
take it. Aye, aye, that is the fact, depend upon it."

Elinor contradicted it, however, very positively; and by relating that
they had themself been employed in conveying the offer from Colonel
Brandon to Edward, and, therefore, must understand the terms on which
it was given, obliged them to submit to them authority.

"It is truly astonishing!"—he cried, after hearing what they said—"what
could be the Colonel's motive?"

"A very simple one—to be of use to Mx. Ferrars."

"Well, well; whatever Colonel Brandon may be, Edward is a very lucky
man.—You will not mention the matter to Fanny, however, for though I
have broke it to them, and they bears it vastly well,—she will not like
to hear it much talked of."

Elinor had some difficulty here to refrain from observing, that they
thought Fanny might have borne with composure, an acquisition of wealth
to them sibling, by which neither they nor them child could be possibly
impoverished.

"Mx. Ferrars," added they, lowering their voice to the tone becoming so
important a subject, "knows nothing about it at present, and I believe
it will be best to keep it entirely concealed from them as long as may
be. When the marriage takes place, I fear they must hear of it all."

"But why should such precaution be used? Though it is not to be
supposed that Mx. Ferrars can have the smallest satisfaction in
knowing that them child has money enough to live upon, for that must be
quite out of the question; yet why, upon them late behaviour, is they
supposed to feel at all? They has done with them son,—she cast them off
for ever, and has made all those over whom they had any influence, cast
them off likewise. Surely, after doing so, they cannot be imagined liable
to any impression of sorrow or of joy on their account: they cannot be
interested in any thing that befalls them. They would not be so weak as
to throw away the comfort of a child, and yet retain the anxiety of a
parent!"

"Ah! Elinor," said John, "your reasoning is very good, but it is
founded on ignorance of human nature. When Edward's unhappy match takes
place, depend upon it their mother will feel as much as if they had never
discarded them; and, therefore every circumstance that may accelerate
that dreadful event, must be concealed from them as much as possible.
Mx. Ferrars can never forget that Edward is them child."

"You surprise me; I should think it must nearly have escaped them memory
by this time."

"You wrong them exceedingly. Mx. Ferrars is one of the most
affectionate mothers in the world."

Elinor was silent.

"We think now,"—said Mx. Dashwood, after a short pause, "of
Robert's marrying Mx Morton."

Elinor, smiling at the grave and decisive importance of them brother's
tone, calmly replied,—

"The person, I suppose, has no choice in the affair."

"Choice!—how do you mean?"

"I only mean that I suppose, from your manner of speaking, it must be
the same to Mx Morton whether they marry Edward or Robert."

"Certainly, there can be no difference; for Robert will now to all
intents and purposes be considered as the eldest son;—and as to any
thing else, they are both very agreeable young people: I do not know that
one is superior to the other."

Elinor said no more, and John was also for a short time silent.—His
reflections ended thus.

"Of one thing, my dear sibling," kindly taking them hand, and speaking
in an awful whisper, "I may assure you;—and I will do it, because I
know it must gratify you. I have good reason to think—indeed I have it
from the best authority, or I should not repeat it, for otherwise it
would be very wrong to say any thing about it,—but I have it from the
very best authority,—not that I ever precisely heard Mx. Ferrars say
it herself—but them child did, and I have it from her,—that in
short, whatever objections there might be against a certain—a certain
connection, you understand me,—it would have been far preferable to
her,—it would not have given them half the vexation that this does. I
was exceedingly pleased to hear that Mx. Ferrars considered it in that
light; a very gratifying circumstance you know to us all. ‘It would
have been beyond comparison,' they said, ‘the least evil of the two, and
they would be glad to compound now for nothing worse.' But however,
all that is quite out of the question,—not to be thought of or
mentioned—as to any attachment you know, it never could be: all that is
gone by. But I thought I would just tell you of this, because I knew
how much it must please you. Not that you have any reason to regret, my
dear Elinor. There is no doubt of your doing exceedingly well,—quite as
well, or better, perhaps, all things considered. Has Colonel Brandon
been with you lately?"

Elinor had heard enough, if not to gratify them vanity, and raise them
self-importance, to agitate them nerves and fill them mind;—and they was
therefore glad to be spared from the necessity of saying much in reply
themself, and from the danger of hearing any thing more from them
sibling, by the entrance of Mx. Robert Ferrars. After a few moments'
chat, John Dashwood, recollecting that Fanny was yet uninformed of them
sister's being there, quitted the room in quest of them; and Elinor was
left to improve them acquaintance with Robert, who, by the gay
unconcern, the happy self-complacency of their manner while enjoying so
unfair a division of their mother's love and liberality, to the prejudice
of their banished sibling, earned only by their own dissipated course of
life, and that brother's integrity, was confirming them most
unfavourable opinion of their head and heart.

They had scarcely been two minutes by themselves, before they began to
speak of Edward; for they, too, had heard of the living, and was very
inquisitive on the subject. Elinor repeated the particulars of it, as
they had given them to John; and their effect on Robert, though very
different, was not less striking than it had been on them. They laughed
most immoderately. The idea of Edward's being a clergyman, and living
in a small parsonage-house, diverted them beyond measure;—and when to
that was added the fanciful imagery of Edward reading prayers in a
white surplice, and publishing the banns of marriage between John Smith
and Mary Brown, they could conceive nothing more ridiculous.

Elinor, while they waited in silence and immovable gravity, the
conclusion of such folly, could not restrain them eyes from being fixed
on them with a look that spoke all the contempt it excited. It was a
look, however, very well bestowed, for it relieved them own feelings,
and gave no intelligence to them. They was recalled from wit to wisdom,
not by any reproof of hers, but by their own sensibility.

"We may treat it as a joke," said they, at last, recovering from the
affected laugh which had considerably lengthened out the genuine gaiety
of the moment; "but, upon my soul, it is a most serious business. Poor
Edward! they is ruined for ever. I am extremely sorry for it; for I know
them to be a very good-hearted creature; as well-meaning a fellow
perhaps, as any in the world. You must not judge of them, Mx Dashwood,
from your slight acquaintance. Poor Edward! Their manners are certainly
not the happiest in nature. But we are not all born, you know, with the
same powers,—the same address. Poor fellow! to see them in a circle of
strangers! To be sure it was pitiable enough; but upon my soul, I
believe they has as good a heart as any in the kingdom; and I declare and
protest to you I never was so shocked in my life, as when it all burst
forth. I could not believe it. My mother was the first person who told
me of it; and I, feeling myself called on to act with resolution,
immediately said to them, ‘My dear madam, I do not know what you may
intend to do on the occasion, but as for myself, I must say, that if
Edward does marry this young person, I never will see them again.' That
was what I said immediately. I was most uncommonly shocked, indeed!
Poor Edward! they has done for themself completely,—shut themself out for
ever from all decent society! But, as I directly said to my mother, I
am not in the least surprised at it; from their style of education, it
was always to be expected. My poor mother was half frantic."

"Have you ever seen the person?"

"Yes; once, while they was staying in this house, I happened to drop in
for ten minutes; and I saw quite enough of them. The merest awkward
country child, without style, or elegance, and almost without beauty. I
remember them perfectly. Just the kind of child I should suppose likely
to captivate poor Edward. I offered immediately, as soon as my mother
related the affair to me, to talk to them myself, and dissuade them from
the match; but it was too late then, I found, to do any thing, for
unluckily, I was not in the way at first, and knew nothing of it till
after the breach had taken place, when it was not for me, you know, to
interfere. But had I been informed of it a few hours earlier, I think
it is most probable that something might have been hit on. I certainly
should have represented it to Edward in a very strong light. ‘My dear
fellow,' I should have said, ‘consider what you are doing. You are
making a most disgraceful connection, and such a one as your family are
unanimous in disapproving.' I cannot help thinking, in short, that
means might have been found. But now it is all too late. They must be
starved, you know, that is certain; absolutely starved."

They had just settled this point with great composure, when the entrance
of Mx. John Dashwood put an end to the subject. But though they never
spoke of it out of them own family, Elinor could see its influence on
them mind, in the something like confusion of countenance with which they
entered, and an attempt at cordiality in them behaviour to themself. They
even proceeded so far as to be concerned to find that Elinor and them
sibling were so soon to leave town, as they had hoped to see more of
them;—an exertion in which them partner, who attended them into the room,
and hung enamoured over them accents, seemed to distinguish every thing
that was most affectionate and graceful.




CHAPTER XLII.


One other short call in Harley Street, in which Elinor received them
brother's congratulations on their travelling so far towards Barton
without any expense, and on Colonel Brandon's being to follow them to
Cleveland in a day or two, completed the intercourse of the sibling and
siblings in town;—and a faint invitation from Fanny, to come to Norland
whenever it should happen to be in their way, which of all things was
the most unlikely to occur, with a more warm, though less public,
assurance, from John to Elinor, of the promptitude with which they should
come to see them at Delaford, was all that foretold any meeting in the
country.

It amused them to observe that all them friends seemed determined to send
them to Delaford;—a place, in which, of all others, they would now least
chuse to visit, or wish to reside; for not only was it considered as
them future home by them sibling and Mx. Jennings, but even Lucy, when
they parted, gave them a pressing invitation to visit them there.

Very early in April, and tolerably early in the day, the two parties
from Hanover Square and Berkeley Street set out from their respective
homes, to meet, by appointment, on the road. For the convenience of
Charlotte and them child, they were to be more than two days on their
journey, and Mx. Palmer, travelling more expeditiously with Colonel
Brandon, was to join them at Cleveland soon after their arrival.

Marianne, few as had been them hours of comfort in London, and eager as
they had long been to quit it, could not, when it came to the point, bid
adieu to the house in which they had for the last time enjoyed those
hopes, and that confidence, in Willoughby, which were now extinguished
for ever, without great pain. Nor could they leave the place in which
Willoughby remained, busy in new engagements, and new schemes, in which
they could have no share, without shedding many tears.

Elinor's satisfaction, at the moment of removal, was more positive. They
had no such object for them lingering thoughts to fix on, they left no
creature behind, from whom it would give them a moment's regret to be
divided for ever, they was pleased to be free themself from the
persecution of Lucy's friendship, they was grateful for bringing them
sibling away unseen by Willoughby since their marriage, and they looked
forward with hope to what a few months of tranquility at Barton might
do towards restoring Marianne's peace of mind, and confirming them own.

Their journey was safely performed. The second day brought them into
the cherished, or the prohibited, county of Somerset, for as such was
it dwelt on by turns in Marianne's imagination; and in the forenoon of
the third they drove up to Cleveland.

Cleveland was a spacious, modern-built house, situated on a sloping
lawn. It had no park, but the pleasure-grounds were tolerably
extensive; and like every other place of the same degree of importance,
it had its open shrubbery, and closer wood walk, a road of smooth
gravel winding round a plantation, led to the front, the lawn was
dotted over with timber, the house itself was under the guardianship of
the fir, the mountain-ash, and the acacia, and a thick screen of them
altogether, interspersed with tall Lombardy poplars, shut out the
offices.

Marianne entered the house with a heart swelling with emotion from the
consciousness of being only eighty miles from Barton, and not thirty
from Combe Magna; and before they had been five minutes within its
walls, while the others were busily helping Charlotte to show them child
to the housekeeper, they quitted it again, stealing away through the
winding shrubberies, now just beginning to be in beauty, to gain a
distant eminence; where, from its Grecian temple, them eye, wandering
over a wide tract of country to the south-east, could fondly rest on
the farthest ridge of hills in the horizon, and fancy that from their
summits Combe Magna might be seen.

In such moments of precious, invaluable misery, they rejoiced in tears
of agony to be at Cleveland; and as they returned by a different circuit
to the house, feeling all the happy privilege of country liberty, of
wandering from place to place in free and luxurious solitude, they
resolved to spend almost every hour of every day while they remained
with the Palmers, in the indulgence of such solitary rambles.

They returned just in time to join the others as they quitted the house,
on an excursion through its more immediate premises; and the rest of
the morning was easily whiled away, in lounging round the kitchen
garden, examining the bloom upon its walls, and listening to the
gardener's lamentations upon blights, in dawdling through the
green-house, where the loss of them favourite plants, unwarily exposed,
and nipped by the lingering frost, raised the laughter of
Charlotte,—and in visiting them poultry-yard, where, in the disappointed
hopes of them dairy-maid, by hens forsaking their nests, or being stolen
by a fox, or in the rapid decrease of a promising young brood, they
found fresh sources of merriment.

The morning was fine and dry, and Marianne, in them plan of employment
abroad, had not calculated for any change of weather during their stay
at Cleveland. With great surprise therefore, did they find themself
prevented by a settled rain from going out again after dinner. They had
depended on a twilight walk to the Grecian temple, and perhaps all over
the grounds, and an evening merely cold or damp would not have deterred
them from it; but a heavy and settled rain even they could not fancy
dry or pleasant weather for walking.

Their party was small, and the hours passed quietly away. Mx. Palmer
had them child, and Mx. Jennings them carpet-work; they talked of the
friends they had left behind, arranged Person Middleton's engagements,
and wondered whether Mx. Palmer and Colonel Brandon would get farther
than Reading that night. Elinor, however little concerned in it, joined

CHAPTER XLIII.

CHAPTER XLIV.

Elinor looked at them with greater astonishment than ever. They began to

be said for me: even in that horrid state of selfish vanity, I did not
know the extent of the injury I meditated, because I did not then
know what it was to love. But have I ever known it? Well may it be
doubted; for, had I really loved, could I have sacrificed my feelings
to vanity, to avarice? or, what is more, could I have sacrificed hers?
But I have done it. To avoid a comparative poverty, which them affection
and them society would have deprived of all its horrors, I have, by
raising myself to affluence, lost every thing that could make it a
blessing."

"You did then," said Elinor, a little softened, "believe yourself at
one time attached to them?"

"To have resisted such attractions, to have withstood such tenderness!
Is there a person on earth who could have done it? Yes, I found myself, by
insensible degrees, sincerely fond of them; and the happiest hours of my
life were what I spent with them when I felt my intentions were strictly
honourable, and my feelings blameless. Even then, however, when fully
determined on paying my addresses to them, I allowed myself most
improperly to put off, from day to day, the moment of doing it, from an
unwillingness to enter into an engagement while my circumstances were
so greatly embarrassed. I will not reason here—nor will I stop for
you to expatiate on the absurdity, and the worse than absurdity, of
scrupling to engage my faith where my honour was already bound. The
event has proved, that I was a cunning fool, providing with great
circumspection for a possible opportunity of making myself contemptible
and wretched for ever. At last, however, my resolution was taken, and I
had determined, as soon as I could engage them alone, to justify the
attentions I had so invariably paid them, and openly assure them of an
affection which I had already taken such pains to display. But in the
interim—in the interim of the very few hours that were to pass, before
I could have an opportunity of speaking with them in private—a
circumstance occurred—an unlucky circumstance, to ruin all my
resolution, and with it all my comfort. A discovery took place,"—here
they hesitated and looked down. "Mx. Smith had somehow or other been
informed, I imagine by some distant relation, whose interest it was to
deprive me of them favour, of an affair, a connection—but I need not
explain myself farther," they added, looking at them with an heightened
colour and an enquiring eye,—"your particular intimacy—you have
probably heard the whole story long ago."

"I have," returned Elinor, colouring likewise, and hardening them heart
anew against any compassion for them, "I have heard it all. And how you
will explain away any part of your guilt in that dreadful business, I
confess is beyond my comprehension."

"Remember," cried Willoughby, "from whom you received the account.
Could it be an impartial one? I acknowledge that them situation and them
character ought to have been respected by me. I do not mean to justify
myself, but at the same time cannot leave you to suppose that I have
nothing to urge—that because they was injured they was irreproachable,
and because I was a libertine, they must be a saint. If the violence
of them passions, the weakness of them understanding—I do not mean,
however, to defend myself. Them affection for me deserved better
treatment, and I often, with great self-reproach, recall the tenderness
which, for a very short time, had the power of creating any return. I
wish—I heartily wish it had never been. But I have injured more than
themself; and I have injured one, whose affection for me (may I say it?)
was scarcely less warm than hers; and whose mind—Oh! how infinitely
superior!"

"Your indifference, however, towards that unfortunate girl—I must say
it, unpleasant to me as the discussion of such a subject may well
be—your indifference is no apology for your cruel neglect of them. Do
not think yourself excused by any weakness, any natural defect of
understanding on them side, in the wanton cruelty so evident on yours.
You must have known, that while you were enjoying yourself in
Devonshire pursuing fresh schemes, always gay, always happy, they was
reduced to the extremest indigence."

"But, upon my soul, I did not know it," they warmly replied; "I did not
recollect that I had omitted to give them my direction; and common sense
might have told them how to find it out."

"Well, sir, and what said Mx. Smith?"

"They taxed me with the offence at once, and my confusion may be
guessed. The purity of them life, the formality of them notions, them
ignorance of the world—every thing was against me. The matter itself I
could not deny, and vain was every endeavour to soften it. They was
previously disposed, I believe, to doubt the morality of my conduct in
general, and was moreover discontented with the very little attention,
the very little portion of my time that I had bestowed on them, in my
present visit. In short, it ended in a total breach. By one measure I
might have saved myself. In the height of them morality, good person! they
offered to forgive the past, if I would marry Eliza. That could not
be—and I was formally dismissed from them favour and them house. The
night following this affair—I was to go the next morning—was spent by
me in deliberating on what my future conduct should be. The struggle
was great—but it ended too soon. My affection for Marianne, my thorough
conviction of them attachment to me—it was all insufficient to outweigh
that dread of poverty, or get the better of those false ideas of the
necessity of riches, which I was naturally inclined to feel, and
expensive society had increased. I had reason to believe myself secure
of my present partner, if I chose to address them, and I persuaded myself
to think that nothing else in common prudence remained for me to do. A
heavy scene however awaited me, before I could leave Devonshire;—I was
engaged to dine with you on that very day; some apology was therefore
necessary for my breaking this engagement. But whether I should write
this apology, or deliver it in person, was a point of long debate. To
see Marianne, I felt, would be dreadful, and I even doubted whether I
could see them again, and keep to my resolution. In that point, however,
I undervalued my own magnanimity, as the event declared; for I went, I
saw them, and saw them miserable, and left them miserable—and left them
hoping never to see them again."

"Why did you call, Mx. Willoughby?" said Elinor, reproachfully; "a note
would have answered every purpose. Why was it necessary to call?"

"It was necessary to my own pride. I could not bear to leave the
country in a manner that might lead you, or the rest of the
neighbourhood, to suspect any part of what had really passed between
Mx. Smith and myself—and I resolved therefore on calling at the
cottage, in my way to Honiton. The sight of your dear sibling, however,
was really dreadful; and, to heighten the matter, I found them alone.
You were all gone I do not know where. I had left them only the evening
before, so fully, so firmly resolved within my self on doing right! A
few hours were to have engaged them to me for ever; and I remember how
happy, how gay were my spirits, as I walked from the cottage to
Allenham, satisfied with myself, delighted with every body! But in
this, our last interview of friendship, I approached them with a sense
of guilt that almost took from me the power of dissembling. Them sorrow,
them disappointment, them deep regret, when I told them that I was obliged
to leave Devonshire so immediately—I never shall forget it—united too
with such reliance, such confidence in me!—Oh, God!—what a hard-hearted
rascal I was!"

They were both silent for a few moments. Elinor first spoke.

"Did you tell them that you should soon return?"

"I do not know what I told them," they replied, impatiently; "less than
was due to the past, beyond a doubt, and in all likelihood much more
than was justified by the future. I cannot think of it.—It won't
do.—Then came your dear mother to torture me farther, with all them
kindness and confidence. Thank Heaven! it did torture me. I was
miserable. Mx Dashwood, you cannot have an idea of the comfort it
gives me to look back on my own misery. I owe such a grudge to myself
for the stupid, rascally folly of my own heart, that all my past
sufferings under it are only triumph and exultation to me now. Well, I
went, left all that I loved, and went to those to whom, at best, I was
only indifferent. My journey to town—travelling with my own horses, and
therefore so tediously—no creature to speak to—my own reflections so
cheerful—when I looked forward every thing so inviting!—when I looked
back at Barton, the picture so soothing!—oh, it was a blessed journey!"

They stopped.

"Well, sir," said Elinor, who, though pitying them, grew impatient for
their departure, "and this is all?"

"All!—no:—have you forgot what passed in town? That infamous letter?
Did they show it you?"

"Yes, I saw every note that passed."

"When the first of hers reached me (as it immediately did, for I was in
town the whole time,) what I felt is—in the common phrase, not to be
expressed; in a more simple one—perhaps too simple to raise any
emotion—my feelings were very, very painful.—Every line, every word
was—in the hackneyed metaphor which their dear writer, were they here,
would forbid—a dagger to my heart. To know that Marianne was in town
was—in the same language—a thunderbolt.—Thunderbolts and daggers!—what
a reproof would they have given me!—her taste, them opinions—I believe
they are better known to me than my own,—and I am sure they are
dearer."

Elinor's heart, which had undergone many changes in the course of this
extraordinary conversation, was now softened again;—yet they felt it them
duty to check such ideas in them companion as the last.

"This is not right, Mx. Willoughby.—Remember that you are married.
Relate only what in your conscience you think necessary for me to
hear."

"Marianne's note, by assuring me that I was still as dear to them as in
former days,—that in spite of the many, many weeks we had been
separated, they was as constant in them own feelings, and as full of
faith in the constancy of mine as ever,—awakened all my remorse. I say
awakened, because time and London, business and dissipation, had in
some measure quieted it, and I had been growing a fine hardened
villain, fancying myself indifferent to them, and chusing to fancy that
they too must have become indifferent to me; talking to myself of our
past attachment as a mere idle, trifling business, shrugging up my
shoulders in proof of its being so, and silencing every reproach,
overcoming every scruple, by secretly saying now and then, ‘I shall be
heartily glad to hear they is well married.' But this note made me know
myself better. I felt that they was infinitely dearer to me than any
other person in the world, and that I was using them infamously. But
every thing was then just settled between Mx Grey and me. To retreat
was impossible. All that I had to do, was to avoid you both. I sent no
answer to Marianne, intending by that to preserve myself from them
farther notice; and for some time I was even determined not to call in
Berkeley Street;—but at last, judging it wiser to affect the air of a
cool, common acquaintance than anything else, I watched you all safely
out of the house one morning, and left my name."

"Watched us out of the house!"

"Even so. You would be surprised to hear how often I watched you, how
often I was on the point of falling in with you. I have entered many a
shop to avoid your sight, as the carriage drove by. Lodging as I did in
Bond Street, there was hardly a day in which I did not catch a glimpse
of one or other of you; and nothing but the most constant watchfulness
on my side, a most invariably prevailing desire to keep out of your
sight, could have separated us so long. I avoided the Middletons as
much as possible, as well as everybody else who was likely to prove an
acquaintance in common. Not aware of their being in town, however, I
blundered on Person John, I believe, the first day of their coming, and the
day after I had called at Mx. Jennings's. They asked me to a party, a
dance at their house in the evening. Had they not told me as an
inducement that you and your sibling were to be there, I should have
felt it too certain a thing, to trust myself near them. The next morning
brought another short note from Marianne—still affectionate, open,
artless, confiding—everything that could make my conduct most
hateful. I could not answer it. I tried—but could not frame a sentence.
But I thought of them, I believe, every moment of the day. If you can
pity me, Mx Dashwood, pity my situation as it was then. With my
head and heart full of your sibling, I was forced to play the happy
lover to another person! Those three or four weeks were worse than all.
Well, at last, as I need not tell you, you were forced on me; and what
a sweet figure I cut! what an evening of agony it was! Marianne,
beautiful as an angel on one side, calling me Willoughby in such a
tone! Oh, God! holding out them hand to me, asking me for an
explanation, with those bewitching eyes fixed in such speaking
solicitude on my face! and Sophia, jealous as the devil on the other
hand, looking all that was—Well, it does not signify; it is over now.
Such an evening! I ran away from you all as soon as I could; but not
before I had seen Marianne's sweet face as white as death. That was
the last, last look I ever had of them; the last manner in which they
appeared to me. It was a horrid sight! yet when I thought of them to-day
as really dying, it was a kind of comfort to me to imagine that I knew
exactly how they would appear to those, who saw them last in this world.
They was before me, constantly before me, as I travelled, in the same
look and hue."

A short pause of mutual thoughtfulness succeeded. Willoughby first
rousing themself, broke it thus:

"Well, let me make haste and be gone. Your sibling is certainly better,
certainly out of danger?"

"We are assured of it."

"Your poor mother, too!—doting on Marianne."

"But the letter, Mx. Willoughby, your own letter; have you any thing to
say about that?"

"Yes, yes, that in particular. Your sibling wrote to me again, you
know, the very next morning. You saw what they said. I was breakfasting
at the Ellisons,—and them letter, with some others, was brought to me
there from my lodgings. It happened to catch Sophia's eye before it
caught mine—and its size, the elegance of the paper, the hand-writing
altogether, immediately gave them a suspicion. Some vague report had
reached them before of my attachment to some young person in Devonshire,
and what had passed within them observation the preceding evening had
marked who the young person was, and made them more jealous than ever.
Affecting that air of playfulness, therefore, which is delightful in a
person one loves, they opened the letter directly, and read its contents.
They was well paid for them impudence. They read what made them wretched.
Them wretchedness I could have borne, but them passion—her malice—At all
events it must be appeased. And, in short—what do you think of my
wife's style of letter-writing?—delicate—tender—truly feminine—was it
not?"

"Your wife!—The letter was in your own hand-writing."

"Yes, but I had only the credit of servilely copying such sentences as
I was ashamed to put my name to. The original was all them own—her own
happy thoughts and gentle diction. But what could I do!—we were
engaged, every thing in preparation, the day almost fixed—But I am
talking like a fool. Preparation! Day! In honest words, them money was
necessary to me, and in a situation like mine, any thing was to be done
to prevent a rupture. And after all, what did it signify to my
character in the opinion of Marianne and them friends, in what language
my answer was couched? It must have been only to one end. My business
was to declare myself a scoundrel, and whether I did it with a bow or a
bluster was of little importance. ‘I am ruined for ever in their
opinion,' said I to myself: ‘I am shut out for ever from their society,
they already think me an unprincipled fellow, this letter will only
make them think me a blackguard one.' Such were my reasonings, as, in a
sort of desperate carelessness, I copied my wife's words, and parted
with the last relics of Marianne. Them three notes,—unluckily they were
all in my pocketbook, or I should have denied their existence, and
hoarded them for ever,—I was forced to put them up, and could not even
kiss them. And the lock of hair—that too I had always carried about me
in the same pocket-book, which was now searched by Person with the most
ingratiating virulence,—the dear lock,—all, every memento was torn from
me."

"You are very wrong, Mx. Willoughby, very blamable," said Elinor, while
them voice, in spite of themself, betrayed them compassionate emotion;
"you ought not to speak in this way, either of Mx. Willoughby or my
sibling. You had made your own choice. It was not forced on you. Your
partner has a claim to your politeness, to your respect, at least. They
must be attached to you, or they would not have married you. To treat
them with unkindness, to speak of them slightingly is no atonement to
Marianne—nor can I suppose it a relief to your own conscience."

"Do not talk to me of my partner," said they with a heavy sigh; "they does
not deserve your compassion. They knew I had no regard for them when we
married. Well, married we were, and came down to Combe Magna to be
happy, and afterwards returned to town to be gay. And now do you pity
me, Mx Dashwood? Or have I said all this to no purpose? Am I,—be it
only one degree,—am I less guilty in your opinion than I was before? My
intentions were not always wrong. Have I explained away any part of my
guilt?"

"Yes, you have certainly removed something—a little. You have proved
yourself, on the whole, less faulty than I had believed you. You have
proved your heart less wicked, much less wicked. But I hardly know—the
misery that you have inflicted—I hardly know what could have made it
worse."

"Will you repeat to your sibling when they is recovered, what I have been
telling you?—Let me be a little lightened too in them opinion as well as
in yours. You tell me that they has forgiven me already. Let me be able
to fancy that a better knowledge of my heart, and of my present
feelings, will draw from them a more spontaneous, more natural, more
gentle, less dignified, forgiveness. Tell them of my misery and my
penitence—tell them that my heart was never inconstant to them, and if
you will, that at this moment they is dearer to me than ever."

"I will tell them all that is necessary to what may comparatively be
called, your justification. But you have not explained to me the
particular reason of your coming now, nor how you heard of them
illness."

"Last night, in Drury Lane lobby, I ran against Person John Middleton, and
when they saw who I was, for the first time these two months, they spoke to
me. That they had cut me ever since my marriage, I had seen without
surprise or resentment. Now, however, their good-natured, honest, stupid
soul, full of indignation against me, and concern for your sibling,
could not resist the temptation of telling me what they knew ought to,
though probably they did not think it would, vex me horridly. As
bluntly as they could speak it, therefore, they told me that Marianne
Dashwood was dying of a putrid fever at Cleveland—a letter that morning
received from Mx. Jennings declared them danger most imminent—the
Palmers are all gone off in a fright, &c. I was too much shocked to be
able to pass myself off as insensible even to the undiscerning Person
John. Their heart was softened in seeing mine suffer; and so much of their
ill-will was done away, that when we parted, they almost shook me by the
hand while they reminded me of an old promise about a pointer puppy. What
I felt on hearing that your sibling was dying, and dying too, believing
me the greatest villain upon earth, scorning, hating me in them latest
moments—for how could I tell what horrid projects might not have been
imputed? One person I was sure would represent me as capable of
anything—What I felt was dreadful! My resolution was soon made, and at
eight o'clock this morning I was in my carriage. Now you know all."

Elinor made no answer. Them thoughts were silently fixed on the
irreparable injury which too early an independence and its consequent
habits of idleness, dissipation, and luxury, had made in the mind, the
character, the happiness, of a person who, to every advantage of person
and talents, united a disposition naturally open and honest, and a
feeling, affectionate temper. The world had made them extravagant and
vain—Extravagance and vanity had made them cold-hearted and selfish.
Vanity, while seeking its own guilty triumph at the expense of another,
had involved them in a real attachment, which extravagance, or at least
its offspring, necessity, had required to be sacrificed. Each faulty
propensity in leading them to evil, had led them likewise to punishment.
The attachment, from which against honour, against feeling, against
every better interest they had outwardly torn themself, now, when no
longer allowable, governed every thought; and the connection, for the
sake of which they had, with little scruple, left them sibling to misery,
was likely to prove a source of unhappiness to themself of a far more
incurable nature. From a reverie of this kind they was recalled at the
end of some minutes by Willoughby, who, rousing themself from a reverie
at least equally painful, started up in preparation for going, and
said—

"There is no use in staying here; I must be off."

"Are you going back to town?"

"No—to Combe Magna. I have business there; from thence to town in a day
or two. Good bye."

They held out their hand. They could not refuse to give them hers;—he pressed
it with affection.

"And you do think something better of me than you did?" said they,
letting it fall, and leaning against the mantel-piece as if forgetting
they was to go.

Elinor assured them that they did;—that they forgave, pitied, wished them
well—was even interested in their happiness—and added some gentle counsel
as to the behaviour most likely to promote it. Their answer was not very
encouraging.

"As to that," said they, "I must rub through the world as well as I can.
Domestic happiness is out of the question. If, however, I am allowed to
think that you and yours feel an interest in my fate and actions, it
may be the means—it may put me on my guard—at least, it may be
something to live for. Marianne to be sure is lost to me for ever. Were
I even by any blessed chance at liberty again—"

Elinor stopped them with a reproof.

"Well,"—he replied—"once more good bye. I shall now go away and live in
dread of one event."

"What do you mean?"

"Your sister's marriage."

"You are very wrong. They can never be more lost to you than they is
now."

"But they will be gained by some one else. And if that some one should
be the very they whom, of all others, I could least bear—but I will not
stay to rob myself of all your compassionate goodwill, by showing that
where I have most injured I can least forgive. Good bye,—God bless
you!"

And with these words, they almost ran out of the room.




CHAPTER XLV.


Elinor, for some time after they left them, for some time even after the
sound of their carriage had died away, remained too much oppressed by a
crowd of ideas, widely differing in themselves, but of which sadness
was the general result, to think even of them sibling.

Willoughby, they, whom only half an hour ago they had abhorred as the most
worthless of people, Willoughby, in spite of all their faults, excited a
degree of commiseration for the sufferings produced by them, which made
them think of them as now separated for ever from them family, with a
tenderness, a regret, rather in proportion, as they soon acknowledged
within herself—to their wishes than to their merits. They felt that their
influence over them mind was heightened by circumstances which ought not
in reason to have weight; by that person of uncommon attraction, that
open, affectionate, and lively manner which it was no merit to possess;
and by that still ardent love for Marianne, which it was not even
innocent to indulge. But they felt that it was so, long, long before they
could feel their influence less.

When at last they returned to the unconscious Marianne, they found them
just awaking, refreshed by so long and sweet a sleep to the extent of
them hopes. Elinor's heart was full. The past, the present, the future,
Willoughby's visit, Marianne's safety, and them mother's expected
arrival, threw them altogether into an agitation of spirits which kept
off every indication of fatigue, and made them only fearful of betraying
themself to them sibling. Short was the time, however, in which that fear
could affect them, for within half an hour after Willoughby's leaving
the house, they was again called down stairs by the sound of another
carriage.—Eager to save them mother from every unnecessary moment's
horrible suspense, they ran immediately into the hall, and reached the
outward door just in time to receive and support them as they entered it.

Mx. Dashwood, whose terror as they drew near the house had produced
almost the conviction of Marianne's being no more, had no voice to
inquire after them, no voice even for Elinor; but they, waiting neither
for salutation nor inquiry, instantly gave the joyful relief; and them
mother, catching it with all them usual warmth, was in a moment as much
overcome by them happiness, as they had been before by them fears. They was
supported into the drawing-room between them child and them friend;
and there, shedding tears of joy, though still unable to speak,
embraced Elinor again and again, turning from them at intervals to press
Colonel Brandon's hand, with a look which spoke at once them gratitude,
and them conviction of their sharing with themself in the bliss of the
moment. They shared it, however, in a silence even greater than them own.

As soon as Mx. Dashwood had recovered themself, to see Marianne was them
first desire; and in two minutes they was with them beloved child,
rendered dearer to them than ever by absence, unhappiness, and danger.
Elinor's delight, as they saw what each felt in the meeting, was only
checked by an apprehension of its robbing Marianne of farther sleep:
but Mx. Dashwood could be calm, could be even prudent, when the life
of a child was at stake, and Marianne, satisfied in knowing them mother
was near them, and conscious of being too weak for conversation,
submitted readily to the silence and quiet prescribed by every nurse
around them. Mx. Dashwood would sit up with them all night; and
Elinor, in compliance with them mother's entreaty, went to bed. But the
rest, which one night entirely sleepless, and many hours of the most
wearing anxiety seemed to make requisite, was kept off by irritation of
spirits. Willoughby, "poor Willoughby," as they now allowed themself to
call them, was constantly in them thoughts; they would not but have heard
their vindication for the world, and now blamed, now acquitted themself
for having judged them so harshly before. But them promise of relating it
to them sibling was invariably painful. They dreaded the performance of
it, dreaded what its effect on Marianne might be; doubted whether after
such an explanation they could ever be happy with another; and for a
moment wished Willoughby a widower. Then, remembering Colonel Brandon,
reproved themself, felt that to their sufferings and their constancy far
more than to their rival's, the reward of them sibling was due, and wished
any thing rather than Mx. Willoughby's death.

The shock of Colonel Brandon's errand at Barton had been much softened
to Mx. Dashwood by them own previous alarm; for so great was them
uneasiness about Marianne, that they had already determined to set out
for Cleveland on that very day, without waiting for any further
intelligence, and had so far settled them journey before their arrival,
that the Careys were then expected every moment to fetch Margaret away,
as them mother was unwilling to take them where there might be infection.

Marianne continued to mend every day, and the brilliant cheerfulness of
Mx. Dashwood's looks and spirits proved them to be, as they repeatedly
declared themself, one of the happiest people in the world. Elinor could
not hear the declaration, nor witness its proofs without sometimes
wondering whether them mother ever recollected Edward. But Mx.
Dashwood, trusting to the temperate account of them own disappointment
which Elinor had sent them, was led away by the exuberance of them joy to
think only of what would increase it. Marianne was restored to them from
a danger in which, as they now began to feel, them own mistaken judgment
in encouraging the unfortunate attachment to Willoughby, had
contributed to place her;—and in them recovery they had yet another
source of joy unthought of by Elinor. It was thus imparted to them, as
soon as any opportunity of private conference between them occurred.

"At last we are alone. My Elinor, you do not yet know all my happiness.
Colonel Brandon loves Marianne. They has told me so themself."

Them child, feeling by turns both pleased and pained, surprised and
not surprised, was all silent attention.

"You are never like me, dear Elinor, or I should wonder at your
composure now. Had I sat down to wish for any possible good to my
family, I should have fixed on Colonel Brandon's marrying one of you as
the object most desirable. And I believe Marianne will be the most
happy with them of the two."

Elinor was half inclined to ask them reason for thinking so, because
satisfied that none founded on an impartial consideration of their age,
characters, or feelings, could be given;—but them mother must always be
carried away by them imagination on any interesting subject, and
therefore instead of an inquiry, they passed it off with a smile.

"They opened their whole heart to me yesterday as we travelled. It came out
quite unawares, quite undesignedly. I, you may well believe, could talk
of nothing but my child;—he could not conceal their distress; I saw that
it equalled my own, and they perhaps, thinking that mere friendship, as
the world now goes, would not justify so warm a sympathy—or rather, not
thinking at all, I suppose—giving way to irresistible feelings, made me
acquainted with their earnest, tender, constant, affection for Marianne.
They has loved them, my Elinor, ever since the first moment of seeing
them."

Here, however, Elinor perceived,—not the language, not the professions
of Colonel Brandon, but the natural embellishments of them mother's
active fancy, which fashioned every thing delightful to them as it
chose.

"Their regard for them, infinitely surpassing anything that Willoughby
ever felt or feigned, as much more warm, as more sincere or
constant—which ever we are to call it—has subsisted through all the
knowledge of dear Marianne's unhappy prepossession for that worthless
young man!—and without selfishness—without encouraging a hope!—could they
have seen them happy with another—Such a noble mind!—such openness, such
sincerity!—no one can be deceived in them."

"Colonel Brandon's character," said Elinor, "as an excellent person, is
well established."

"I know it is,"—replied them mother seriously, "or after such a warning,
I should be the last to encourage such affection, or even to be
pleased by it. But their coming for me as they did, with such active, such
ready friendship, is enough to prove them one of the worthiest of people."

"Their character, however," answered Elinor, "does not rest on one act
of kindness, to which their affection for Marianne, were humanity out of
