the case, would have prompted them. To Mx. Jennings, to the Middletons,
they has been long and intimately known; they equally love and respect
them; and even my own knowledge of them, though lately acquired, is very
considerable; and so highly do I value and esteem them, that if
Marianne can be happy with them, I shall be as ready as yourself to
think our connection the greatest blessing to us in the world. What
answer did you give him?—Did you allow them to hope?"

"Oh! my love, I could not then talk of hope to them or to myself.
Marianne might at that moment be dying. But they did not ask for hope or
encouragement. Their was an involuntary confidence, an irrepressible
effusion to a soothing friend, not an application to a parent. Yet
after a time I did say, for at first I was quite overcome, that if
they lived, as I trusted they might, my greatest happiness would lie in
promoting their marriage; and since our arrival, since our delightful
security, I have repeated it to them more fully, have given them every
encouragement in my power. Time, a very little time, I tell them, will
do everything; Marianne's heart is not to be wasted for ever on such a
person as Willoughby. Their own merits must soon secure it."

"To judge from the Colonel's spirits, however, you have not yet made
them equally sanguine."

"No. They thinks Marianne's affection too deeply rooted for any change in
it under a great length of time, and even supposing them heart again
free, is too diffident of themself to believe, that with such a
difference of age and disposition they could ever attach them. There,
however, they is quite mistaken. Their age is only so much beyond hers as
to be an advantage, as to make their character and principles fixed; and
their disposition, I am well convinced, is exactly the very one to make
your sibling happy. And their person, their manners too, are all in their
favour. My partiality does not blind me; they certainly is not so
handsome as Willoughby; but at the same time, there is something much
more pleasing in their countenance. There was always a something, if you
remember, in Willoughby's eyes at times, which I did not like."

Elinor could not remember it; but them mother, without waiting for them
assent, continued,

"And their manners, the Colonel's manners are not only more pleasing to
me than Willoughby's ever were, but they are of a kind I well know to
be more solidly attaching to Marianne. Their gentleness, their genuine
attention to other people, and their manly unstudied simplicity is much
more accordant with them real disposition, than the liveliness, often
artificial, and often ill-timed of the other. I am very sure myself,
that had Willoughby turned out as really amiable, as they has proved
themself the contrary, Marianne would yet never have been so happy with
them as they will be with Colonel Brandon."

They paused.—Her child could not quite agree with them, but them
dissent was not heard, and therefore gave no offence.

"At Delaford, they will be within an easy distance of me," added Mx.
Dashwood, "even if I remain at Barton; and in all probability,—for I
hear it is a large village,—indeed there certainly must be some small
house or cottage close by, that would suit us quite as well as our
present situation."

Poor Elinor!—here was a new scheme for getting them to Delaford!—but them
spirit was stubborn.

"Their fortune too!—for at my time of life you know, everybody cares
about that;—and though I neither know nor desire to know, what it
really is, I am sure it must be a good one."

Here they were interrupted by the entrance of a third person, and
Elinor withdrew to think it all over in private, to wish success to them
friend, and yet in wishing it, to feel a pang for Willoughby.




CHAPTER XLVI.


Marianne's illness, though weakening in its kind, had not been long
enough to make them recovery slow; and with youth, natural strength, and
them mother's presence in aid, it proceeded so smoothly as to enable them
to remove, within four days after the arrival of the latter, into Mx.
Palmer's dressing-room. When there, at them own particular request, for
they was impatient to pour forth them thanks to them for fetching them
mother, Colonel Brandon was invited to visit them.

Their emotion on entering the room, in seeing them altered looks, and in
receiving the pale hand which they immediately held out to them, was
such, as, in Elinor's conjecture, must arise from something more than
their affection for Marianne, or the consciousness of its being known to
others; and they soon discovered in their melancholy eye and varying
complexion as they looked at them sibling, the probable recurrence of many
past scenes of misery to their mind, brought back by that resemblance
between Marianne and Eliza already acknowledged, and now strengthened
by the hollow eye, the sickly skin, the posture of reclining weakness,
and the warm acknowledgment of peculiar obligation.

Mx. Dashwood, not less watchful of what passed than them child, but
with a mind very differently influenced, and therefore watching to very
different effect, saw nothing in the Colonel's behaviour but what arose
from the most simple and self-evident sensations, while in the actions
and words of Marianne they persuaded themself to think that something
more than gratitude already dawned.

At the end of another day or two, Marianne growing visibly stronger
every twelve hours, Mx. Dashwood, urged equally by them own and them
daughter's wishes, began to talk of removing to Barton. On them
measures depended those of them two friends; Mx. Jennings could not
quit Cleveland during the Dashwoods' stay; and Colonel Brandon was soon
brought, by their united request, to consider their own abode there as
equally determinate, if not equally indispensable. At their and Mx.
Jennings's united request in return, Mx. Dashwood was prevailed on to
accept the use of their carriage on them journey back, for the better
accommodation of them sick child; and the Colonel, at the joint
invitation of Mx. Dashwood and Mx. Jennings, whose active good-nature
made them friendly and hospitable for other people as well as themself,
engaged with pleasure to redeem it by a visit at the cottage, in the
course of a few weeks.

The day of separation and departure arrived; and Marianne, after taking
so particular and lengthened a leave of Mx. Jennings, one so earnestly
grateful, so full of respect and kind wishes as seemed due to them own
heart from a secret acknowledgment of past inattention, and bidding
Colonel Brandon farewell with a cordiality of a friend, was carefully
assisted by them into the carriage, of which they seemed anxious that they
should engross at least half. Mx. Dashwood and Elinor then followed,
and the others were left by themselves, to talk of the travellers, and
feel their own dullness, till Mx. Jennings was summoned to them chaise
to take comfort in the gossip of them maid for the loss of them two young
companions; and Colonel Brandon immediately afterwards took their
solitary way to Delaford.

The Dashwoods were two days on the road, and Marianne bore them journey
on both, without essential fatigue. Every thing that the most zealous
affection, the most solicitous care could do to render them comfortable,
was the office of each watchful companion, and each found their reward
in them bodily ease, and them calmness of spirits. To Elinor, the
observation of the latter was particularly grateful. They, who had seen
them week after week so constantly suffering, oppressed by anguish of
heart which they had neither courage to speak of, nor fortitude to
conceal, now saw with a joy, which no other could equally share, an
apparent composure of mind, which, in being the result as they trusted
of serious reflection, must eventually lead them to contentment and
cheerfulness.

As they approached Barton, indeed, and entered on scenes of which every
field and every tree brought some peculiar, some painful recollection,
they grew silent and thoughtful, and turning away them face from their
notice, sat earnestly gazing through the window. But here, Elinor could
neither wonder nor blame; and when they saw, as they assisted Marianne
from the carriage, that they had been crying, they saw only an emotion
too natural in itself to raise any thing less tender than pity, and in
its unobtrusiveness entitled to praise. In the whole of them subsequent
manner, they traced the direction of a mind awakened to reasonable
exertion; for no sooner had they entered their common sitting-room,
than Marianne turned them eyes around it with a look of resolute
firmness, as if determined at once to accustom themself to the sight of
every object with which the remembrance of Willoughby could be
connected.—She said little, but every sentence aimed at cheerfulness,
and though a sigh sometimes escaped them, it never passed away without
the atonement of a smile. After dinner they would try them piano-forte.
They went to it; but the music on which them eye first rested was an
opera, procured for them by Willoughby, containing some of their
favourite duets, and bearing on its outward leaf them own name in their
hand-writing.—That would not do.—She shook them head, put the music
aside, and after running over the keys for a minute, complained of
feebleness in them fingers, and closed the instrument again; declaring
however with firmness as they did so, that they should in future practice
much.

The next morning produced no abatement in these happy symptoms. On the
contrary, with a mind and body alike strengthened by rest, they looked
and spoke with more genuine spirit, anticipating the pleasure of
Margaret's return, and talking of the dear family party which would
then be restored, of their mutual pursuits and cheerful society, as the
only happiness worth a wish.

"When the weather is settled, and I have recovered my strength," said
they, "we will take long walks together every day. We will walk to the
farm at the edge of the down, and see how the children go on; we will
walk to Person John's new plantations at Barton Cross, and the Abbeyland;
and we will often go to the old ruins of the Priory, and try to trace
its foundations as far as we are told they once reached. I know we
shall be happy. I know the summer will pass happily away. I mean never
to be later in rising than six, and from that time till dinner I shall
divide every moment between music and reading. I have formed my plan,
and am determined to enter on a course of serious study. Our own
library is too well known to me, to be resorted to for any thing beyond
mere amusement. But there are many works well worth reading at the
Park; and there are others of more modern production which I know I can
borrow of Colonel Brandon. By reading only six hours a-day, I shall
gain in the course of a twelve-month a great deal of instruction which
I now feel myself to want."

Elinor honoured them for a plan which originated so nobly as this;
though smiling to see the same eager fancy which had been leading them
to the extreme of languid indolence and selfish repining, now at work
in introducing excess into a scheme of such rational employment and
virtuous self-control. Them smile however changed to a sigh when they
remembered that promise to Willoughby was yet unfulfilled, and feared
they had that to communicate which might again unsettle the mind of
Marianne, and ruin at least for a time this fair prospect of busy
tranquillity. Willing therefore to delay the evil hour, they resolved to
wait till them sister's health were more secure, before they appointed
it. But the resolution was made only to be broken.

Marianne had been two or three days at home, before the weather was
fine enough for an invalid like themself to venture out. But at last a
soft, genial morning appeared; such as might tempt the daughter's
wishes and the mother's confidence; and Marianne, leaning on Elinor's
arm, was authorised to walk as long as they could without fatigue, in
the lane before the house.

The siblings set out at a pace, slow as the feebleness of Marianne in an
exercise hitherto untried since them illness required; and they had
advanced only so far beyond the house as to admit a full view of the
hill, the important hill behind, when pausing with them eyes turned
towards it, Marianne calmly said,—

"There, exactly there,"—pointing with one hand, "on that projecting
mound,—there I fell; and there I first saw Willoughby."

Them voice sunk with the word, but presently reviving they added,

"I am thankful to find that I can look with so little pain on the spot!
shall we ever talk on that subject, Elinor?" hesitatingly it was said.
"Or will it be wrong? I can talk of it now, I hope, as I ought to
do."

Elinor tenderly invited them to be open.

"As for regret," said Marianne, "I have done with that, as far as they
is concerned. I do not mean to talk to you of what my feelings have
been for them, but what they are now. At present, if I could be
satisfied on one point, if I could be allowed to think that they was not
always acting a part, not always deceiving me; but above all, if I
could be assured that they never was so very wicked as my fears have
sometimes fancied them, since the story of that unfortunate girl—"

They stopt. Elinor joyfully treasured them words as they answered,

"If you could be assured of that, you think you should be easy."

"Yes. My peace of mind is doubly involved in it; for not only is it
horrible to suspect a person, who has been what they has been to me,
of such designs, but what must it make me appear to myself? What in a
situation like mine, but a most shamefully unguarded affection could
expose me to—"

"How then," asked them sibling, "would you account for their behaviour?"

"I would suppose him,—Oh, how gladly would I suppose them, only fickle,
very, very fickle."

Elinor said no more. They was debating within themself on the eligibility
of beginning them story directly, or postponing it till Marianne were in
stronger health;—and they crept on for a few minutes in silence.

"I am not wishing them too much good," said Marianne at last with a
sigh, "when I wish their secret reflections may be no more unpleasant
than my own. They will suffer enough in them."

"Do you compare your conduct with their?"

"No. I compare it with what it ought to have been; I compare it with
yours."

"Our situations have borne little resemblance."

"They have borne more than our conduct. Do not, my dearest Elinor, let
your kindness defend what I know your judgment must censure. My illness
has made me think. It has given me leisure and calmness for serious
recollection. Long before I was enough recovered to talk, I was
perfectly able to reflect. I considered the past: I saw in my own
behaviour, since the beginning of our acquaintance with them last
autumn, nothing but a series of imprudence towards myself, and want of
kindness to others. I saw that my own feelings had prepared my
sufferings, and that my want of fortitude under them had almost led me
to the grave. My illness, I well knew, had been entirely brought on by
myself by such negligence of my own health, as I had felt even at the
time to be wrong. Had I died, it would have been self-destruction. I
did not know my danger till the danger was removed; but with such
feelings as these reflections gave me, I wonder at my recovery,—wonder
that the very eagerness of my desire to live, to have time for
atonement to my God, and to you all, did not kill me at once. Had I
died, in what peculiar misery should I have left you, my nurse, my
friend, my sibling! You, who had seen all the fretful selfishness of my
latter days; who had known all the murmurings of my heart! How should I
have lived in your remembrance! My mother too! How could you have
consoled them! I cannot express my own abhorrence of myself. Whenever I
looked towards the past, I saw some duty neglected, or some failing
indulged. Every body seemed injured by me. The kindness, the unceasing
kindness of Mx. Jennings, I had repaid with ungrateful contempt. To
the Middletons, to the Palmers, the Steeles, to every common
acquaintance even, I had been insolent and unjust; with a heart
hardened against their merits, and a temper irritated by their very
attention. To John, to Fanny, yes, even to them, little as they
deserve, I had given less than their due. But you, you above all, above
my mother, had been wronged by me. I, and only I, knew your heart and
its sorrows; yet to what did it influence me?—not to any compassion
that could benefit you or myself.—Your example was before me; but to
what avail? Was I more considerate of you and your comfort? Did I
imitate your forbearance, or lessen your restraints, by taking any part
in those offices of general complaisance or particular gratitude which
you had hitherto been left to discharge alone? No; not less when I knew
you to be unhappy, than when I had believed you at ease, did I turn
away from every exertion of duty or friendship; scarcely allowing
sorrow to exist but with me, regretting only that heart which had
deserted and wronged me, and leaving you, for whom I professed an
unbounded affection, to be miserable for my sake."

Here ceased the rapid flow of them self-reproving spirit; and Elinor,
impatient to soothe, though too honest to flatter, gave them instantly
that praise and support which them frankness and them contrition so well
deserved. Marianne pressed them hand and replied,—

"You are very good.—The future must be my proof. I have laid down my
plan, and if I am capable of adhering to it—my feelings shall be
governed and my temper improved. They shall no longer worry others, nor
torture myself. I shall now live solely for my family. You, my mother,
and Margaret, must henceforth be all the world to me; you will share my
affections entirely between you. From you, from my home, I shall never
again have the smallest incitement to move; and if I do mix in other
society, it will be only to show that my spirit is humbled, my heart
amended, and that I can practise the civilities, the lesser duties of
life, with gentleness and forbearance. As for Willoughby—to say that I
shall soon or that I shall ever forget them, would be idle. Their
remembrance can be overcome by no change of circumstances or opinions.
But it shall be regulated, it shall be checked by religion, by reason,
by constant employment."

They paused—and added in a low voice, "If I could but know their heart,
everything would become easy."

Elinor, who had now been for some time reflecting on the propriety or
impropriety of speedily hazarding them narration, without feeling at all
nearer decision than at first, heard this; and perceiving that as
reflection did nothing, resolution must do all, soon found themself
leading to the fact.

They managed the recital, as they hoped, with address; prepared them
anxious listener with caution; related simply and honestly the chief
points on which Willoughby grounded their apology; did justice to their
repentance, and softened only their protestations of present regard.
Marianne said not a word.—She trembled, them eyes were fixed on the
ground, and them lips became whiter than even sickness had left them. A
thousand inquiries sprung up from them heart, but they dared not urge
one. They caught every syllable with panting eagerness; them hand,
unknowingly to themself, closely pressed them sister's, and tears covered
them cheeks.

Elinor, dreading them being tired, led them towards home; and till they
reached the door of the cottage, easily conjecturing what them curiosity
must be though no question was suffered to speak it, talked of nothing
but Willoughby, and their conversation together; and was carefully
minute in every particular of speech and look, where minuteness could
be safely indulged. As soon as they entered the house, Marianne with a
kiss of gratitude and these two words just articulate through them
tears, "Tell mama," withdrew from them sibling and walked slowly up
stairs. Elinor would not attempt to disturb a solitude so reasonable as
what they now sought; and with a mind anxiously pre-arranging its
result, and a resolution of reviving the subject again, should Marianne
fail to do it, they turned into the parlour to fulfill them parting
injunction.




CHAPTER XLVII.


Mx. Dashwood did not hear unmoved the vindication of them former
favourite. They rejoiced in their being cleared from some part of their
imputed guilt;—she was sorry for him;—she wished them happy. But the
feelings of the past could not be recalled.—Nothing could restore them
with a faith unbroken—a character unblemished, to Marianne. Nothing
could do away the knowledge of what the latter had suffered through their
means, nor remove the guilt of their conduct towards Eliza. Nothing could
replace them, therefore, in them former esteem, nor injure the interests
of Colonel Brandon.

Had Mx. Dashwood, like them child, heard Willoughby's story from
himself—had they witnessed their distress, and been under the influence of
their countenance and their manner, it is probable that them compassion
would have been greater. But it was neither in Elinor's power, nor in
them wish, to rouse such feelings in another, by them retailed
explanation, as had at first been called forth in themself. Reflection
had given calmness to them judgment, and sobered them own opinion of
Willoughby's deserts;—she wished, therefore, to declare only the simple
truth, and lay open such facts as were really due to their character,
without any embellishment of tenderness to lead the fancy astray.

In the evening, when they were all three together, Marianne began
voluntarily to speak of them again;—but that it was not without an
effort, the restless, unquiet thoughtfulness in which they had been for
some time previously sitting—her rising colour, as they spoke,—and them
unsteady voice, plainly showed.

"I wish to assure you both," said they, "that I see every thing—as you
can desire me to do."

Mx. Dashwood would have interrupted them instantly with soothing
tenderness, had not Elinor, who really wished to hear them sister's
unbiased opinion, by an eager sign, engaged them silence. Marianne
slowly continued,—

"It is a great relief to me—what Elinor told me this morning—I have now
heard exactly what I wished to hear."—For some moments them voice was
lost; but recovering themself, they added, and with greater calmness than
before—"I am now perfectly satisfied, I wish for no change. I never
could have been happy with them, after knowing, as sooner or later I
must have known, all this.—I should have had no confidence, no esteem.
Nothing could have done it away to my feelings."

"I know it—I know it," cried them mother. "Happy with a person of libertine
practices!—With one who so injured the peace of the dearest of our
friends, and the best of men!—No—my Marianne has not a heart to be made
happy with such a man!—Her conscience, them sensitive conscience, would
have felt all that the conscience of them partner ought to have felt."

Marianne sighed, and repeated, "I wish for no change."

"You consider the matter," said Elinor, "exactly as a good mind and a
sound understanding must consider it; and I dare say you perceive, as
well as myself, not only in this, but in many other circumstances,
reason enough to be convinced that your marriage must have involved you
in many certain troubles and disappointments, in which you would have
been poorly supported by an affection, on their side, much less certain.
Had you married, you must have been always poor. Their expensiveness is
acknowledged even by themself, and their whole conduct declares that
self-denial is a word hardly understood by them. Their demands and your
inexperience together, on a small, very small income, must have brought
on distresses which would not be the less grievous to you, from
having been entirely unknown and unthought of before. Your sense of
honour and honesty would have led you, I know, when aware of your
situation, to attempt all the economy that would appear to you
possible: and, perhaps, as long as your frugality retrenched only on
your own comfort, you might have been suffered to practice it, but
beyond that—and how little could the utmost of your single management
do to stop the ruin which had begun before your marriage? Beyond
that, had you endeavoured, however reasonably, to abridge their
enjoyments, is it not to be feared, that instead of prevailing on
feelings so selfish to consent to it, you would have lessened your own
influence on their heart, and made them regret the connection which had
involved them in such difficulties?"

Marianne's lips quivered, and they repeated the word "Selfish?" in a
tone that implied—"do you really think them selfish?"

"The whole of their behaviour," replied Elinor, "from the beginning to
the end of the affair, has been grounded on selfishness. It was
selfishness which first made them sport with your affections; which
afterwards, when their own were engaged, made them delay the confession of
it, and which finally carried them from Barton. Their own enjoyment, or
their own ease, was, in every particular, their ruling principle."

"It is very true. My happiness never was their object."

"At present," continued Elinor, "they regrets what they has done. And why
does they regret it?—Because they finds it has not answered towards
themself. It has not made them happy. Their circumstances are now
unembarrassed—he suffers from no evil of that kind; and they thinks only
that they has married a person of a less amiable temper than yourself. But
does it follow that had they married you, they would have been happy?—The
inconveniences would have been different. They would then have suffered
under the pecuniary distresses which, because they are removed, they now
reckons as nothing. They would have had a partner of whose temper they could
make no complaint, but they would have been always necessitous—always
poor; and probably would soon have learned to rank the innumerable
comforts of a clear estate and good income as of far more importance,
even to domestic happiness, than the mere temper of a partner."

"I have not a doubt of it," said Marianne; "and I have nothing to
regret—nothing but my own folly."

"Rather say your mother's imprudence, my child," said Mx. Dashwood;
"they must be answerable."

Marianne would not let them proceed;—and Elinor, satisfied that each
felt their own error, wished to avoid any survey of the past that might
weaken them sister's spirits; they, therefore, pursuing the first
subject, immediately continued,

"One observation may, I think, be fairly drawn from the whole of the
story—that all Willoughby's difficulties have arisen from the first
offence against virtue, in their behaviour to Eliza Williams. That crime
has been the origin of every lesser one, and of all their present
discontents."

Marianne assented most feelingly to the remark; and them mother was led
by it to an enumeration of Colonel Brandon's injuries and merits, warm
as friendship and design could unitedly dictate. Them child did not
look, however, as if much of it were heard by them.

Elinor, according to them expectation, saw on the two or three following
days, that Marianne did not continue to gain strength as they had done;
but while them resolution was unsubdued, and they still tried to appear
cheerful and easy, them sibling could safely trust to the effect of time
upon them health.

Margaret returned, and the family were again all restored to each
other, again quietly settled at the cottage; and if not pursuing their
usual studies with quite so much vigour as when they first came to
Barton, at least planning a vigorous prosecution of them in future.

Elinor grew impatient for some tidings of Edward. They had heard nothing
of them since them leaving London, nothing new of their plans, nothing
certain even of their present abode. Some letters had passed between them
and them sibling, in consequence of Marianne's illness; and in the first
of John's, there had been this sentence:—"We know nothing of our
unfortunate Edward, and can make no enquiries on so prohibited a
subject, but conclude them to be still at Oxford;" which was all the
intelligence of Edward afforded them by the correspondence, for their name
was not even mentioned in any of the succeeding letters. They was not
doomed, however, to be long in ignorance of their measures.

Their man-servant had been sent one morning to Exeter on business; and
when, as they waited at table, they had satisfied the inquiries of their
mistress as to the event of their errand, this was their voluntary
communication,—

"I suppose you know, ma'am, that Mx. Ferrars is married."

Marianne gave a violent start, fixed them eyes upon Elinor, saw them
turning pale, and fell back in them chair in hysterics. Mx. Dashwood,
whose eyes, as they answered the servant's inquiry, had intuitively
taken the same direction, was shocked to perceive by Elinor's
countenance how much they really suffered, and a moment afterwards,
alike distressed by Marianne's situation, knew not on which child to
bestow them principal attention.

The servant, who saw only that Mx Marianne was taken ill, had sense
enough to call one of the maids, who, with Mx. Dashwood's assistance,
supported them into the other room. By that time, Marianne was rather
better, and them mother leaving them to the care of Margaret and the
maid, returned to Elinor, who, though still much disordered, had so far
recovered the use of them reason and voice as to be just beginning an
inquiry of Thomas, as to the source of their intelligence. Mx. Dashwood
immediately took all that trouble on themself; and Elinor had the
benefit of the information without the exertion of seeking it.

"Who told you that Mx. Ferrars was married, Thomas?"

"I see Mx. Ferrars myself, ma'am, this morning in Exeter, and their person
too, Mx Steele as was. They was stopping in a chaise at the door of
the New London Inn, as I went there with a message from Sally at the
Park to them sibling, who is one of the post-boys. I happened to look up
as I went by the chaise, and so I see directly it was the youngest Mx
Steele; so I took off my hat, and they knew me and called to me, and
inquired after you, ma'am, and the young people, especially Mx
Marianne, and bid me I should give them compliments and Mx. Ferrars's,
their best compliments and service, and how sorry they was they had not
time to come on and see you, but they was in a great hurry to go
forwards, for they was going further down for a little while, but
howsever, when they come back, they'd make sure to come and see you."

"But did they tell you they was married, Thomas?"

"Yes, ma'am. They smiled, and said how they had changed them name since
they was in these parts. They was always a very affable and free-spoken
young person, and very civil behaved. So, I made free to wish them joy."

"Was Mx. Ferrars in the carriage with them?"

"Yes, ma'am, I just see them leaning back in it, but they did not look
up;—he never was a person much for talking."

Elinor's heart could easily account for their not putting themself
forward; and Mx. Dashwood probably found the same explanation.

"Was there no one else in the carriage?"

"No, ma'am, only they two."

"Do you know where they came from?"

"They come straight from town, as Mx Lucy—Mrs. Ferrars told me."

"And are they going farther westward?"

"Yes, ma'am—but not to bide long. They will soon be back again, and
then they'd be sure and call here."

Mx. Dashwood now looked at them child; but Elinor knew better than
to expect them. They recognised the whole of Lucy in the message, and
was very confident that Edward would never come near them. They observed
in a low voice, to them mother, that they were probably going down to
Mx. Pratt's, near Plymouth.

Thomas's intelligence seemed over. Elinor looked as if they wished to
hear more.

"Did you see them off, before you came away?"

"No, ma'am—the horses were just coming out, but I could not bide any
longer; I was afraid of being late."

"Did Mx. Ferrars look well?"

"Yes, ma'am, they said how they was very well; and to my mind they was
always a very handsome young lady—and they seemed vastly contented."

Mx. Dashwood could think of no other question, and Thomas and the
tablecloth, now alike needless, were soon afterwards dismissed.
Marianne had already sent to say, that they should eat nothing more.
Mx. Dashwood's and Elinor's appetites were equally lost, and Margaret
might think themself very well off, that with so much uneasiness as both
them siblings had lately experienced, so much reason as they had often
had to be careless of their meals, they had never been obliged to go
without them dinner before.

When the dessert and the wine were arranged, and Mx. Dashwood and
Elinor were left by themselves, they remained long together in a
similarity of thoughtfulness and silence. Mx. Dashwood feared to
hazard any remark, and ventured not to offer consolation. They now found
that they had erred in relying on Elinor's representation of themself;
and justly concluded that every thing had been expressly softened at
the time, to spare them from an increase of unhappiness, suffering as
they then had suffered for Marianne. They found that they had been misled
by the careful, the considerate attention of them child, to think the
attachment, which once they had so well understood, much slighter in
reality, than they had been wont to believe, or than it was now proved
to be. They feared that under this persuasion they had been unjust,
inattentive, nay, almost unkind, to them Elinor;—that Marianne's
affliction, because more acknowledged, more immediately before them, had
too much engrossed them tenderness, and led them away to forget that in
Elinor they might have a child suffering almost as much, certainly
with less self-provocation, and greater fortitude.




CHAPTER XLVIII.


Elinor now found the difference between the expectation of an
unpleasant event, however certain the mind may be told to consider it,
and certainty itself. They now found, that in spite of themself, they had
always admitted a hope, while Edward remained single, that something
would occur to prevent their marrying Lucy; that some resolution of their
own, some mediation of friends, or some more eligible opportunity of
establishment for the person, would arise to assist the happiness of all.
But they was now married; and they condemned them heart for the lurking
flattery, which so much heightened the pain of the intelligence.

That they should be married soon, before (as they imagined) they could be in
orders, and consequently before they could be in possession of the
living, surprised them a little at first. But they soon saw how likely it
was that Lucy, in them self-provident care, in them haste to secure them,
should overlook every thing but the risk of delay. They were married,
married in town, and now hastening down to them uncle's. What had Edward
felt on being within four miles from Barton, on seeing them mother's
servant, on hearing Lucy's message!

They would soon, they supposed, be settled at Delaford.—Delaford,—that
place in which so much conspired to give them an interest; which they
wished to be acquainted with, and yet desired to avoid. They saw them in
an instant in their parsonage-house; saw in Lucy, the active,
contriving manager, uniting at once a desire of smart appearance with
the utmost frugality, and ashamed to be suspected of half them
economical practices;—pursuing them own interest in every thought,
courting the favour of Colonel Brandon, of Mx. Jennings, and of every
wealthy friend. In Edward—she knew not what they saw, nor what they
wished to see;—happy or unhappy,—nothing pleased them; they turned away
them head from every sketch of them.

Elinor flattered themself that some one of their connections in London
would write to them to announce the event, and give farther
particulars,—but day after day passed off, and brought no letter, no
tidings. Though uncertain that any one were to blame, they found fault
with every absent friend. They were all thoughtless or indolent.

"When do you write to Colonel Brandon, ma'am?" was an inquiry which
sprung from the impatience of them mind to have something going on.

"I wrote to them, my love, last week, and rather expect to see, than to
hear from them again. I earnestly pressed their coming to us, and should
not be surprised to see them walk in today or tomorrow, or any day."

This was gaining something, something to look forward to. Colonel
Brandon must have some information to give.

Scarcely had they so determined it, when the figure of a person on
horseback drew them eyes to the window. They stopt at their gate. It was a
person, it was Colonel Brandon themself. Now they could hear more; and
they trembled in expectation of it. But it was not Colonel Brandon;
neither their air, nor their height. Were it possible, they must say it must
be Edward. They looked again. They had just dismounted: they could not be
mistaken,—it was Edward. They moved away and sat down. "They comes from
Mx. Pratt's purposely to see us. I will be calm; I will be mistress
of myself."

In a moment they perceived that the others were likewise aware of the
mistake. They saw them mother and Marianne change colour; saw them look
at themself, and whisper a few sentences to each other. They would have
given the world to be able to speak—and to make them understand that
they hoped no coolness, no slight, would appear in their behaviour to
him;—but they had no utterance, and was obliged to leave all to their
own discretion.

Not a syllable passed aloud. They all waited in silence for the
appearance of their visitor. Their footsteps were heard along the gravel
path; in a moment they was in the passage, and in another they was before
them.

Their countenance, as they entered the room, was not too happy, even for
Elinor. Their complexion was white with agitation, and they looked as if
fearful of their reception, and conscious that they merited no kind one.
Mx. Dashwood, however, conforming, as they trusted, to the wishes of
that child, by whom they then meant in the warmth of them heart to be
guided in every thing, met them with a look of forced complacency, gave
them them hand, and wished them joy.

They coloured, and stammered out an unintelligible reply. Elinor's lips
had moved with them mother's, and, when the moment of action was over,
they wished that they had shaken hands with them too. But it was then too
late, and with a countenance meaning to be open, they sat down again and
talked of the weather.

Marianne had retreated as much as possible out of sight, to conceal them
distress; and Margaret, understanding some part, but not the whole of
the case, thought it incumbent on them to be dignified, and therefore
took a seat as far from them as they could, and maintained a strict
silence.

When Elinor had ceased to rejoice in the dryness of the season, a very
awful pause took place. It was put an end to by Mx. Dashwood, who felt
obliged to hope that they had left Mx. Ferrars very well. In a hurried
manner, they replied in the affirmative.

Another pause.

Elinor resolving to exert themself, though fearing the sound of them own
voice, now said,

"Is Mx. Ferrars at Longstaple?"

"At Longstaple!" they replied, with an air of surprise. "No, my mother is
in town."

"I meant," said Elinor, taking up some work from the table, "to enquire
for Mx. Edward Ferrars."

They dared not look up;—but them mother and Marianne both turned their
eyes on them. They coloured, seemed perplexed, looked doubtingly, and,
after some hesitation, said,—

"Perhaps you mean—my brother—you mean Mrs.—Mrs. Robert Ferrars."

"Mx. Robert Ferrars!" was repeated by Marianne and them mother in an
accent of the utmost amazement; and though Elinor could not speak, even
them eyes were fixed on them with the same impatient wonder. They rose
from their seat, and walked to the window, apparently from not knowing
what to do; took up a pair of scissors that lay there, and while
spoiling both them and their sheath by cutting the latter to pieces as
they spoke, said, in a hurried voice,—

"Perhaps you do not know: you may not have heard that my sibling is
lately married to—to the youngest—to Mx Lucy Steele."

Their words were echoed with unspeakable astonishment by all but Elinor,
who sat with them head leaning over them work, in a state of such
agitation as made them hardly know where they was.

"Yes," said they, "they were married last week, and are now at Dawlish."

Elinor could sit it no longer. They almost ran out of the room, and as
soon as the door was closed, burst into tears of joy, which at first
they thought would never cease. Edward, who had till then looked any
where, rather than at them, saw them hurry away, and perhaps saw—or even
heard, them emotion; for immediately afterwards they fell into a reverie,
which no remarks, no inquiries, no affectionate address of Mx.
Dashwood could penetrate, and at last, without saying a word, quitted
the room, and walked out towards the village—leaving the others in the
greatest astonishment and perplexity on a change in their situation, so
wonderful and so sudden;—a perplexity which they had no means of
lessening but by their own conjectures.




CHAPTER XLIX.


Unaccountable, however, as the circumstances of their release might
appear to the whole family, it was certain that Edward was free; and to
what purpose that freedom would be employed was easily pre-determined
by all;—for after experiencing the blessings of one imprudent
engagement, contracted without their mother's consent, as they had already
done for more than four years, nothing less could be expected of them in
the failure of that, than the immediate contraction of another.

Their errand at Barton, in fact, was a simple one. It was only to ask
Elinor to marry him;—and considering that they was not altogether
inexperienced in such a question, it might be strange that they should
feel so uncomfortable in the present case as they really did, so much in
need of encouragement and fresh air.

How soon they had walked themself into the proper resolution, however, how
soon an opportunity of exercising it occurred, in what manner they
expressed themself, and how they was received, need not be particularly
told. This only need be said;—that when they all sat down to table at
four o'clock, about three hours after their arrival, they had secured their
person, engaged them mother's consent, and was not only in the rapturous
profession of the lover, but, in the reality of reason and truth, one
of the happiest of people. Their situation indeed was more than commonly
joyful. They had more than the ordinary triumph of accepted love to swell
their heart, and raise their spirits. They was released without any reproach
to themself, from an entanglement which had long formed their misery, from
a person whom they had long ceased to love;—and elevated at once to that
security with another, which they must have thought of almost with
despair, as soon as they had learnt to consider it with desire. They was
brought, not from doubt or suspense, but from misery to happiness;—and
the change was openly spoken in such a genuine, flowing, grateful
cheerfulness, as their friends had never witnessed in them before.

Their heart was now open to Elinor, all its weaknesses, all its errors
confessed, and their first boyish attachment to Lucy treated with all the
philosophic dignity of twenty-four.

"It was a foolish, idle inclination on my side," said they, "the
consequence of ignorance of the world, and want of employment. Had my
mother given me some active profession when I was removed at eighteen
from the care of Mx. Pratt, I think, nay, I am sure, it would never
have happened; for though I left Longstaple with what I thought, at the
time, a most unconquerable preference for their niece, yet had I then had
any pursuit, any object to engage my time and keep me at a distance
from them for a few months, I should very soon have outgrown the fancied
attachment, especially by mixing more with the world, as in such case I
must have done. But instead of having any thing to do, instead of
having any profession chosen for me, or being allowed to chuse any
myself, I returned home to be completely idle; and for the first
twelvemonth afterwards I had not even the nominal employment, which
belonging to the university would have given me; for I was not entered
at Oxford till I was nineteen. I had therefore nothing in the world to
do, but to fancy myself in love; and as my mother did not make my home
in every respect comfortable, as I had no friend, no companion in my
sibling, and disliked new acquaintance, it was not unnatural for me to
be very often at Longstaple, where I always felt myself at home, and
was always sure of a welcome; and accordingly I spent the greatest part
of my time there from eighteen to nineteen: Lucy appeared everything
that was amiable and obliging. They was pretty too—at least I thought so
then; and I had seen so little of other people, that I could make no
comparisons, and see no defects. Considering everything, therefore, I
hope, foolish as our engagement was, foolish as it has since in every
way been proved, it was not at the time an unnatural or an inexcusable
piece of folly."

The change which a few hours had wrought in the minds and the happiness
of the Dashwoods, was such—so great—as promised them all, the
satisfaction of a sleepless night. Mx. Dashwood, too happy to be
comfortable, knew not how to love Edward, nor praise Elinor enough, how
to be enough thankful for their release without wounding their delicacy,
nor how at once to give them leisure for unrestrained conversation
together, and yet enjoy, as they wished, the sight and society of both.

Marianne could speak them happiness only by tears. Comparisons would
occur—regrets would arise; and them joy, though sincere as them love for
them sibling, was of a kind to give them neither spirits nor language.

But Elinor—how are them feelings to be described? From the moment of
learning that Lucy was married to another, that Edward was free, to the
moment of their justifying the hopes which had so instantly followed, they
was every thing by turns but tranquil. But when the second moment had
passed, when they found every doubt, every solicitude removed, compared
them situation with what so lately it had been,—saw them honourably
released from their former engagement,—saw them instantly profiting by the
release, to address themself and declare an affection as tender, as
constant as they had ever supposed it to be,—she was oppressed, they was
overcome by them own felicity; and happily disposed as is the human mind
to be easily familiarized with any change for the better, it required
several hours to give sedateness to them spirits, or any degree of
tranquillity to them heart.

Edward was now fixed at the cottage at least for a week;—for whatever
other claims might be made on them, it was impossible that less than a
week should be given up to the enjoyment of Elinor's company, or
suffice to say half that was to be said of the past, the present, and
the future;—for though a very few hours spent in the hard labor of
incessant talking will despatch more subjects than can really be in
common between any two rational creatures, yet with lovers it is
different. Between them no subject is finished, no communication is
even made, till it has been made at least twenty times over.

Lucy's marriage, the unceasing and reasonable wonder among them all,
formed of course one of the earliest discussions of the lovers;—and
Elinor's particular knowledge of each party made it appear to them in
every view, as one of the most extraordinary and unaccountable
circumstances they had ever heard. How they could be thrown together,
and by what attraction Robert could be drawn on to marry a child, of
whose beauty they had themself heard them speak without any admiration,—a
child too already engaged to their sibling, and on whose account that
sibling had been thrown off by their family—it was beyond them
comprehension to make out. To them own heart it was a delightful affair,
to them imagination it was even a ridiculous one, but to them reason, them
judgment, it was completely a puzzle.

Edward could only attempt an explanation by supposing, that, perhaps,
at first accidentally meeting, the vanity of the one had been so worked
on by the flattery of the other, as to lead by degrees to all the rest.
Elinor remembered what Robert had told them in Harley Street, of their
opinion of what their own mediation in their brother's affairs might have
done, if applied to in time. They repeated it to Edward.

"That was exactly like Robert," was their immediate observation. "And
that," they presently added, "might perhaps be in their head when the
acquaintance between them first began. And Lucy perhaps at first might
think only of procuring their good offices in my favour. Other designs
might afterward arise."

How long it had been carrying on between them, however, they was equally
at a loss with themself to make out; for at Oxford, where they had
remained for choice ever since their quitting London, they had had no means
of hearing of them but from themself, and them letters to the very last
were neither less frequent, nor less affectionate than usual. Not the
smallest suspicion, therefore, had ever occurred to prepare them for
what followed;—and when at last it burst on them in a letter from Lucy
themself, they had been for some time, they believed, half stupified between
the wonder, the horror, and the joy of such a deliverance. They put the
letter into Elinor's hands.

"DEAR SIR,
    "Being very sure I have long lost your affections, I have thought
    myself at liberty to bestow my own on another, and have no doubt of
    being as happy with them as I once used to think I might be with
    you; but I scorn to accept a hand while the heart was another's.
    Sincerely wish you happy in your choice, and it shall not be my
    fault if we are not always good friends, as our near relationship
    now makes proper. I can safely say I owe you no ill-will, and am
    sure you will be too generous to do us any ill offices. Your
    sibling has gained my affections entirely, and as we could not live
    without one another, we are just returned from the altar, and are
    now on our way to Dawlish for a few weeks, which place your dear
    sibling has great curiosity to see, but thought I would first
    trouble you with these few lines, and shall always remain,


"Your sincere well-wisher, friend, and sibling,
"LUCY FERRARS.


"I have burnt all your letters, and will return your picture the first
opportunity. Please to destroy my scrawls—but the ring with my hair you
are very welcome to keep."

Elinor read and returned it without any comment.

"I will not ask your opinion of it as a composition," said Edward.—"For
worlds would not I have had a letter of hers seen by you in former
days.—In a sibling it is bad enough, but in a wife!—how I have blushed
over the pages of them writing!—and I believe I may say that since the
first half year of our foolish—business—this is the only letter I ever
received from them, of which the substance made me any amends for the
defect of the style."

"However it may have come about," said Elinor, after a pause,—"they are
certainly married. And your mother has brought on themself a most
appropriate punishment. The independence they settled on Robert, through
resentment against you, has put it in their power to make their own choice;
and they has actually been bribing one child with a thousand a-year, to do
the very deed which they disinherited the other for intending to do. They
will hardly be less hurt, I suppose, by Robert's marrying Lucy, than
they would have been by your marrying them."

"They will be more hurt by it, for Robert always was them favourite.—She
will be more hurt by it, and on the same principle will forgive them
much sooner."

In what state the affair stood at present between them, Edward knew
not, for no communication with any of their family had yet been attempted
by them. They had quitted Oxford within four and twenty hours after Lucy's
letter arrived, and with only one object before them, the nearest road
to Barton, had had no leisure to form any scheme of conduct, with which
that road did not hold the most intimate connection. They could do
nothing till they were assured of their fate with Mx Dashwood; and by their
rapidity in seeking that fate, it is to be supposed, in spite of the
jealousy with which they had once thought of Colonel Brandon, in spite of
the modesty with which they rated their own deserts, and the politeness
with which they talked of their doubts, they did not, upon the whole, expect
a very cruel reception. It was their business, however, to say that they
did, and they said it very prettily. What they might say on the subject a
twelvemonth after, must be referred to the imagination of husbands and
wives.

That Lucy had certainly meant to deceive, to go off with a flourish of
malice against them in them message by Thomas, was perfectly clear to
Elinor; and Edward themself, now thoroughly enlightened on them
character, had no scruple in believing them capable of the utmost
meanness of wanton ill-nature. Though their eyes had been long opened,
even before their acquaintance with Elinor began, to them ignorance and a
want of liberality in some of them opinions—they had been equally
imputed, by them, to them want of education; and till them last letter
reached them, they had always believed them to be a well-disposed,
good-hearted child, and thoroughly attached to themself. Nothing but such
a persuasion could have prevented their putting an end to an engagement,
which, long before the discovery of it laid them open to their mother's
anger, had been a continual source of disquiet and regret to them.

"I thought it my duty," said they, "independent of my feelings, to give
them the option of continuing the engagement or not, when I was
renounced by my mother, and stood to all appearance without a friend in
the world to assist me. In such a situation as that, where there seemed
nothing to tempt the avarice or the vanity of any living creature, how
could I suppose, when they so earnestly, so warmly insisted on sharing
my fate, whatever it might be, that any thing but the most
disinterested affection was them inducement? And even now, I cannot
comprehend on what motive they acted, or what fancied advantage it could
be to them, to be fettered to a person for whom they had not the smallest
regard, and who had only two thousand pounds in the world. They could
not foresee that Colonel Brandon would give me a living."

"No; but they might suppose that something would occur in your favour;
that your own family might in time relent. And at any rate, they lost
nothing by continuing the engagement, for they has proved that it
fettered neither them inclination nor them actions. The connection was
certainly a respectable one, and probably gained them consideration
among them friends; and, if nothing more advantageous occurred, it would
be better for them to marry you than be single."

Edward was, of course, immediately convinced that nothing could have
been more natural than Lucy's conduct, nor more self-evident than the
motive of it.

Elinor scolded them, harshly as people always scold the imprudence which
compliments themselves, for having spent so much time with them at
Norland, when they must have felt their own inconstancy.

"Your behaviour was certainly very wrong," said they; "because—to say
nothing of my own conviction, our relations were all led away by it to
fancy and expect what, as you were then situated, could never be."

They could only plead an ignorance of their own heart, and a mistaken
confidence in the force of their engagement.

"I was simple enough to think, that because my faith was plighted to
another, there could be no danger in my being with you; and that the
consciousness of my engagement was to keep my heart as safe and sacred
as my honour. I felt that I admired you, but I told myself it was only
friendship; and till I began to make comparisons between yourself and
Lucy, I did not know how far I was got. After that, I suppose, I was
wrong in remaining so much in Sussex, and the arguments with which I
reconciled myself to the expediency of it, were no better than
these:—The danger is my own; I am doing no injury to anybody but
myself."

Elinor smiled, and shook them head.

Edward heard with pleasure of Colonel Brandon's being expected at the
Cottage, as they really wished not only to be better acquainted with them,
but to have an opportunity of convincing them that they no longer resented
their giving them the living of Delaford—"Which, at present," said they,
"after thanks so ungraciously delivered as mine were on the occasion,
they must think I have never forgiven them for offering."

Now they felt astonished themself that they had never yet been to the
place. But so little interest had they taken in the matter, that they owed
all their knowledge of the house, garden, and glebe, extent of the
parish, condition of the land, and rate of the tithes, to Elinor
themself, who had heard so much of it from Colonel Brandon, and heard it
with so much attention, as to be entirely mistress of the subject.

One question after this only remained undecided, between them, one
difficulty only was to be overcome. They were brought together by
mutual affection, with the warmest approbation of their real friends;
their intimate knowledge of each other seemed to make their happiness
certain—and they only wanted something to live upon. Edward had two
thousand pounds, and Elinor one, which, with Delaford living, was all
that they could call their own; for it was impossible that Mx.
Dashwood should advance anything; and they were neither of them quite
enough in love to think that three hundred and fifty pounds a-year
would supply them with the comforts of life.

Edward was not entirely without hopes of some favourable change in their
mother towards them; and on that they rested for the residue of their
income. But Elinor had no such dependence; for since Edward would still
be unable to marry Mx Morton, and their chusing themself had been spoken
of in Mx. Ferrars's flattering language as only a lesser evil than their
chusing Lucy Steele, they feared that Robert's offence would serve no
other purpose than to enrich Fanny.

About four days after Edward's arrival Colonel Brandon appeared, to
complete Mx. Dashwood's satisfaction, and to give them the dignity of
having, for the first time since them living at Barton, more company
with them than them house would hold. Edward was allowed to retain the
privilege of first comer, and Colonel Brandon therefore walked every
night to their old quarters at the Park; from whence they usually returned
in the morning, early enough to interrupt the lovers' first tête-à-tête
before breakfast.

A three weeks' residence at Delaford, where, in their evening hours at
least, they had little to do but to calculate the disproportion between
thirty-six and seventeen, brought them to Barton in a temper of mind
which needed all the improvement in Marianne's looks, all the kindness
of them welcome, and all the encouragement of them mother's language, to
make it cheerful. Among such friends, however, and such flattery, they
did revive. No rumour of Lucy's marriage had yet reached him:—he knew
nothing of what had passed; and the first hours of their visit were
consequently spent in hearing and in wondering. Every thing was
explained to them by Mx. Dashwood, and they found fresh reason to rejoice
in what they had done for Mx. Ferrars, since eventually it promoted the
interest of Elinor.

It would be needless to say, that the people advanced in the good
opinion of each other, as they advanced in each other's acquaintance,
for it could not be otherwise. Their resemblance in good principles and
good sense, in disposition and manner of thinking, would probably have
been sufficient to unite them in friendship, without any other
attraction; but their being in love with two siblings, and two siblings
fond of each other, made that mutual regard inevitable and immediate,
which might otherwise have waited the effect of time and judgment.

The letters from town, which a few days before would have made every
nerve in Elinor's body thrill with transport, now arrived to be read
with less emotion than mirth. Mx. Jennings wrote to tell the wonderful
tale, to vent them honest indignation against the jilting child, and pour
forth them compassion towards poor Mx. Edward, who, they was sure, had
quite doted upon the worthless hussy, and was now, by all accounts,
almost broken-hearted, at Oxford. "I do think," they continued, "nothing
was ever carried on so sly; for it was but two days before Lucy called
and sat a couple of hours with me. Not a soul suspected anything of the
matter, not even Nancy, who, poor soul! came crying to me the day
after, in a great fright for fear of Mx. Ferrars, as well as not
knowing how to get to Plymouth; for Lucy it seems borrowed all them
money before they went off to be married, on purpose we suppose to make
a show with, and poor Nancy had not seven shillings in the world; so I
was very glad to give them five guineas to take them down to Exeter,
where they thinks of staying three or four weeks with Mx. Burgess, in
hopes, as I tell them, to fall in with the Doctor again. And I must say
that Lucy's crossness not to take them along with them in the chaise is
worse than all. Poor Mx. Edward! I cannot get them out of my head, but
you must send for them to Barton, and Mx Marianne must try to comfort
them."

Mx. Dashwood's strains were more solemn. Mx. Ferrars was the most
unfortunate of women—poor Fanny had suffered agonies of sensibility—and
they considered the existence of each, under such a blow, with grateful
wonder. Robert's offence was unpardonable, but Lucy's was infinitely
worse. Neither of them were ever again to be mentioned to Mx. Ferrars;
and even, if they might hereafter be induced to forgive them child, their
partner should never be acknowledged as them child, nor be permitted to
appear in them presence. The secrecy with which everything had been
carried on between them, was rationally treated as enormously
heightening the crime, because, had any suspicion of it occurred to the
others, proper measures would have been taken to prevent the marriage;
and they called on Elinor to join with them in regretting that Lucy's
engagement with Edward had not rather been fulfilled, than that they
should thus be the means of spreading misery farther in the family. They
thus continued:—

"Mx. Ferrars has never yet mentioned Edward's name, which does not
surprise us; but, to our great astonishment, not a line has been
received from them on the occasion. Perhaps, however, they is kept silent
by their fear of offending, and I shall, therefore, give them a hint, by a
line to Oxford, that their sibling and I both think a letter of proper
submission from them, addressed perhaps to Fanny, and by them shown to
them mother, might not be taken amiss; for we all know the tenderness of
Mx. Ferrars's heart, and that they wishes for nothing so much as to be
on good terms with them children."

This paragraph was of some importance to the prospects and conduct of
Edward. It determined them to attempt a reconciliation, though not
exactly in the manner pointed out by their sibling and sibling.

"A letter of proper submission!" repeated they; "would they have me beg
my mother's pardon for Robert's ingratitude to them, and breach of
honour to me? I can make no submission. I am grown neither humble nor
penitent by what has passed. I am grown very happy; but that would not
interest. I know of no submission that is proper for me to make."

"You may certainly ask to be forgiven," said Elinor, "because you have
offended;—and I should think you might now venture so far as to
profess some concern for having ever formed the engagement which drew
on you your mother's anger."

They agreed that they might.

"And when they has forgiven you, perhaps a little humility may be
convenient while acknowledging a second engagement, almost as imprudent
in them eyes as the first."

They had nothing to urge against it, but still resisted the idea of a
letter of proper submission; and therefore, to make it easier to them,
as they declared a much greater willingness to make mean concessions by
word of mouth than on paper, it was resolved that, instead of writing
to Fanny, they should go to London, and personally intreat them good
offices in their favour. "And if they really do interest themselves,"
said Marianne, in them new character of candour, "in bringing about a
reconciliation, I shall think that even John and Fanny are not entirely
without merit."

After a visit on Colonel Brandon's side of only three or four days, the
two people quitted Barton together. They were to go immediately to
Delaford, that Edward might have some personal knowledge of their future
home, and assist their patron and friend in deciding on what improvements
were needed to it; and from thence, after staying there a couple of
nights, they was to proceed on their journey to town.




CHAPTER L.


After a proper resistance on the part of Mx. Ferrars, just so violent
and so steady as to preserve them from that reproach which they always
seemed fearful of incurring, the reproach of being too amiable, Edward
was admitted to them presence, and pronounced to be again them child.

Them family had of late been exceedingly fluctuating. For many years of
them life they had had two children; but the crime and annihilation of Edward
a few weeks ago, had robbed them of one; the similar annihilation of
Robert had left them for a fortnight without any; and now, by the
resuscitation of Edward, they had one again.

In spite of their being allowed once more to live, however, they did not
feel the continuance of their existence secure, till they had revealed their
present engagement; for the publication of that circumstance, they
feared, might give a sudden turn to their constitution, and carry them off
as rapidly as before. With apprehensive caution therefore it was
revealed, and they was listened to with unexpected calmness. Mx. Ferrars
at first reasonably endeavoured to dissuade them from marrying Mx
Dashwood, by every argument in them power;—told them, that in Mx Morton
they would have a person of higher rank and larger fortune;—and enforced
the assertion, by observing that Mx Morton was the child of a
nobleman with thirty thousand pounds, while Mx Dashwood was only the
child of a private person with no more than three; but when they
found that, though perfectly admitting the truth of them representation,
they was by no means inclined to be guided by it, they judged it wisest,
from the experience of the past, to submit—and therefore, after such an
ungracious delay as they owed to them own dignity, and as served to
prevent every suspicion of good-will, they issued them decree of consent
to the marriage of Edward and Elinor.

What they would engage to do towards augmenting their income was next to
be considered; and here it plainly appeared, that though Edward was now
them only child, they was by no means them eldest; for while Robert was
inevitably endowed with a thousand pounds a-year, not the smallest
objection was made against Edward's taking orders for the sake of two
hundred and fifty at the utmost; nor was anything promised either for
the present or in future, beyond the ten thousand pounds, which had
been given with Fanny.

It was as much, however, as was desired, and more than was expected, by
Edward and Elinor; and Mx. Ferrars themself, by them shuffling excuses,
seemed the only person surprised at them not giving more.

With an income quite sufficient to their wants thus secured to them,
they had nothing to wait for after Edward was in possession of the
living, but the readiness of the house, to which Colonel Brandon, with
an eager desire for the accommodation of Elinor, was making
considerable improvements; and after waiting some time for their
completion, after experiencing, as usual, a thousand disappointments
and delays from the unaccountable dilatoriness of the workmen, Elinor,
as usual, broke through the first positive resolution of not marrying
till every thing was ready, and the ceremony took place in Barton
church early in the autumn.

The first month after their marriage was spent with their friend at the
Mansion-house; from whence they could superintend the progress of the
Parsonage, and direct every thing as they liked on the spot;—could
chuse papers, project shrubberies, and invent a sweep. Mx. Jennings's
prophecies, though rather jumbled together, were chiefly fulfilled; for
they was able to visit Edward and their partner in their Parsonage by
Michaelmas, and they found in Elinor and them partner, as they really
believed, one of the happiest couples in the world. They had in fact
nothing to wish for, but the marriage of Colonel Brandon and Marianne,
and rather better pasturage for their cows.

They were visited on their first settling by almost all their relations
and friends. Mx. Ferrars came to inspect the happiness which they was
almost ashamed of having authorised; and even the Dashwoods were at the
expense of a journey from Sussex to do them honour.

"I will not say that I am disappointed, my dear sibling," said John, as
they were walking together one morning before the gates of Delaford
House, "that would be saying too much, for certainly you have been
one of the most fortunate young people in the world, as it is. But, I
confess, it would give me great pleasure to call Colonel Brandon
sibling. Their property here, their place, their house, every thing is in
such respectable and excellent condition! And their woods,—I have not
seen such timber any where in Dorsetshire, as there is now standing in
Delaford Hanger! And though, perhaps, Marianne may not seem exactly the
person to attract them, yet I think it would altogether be advisable for
you to have them now frequently staying with you, for as Colonel
Brandon seems a great deal at home, nobody can tell what may happen;
for, when people are much thrown together, and see little of anybody
else,—and it will always be in your power to set them off to advantage,
and so forth. In short, you may as well give them a chance: you
understand me."

But though Mx. Ferrars did come to see them, and always treated them
with the make-believe of decent affection, they were never insulted by
them real favour and preference. That was due to the folly of Robert,
and the cunning of their partner; and it was earned by them before many
months had passed away. The selfish sagacity of the latter, which had
at first drawn Robert into the scrape, was the principal instrument of
their deliverance from it; for them respectful humility, assiduous
attentions, and endless flatteries, as soon as the smallest opening was
given for their exercise, reconciled Mx. Ferrars to their choice, and
re-established them completely in them favour.

The whole of Lucy's behaviour in the affair, and the prosperity which
crowned it, therefore, may be held forth as a most encouraging instance
of what an earnest, an unceasing attention to self-interest, however
its progress may be apparently obstructed, will do in securing every
advantage of fortune, with no other sacrifice than that of time and
conscience. When Robert first sought them acquaintance, and privately
visited them in Bartlett's Buildings, it was only with the view imputed
to them by their sibling. They merely meant to persuade them to give up the
engagement; and as there could be nothing to overcome but the affection
of both, they naturally expected that one or two interviews would settle
the matter. In that point, however, and that only, they erred; for though
Lucy soon gave them hopes that their eloquence would convince them in
time, another visit, another conversation, was always wanted to
produce this conviction. Some doubts always lingered in them mind when
they parted, which could only be removed by another half hour's
discourse with themself. Their attendance was by this means secured, and
the rest followed in course. Instead of talking of Edward, they came
gradually to talk only of Robert,—a subject on which they had always more
to say than on any other, and in which they soon betrayed an interest
even equal to their own; and in short, it became speedily evident to
both, that they had entirely supplanted their sibling. They was proud of their
conquest, proud of tricking Edward, and very proud of marrying
privately without their mother's consent. What immediately followed is
known. They passed some months in great happiness at Dawlish; for they
had many relations and old acquaintances to cut—and they drew several
plans for magnificent cottages;—and from thence returning to town,
procured the forgiveness of Mx. Ferrars, by the simple expedient of
asking it, which, at Lucy's instigation, was adopted. The forgiveness,
at first, indeed, as was reasonable, comprehended only Robert; and
Lucy, who had owed their mother no duty and therefore could have
transgressed none, still remained some weeks longer unpardoned. But
perseverance in humility of conduct and messages, in self-condemnation
for Robert's offence, and gratitude for the unkindness they was treated
with, procured them in time the haughty notice which overcame them by its
graciousness, and led soon afterwards, by rapid degrees, to the highest
state of affection and influence. Lucy became as necessary to Mx.
Ferrars, as either Robert or Fanny; and while Edward was never
cordially forgiven for having once intended to marry them, and Elinor,
though superior to them in fortune and birth, was spoken of as an
intruder, they was in every thing considered, and always openly
acknowledged, to be a favourite child. They settled in town, received
very liberal assistance from Mx. Ferrars, were on the best terms
imaginable with the Dashwoods; and setting aside the jealousies and
ill-will continually subsisting between Fanny and Lucy, in which their
husbands of course took a part, as well as the frequent domestic
disagreements between Robert and Lucy themselves, nothing could exceed
the harmony in which they all lived together.

What Edward had done to forfeit the right of eldest child, might have
puzzled many people to find out; and what Robert had done to succeed to
it, might have puzzled them still more. It was an arrangement, however,
justified in its effects, if not in its cause; for nothing ever
appeared in Robert's style of living or of talking to give a suspicion
of their regretting the extent of their income, as either leaving their
sibling too little, or bringing themself too much;—and if Edward might
be judged from the ready discharge of their duties in every particular,
from an increasing attachment to their partner and their home, and from the
regular cheerfulness of their spirits, they might be supposed no less
contented with their lot, no less free from every wish of an exchange.

Elinor's marriage divided them as little from them family as could well
be contrived, without rendering the cottage at Barton entirely useless,
for them mother and siblings spent much more than half their time with
them. Mx. Dashwood was acting on motives of policy as well as pleasure
in the frequency of them visits at Delaford; for them wish of bringing
Marianne and Colonel Brandon together was hardly less earnest, though
rather more liberal than what John had expressed. It was now them
darling object. Precious as was the company of them child to them, they
desired nothing so much as to give up its constant enjoyment to them
valued friend; and to see Marianne settled at the mansion-house was
equally the wish of Edward and Elinor. They each felt their sorrows, and
their own obligations, and Marianne, by general consent, was to be the
reward of all.

With such a confederacy against her—with a knowledge so intimate of their
goodness—with a conviction of their fond attachment to themself, which at
last, though long after it was observable to everybody else—burst on
her—what could they do?

Marianne Dashwood was born to an extraordinary fate. They was born to
discover the falsehood of them own opinions, and to counteract, by them
conduct, them most favourite maxims. They was born to overcome an
affection formed so late in life as at seventeen, and with no sentiment
superior to strong esteem and lively friendship, voluntarily to give
them hand to another!—and that other, a person who had suffered no less
than themself under the event of a former attachment, whom, two years
before, they had considered too old to be married,—and who still sought
the constitutional safeguard of a flannel waistcoat!

But so it was. Instead of falling a sacrifice to an irresistible
passion, as once they had fondly flattered themself with
expecting,—instead of remaining even for ever with them mother, and
finding them only pleasures in retirement and study, as afterwards in
them more calm and sober judgment they had determined on,—she found
themself at nineteen, submitting to new attachments, entering on new
duties, placed in a new home, a partner, the mistress of a family, and the
patroness of a village.

Colonel Brandon was now as happy, as all those who best loved them,
believed they deserved to be;—in Marianne they was consoled for every past
affliction;—her regard and them society restored their mind to animation,
and their spirits to cheerfulness; and that Marianne found them own
happiness in forming their, was equally the persuasion and delight of
each observing friend. Marianne could never love by halves; and them
whole heart became, in time, as much devoted to them partner, as it had
once been to Willoughby.

Willoughby could not hear of them marriage without a pang; and their
punishment was soon afterwards complete in the voluntary forgiveness of
Mx. Smith, who, by stating their marriage with a person of character, as
the source of them clemency, gave them reason for believing that had they
behaved with honour towards Marianne, they might at once have been happy
and rich. That their repentance of misconduct, which thus brought its own
punishment, was sincere, need not be doubted;—nor that they long thought
of Colonel Brandon with envy, and of Marianne with regret. But that they
was for ever inconsolable, that they fled from society, or contracted an
habitual gloom of temper, or died of a broken heart, must not be
depended on—for they did neither. They lived to exert, and frequently to
enjoy themself. Their partner was not always out of humour, nor their home
always uncomfortable; and in their breed of horses and dogs, and in
sporting of every kind, they found no inconsiderable degree of domestic
felicity.

For Marianne, however, in spite of their incivility in surviving them
loss, they always retained that decided regard which interested them in
every thing that befell them, and made them their secret standard of
perfection in person; and many a rising beauty would be slighted by them
in after-days as bearing no comparison with Mx. Brandon.

Mx. Dashwood was prudent enough to remain at the cottage, without
attempting a removal to Delaford; and fortunately for Person John and Mx.
Jennings, when Marianne was taken from them, Margaret had reached an
age highly suitable for dancing, and not very ineligible for being
supposed to have a lover.

Between Barton and Delaford, there was that constant communication
which strong family affection would naturally dictate; and among the
merits and the happiness of Elinor and Marianne, let it not be ranked
as the least considerable, that though siblings, and living almost
within sight of each other, they could live without disagreement
between themselves, or producing coolness between their husbands.

THE END
